Refutation of Qadariyya's 'Take It with Strength' Proof
Maturidi refutes Qadariyya's proof from 'Take it with strength'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Qadariyya's argument that 'strength' in the Quranic command implies independent human power preceding the act.

Core Idea
The strength to act is simultaneous with the act, not prior to it.

The Qadariyya claimed that the Quranic command 'Take it with strength' proves that humans possess an independent power to act before the act occurs. Maturidi examines this verse closely and shows that the apparent meaning actually supports the opposite view: that power is present only at the moment of action. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the analogy he uses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the flow from the Qadariyya argument to Maturidi's counter.
Pay attention to the key term 'quwwa' (strength).
2
Identify the Qadariyya's argument
The Qadariyya used the verse 'Take it with strength' to argue that power (qudra) precedes the act. See how they interpreted 'strength' as independent ability.
3
Understand Maturidi's reinterpretation
Maturidi says 'strength' refers to the time of taking, not a pre-existing power. He uses an analogy of taking with hands and looking with eyes to show simultaneity.
The analogy is key: the command implies the act and its power occur together.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this refutes the Qadariyya position and supports the Ahl al-Sunna view that power is created at the moment of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم احتجت القدرية في تقديم القدرة الفعل بآي من كتاب الله تعالى، منها قوله: فخذها بقوة. وقال أهل التأويل فاعمل بها بجد واجتهاد؛ فكأنهم رأوا القوة ههنا الأسباب. لكن الظاهر من ذلك قولنا: خذها بقوة، أي وقت الأخذ لأنها إذا لم تكن في وقت الأخذ يكون الأخذ بلا قوة؛ فثبت به الذي نذهب. كمن يقول لآخر: خذه بيديك وانظر إليه ببصرك، فهو على الالتقاء؛ وعلى ذلك قوله لموسى: فخذها بقوة وامر قومك يأخذوا بأحسنها.
Then the Qadariyya argued for the precedence of power over the act with verses from the Book of God, among them His saying: 'Take it with strength.' And the commentators said: act with it with seriousness and effort; as if they considered strength here to mean means. But the apparent meaning of that is our saying: 'Take it with strength,' meaning at the time of taking, because if it is not at the time of taking, the taking would be without strength; so what we hold is established by it. Like one who says to another: 'Take it with your hands and look at it with your eyes,' that is upon meeting; and accordingly His saying to Moses: 'Take it with strength and command your people to take the best of it.'
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a Qadariyya argument. The Qadariyya used the Quranic command 'Take it with strength' to argue that humans have independent power (quwwa) to perform acts. Maturidi counters that 'strength' here refers to the time of taking the action, not a pre-existing power. He uses an analogy: if you tell someone to take something with his hands and look with his eyes, that is upon the very action of taking and looking, not a separate power. Thus the verse supports that power is simultaneous with the act, not prior, which agrees with Maturidi's view (that power is created at the moment of action).

Key Terms
القدرة
الفعل
القوة
التقديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Quranic command 'Take it with strength' proves humans have independent power (qudra) prior to performing an act.
Correction: Maturidi shows that 'strength' in the verse refers to the time of the act itself, not a pre-existing power. The power to act is simultaneous with the action.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the phrase 'Take it with strength' really mean?
A) It commands using inherent power
B) It indicates power at the moment of action
C) It means physical strength
D) It refers to divine strength

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command 'Take it with strength' actually proves that power is simultaneous with the act, not prior to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I will do this with my own strength,' do you truly believe that the strength to act is given by God at that moment, or do you assume you have an independent reserve of power? Reflect on a recent action you took with confidence. Did you consciously acknowledge that the strength to perform it was only present at the time of action? How might this shift your understanding of tawakkul?
?
The Qadariyya used a Quranic verse to support their view that humans have independent power prior to the act. Are there any verses or phrases you commonly use to justify your own sense of autonomy or self-sufficiency? What would Maturidi's refutation say to you about those interpretations?
?
If the strength to act is given only at the moment of acting, then what about your past sins? Can you truly say 'I had the power to avoid it'? Or does this lesson force you to reconsider your view of human responsibility? How does this affect your repentance?

Power Must Be at the Time of Action
Maturidi argues that strength must be at the time of taking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that power must be contemporaneous with the action and how he rebuts scriptural objections.

Core Idea
Strength is not a stored fuel; it must be present at the moment of action.

Maturidi tackles a key theological principle: when must an agent's power exist? In this passage, he argues that true strength (quwwa) is only meaningful if it coincides with the act. He uses a Quranic command and responds to counterexamples to establish his view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the concise style of Maturidi's reasoning.
2
Identify the main principle
The passage opens with interpreting 'Take it with strength' as meaning strength at the time of taking. This is the central claim.
Contrast this with the idea of strength existing before the action.
3
Examine the analogy and Quranic support
Maturidi gives an analogy (take with hands, look with eyes) and cites the Moses verse to support simultaneity.
Both illustrate that power must be present at the moment of action.
4
See how Maturidi counters opponents
Opponents cite verses about the jinn and the woman. Maturidi rebuts by showing those refer to strength known at a past time, not concurrent with the action.
This is the key refutation.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Think about why this matters for kalam: if power could exist before action, an agent could act without it, undermining causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن الظاهر من ذلك قولنا : خذها بقوة، أي وقت الأخذ لأنها إذا لم تكن في وقت الأخذ يكون الأخذ بلا قوة؛ فثبت به الذي نذهب. كمن يقول لآخر: خذه بيديك وانظر إليه ببصرك، فهو على الالتقاء؛ وعلى ذلك قوله لموسى: فخذها بقوة وامر قومك يأخذوا بأحسنها [الأعراف، 145/7]. واحتجوا أيضا بقول الجنّي: وإني عليه لقوي أمين، وقول المرأة: إن خير من استأجرت القوي الأمين [القصص، 26/28]. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: والحرفان مما ليس لهم التعلق به، لما كانت قوة موسى التي علمت بها إنما علمتها وقت النزح، وهي لا تبقى إلى ذلك الوقت؛ وكذلك قوة الجنّي على ما امتحن نفسه فيما سبق. والله الموفق.
But the apparent meaning of that is our saying: 'Take it with strength,' meaning the time of taking, because if it [strength] is not at the time of taking, the taking is without strength; so what we hold is established by it. Like one who says to another: 'Take it with your hands and look at it with your sight,' so it is upon encounter. And accordingly is His saying to Moses: 'Take it with strength and command your people to take the best thereof' [al-A'raf, 7:145]. And they also argued by the saying of the jinn: 'Indeed I am strong and trustworthy' and the saying of the woman: 'Indeed the best of those you hire is the strong and trustworthy' [al-Qasas, 28:26]. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Both expressions are not a valid attachment for them, because the strength of Moses that you knew was only known at the time of drawing water, and it does not remain until that time; likewise the strength of the jinn concerning what he tested himself in earlier. And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. Maturidi explains the principle that power (quwwa) must be contemporaneous with the action. He begins by interpreting the command 'Take it with strength' to mean at the time of taking, not a potential strength before. If strength were not present at the time of action, the action would be without strength, which is absurd. He gives an analogy: when you tell someone to take something with his hands and look with his eyes, this implies simultaneous use of the limbs. Then he cites the Quranic verse to Moses (take the tablets with strength) as support. Opponents cite two other verses: the jinn saying he is strong and trustworthy, and the woman saying to hire the strong and trustworthy. Maturidi rebuts: Moses' strength was known at the time of drawing water, not enduring to a later time; similarly the jinn's strength was in a past incident. Hence these verses do not prove strength existing before the action. The lesson concludes with 'Allah is the grantor of success'.

Key Terms
قوة
وقت الأخذ
النزح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Strength can exist as a latent capacity before the action.
Correction: Maturidi argues strength must be present at the moment of action, otherwise the action would be without strength.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main argument in this passage?
A) Strength must be present before the action.
B) Strength must be present at the time of action.
C) Strength is irrelevant to action.
D) Strength is only metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi establishes that power must be simultaneous with the action, refuting the notion of a stored capacity.

The Second Interpretation: Istita'a at the Time of Usage
Maturidi presents a second interpretation regarding the time of usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's second interpretation that capability (istita'a) is intended for the time of usage, not an autonomous power before the action.

Core Idea
Capability is granted by Allah at the moment of action, not before.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a second interpretation of the concept of power, focusing on its timing. He argues that the power (qudra) that creation possesses is intended for the time of using it, following the customary way Allah brings things into existence at each moment. This guards against the misconception that creatures have independent power stored before the action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words guide your understanding.
Notice the repetition of 'la quwwata illa billah'.
2
Identify the second interpretation
Maturidi states that the second interpretation is 'upon intending the time of usage'. Consider what this means for the timing of power.
3
Understand the custom mentioned
The phrase 'according to the prevailing custom of coming into existence at every time as He wills' connects to the Ash'ari concept of continuous creation. Reflect on how this supports Maturidi's point.
4
Note the opponents' argument and Maturidi's response
Opponents cite Quranic verses about istita'a. Maturidi says he has already clarified that aspect. The final phrase underscores that all power belongs to Allah.
This shows Maturidi's confidence in his earlier refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني على إرادة وقت الاستعمال، بالعادة الجارية بالحدوث في كل وقت لما شاء ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقد احتجوا بما في القرآن من ذكر الاستطاعة، وقد بيّنا ذلك الوجه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the second [interpretation] is upon intending the time of usage, according to the prevailing custom of coming into existence at every time as He wills, and there is no power except with Allah. And they have argued by what is in the Quran of mentioning capability (al-istita'a), and we have clarified that aspect. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a continuation and a second interpretation. Maturidi offers another way to understand the need for power: it is intended for the time of using something (isti'mal). This follows the usual custom that things come into existence at the time God wills. He then mentions that opponents have argued using Quranic verses about 'istita'a' (capability), but Maturidi says he has already clarified that point (perhaps in an earlier section). He ends with the phrase 'There is no power except with Allah,' emphasizing divine omnipotence.

Key Terms
وقت الاستعمال (time of usage)
الاستطاعة (capability / istita'a)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That capability (istita'a) implies a power that exists before the action, independent of Allah.
Correction: Maturidi explains that capability is intended for the time of usage, meaning it is granted at the moment of action, not stored beforehand. All power comes from Allah at each instant.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say the second interpretation is about?
A) Strength before creation.
B) Power at the time of usage.
C) Power after the action.
D) Power in the past.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that capability (istita'a) is granted by Allah at the time of its use, not as an independent power preceding the action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you perform your next action, do you assume the ability is yours or that Allah gives it at that instant? What would change if you consciously affirmed 'la quwwata illa billah' right before each deed?
?
Think of a moment you felt proud of your own capability. How does Maturidi's teaching that istita'a is only at the time of usage challenge that pride? Will you now attribute every success to Allah's grant of ability?
?
If capability is not stored but granted moment by moment, how should this affect your planning and reliance on Allah? What specific step can you take today to live this truth in a situation you feel underprepared for?

Maturidi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila Definition of Will
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila concept of will as prior choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that will (irada) is choice (ikhtiyar) preceding the act, and why that view leads to coercion (idtirar).

Core Idea
If will disappears at the moment of action, the act is coerced, not chosen.

Maturidi presents a sharp logical argument against the Mu'tazila definition of will. The Mu'tazila claim that will is choice that exists only before the act, vanishing at the time of action. Maturidi shows that this position undermines free will, because if will is absent when the act occurs, then the act happens without choice—making it coerced. In this passage, he establishes that their definition collapses into its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the logical connectors like 'إذ' (because) and 'محال' (impossible).
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Find where Maturidi states the Mu'tazila definition: will is choice that precedes the act and is not present at the act.
This is the premise that will be refuted.
3
Follow the refutation steps
Trace how Maturidi argues that if will is only prior, the act occurs without will or choice, and prior choice is gone. Then coercion becomes possible at the time of the act.
Pay attention to the contrast between the two times (prior and second).
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the act is not by choice but by coercion, which refutes the Mu'tazila position.
This demonstrates the self-contradiction in their view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر، أن قول المعتزلة : إن الإرادة هي اختيار الفعل وإنما تكون متقدمة على الفعل؛ وليست بموجودة، وأنه وجد في وقت الوجود بلا إرادة منه ولا اختيار؛ وحق اختيار الأول عنه زائل، إذ يجوز ورود الاضطرار في الوقت الثاني، ومحال وروده في الوقت الذي فيه الاختيار، والاختيار قائم؛ ثبت أن فعله في التحصيل ليس باختيار، وأنه اضطرار؛
And another aspect: that the saying of the Mu'tazila that 'will (al-irada) is the choice (ikhtiyar) of the act, and it is only prior to the act; and it is not existent [at the time of the act], and that [the act] occurred at the time of existence without any will from him nor choice; and the right of the prior choice from him is gone, because coercion (idtirar) can occur in the second time, and it is impossible for it to occur in the time in which choice exists and choice is present; it is established that his act in attainment is not by choice, and that it is coercion.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of the Mu'tazila position on the will. The Mu'tazila define will (irada) as choice (ikhtiyar) that precedes the act; they claim that at the moment of the act, will is not present. Maturidi points out the contradiction: if the will is only prior and vanishes at the time of the act, then the act occurs without will or choice. Since the prior choice is no longer present, and the agent may be subject to coercion in the second moment (the time of the act), it is impossible for the act to be truly voluntary. Therefore, the act is in fact coerced (idtirar), not chosen. This destroys their claim of free will.

Key Terms
الإرادة (irada / will)
الاختيار (ikhtiyar / choice)
الاضطرار (idtirar / coercion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila concept of will as prior choice guarantees free will.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if will is only prior, the act occurs without will, making it coerced.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude about the Mu'tazila position on will?
A) It guarantees free will.
B) It leads to coercion.
C) It is the same as the Ash'ari view.
D) It is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the Mu'tazila definition of will as prior choice logically entails coercion, thereby refuting their claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a past sin by saying 'I intended to stop, but when the time came I just did it'? According to Maturidi's refutation, that excuse implies you believe your will was only prior and not present in the act. Does that mean your act was actually coerced? What does that say about the sincerity of your repentance?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending free will by making will prior to the act. Maturidi shows it leads to the opposite. In your own life, have you ever claimed you 'chose' something but at the moment of action felt powerless? Is your everyday experience of choice more like the Mu'tazila definition or Maturidi's requirement of concurrent will?
?
Maturidi argues that if will disappears at the act, the act is coerced. Consider a habit you struggle with: you intend to avoid it, but when the moment comes, you act automatically. Is that act truly yours? What would it mean to have your will present in that moment, and are you willing to cultivate that presence?

The Second Way: Why Shirk Deserves Eternal Punishment
Maturidi argues shirk deserves eternal punishment via proportional justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that shirk uniquely deserves eternal punishment due to its magnitude and lack of redeeming good deeds.

Core Idea
Shirk's eternal punishment is the only just recompense for a sin with no good deeds.

Maturidi continues his argument by focusing on proportional justice. He establishes that shirk, being the greatest sin and completely devoid of good deeds, cannot be punished proportionally within time. This passage presents a logical proof that only eternal punishment fits the crime of shirk.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument from a divine promise and rational comparison.
Pay attention to the phrase 'like of shirk' and the absence of good deeds.
2
Identify the key premises
Find the two main premises: (1) God promised to recompense with the like of the deed, and (2) shirk is the greatest sin with no good deeds. Then see how these lead to eternal punishment.
3
Reflect on the counterfactual
Consider Maturidi's hypothetical: if a lesser sin received the same eternal punishment, that would be unjust. This shows why shirk is unique.
Think about why injustice in wisdom is impossible for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الله جل ثناؤه وعد أن لا يجزى إلا مثلها، ومثل الشرك الذي في العقل أكبر من كل ذنب، مع ما لا حسنة تكون معه و [ تكون] مع غيره ، إنما [ جزاؤه] هو الخلود في النار. إذ معلوم أن الكافر يرضى بأضعاف ما يعذب مع النجاة يوما من الدهر، فيبين ذلك أن تمام جزائه الخلود. فإذا كان لغيره مثله فيُجْزَى غيره أكثر من مثل الفعل، وذلك جور في حكمة ، والله يَجِلّ عنه. فهذا مع ما كان لمرتكب ما دونه حسنات، وليس معه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And the second: that Allah, exalted be His praise, promised that He will not recompense except with its like, and the like of shirk, which in the intellect is greater than every sin, along with there being no good deed with it while [good deeds] are with others; rather, its recompense is eternal abiding in the Fire. For it is known that the disbeliever would be content with multiples of what he is punished with along with salvation for a day of time, so that shows that the completion of his recompense is eternity. If for other than him there is its like, then other than him would be recompensed with more than the like of the act, and that is injustice in wisdom, and Allah is exalted above that. So this is while the committer of less than shirk has good deeds, but with shirk there are none. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents the second way of distinguishing sins. The author argues based on God's promise to recompense only with the like of the deed. Shirk, in reason, is greater than any other sin, and it has no accompanying good deeds. Therefore, its just recompense is eternal damnation. The author explains: even if a disbeliever were offered multiple times the punishment he deserves along with eventual salvation, he would accept it, indicating that his complete punishment must be eternal to match the enormity of shirk. If a non-shirk sinner received the same 'like' punishment, it would be more than the like of the act (since the act is less severe), which would be unjust. So shirk uniquely demands eternal punishment, while lesser sins, having good deeds alongside, do not.

Key Terms
الشرك (shirk)
الخلود (eternity)
المثل (the like)
الجور (injustice)
الحكمة (wisdom)
الحسنات (good deeds)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin deserves eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi restricts eternal punishment to shirk because only shirk is the greatest sin and has no redeeming good deeds; lesser sins have good deeds that can mitigate punishment.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say that shirk requires eternal punishment?
A) Because God is vengeful.
B) Because shirk is the greatest sin and has no good deeds with it.
C) Because the disbeliever would be content with eternal punishment.
D) Because other sins have like punishments.

Key Takeaway
Shirk uniquely deserves eternal punishment because it is the greatest sin and has no accompanying good deeds, making it impossible to punish proportionally within time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You were just told that shirk is the only sin with no good deeds alongside it. Is there any attachment in your heart—like a habit, a relationship, or a worldly comfort—that competes with your devotion to Allah? If so, what concrete step will you take today to prioritize His right over your desire?
?
The disbeliever would accept multiplied pain to escape Hell even for a day. You have committed sins less than shirk, but do you truly fear the punishment of Allah, or do you rely on your good deeds as insurance? What would it mean for you to live with a constant awareness that only Allah's mercy, not your deeds, guarantees salvation?
?
This lesson argues that shirk's eternal punishment is just, not arbitrary. If you ever minimize shirk by calling it 'only a small sin,' will you repent immediately and remove that thought? What one change in your daily life will you make to guard against both major and subtle forms of shirk starting today?

Maturidi's Argument for Specificity in Ambiguous Terms
Maturidi argues for specificity (takhsis) when a term has multiple possible meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical reasoning for directing ambiguous terms to a specific meaning and the attitude of fear (khawf) over certainty (qat) when ambiguity remains.

Core Idea
When a term has multiple distant meanings, specificity must be presumed to avoid contradiction.

Maturidi addresses a key methodological issue in kalam: how to interpret a term that can mean multiple things, especially in theological contexts. He argues that it is irrational to intend two far apart meanings at once, and that the correct approach is to specify the meaning that is free from doubt. This passage presents a tight logical argument for specificity (takhsis) and warns against claiming certainty where ambiguity exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the translation
Read the Arabic text and the literal English translation together. Notice the repeated phrase 'La quwwata illa billah' which punctuates the argument.
This phrase signals reliance on divine power in this intellectual exercise.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument unfolds in stages: first a general principle, then a division into three cases, then a refutation of generality, and finally a conclusion about the appropriate attitude.
Maturidi builds his case step by step, so trace each 'if-then' clause.
3
Understand the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'khusus' (specificity), 'umum' (generality), 'qat' (certainty), and 'khawf' (fear). See how they relate to each other in the argument.
These are technical terms in usul al-fiqh and kalam.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi's final point is that when a term admits both specific and general meanings, the proper stance is fear (khawf), not certainty (qat). Ask yourself why certainty would be unwise.
This shows Maturidi's cautious, evidence-based approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن البعيد قصد شيئين معا بينهما هذا التباعد، فحق ذلك أن يُصرف إلى ما لا يرتاب في الاسم والحكم. وإذ كان على أقسام ثلاثة ولم يتضمن كل الأقسام ثبت الخصوص في ذلك، فلزم صرف ذلك إلى ما لا يشك فيه. ولا قوة إلا بالله على أن في الصرف إلى العموم يحقق له التناقض لمجيء أخبار العفو، فثبت بذلك الخصوص. ولا قوة إلا بالله. أو إذا احتمل الخصوص والعموم بما ينقسم المسمى به ما ذكرت فحق مثله الخوف لا القطع، فمن قطع خرج مما يوجبه الحكمة عند الشبه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And it is far-fetched to intend two things together between which there is this distance, so the right thing is to direct it to what is not doubted in name and judgment. And since it was upon three divisions and did not encompass all the divisions, specificity (al-khusūs) is established in that, so it is necessary to direct it to what is not doubted in it. And there is no power except with Allah. That in directing to generality (al-ʿumūm) realizes for him contradiction because of the reports of pardon, so specificity is established by that. And there is no power except with Allah. Or if it admits specificity and generality by which the named thing is divided as I mentioned, then the right of the like of it is fear (al-khawf), not certainty (al-qaṭʿ). So whoever cuts off departs from what wisdom necessitates at the time of ambiguity. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument for specificity (takhsīs) in interpreting terms that can have multiple meanings, particularly in theological contexts. Maturidi begins by stating that it is far-fetched to intend two meanings that are far apart when a term can refer to both. Instead, the term should be directed to what is not doubted in name and judgment. He then notes that if the term has three possible divisions but does not cover all, specificity is established, necessitating a restricted interpretation. He warns that interpreting generally leads to contradiction due to revealed texts about pardon, so specificity is confirmed. Alternatively, if the term admits both specific and general meanings, the appropriate attitude is fear (khawf) rather than certainty (qaṭʿ), as certainty would depart from wisdom in ambiguous matters. The argument emphasizes caution and reliance on clear evidence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a term has multiple meanings, it must be taken in all senses simultaneously.
Correction: Maturidi argues that intending two distant meanings together is far-fetched, and that specificity (takhsis) should be applied to avoid contradiction and align with revealed texts.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the appropriate attitude when a term admits both specific and general meanings?
A) Certainty (qaṭʿ)
B) Fear (khawf)
C) Indifference
D) Acceptance of both

Key Takeaway
In the face of ambiguous terms, Maturidi directs us to adopt specificity (takhsīs) and a posture of fear (khawf), not certainty (qaṭʿ), to remain within the bounds of wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt absolutely certain about a theological or legal matter, only to later realize you were overlooking ambiguity? In light of Maturidi's lesson, will you now examine such certainties and replace them with fear (khawf)? What specific belief are you holding too tightly?
?
Are there verses or hadiths you have interpreted as general (ʿāmm) despite clear evidence of specification? Will you commit to seeking specific meanings to avoid contradiction with other texts? Which text comes to mind right now?
?
Maturidi says claiming certainty at the time of ambiguity departs from wisdom. Is there an issue in your faith or practice where you have been 'cutting off' rather than adopting a posture of fear? Will you today take one step toward that fear—perhaps by consulting a scholar or suspending judgment?

Religion is Belief: Maturidi's Hadith Argument
Maturidi argues from the hadith that dīn is iʿtiqād only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that religion (dīn) is solely belief (iʿtiqād), based on the hadith about changing religion.

Core Idea
Religion is defined by belief, not actions, because actions change constantly.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi uses a powerful textual argument from a prophetic hadith to establish the nature of religion (dīn). He shows that the punishment for changing religion only makes sense if religion is essentially belief, not outward actions. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and verse
Start by reading the source passage carefully. Note the hadith: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' Then notice the verse cited: 'Whoever disbelieves in the taghut and believes in God.'
The hadith sets the stage for a logical inquiry into what 'religion' means.
2
Follow the logical test
Maturidi presents a test: if a person is asked 'Are you a Muslim?' and says 'No,' they have changed religion; if 'Yes,' they become Muslim by faith alone. See how this frames religion as belief.
The question hinges on a verbal acknowledgement of faith, not actions.
3
Understand the reductio argument
Maturidi then argues: if actions were part of religion, then every person would be constantly changing religion because their actions vary. This absurdity proves that religion is exclusively belief.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum – if a premise leads to an absurd consequence, the premise must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : من بدل دينه فاقتلوه ، ثم بيّن الله دينه فقال: فمن يكفر بالطاغوت ويؤمن بالله ، فيقال: «أهو مسلم أو لا؟». فإن قال: «لا»، فقد بدل إذا دين الله . وإن قال: «نعم»، صار مسلما بفعل الإيمان لا غير. على أن هذا الخبر في تبديل الدين، وأنه معروف أنه الاعتقاد لا غير، وأن المراد في ذلك راجع إلى الدين الذي هو الإسلام؛ ثبت أنه معروف الحد والقدر، يُعرف مبدله . ولو كانت الأفعال سوى الاعتقاد دينا كان كل واحد في كل أحواله مبدل الدين لوجود [ في غير] تلك الأفعال أفعال من القرب في كل وقت. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' Then God explained His religion and said: 'Whoever disbelieves in the taghut and believes in God.' So it is said: 'Is he a Muslim or not?' If he says 'No,' then he has changed the religion of God. If he says 'Yes,' he becomes a Muslim by the act of faith (īmān) alone. This hadith concerns the changing of religion, and it is known that religion (dīn) is belief (iʿtiqād) alone, and that the intended meaning refers to the religion (dīn) which is Islam. It is established that it is known in its definition and extent, and its changer is known. If actions other than belief were religion, then every person would at all times be a changer of the religion due to the existence of acts of obedience other than those actions at every moment. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the hadith that religion (dīn) is essentially belief (iʿtiqād), not actions. The argument begins by citing the hadith: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' Then it notes that God explained the religion of Islam through the verse about disbelieving in taghut and believing in God. The reasoning proceeds: if a person who claims to be a Muslim is asked if he is a Muslim, and he says no, he has changed his religion; if he says yes, he becomes a Muslim by the act of faith (īmān) alone—meaning that faith itself constitutes Islam. The hadith is about changing the religion, and it is known that the religion in question is belief (iʿtiqād) alone, not actions. If actions were included in the definition of religion, then every person would be constantly changing the religion because their actions vary moment by moment—sometimes performing acts of obedience, sometimes not. Thus, the hadith proves that religion is belief, not outward acts. The phrase 'there is no power except with God' is a formulaic ending.

Key Terms
دين
اعتقاد
إيمان
تبديل الدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith refers to changing outward actions or rituals.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the punishment for changing religion only makes sense if religion is defined by belief, not actions, because actions vary constantly and would make everyone a changer.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that actions cannot be part of the definition of religion (dīn)?
A) Because actions are not mentioned in the hadith
B) Because if actions were part of religion, everyone would be constantly changing religion due to varying actions
C) Because faith (īmān) is only in the heart and actions are separate
D) Because the hadith specifically says 'belief' (iʿtiqād)

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that religion is essentially belief, because only belief can define a fixed identity; actions vary and cannot serve as the basis for the severe judgment of 'changing religion'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your identity as a Muslim hinges on your belief alone, how honest are you about what you truly believe in moments of doubt? What would it take for you to be able to say 'I am a Muslim' with full conviction, not just by habit?
?
Maturidi argues that if actions were part of religion, everyone would be constantly changing. Does the fluctuation in your religious practices make you feel insecure? How does knowing that your core identity is fixed in belief give you stability?
?
Consider the grave warning of the hadith: 'Whoever changes his religion, kill him.' Are there any beliefs you hold that contradict or dilute your Islam? Are you actively guarding your belief from being quietly changed by culture, desires, or intellectual trends?

Maturidi's Position on the Vision of Allah (Ru'yah)
Maturidi defines the vision of Allah as true without perception and answers 'how' with 'bila kayf'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that the vision of Allah is true and necessary, but without perception (idrak) or modality (kayf), based on the Quran and rational reasoning.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah is affirmed, but not encompassed by sight.

Imam Maturidi presents a nuanced position on the beatific vision: he affirms it as a reality for the believers in the afterlife, but carefully distinguishes between ru'yah (seeing) and idrak (perceiving/encompassing). This passage from Kitab al-Tawhid establishes the key Quranic proof and the principle of 'bila kayf' to safeguard divine transcendence. Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: ru'yah, idrak, bila kayf.
The passage is dense; focus on the flow of the argument.
2
Understand the ru'yah-idrak distinction
Maturidi affirms ru'yah but denies idrak. Ask: Why does he insist on separating them? The Quranic verse negates idrak, not ru'yah.
Idrak is defined as encompassing the limited.
3
Explore the definition of idrak
Idrak is 'encompassing of the limited'. Since Allah transcends limitation, idrak cannot apply to Him. This refutes the misconception that negation of idrak implies negation of ru'yah.
4
Examine the answer to 'How is He seen?'
The answer is 'bila kayf' (without modality). Modality applies only to things with form. Maturidi then lists negations to illustrate that no human concept can describe the vision.
The list covers attributes like standing, sitting, light, darkness, etc.
5
Reflect on the overall principle
Affirmation without modality: ru'yah is real but transcends all created modes of perception. This preserves divine transcendence while upholding the scriptural promise of seeing Allah.
This is a key Maturidi doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى مثل ذلك أيضاً يذهب الماتريدي، فنقرأ في كتاب التوحيد القول في رؤية الرب عز وجل عندنا لازم وحق من غير إدراك ولا تفسير ... ولا نقول بالإدراك لقوله : لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ فقد امتدح به بنفي الإدراك لا بنفي الرؤية . وأيضاً إن الإدراك إنما هو الإحاطة بالمحدود ، والله يتعالى عن وصف الحد فإن قيل : كيف يرى ؟ قيل : بلا كيف ؛ إذ الكيفية تكون لذي صورة ، بل يرى بلا وصف قيام وقعود ، واتكاء وتعلق ، واتصال وانفصال ، ومقابلة ومدابرة ، وقصير وطويل، ونور وظلمة ، وساكن ومتحرك ، ومماس ومباين ، وخارج وداخل ، ولا معنى يأخذه الوهم أو يقدره العقل
And likewise Maturidi also goes to that effect. We read in Kitab al-Tawhid: The statement on the vision of the Lord, Mighty and Majestic, with us is necessary and true, without perception (idrak) and without interpretation (tafsir) ... And we do not affirm perception (idrak) because of His saying: 'Vision does not perceive Him' (al-An'am 6:103) — He praised Himself by negating perception, not by negating vision. Also, perception (idrak) is but encompassing of the limited, and Allah is exalted beyond the description of limitation. If it is said: How is He seen? It is said: Without how (bila kayf); for modality (kayfiyya) belongs to that which has form. Rather, He is seen without the description of standing or sitting, leaning or attaching, connecting or separating, facing or backing, short or tall, light or darkness, still or moving, touching or separating, outside or inside, and without any meaning that imagination takes or the mind perceives.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the Maturidi position on the vision of Allah (ru'yat Allah) in the afterlife. The author begins by stating that the vision is 'necessary and true' for the believers, but without perception (idrak) and without interpretation (tafsir). The distinction between ru'yah (seeing) and idrak (perceiving/encompassing) is crucial: Allah is seen but not encompassed by sight. He supports this with the Quranic verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision does not perceive Him), arguing that the praise is for negating idrak, not ru'yah. Then he defines idrak as encompassing a limited thing, and since Allah transcends limitation, idrak cannot apply. Next, he addresses the question 'How is He seen?' with the answer 'Bila kayf' (without modality). Modality belongs to things with form, and Allah has no form. He then lists a series of negations that discard all conceivable modalities: standing, sitting, leaning, etc. The passage ends by asserting that no meaning that imagination or intellect can grasp applies to the vision of Allah. Thus, the vision is affirmed but transcends all created modes of perception.

Key Terms

إدراك
بلا كيف
كيفية
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negation of idrak means Allah cannot be seen.
Correction: The Quran praises Allah by negating idrak (encompassing), not ru'yah (seeing). Seeing without encompassing is possible for Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the Maturidi position on the vision of Allah?
A) Affirmed as necessary and true without perception or modality
B) Denied because Allah is not limited by sight
C) Affirmed only through metaphorical interpretation
D) Affirmed but only as a spiritual vision

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms the vision of Allah as true but without perception or modality, preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the vision of Allah is beyond all perception and modality, what does that mean for how you anticipate the greatest reward in Paradise? Are you subconsciously imagining something finite and comfortable, or are you preparing your heart for an encounter that defies all your current senses?
?
Maturidi lists negations: standing, sitting, light, darkness, etc. If you, even unintentionally, attach any of these to Allah when thinking of seeing Him, what must you actively, right now, uproot from your mind?
?
The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' is used to praise Allah by negating perception, not vision. Have you ever mistakenly used a supposed limitation of Allah to distance yourself from Him, rather than to magnify His transcendence? How will you turn that into yearning?

The Ash'ari Proof of Existence for Ru'ya
Ash'ari presents the proof of existence for ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the Ash'ari proof of existence for ru'ya and how it uses qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid.

Core Idea
Existence, not materiality, is the cause of visibility — and Allah exists.

The Ash'ari school offers a rational proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah. This proof, called dalīl al-wujūd, draws an analogy between the witnessed world and the unseen. By examining what makes physical things visible, they isolate existence as the sole common factor — and then apply it to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully once. Notice the logical connectors (fa, li-anna, idhan).
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the main claim
The first sentence gives the conclusion: because existence alone permits vision in this world, and Allah exists, He can be seen.
3
Follow the induction (sabr wa-taqsīm)
The proof finds the common attribute among all visible things. It considers wujūd and hudūth, then rejects hudūth because it involves non-existence.
Notice the logic: one judgment cannot have two different causes.
4
Apply the analogy to Allah
Since existence is shared between seen and unseen, Allah's existence is sufficient cause for visibility.
This does not mean Allah is material — only that existence is the necessary condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأشعري فقد ذهب إلى أنه يمكن التدليل عقلاً على جواز رؤية الله، وله على ذلك دليل مشهور يسميه المتكلمون بدليل الوجود يمكن أن نلخصه على الوجه الآتي: إن إمكان الرؤية في الشاهد إنما نشأ من الوجود لا غير، والله تعالى موجود، فيجوز أن يرى. أما أن المرئيات في الشاهد جائزة الرؤية لوجودها ليس لشيء آخر؛ فالدليل عليه أننا نرى أشياء مختلفة الحقائق من جواهر وأعراض، ولا يجوز أن يكون المصحح للرؤية ما يختلف فيه؛ لأن ذلك يؤدي إلى أن يكون للحكم الواحد علتان مختلفتان، وذلك مدفوع في بداية العقول. فلا بد إذن من إيجاد وصف مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة التي نراها نرجع إليه جواز هذه الرؤية حتى تطرد العلة وتنعكس. ولقد دل السبر والتقسيم على أنه لا مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة من الجواهر والأعراض إلا الوجود والحدوث، لكن الحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون علة لصحة الرؤية؛ لأن الحدوث عبارة عن وجود مسبوق بعدم، والعدم لا تأثير له في الحكم. وإذا لم يصح الحدوث لم يبق إلا الوجود، والوجود مشترك فيه بين الشاهد والغائب، فإذن وجود الله علة صالحة لصحة رؤيته.
As for Al-Ash'ari, he held that it is possible to demonstrate rationally the permissibility of vision of Allah, and he has for that a famous proof which the theologians call the proof of existence (dalīl al-wujūd), which we can summarize as follows: The possibility of vision in the witnessed realm arises solely from existence, and Allah, Exalted, exists, so it is permissible that He be seen. As for the visible things in the witnessed realm being permissible of vision because of their existence, not for any other reason; the proof for it is that we see things of different realities, such as substances and accidents, and it is not permissible that the rectifier of vision be something that differs among them; because that leads to there being for one judgment two different causes, and that is rejected at the outset of intellects. Therefore, it is necessary to find a common attribute among these different realities that we see to which the permissibility of this vision is attributed, so that the cause (ʿilla) may be consistent and coextensive. And examination and division (sabr wa-taqsīm) have indicated that there is no common attribute among these different realities of substances and accidents except existence (wujūd) and temporal origination (ḥudūth). But temporal origination cannot be the cause for the validity of vision, because temporal origination is an existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect on the judgment. And if temporal origination is not valid, nothing remains except existence, and existence is shared between the witnessed and the absent. Therefore, Allah's existence is a suitable cause for the validity of seeing Him.
Source pages p. 565

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari rational proof for the permissibility of ru'ya, known as 'dalīl al-wujūd' (the proof of existence). It is a logical proof structured as an argument from analogy (qiyās al-ghā'ib 'alā al-shāhid). The author begins by stating the conclusion: the possibility of vision in the witnessed world arises solely from existence, and since Allah exists, it is permissible that He be seen. The proof proceeds by induction: we observe that many different things in the witnessed world are seen—both substances (jawahir) and accidents (a'rad). The cause (musahhih) that makes vision possible cannot be something that varies among these objects, because that would imply different causes for the same effect, which is absurd. Therefore, there must be a common attribute shared by all visible things that serves as the cause for the permissibility of vision. Using the method of sabr wa-taqsīm (examination and division), the only two candidates are existence (wujūd) and temporal origination (hudūth). Hudūth is rejected because it implies an existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence cannot be a cause. Thus, existence remains as the sole common attribute. Since existence is shared between the witnessed (al-shāhid) and the absent (al-ghā'ib), i.e., Allah, Allah's existence is a valid cause for the permissibility of seeing Him. The proof is deductive: it infers from the universal principle (existence is the cause of visibility) to the particular case (Allah's existence makes Him visible).

Key Terms
دليل الوجود
شاهد
غائب
علة
سبر وتقسيم
حدوث
وجود
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims Allah is visible like physical objects (materialistic vision).
Correction: The proof only establishes that existence is a necessary condition for visibility, not that Allah is material or that vision is physical. It is an intellectual permissibility, not a claim of modality.

Checkpoint
Why does the Ash'ari proof reject temporal origination (huduth) as the cause of visibility?
A) Because temporal origination is unique to accidents
B) Because it implies non-existence, which has no effect
C) Because it is not shared between shahid and gha'ib
D) Because it is a cause for existence, not vision

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof demonstrates that existence, not materiality, is the rational basis for the possibility of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if Allah's existence alone makes Him worthy of being sought with your heart and eyes, or do you treat Him as distant and invisible in your daily life? What would change if you acted on the certainty that He can be 'seen' with the eye of the heart now?
?
The proof argues that non-existence cannot cause anything. Is there an area of your life where you are allowing a 'non-existence'—a fear, doubt, or absence—to dictate your actions instead of trusting in the reality of Allah's existence?
?
You affirm that Allah exists and is the cause of all visibility. Yet, how often do you actually look for signs of His presence in the world around you? Commit today to one specific practice that trains your 'sight' toward Him.

Maturidi's Rational Proof for the Creation of Bodies
Presents the kalam principle that bodies never lack accidents and thus are originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam principle that bodies are never free from accidents and how this leads to the conclusion that bodies are created (originated).

Core Idea
Bodies are never free from accidents; therefore they are created.

Imam Maturidi presents a rational proof for the creation of bodies based on a foundational principle of kalam: bodies are inseparable from originated accidents. Since whatever is never free from originated things must itself be originated, bodies are proven to be created. This lesson unpacks that argument, examining the key steps: the principle, the enumeration of accidents, and the analogy with writing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind before analyzing it.
Notice the concise style of kalam reasoning.
2
Understand the foundational principle
The argument opens with the principle: bodies are never free from accidents, and whatever is never free from accidents is originated. Recognize that this is a universal rule applied to all bodies.
This principle is the backbone of the proof.
3
Examine the enumeration of accidents
Maturidi lists several pairs of opposites: motion/rest, composition/separation, good/bad, beautiful/ugly, increase/decrease. These are accidents that bodies constantly undergo. He then states that these are originated by sense and reason because opposites cannot coexist, so succession (change) is established, implying a bringer into existence.
The enumeration shows that bodies are always changing.
4
See the analogical reasoning
Maturidi draws an analogy from everyday experience: writing requires a writer, separation requires a separator, etc. Similarly, the world's composition and motion require a creator. The wonder of the world's composition makes it even more deserving of a composer.
Analogy from the witnessed world reinforces the argument.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The final sentence concludes: everything in the witnessed world is originated, so the entire world is like it, in the same meaning. Thus, bodies are originated.
The conclusion is a direct application of the principle to all bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أدلته العقلية فانها مبنية على قاعدة مشهورة عند المتكلمين تقرر بأن الأجسام لا تخلو عن الحوادث، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث. لا تخلو الأجسام عن حركة أو سكون، واجتماع أو تفرق، وطيب أو خبيث، وحسن أو قبيح، وزيادة أو نقصان، وهن حوادث بالحس والعقل؛ لأن الضدين لا يجتمعان، فثبت التعاقب وفيه المحدث: كذلك فإننا لا نعلم كتابة بلا كاتب، ولا تفرقا إلا بمفرق؛ وكذلك الاجتماع، وكذلك السكون والحركة، فيلزم في جملة العالم ذلك؛ إذ هو مؤلف مفرق، بل الأعجوبة في تأليف العالم أرفع، فهو أحق أن لا يتشرق ولا يجتمع إلا بغيره. ثم كل ما في الشاهد والكتابة يكون أحدث كان، فمثله جميع العالم؛ إذ هو؛ إذ هو في معنى ما ذكرت.
As for his rational proofs, they are based on a well-known principle among the theologians: that bodies are never free of originated accidents, and whatever is never free of originated accidents is itself originated. Bodies are never free of motion or rest, composition or separation, good or bad, beautiful or ugly, increase or decrease, and these are originated by sense and reason; because two opposites do not coexist, thus succession is established and in it the originator. Likewise, we know no writing without a writer, no separation without a separator, and similarly composition, rest, and motion. Therefore that holds for the entire world, for it is composed and separated. But the wonder of the world's composition is higher, so it is more deserving that it is not brought together or composed except by another. Then everything in the observed world is originated, so the entire world is like it, for it is in the same meaning as what I mentioned.
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known kalam proof for the origination (ḥudūth) of bodies. It begins by stating the foundational principle: bodies are never free from originated accidents (ḥawādith). Since whatever is never free from such accidents must itself be originated, bodies are therefore originated. The argument enumerates specific accidents: motion/rest, composition/separation, good/evil, increase/decrease—all of which are perceived as changing and thus originated. The impossibility of opposites coexisting proves succession, indicating a bringer into existence. The analogy of writing demonstrates that every effect requires a cause. The world's intricate composition demands a composer. The conclusion: everything in the observable world is originated, so the entire world is originated.

Key Terms
الأجسام
الحوادث
الحادث
القديم
الضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is purely inductive based on empirical observation.
Correction: The proof uses a metaphysical principle: whatever is inseparable from contingency (represented by accidents) is itself contingent/originated. Observation of accidents is only a trigger; the reasoning is a priori.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are bodies considered originated?
A) Because they are composed of parts.
B) Because they are never free from originated accidents.
C) Because they change over time.
D) Because they have a beginning in time.

Key Takeaway
Bodies are never free from originated accidents; therefore they must themselves be originated, proving creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that your very body, with its constant changes, is created and dependent on a Creator. When you look in the mirror, do you see yourself as a self-sufficient being, or do you see a sign of Allah's power? How can you train yourself to see every heartbeat and every breath as a reminder that you are not eternal?
?
The proof uses the principle that whatever never lacks originated things is itself originated. Think of something in your life that you treat as permanent or self-sustaining—your health, your wealth, your relationships. Are you aware that these, too, are continuously renewed by Allah? What would change if you truly internalized that?
?
The author compares the world's composition to writing that requires a writer. Have you ever marveled at the intricate order of the universe and attributed it to chance? This lesson demands that you stop and recognize the Author behind it. What one step will you take today to strengthen your conviction that everything around you is created and sustained by Allah?

Al-Nazzam's Proof from Opposites
Al-Nazzam argues from the forced union of opposites to the existence of a unique Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's rational proof for the existence of a single Creator based on the forced union of opposites.

Core Idea
Opposites cannot unite by themselves—a coercer must exist.

Al-Nazzam presents a refined rational argument for God's existence. Observing that opposites like heat and cold naturally repel, yet we find them coexisting, he infers an external coercer. This proof not only establishes a Creator but also distinguishes Him from creation. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Nazzam's observation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Nazzam begins with a direct empirical observation: heat and cold are opposites that do not naturally unite.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وجدت الحر مضاداً للبرد'.
2
Identify the logical inference
From the observed coexistence, al-Nazzam infers a uniter and coercer. Understand why opposites cannot unite by themselves and why this necessity leads to an external agent.
The key term is 'قهر' (coercion).
3
Understand the implications of coercion
Whatever is coerced is weak and therefore originated. This step connects the physical observation to the metaphysical conclusion of contingency.
4
Distinguish the true Creator
Al-Nazzam argues that humans who gather opposites are also subject to coercion, so they cannot be the ultimate Creator. The true Creator is God, who is unlike anything.
Ends with Quranic verse 'ليس كمثله شيء'.
5
Reflect on the proof's structure
Review the entire argument as a logical chain: observation → coercion → origination → Creator. This is a crisp example of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجدت الحر مضاداً للبرد، ووجدت الضدين لا يجتمعان في موضع واحد من ذات أنفسهما، فعلمت بوجودي لهما مجتمعين أن لما جامعاً جمعها وقاهرا قهرهما على خلاف شأنهما. وما جرى عليه القهر والمنع فضعيف، وضعفه ونفوذ تدبير قاهره فيه دليل على حدثه وعلى أن محدثاً أحدثه ومخترعاً احترمه لا وهو رب لا يشبهه؛ لأن حكم ما أشبهه حكمه في دلالته على الحدث، الله جمع من سوى الله بين النار والماء والتراب والهواء فذلك دليل أيضاً على حدثها، غير أن حدثها ليس هو الإنسان الذي جمعها؛ لأن الإنسان يجري عليه من القهر ما يجري عليها، مخترع هذه الأشياء ومخترع الإنسان المشبه لها الله الذي لا يشبه شيء لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ.
I found heat opposite to cold, and I found that two opposites do not unite in one place by themselves. So I knew, by finding them united, that there is a uniter who united them and a coercer who coerced them contrary to their nature. Whatever is subjected to coercion and prevention is weak, and its weakness and the effectiveness of its coercer's management are evidence of its origination and that an originator originated it and a creator created it—none other than a Lord unlike Him; because the ruling of whatever resembles Him is the same as His ruling in indicating origination. God gathered other than God between fire, water, earth, and air, and that is also evidence of their origination, but their origination is not due to the human who gathered them; because the human is subject to coercion just as they are. The creator of these things and the creator of the human similar to them is God, who is unlike anything: 'There is nothing like Him.'
Source pages p. 572

Commentary
This passage contains al-Nazzam's proof for the existence of a single Creator based on the observation of opposites. He observes that heat and cold are opposites that naturally repel each other. Yet we find them coexisting in the world—for example, in mixtures of elements. This coexistence cannot be by their own nature, so there must be an external force that coerces them to unite against their natural inclination. This coercive force is a strong proof that the composite things are originated, because they are weak and subject to control. The argument extends to show that the human being who may gather opposites is also subject to coercion, so he cannot be the ultimate creator. The true Creator is God, who is unlike His creation. The passage ends with the Quranic verse 'There is nothing like Him' (Shura 42:11).

Key Terms
ضدان
قهر
حادث
محدث
رب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nazzam's argument is merely an illustrative example, not a rigorous logical proof.
Correction: This is actually a formal proof (dalil) that follows a logical structure: from observed coexistence of opposites, necessity of a coercer, to origination and ultimately a single Creator.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nazzam conclude from the coexistence of opposites?
A) That opposites have no real existence.
B) That there must be a uniter and coercer.
C) That the world is eternal.
D) That humans created the universe.

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of opposites in the universe demands a single omnipotent Creator who is unlike His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nazzam argues that the union of opposites you observe daily—like fire and water, heat and cold—demands a coercer. Where in your own life do you find contradictory forces at work? How does recognizing that they are forced together by God change the way you view your own struggles? Will you consciously acknowledge His hand in every conflict you face today?
?
You are subject to coercion just like the elements—weak, dependent, and not the ultimate cause of your own existence. Do you truly live as if you are not self-sufficient? What would it look like to let that reality shape your entire day, from your thoughts to your actions?
?
If the existence of opposites united points to a Uniter, what does the existence of your own conflicting desires and choices point to? Could it be that the inner tension you feel is a sign that you are not designed to be self-governing? How will you submit those competing impulses to the One who alone can unite them in obedience to Him?

Al-Nazzam's Argument: Coercion of Opposites
Al-Nazzam explains that opposites are forced to unite by God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's argument that the union of opposites in the world requires a coercive Creator.

Core Idea
Opposites unite only by God's coercion, not by their own nature.

Al-Nazzam presents a powerful observation: opposites naturally repel each other. Their existence together in the world cannot be explained by their own nature. This points directly to a force that overrides their aversion. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms 'قهر' (coercion) and 'المتضادات' (opposites).
The text is incomplete due to OCR issues, but the core idea is preserved.
2
Identify the natural state of opposites
According to al-Nazzam, what is the natural behavior of opposites when left to themselves? The passage says they are averse to each other.
3
Understand the role of coercion
Al-Nazzam argues that when opposites are prevented from their natural aversion and coerced to unite, it is their nature to obey the coercion. This shows that their union comes from an external agent.
This argument is a key proof for the existence of a powerful Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ابراهيم رأي أن الله قهر الأشياء المتضادات على الاجتماع جوهرها إذا خليت وما عليه، فأما إذا منعت مما عليه من المنافرة وقهرت على الاجتماع، فإن من جوهرها وشأنها الاجتماع عند القهر لها، كما أن من جوهرها...
For Ibrahim [al-Nazzam] held that God coerced the opposite things to unite, while their substance, if left to itself, is [naturally] averse. But if they are prevented from their natural aversion and coerced to unite, then it is of their substance and character to unite when coerced, just as it is of their substance...
Source pages p. 573

Commentary
This short passage presents al-Nazzam's view that God's coercion is what brings opposites together. In their natural state, opposites repel each other. However, when God prevents that repulsion and forcibly gathers them, they have no choice but to unite. This indicates that their union is not from their own nature but from an external powerful agent. The argument reinforces the proof from the previous lesson, showing that the world's composition requires a coercive Creator.

The Ash'ari Proof of Existence for Seeing Allah
Ash'ari presents the proof of existence for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari rational proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, including the method of investigation and division (al-sabr wa al-taqsim) and why existence is the sole cause.

Core Idea
Existence, not temporality, is what makes things visible.

The Ash'ari school offers a logical argument for the possibility of seeing Allah, based on common attributes of visible things. This passage presents the proof known as dafil al-wujud (the proof of existence), using the method of investigation and division to identify the cause of visibility. By examining this argument, you will see how kalam reasoning proceeds through shared properties between the witnessed and unseen worlds.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the proof
The argument begins by observing that we see many different types of objects. It then seeks a common cause for their visibility. Read the passage to identify these stages.
Pay attention to the use of 'al-sabr wa al-taqsim' as a method.
2
Examine the method of investigation and division
Notice how the argument narrows down possible causes to two: existence and temporality. Understand why this method is used to find the 'illa (cause).
3
Reject temporality (huduth) as a cause
The argument rejects huduth because it involves non-existence, which cannot be a cause. Reflect on why non-existence has no effect in logical rulings.
Think about why temporality might seem plausible at first.
4
Conclude by establishing existence as the cause
Since existence is common to all visible things and to Allah, the existence of Allah makes His vision possible. Understand that this proof only establishes possibility, not actual vision.
This conclusion is a key point in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل الأشعري على الرؤية يسمى بدليل الوجود. إن المرئيات في الشاهد قد صحت رؤيتها لأنها موجودة، والله موجود، إذن تصح رؤيته. أما أن المرئيات في الشاهد قد صحت رؤيتها لأنها موجودة، فذلك لأننا نرى أشياء مختلفة الحقائق من جواهر وأعراض، نرى الأجسام كما نرى الألوان، ولا بد من البحث عن وصف مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة تعلل به إمكان رؤيتها حتى تطرد العلة وتنعكس، ولقد دل السبر والتقسيم على أنه لا مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة من الجواهر والأعراض إلا الوجود والحدوث. لكن الحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون علة لإمكان الرؤية، لأن الحدوث عبارة عن وجود مسبوق بعدم، والعدم لا تأثير له في الحكم. وإذا لم يصح الحدوث لم يبق إلا الوجود، والوجود مشترك فيه بين الشاهد والغائب، فإذن وجود الله علة صالحة لجواز رؤيته، وإذا حصلت العلة حصل الحكم لا محالة فوجب القول بصحة رؤيته.
And the proof of al-Ash'ari for vision is called the proof of existence. Indeed, visible things in the witnessed world have been proven to be seen because they exist, and Allah exists, so His vision is valid. As for the fact that visible things in the witnessed world have been proven to be seen because they exist, that is because we see things of different realities: substances and accidents, we see bodies as we see colors, and it is necessary to seek a common description among these different realities by which the possibility of seeing them is explained, so that the cause (illa) applies universally and inversely. And investigation and division (al-sabr wa al-taqsim) have indicated that there is no commonality among these different realities of substances and accidents except existence and temporality (huduth). But temporality cannot be the cause for the possibility of vision, because temporality means existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect in the ruling. And when temporality is invalid, nothing remains except existence, and existence is common between the witnessed and the absent (al-shahed wa al-gha'ib). So then the existence of Allah is a valid cause for the permissibility of His vision. And when the cause is established, the ruling necessarily follows, so it is obligatory to affirm the validity of His vision.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari rational proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah, known as dafil al-wujud (the proof of existence). The argument begins by noting that we see many different kinds of objects in this world – substances (jawahir) and accidents (a'rad) – and they are all visible simply because they exist. To generalize, we look for a common attribute shared by all visible things that explains why they can be seen, and this attribute must be both necessary and sufficient for visibility. Through the method of investigation and division (al-sabr wa al-taqsim), the only possible candidates are existence and temporality (huduth). Temporality means coming into existence after non-existence, but non-existence cannot be a cause for anything, so it is rejected. This leaves existence as the sole cause. Since existence is shared by both visible things (in the witnessed world) and Allah (in the unseen world), the existence of Allah serves as a valid cause for the possibility of seeing Him. Thus, the ruling (hukm) – the permissibility of vision – necessarily follows. The proof concludes that it is obligatory to affirm that Allah can be seen.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الحدوث (al-huduth)
العلة (al-illa)
السبر والتقسيم (al-sabr wa al-taqsim)
الرؤية (al-ru'ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof demonstrates that we actually see Allah now.
Correction: The proof only establishes the logical possibility (jawaz) of seeing Allah, not its actual occurrence in this life.

Checkpoint
In the Ash'ari proof, why is temporality (huduth) rejected as a cause for visibility?
A) Because temporality is not universal
B) Because it is based on non-existence which has no effect
C) Because Allah has no temporality
D) Because existence is sufficient

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari proof establishes that Allah can be seen because existence, the cause of visibility, is shared by all visible things and by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof argues that existence alone makes something visible. You exist right now, and Allah sees you completely. How does the certainty that you are always visible to Him change the way you live this next hour?
?
The proof uses careful reasoning to establish the possibility of seeing Allah. Have you ever truly yearned to see your Creator? What would it take for that desire to become the driving force of your life?
?
The proof eliminates non-existence as having any effect. Your sins, failures, and past 'non-existence' have no effect on Allah's ability to see you. But they affect you. What one action will you take today to ensure that when you stand before Him, you are not ashamed to be seen?

Proof for the Possibility of Seeing God
Maturidi presents a proof from existence for the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the possibility of seeing God based on the shared attribute of existence.

Core Idea
Seeing God is possible because existence is the common cause of visibility.

Maturidi presents a rigorous argument for the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya). Starting from our everyday experience of seeing diverse things, he identifies the one attribute that makes vision possible—existence—and shows that this attribute is shared by God. This lesson walks through his inductive proof and explains why contingency cannot be the cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Try to understand the overall flow of the argument before diving into details.
Notice that the argument is based on induction (sabr wa-taqsim).
2
Identify the two candidates
Maturidi identifies existence and contingency as the only common attributes. Ask yourself why contingency is rejected.
The key point: contingency involves non-existence, which cannot be a cause.
3
Understand the conclusion
Once contingency is eliminated, existence remains as the cause. Since God exists, vision of Him is possible.
This is a rational proof, not a scriptural one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشاهد قد صحت رؤيتها لأنها موجودة ، فذلك لأننا نرى أشياء مختلفة الحقائق من جواهر وأعراض ، نرى الأجسام كما نرى الألوان ، ولا بد من البحث عن وصف مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة يعلل به إمكان رؤيتها حتى تطرد العلة وتنعكس ، ولقد دل السبر والتقسيم على أنه لا مشترك بين هذه الحقائق المختلفة من الجواهر والأعراض إلا الوجود والحدوث. لكن الحدوث لا يجوز أن يكون علة لإمكان الرؤية ، لأن الحدوث عبارة عن وجود مسبوق بعدم ، والعدم لا تأثير له في الحكم. وإذا لم يصح الحدوث لم يبق إلا الوجود ، والوجود مشترك فيه بين الشاهد والغائب ، فاذن وجود الله علة صالحة لجواز رؤيته ، وإذا حصلت العلة حصل الحكم لا محالة فوجب القول بصحة رؤيتها.
The witnessed [world] has its vision proven because it exists, for we see things of different realities: substances and accidents. We see bodies as we see colors. It is necessary to seek a common description among these different realities by which the possibility of their vision is explained, so that the cause is universal and reversible. Examination and division have indicated that there is no commonality among these different realities of substances and accidents except existence and contingency. But contingency cannot be the cause of the possibility of vision, because contingency is existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence has no effect on the judgment. When contingency is invalidated, nothing remains except existence, and existence is shared between the witnessed and the absent. So the existence of God is a valid cause for the permissibility of seeing Him. When the cause is obtained, the judgment is necessarily obtained, so it is obligatory to declare the correctness of seeing Him.
Source pages p. 581

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya), defending the Ash'ari position. The author begins by observing that we see things in the witnessed world that have different realities—substances and accidents—but they share the property of being visible. The question is: what common attribute makes vision possible? Using the method of induction (sabr wa-taqsim), the author concludes that the only common attributes are existence (wujud) and contingency (huduth). Contingency cannot be the cause because it implies prior non-existence, which is negative and cannot ground a positive judgment. Therefore, existence alone remains as the common cause. Since existence is shared between the witnessed and the absent (including God), God's existence is a valid cause for the possibility of seeing Him. If the cause is present, the effect necessarily follows, so it is obligatory to affirm the possibility of seeing God.

Key Terms
رؤية
وجود
حدوث
علة
سبر وتقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Contingency (huduth) could be the cause for the possibility of vision.
Correction: Contingency involves non-existence, which is a negative attribute and cannot ground a positive judgment like the possibility of vision.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject contingency (huduth) as the cause for the possibility of vision?
A) Because contingency is not shared between God and creation.
B) Because contingency involves non-existence, which cannot ground a positive judgment.
C) Because contingency is only found in accidents, not substances.
D) Because contingency contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing God is rationally established because existence, the cause of visibility, is common to all visible things and to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeing God is possible because of His existence, what does that say about the reality of your own existence? Are you living as one who will one day see Him?
?
The proof shows that the cause of visibility is existence itself—not physical form. How does this reshape your understanding of seeking God in this life? What would it mean to seek His 'face' now?
?
You have just read a rational argument that God can be seen. But do you genuinely yearn for that vision? If not, what is holding you back from longing to see your Creator?
