Intention, Causes, and the Nature of Kasb
Maturidi explains the role of intention and defines kash in contrast to Ash'ari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi grounds obligation (taklif) in intention and causes, and how he defines human acquisition (kasb) as a prior choice distinct from the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
Intention grounds obligation; Maturidi's kasb is choice, not mere concurrence.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid lays the foundation for understanding human responsibility. He begins by stating that intention (qasd) and the causes (asbab) are the basis of religious obligation. Then he explains the relationship between human power and action, and finally draws a crucial distinction between the Ash'ari and Maturidi concepts of acquisition (kasb). This distinction is key to the Maturidi school's emphasis on human choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: preamble, basis of obligation, explanation of concurrence, and then the contrast between Ash'ari and Maturidi on kasb.
The Arabic contains some OCR errors; the translation provides clarity.
2
Identify the basis of obligation
Find the sentence that states what obligation depends on. The passage says: 'فالقصد والأسباب هو مناط التكليف' (intention and causes are the basis of obligation). Note that safety of limbs is also mentioned as a condition.
This is a key point for Maturidi theology.
3
Understand the concurrence of power and act
The passage explains that the act occurs with the originated power concurrent in time by Allah's eternal power. Without intention and sound causes, neither the power nor the act would exist. This is the context for defining kasb.
This concurrence is the Ash'ari understanding of kasb.
4
Contrast Ash'ari and Maturidi on kasb
The passage explicitly states: for Ash'ari, kasb is the relation of power to act, inseparable from the act. For Maturidi, kasb is choice (ikhtiyar) or decision (fasl), which can precede the act and power. Reflect on the difference: one is simultaneous, the other is prior and volitional.
This is the core distinction that affects views on human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة مع سلامة الآلات المفصل بعد القصد وسلامة الآلات وضحة الأسباب . فالقصد " والأسباب هو مناط التكليف ، أما الفعل فواقع هو والقدرة الحادثة المقارنة له بالزمان بقدرة الله القديمة ، ولولا القصد مع سلامة الأسباب والآلات لما وقعت مقارنة القدرة الحادثة للمقدور ، أي لما وجدت القدرة الحادثة ولا الفعل المقارن ها بمقدارية القدرة الحادثة للمقدور في الكسب عند الأشعري ، وليس للعبد في عماله سوى الكسب. لكن الكيم لكن الكسب عند الماتريادي هو الاختيار أو إن شئت قل هو الفصل . فكسب الأشارتي مع الفعل لا قياء لأنه نفس تعلق القدرة المحادثة بالمقدور ، أما كسب الماتريدي فيجوز أن يكون سابقاً للفعل والقدرة الحادثة معاً
Preamble: along with the safety of the limbs, the detail after intention (qasd) and safety of the limbs and clarity of the causes. So the intention and the causes are the basis of obligation (taklif). As for the act, it occurs together with the originated power concurrent with it in time by the eternal power of Allah. And were it not for the intention along with the safety of the causes and limbs, the concurrence of the originated power with the object of power would not occur, meaning neither the originated power nor the act concurrent with it would exist. This is [the meaning of] the concomitance of the originated power with the object of power in acquisition (al-kasb) according to al-Ash'ari. And the servant has nothing in his actions except acquisition (al-kasb). But acquisition according to al-Maturidi is choice (al-ikhtiyar) or, if you wish, it is the decision (al-fasl). So the acquisition of al-Ash'ari is inseparable from the act because it is the very relation of the originated power to the object of power. As for the acquisition of al-Maturidi, it is permissible for it to be prior to both the act and the originated power together.
Source pages p. 585

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of intention (qasd) and its role in obligation (taklif). It explains that the act occurs only when there is intention, sound causes, and limbs. The act and the originated power are concurrent in time, created by Allah's eternal power. Without intention and sound causes, there would be no concurrent power and act. Then it distinguishes between the Ash'ari and Maturidi views on acquisition (kasb). For Ash'ari, kasb is exactly the relation of the originated power to the object of power, inseparable from the act. For Maturidi, kasb is choice (ikhtiyar) or decision (fasl), and can be prior to the act and the power. This highlights a key difference: Maturidi emphasizes human choice as a distinct entity preceding the act, whereas Ash'ari defines kasb as the simultaneous relation of power to the act.

Key Terms
القصد (al-qasd)
سلامة الآلات (salama al-alaat)
الأسباب (al-asbaab)
التكليف (al-taklif)
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-haditha)
الكسب (al-kasb)
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyar)
الفصل (al-fasl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi's kasb is identical to Ash'ari's kasb.
Correction: The passage shows Maturidi's kasb is choice, which may precede the act, while Ash'ari's kasb is the simultaneous relation of power to act.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis of obligation (taklif)?
A) The act alone
B) Intention (qasd) and the causes (asbaab)
C) The originated power only
D) Acquisition (kasb) alone

Key Takeaway
Maturidi grounds obligation in intention and causes, and defines human acquisition as prior choice, not mere concurrence with the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your intention (qasd) is the basis of obligation before Allah. Do you treat your intentions as seriously as the act itself? What would change in your daily life if you paused to examine your intention before every action?
?
Maturidi teaches that your choice (ikhtiyar) is real and precedes the act. Do you live as if your choices genuinely affect your spiritual accountability, or do you feel like a passive recipient of fate? What would it mean for you to own every choice you make today?
?
If your acquisition (kasb) is your decision, then every action is a witness. Are you prepared to stand before Allah with the choices you are making right now? What is one decision you need to change today to align your intention with His pleasure?

The Rational Basis for the World's Permanence
Maturidi presents the initial rational premise that the world must be for permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that the world must be created for permanence, not annihilation, based on the nature of intellect and wisdom.

Core Idea
The world cannot be purposeless; its existence demands permanence.

Maturidi begins his proof for prophethood by establishing a foundational rational principle: the world cannot be created for annihilation because that would contradict wisdom, and the intellect—itself a part of the world—cannot be based on something unwise. This lesson unpacks that opening argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the logical flow from the intellect to the world's purpose.
Notice the key terms: 'aql, hikma, fana, baqa.
2
Understand the concept of wisdom
Maturidi uses 'hikma' (wisdom) as a criterion—anything unwise is repugnant to the intellect.
3
See the conclusion
The argument leads to the conclusion that the world is for permanence, setting the stage for the need for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل فهو أن كون هذا العالم للفناء خاصة ليس بحكمة، وخروج فعله كل ذي عقل عن طريق الحكمة قبيح عنه، فلا يحتمل أن يكون الذي العقل منه جزء مؤسسا على غير الحكمة أو مجعولا عبثاً. وإذا ثبت ذلك دل أن إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء. كان العالم بأصله مبنيا على طبائع مختلفة ووجوه متضادة، وبخاصة الذي مقصود من حيث العقل الذي يجمع بين المجتمع ويفرق بين الذي حقه التفريق، وهو الذي سمته الحكماء العالم الصغير، فهو على أهواء مختلفة وطبائع متشتتة، وشهوات ركبت فيهم غالبة، لو تركوا وما عليه جبلوا لتنازعوا في تجاذب المنافع وأنواع العز والشرف والملك والسلطان، فيعقب ذلك التباغض ثم التقاتل، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد الذي لو أمر كون العالم له لبطلت الحكمة في كونه. مع ما جعل البشر وجميع الحيوان غير محتمل للبقاء إلا بالأغذية وما به قوام أبدانهم إلى المدد التي جعلت لهم. فلو لم يرد بتكوينهم سوى فنائهم لم يحتمل إنشاء ما به بقاؤهم. وإذ ثبت ذلك لابد من أصل يؤلف بينهم ويكفهم عن التنازع والتباين الذي لديه الهلاك والفناء. فلزم أن الأحق في ذلك طلب أصل يجمعهم عليه لغاية ما احتمل وسعهم الوقوف عليه. إذ علم بحاجة كل من يشاهد وضرورة كل من المعاين أن لهم مديرا عالما بأحوالهم وبما عليه بقاؤهم، وأنه جبلهم على الحاجات، لا يدعهم وما هم عليه من الجهل وغلبة الأهواء مع ما لهم من الحاجة في معرفة ما دون به معاشهم وبقاؤهم، ولا بد من أن يقيم لهم من يدلهم على ذلك ويعرفهم ذلك. ولا بد من أن يجعل له دليلا وبرهانا يعلمون خصوصه بالذي خصه به من الإمامة.
As for the intellect, it is that this world being exclusively for annihilation is not wisdom, and that the action of every possessor of intellect departing from the way of wisdom is repugnant from him. So it is not possible that that from which intellect is a part is founded upon other than wisdom or made in vain. And when that is established, it indicates that the creation of the world is for permanence, not for annihilation. The world in its origin was built upon different natures and opposing aspects, especially that which is intended from the perspective of the intellect that brings together what should be together and separates what should be separated—which the philosophers called the microcosm (al-‘ālam al-ṣaghīr). It is upon different desires and scattered dispositions, and overwhelming passions were instilled in them. If they were left to what they were created upon, they would dispute in the competition for benefits and kinds of honor, nobility, kingship, and authority, leading to mutual hatred and then fighting, resulting in annihilation and corruption—such that if the existence of the world was aimed at this, wisdom in its existence would be invalid. Moreover, humans and all animals are incapable of survival except through nourishment and that by which their bodies are sustained until the terms appointed for them. So if their creation was intended only for their annihilation, the establishment of what sustains them would not be possible. And once this is established, there must be a principle that unifies them and restrains them from the conflict and divergence that leads to destruction and annihilation. Therefore, the most appropriate in that is to seek a principle that gathers them together to the extent possible within their capacity to comprehend. For it is known from the need of every observer and the necessity of every witness that they have a Director (mudīr) who is knowledgeable of their states and that by which they survive, and that He created them with needs, and He does not leave them with what they have of ignorance and overwhelming desires—along with their need for knowledge of that by which their livelihood and survival are organized—without establishing for them someone who guides them to that and makes it known to them. And it is necessary that He appoint for him a proof and a sign by which they know his specificity by that with which He has distinguished him with leadership (imāmah).
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the necessity of prophethood. Maturidi begins by stating that the intellect judges that the world being created solely for annihilation is not wise, and that any rational being acting unwisely is repugnant. Therefore, the world cannot be founded on wisdom if it were for annihilation, nor can it be created in vain. This establishes that the world is created for permanence. He then observes that the world is composed of diverse natures, especially the microcosm (man), who has conflicting desires and passions. If left unchecked, they would compete for benefits, leading to hatred, fighting, and destruction. Moreover, humans need nourishment to survive; if they were meant only to perish, there would be no need for sustenance. Thus, there must be a unifying principle (a religion) to prevent conflict. The most appropriate principle is to seek a foundation that unites them within their capacity. Reason shows that people have a Director (God) who knows their needs, and He does not leave them ignorant and overwhelmed by desires without guidance. Therefore, God must establish a guide (a prophet) to teach them, and He must provide clear proof to distinguish that guide. This proof is the sign of leadership (imāmah) with which God distinguishes the prophet.

Key Terms
العقل
الحكمة
الفناء
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be repugnant?
A) The world being annihilated
B) A rational being acting unwisely
C) Intellect being part of the world
D) The world being permanent

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's rational proof establishes that the world must be created for permanence because anything less would contradict wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage proves that the world cannot be 'made in vain' (عبثا). Are there areas in your daily life where you act as if your actions have no eternal weight—as if the world is temporary and pointless? What would change if you truly believed every moment is part of a permanent purpose?
?
Imagine you had to explain this rational argument to someone who believes life has no ultimate purpose. What specific words from this lesson would you use? Do you truly accept this truth in your heart, or do you sometimes live as if the world will end in annihilation with no consequence?
?
The intellect that you possess is a part of this world—a world that cannot be founded on non-wisdom. When you use your intellect, do you use it to recognize God's wisdom in creation? Or do you sometimes use it to justify actions that contradict the permanent purpose of existence? Commit today to one concrete change in how you use your mind.

Human Nature and the Necessity of a Unifying Principle
Maturidi describes human nature and the conflict it generates, necessitating a unifying principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's observation that human beings, as a microcosm with conflicting desires, need a unifying principle to prevent self-destruction.

Core Idea
Without divine guidance, human conflict leads to annihilation.

Having established the world's purpose, Maturidi now turns to human nature. He describes the microcosm of man—with diverse dispositions and overwhelming passions—and argues that if left unchecked, humans would compete selfishly, leading to hatred, fighting, and corruption. This demonstrates the rational necessity for a unifying system.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of human nature
Notice the terms 'different desires,' 'scattered dispositions,' and 'overwhelming passions.'
Maturidi calls man the microcosm (al-'alam al-saghir).
2
Understand the consequence
If left to their natural tendencies, humans would dispute over benefits, leading to hatred and fighting.
3
Recognize the need for a principle
Maturidi concludes that there must be a principle to unify and restrain them, otherwise wisdom in creation would be invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل فهو أن كون هذا العالم للفناء خاصة ليس بحكمة، وخروج فعله كل ذي عقل عن طريق الحكمة قبيح عنه، فلا يحتمل أن يكون الذي العقل منه جزء مؤسسا على غير الحكمة أو مجعولا عبثاً. وإذا ثبت ذلك دل أن إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء. كان العالم بأصله مبنيا على طبائع مختلفة ووجوه متضادة، وبخاصة الذي مقصود من حيث العقل الذي يجمع بين المجتمع ويفرق بين الذي حقه التفريق، وهو الذي سمته الحكماء العالم الصغير، فهو على أهواء مختلفة وطبائع متشتتة، وشهوات ركبت فيهم غالبة، لو تركوا وما عليه جبلوا لتنازعوا في تجاذب المنافع وأنواع العز والشرف والملك والسلطان، فيعقب ذلك التباغض ثم التقاتل، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد الذي لو أمر كون العالم له لبطلت الحكمة في كونه. مع ما جعل البشر وجميع الحيوان غير محتمل للبقاء إلا بالأغذية وما به قوام أبدانهم إلى المدد التي جعلت لهم. فلو لم يرد بتكوينهم سوى فنائهم لم يحتمل إنشاء ما به بقاؤهم. وإذ ثبت ذلك لابد من أصل يؤلف بينهم ويكفهم عن التنازع والتباين الذي لديه الهلاك والفناء. فلزم أن الأحق في ذلك طلب أصل يجمعهم عليه لغاية ما احتمل وسعهم الوقوف عليه. إذ علم بحاجة كل من يشاهد وضرورة كل من المعاين أن لهم مديرا عالما بأحوالهم وبما عليه بقاؤهم، وأنه جبلهم على الحاجات، لا يدعهم وما هم عليه من الجهل وغلبة الأهواء مع ما لهم من الحاجة في معرفة ما دون به معاشهم وبقاؤهم، ولا بد من أن يقيم لهم من يدلهم على ذلك ويعرفهم ذلك. ولا بد من أن يجعل له دليلا وبرهانا يعلمون خصوصه بالذي خصه به من الإمامة.
As for the intellect, it is that this world being exclusively for annihilation is not wisdom, and that the action of every possessor of intellect departing from the way of wisdom is repugnant from him. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that if left to our natural desires, we would compete and destroy each other. Think of a recent conflict in your life—whether at home, work, or online. Which of your own desires (like status, control, or comfort) contributed to that conflict? Are you willing to surrender that desire to the unifying principle of Allah's guidance today? What one concrete step will you take this week to prioritize unity over personal gain?
?
Maturidi argues that a unifying principle beyond human intellect is necessary. In what area of your life do you still rely on your own reasoning over complete submission to divine commands? Is there a command of Allah you find difficult to follow because it contradicts your own logic or desires? What would change in your heart if you truly accepted that His guidance is the only safeguard against your own destruction?
?
The passage states that creation is not meant for mere annihilation, as provision is given for survival. Do you live with the awareness that your life has a purpose beyond this fleeting world? How does that belief—or the lack of it—show in your daily choices about time, money, and relationships? If you were to stand before Allah tonight, would He see that you lived by the principle He established, or by your own whims?

The Necessity of Prophethood and Its Proof
Maturidi concludes that God must appoint a prophet with a clear proof of leadership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God must send a prophet with clear proof to guide humanity.

Core Idea
God, the Director, never leaves His creation without guidance.

After establishing the need for a unifying principle, Maturidi identifies that principle as a divinely appointed guide—a prophet. He argues that God, knowing humanity's needs and weaknesses, would not leave them in ignorance and conflict. Therefore, He sends a messenger with a clear proof, and that proof is the sign of leadership (imāmah) distinguishing him.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of the Director
God knows all needs and creates humans with dependencies.
The term 'mudir' (Director) emphasizes God's management.
2
Recognize the need for guidance
God does not abandon humans to their ignorance and desires.
3
Identify the proof
The prophet is distinguished by a clear sign, which is leadership (imamah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل فهو أن كون هذا العالم للفناء خاصة ليس بحكمة، وخروج فعله كل ذي عقل عن طريق الحكمة قبيح عنه، فلا يحتمل أن يكون الذي العقل منه جزء مؤسسا على غير الحكمة أو مجعولا عبثاً. وإذا ثبت ذلك دل أن إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء. كان العالم بأصله مبنيا على طبائع مختلفة ووجوه متضادة، وبخاصة الذي مقصود من حيث العقل الذي يجمع بين المجتمع ويفرق بين الذي حقه التفريق، وهو الذي سمته الحكماء العالم الصغير، فهو على أهواء مختلفة وطبائع متشتتة، وشهوات ركبت فيهم غالبة، لو تركوا وما عليه جبلوا لتنازعوا في تجاذب المنافع وأنواع العز والشرف والملك والسلطان، فيعقب ذلك التباغض ثم التقاتل، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد الذي لو أمر كون العالم له لبطلت الحكمة في كونه. مع ما جعل البشر وجميع الحيوان غير محتمل للبقاء إلا بالأغذية وما به قوام أبدانهم إلى المدد التي جعلت لهم. فلو لم يرد بتكوينهم سوى فنائهم لم يحتمل إنشاء ما به بقاؤهم. وإذ ثبت ذلك لابد من أصل يؤلف بينهم ويكفهم عن التنازع والتباين الذي لديه الهلاك والفناء. فلزم أن الأحق في ذلك طلب أصل يجمعهم عليه لغاية ما احتمل وسعهم الوقوف عليه. إذ علم بحاجة كل من يشاهد وضرورة كل من المعاين أن لهم مديرا عالما بأحوالهم وبما عليه بقاؤهم، وأنه جبلهم على الحاجات، لا يدعهم وما هم عليه من الجهل وغلبة الأهواء مع ما لهم من الحاجة في معرفة ما دون به معاشهم وبقاؤهم، ولا بد من أن يقيم لهم من يدلهم على ذلك ويعرفهم ذلك. ولا بد من أن يجعل له دليلا وبرهانا يعلمون خصوصه بالذي خصه به من الإمامة.
As for the intellect, it is that this world being exclusively for annihilation is not wisdom, and that the action of every possessor of intellect departing from the way of wisdom is repugnant from him. So it is not possible that that from which intellect is a part is founded upon other than wisdom or made in vain. And when that is established, it indicates that the creation of the world is for permanence, not for annihilation. The world in its origin was built upon different natures and opposing aspects, especially that which is intended from the perspective of the intellect that brings together what should be together and separates what should be separated—which the philosophers called the microcosm (al-‘ālam al-ṣaghīr). It is upon different desires and scattered dispositions, and overwhelming passions were instilled in them. If they were left to what they were created upon, they would dispute in the competition for benefits and kinds of honor, nobility, kingship, and authority, leading to mutual hatred and then fighting, resulting in annihilation and corruption—such that if the existence of the world was aimed at this, wisdom in its existence would be invalid. Moreover, humans and all animals are incapable of survival except through nourishment and that by which their bodies are sustained until the terms appointed for them. So if their creation was intended only for their annihilation, the establishment of what sustains them would not be possible. And once this is established, there must be a principle that unifies them and restrains them from the conflict and divergence that leads to destruction and annihilation. Therefore, the most appropriate in that is to seek a principle that gathers them together to the extent possible within their capacity to comprehend. For it is known from the need of every observer and the necessity of every witness that they have a Director (mudīr) who is knowledgeable of their states and that by which they survive, and that He created them with needs, and He does not leave them with what they have of ignorance and overwhelming desires—along with their need for knowledge of that by which their livelihood and survival are organized—without establishing for them someone who guides them to that and makes it known to them. And it is necessary that He appoint for him a proof and a sign by which they know his specificity by that with which He has distinguished him with leadership (imāmah).
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the necessity of prophethood. Maturidi begins by stating that the intellect judges that the world being created solely for annihilation is not wise, and that any rational being acting unwisely is repugnant. Therefore, the world cannot be founded on wisdom if it were for annihilation, nor can it be created in vain. This establishes that the world is created for permanence. He then observes that the world is composed of diverse natures, especially the microcosm (man), who has conflicting desires and passions. If left unchecked, they would compete for benefits, leading to hatred, fighting, and destruction. Moreover, humans need nourishment to survive; if they were meant only to perish, there would be no need for sustenance. Thus, there must be a unifying principle (a religion) to prevent conflict. The most appropriate principle is to seek a foundation that unites them within their capacity. Reason shows that people have a Director (God) who knows their needs, and He does not leave them ignorant and overwhelmed by desires without guidance. Therefore, God must establish a guide (a prophet) to teach them, and He must provide clear proof to distinguish that guide. This proof is the sign of leadership (imāmah) with which God distinguishes the prophet.

Key Terms
المدير
الإمامة
دليلا
برهانا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood is unnecessary; human reason is sufficient for guidance.
Correction: Maturidi argues that despite having intellect, humans are overwhelmed by desires and ignorance, thus needing a divinely appointed guide.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does God establish for the prophet?

B) Proof and sign
C) Authority
D) Followers

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's rational proof concludes that God must send a prophet distinguished by a clear sign to guide humanity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God, in His wisdom, does not leave you in ignorance without a guide. In what specific area of your life are you living as if you have no guidance from God? What would it mean for you to truly accept that God has sent a guide for that area?
?
The passage says God 'created them with needs' and knows their states. When you ignore the teachings of the prophets, are you not rejecting the very guidance that your Creator knows you need? What is one habit or belief you hold that contradicts the guidance brought by the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ, and how will you correct it starting today?
?
Maturidi argues that a guide must have a clear sign of leadership. Have you ever demanded a sign before obeying God's commands? Reflect on a time you required 'proof' before following a clear command from the Qur'an or Sunnah. How does that attitude compare to the trust you claim to have in God?

Rational Judgments

Rational Proof: Impossibility of Two Gods
Maturidi presents a rational argument that more than one god leads to corruption and contradiction in the universe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof that multiple gods would lead to contradiction and corruption, and why the coherent universe points to one God.

Core Idea
Multiple gods cannot coexist; their wills would conflict and the world would not exist in order.

Imam Maturidi presents a foundational rational argument for tawhid. He shows that if there were more than one god, the universe would have to rely on their agreement—a sign of limitation. Moreover, their wills would contradict each other, leading to corruption. This lesson walks through his reasoning and its scriptural foundation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument from agreement
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi argues that multiple gods would need to agree on creation, which implies dependence and corrupts lordship.
Agreement (iṣṭilāḥ) is seen as a weakness, not a strength.
2
Examine the argument from conflicting wills
Notice how Maturidi describes the inevitable conflict: one god wants to create, the other to destroy; their wills cannot both be fulfilled.
This leads to contradiction (tanāquḍ) and opposition.
3
Analyze the analogy to kings
Consider the analogy of earthly kings: each strives to subdue rivals. The fact that one ruler prevails shows sole dominion.
This illustrates that prevailing authority is a sign of oneness.
4
Connect to Quranic verses
See how Maturidi interprets verses like 'If there were gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' as support for the rational argument.
The verses align with the logical conclusion.
5
Synthesize the proof for tawhid
Bring together the arguments: the coherent world cannot arise from multiple conflicting wills; therefore, the Creator is one.
This is a rational proof, not merely scriptural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم دلالة العقل أنه لو كان أكثر من واحد ما احتمل وجود العالم إلا بالإصطلاح وفى ذلك فساد الربوبية. ومعنى آخر : أن كل شيء يُريد أحد ممن يُنسب إليه إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه ، وما يريد أحدهما إيجاده يريد الآخر إعدامه ، وكذلك في الإبقاء والإفناء ، وفي ذلك تناقض وتناف ، فدل الوجود على محدث العالم واحد تدبيره. مع ما كان الأمر المعتاد بين الماوك بذل الوسع منهم في قهر أشكالهم ليكون الملك للقاهر ، ومنع كل منهم غيره عن إنفاذ حكمه ، وإظهار سلطانه ما استطاع ، فإذ لم يكن - بل نفذ سلطان العزيز الحكيم - ثبت أنه الواحد . وهذا تأويل قوله : قُلْ لَوْ كَانَ مَعَهُ آلِهَةٌ كَمَا يَقُولُونَ إِذًا لابْتَغُوا إلى ذِي العَرْشِ سَبِيلاً ، والأول على ما أودع قوله : لَوْ كَانَ فيهما آلِهَةً إِلَّا اللهُ لَفَسَدَنَا.
Then the evidence of reason is that if there were more than one, the existence of the world would not be possible except by agreement, and in that is the corruption of lordship. And another meaning: that everything that one wishes to affirm, the other wishes to deny; what one wishes to bring into existence, the other wishes to annihilate; and likewise in preserving and destroying. In that is contradiction and opposition. So existence indicates that the Originator of the world is one in His management. Along with what was the customary affair among the kings: they exert their effort in subduing their likes so that dominion belongs to the subduer, and each of them prevents the other from executing his judgment and manifesting his authority as much as he can. So since it was not so—rather, the authority of the Mighty, the Wise prevailed—it is established that He is the One. And this is the interpretation of His saying: 'Say, if there were with Him gods as they say, then they would seek a way to the Owner of the Throne.' And the first is according to what His saying contains: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted.'
Source pages p. 619

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for tawhid. Imam Maturidi first argues that if there were more than one god, the world could only exist through mutual agreement (iṣṭilāḥ), which implies limitation and dependence—corrupting the very concept of lordship. He then presents a second meaning: each god would have conflicting wills—one wanting to affirm what the other denies, to create what the other annihilates—leading to contradiction. The existence of a coherent, orderly world therefore points to a single Creator with a unified management. He supports this with an analogy to kings: each strives to subdue rivals and prevent others from exercising authority; if one truly prevails, he is the sole ruler. The fact that God's authority prevails universally proves His oneness. He then cites Quranic verses (al-Isra 42 and al-Anbiya 22) as scriptural support, interpreting them in light of this rational argument.

Key Terms
دلالة العقل
الإصطلاح
فساد الربوبية
تناقض
تدبير
الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple gods could cooperate harmoniously.
Correction: Maturidi shows that agreement itself is a sign of weakness and dependency, incompatible with divinity. Moreover, their wills would inevitably conflict.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's rational proof, why does the existence of a coherent world indicate one God?
A) Because multiple gods would disagree and cause chaos.
B) Because the world is too complex for more than one creator.
C) Because reason cannot conceive of multiple gods.
D) Because all religions agree on one God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's rational proof shows that the very existence of an orderly universe is impossible under multiple gods, thus affirming the oneness of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order in the world—from the rotation of the planets to the rhythm of your own heartbeat—do you truly acknowledge that this could only come from one God? Or do you sometimes live as if the universe runs on its own, as if there is no single hand managing every detail? What would change in your daily life if you deeply believed that nothing happens without His command?
?
Imagine if there were multiple gods, each with their own will. How would your prayers be answered? Whom would you ask for help? In your life, do you ever act as if you serve multiple masters—pleasing people, chasing desires, fearing creatures—as if they have independent power? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to realign your worship and trust to Allah alone?
?
The proof shows that agreement among multiple gods would be a sign of weakness, not cooperation. In your relationships, do you seek agreement for the sake of harmony, even when it means compromising truth? Are you more concerned with being accepted by others than with submitting to the one true God who needs no agreement? What step will you take today to prioritize His will over human approval?

The Impossibility of Two Gods: Both Powerful or Both Incapable
Maturidi analyses two hypothetical scenarios of dual gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the assumption of two gods leads to either mutual ignorance or incapability, both contradicting divinity.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot coexist: either they clash or fail.

Imām al-Māturīdī employs a rational proof to demonstrate the impossibility of multiple gods. He examines the logical consequences of two hypothetical gods having equal power. This lesson explores the first two scenarios: both having power over each other, and neither having power. Both lead to contradictions that negate the very concept of divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two scenarios
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi presents a logical disjunction.
2
Understand why mutual power leads to 'tajhīl'
If both gods have power over each other, each would 'ignorify' the other, leading to the removal of lordship. This is a clear deficiency incompatible with divinity.
The term 'tajhīl' implies making ignorant, a flaw.
3
Understand why mutual powerlessness negates divinity
If neither has power, each is incapable ('ājiz), and incapability ('ajz) negates divinity entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه لو كان مع الله إله لا يخلو من أن يقدر على فعل يسره من الآخر أو لا، وكذلك الله سبحانه منه ، فإن قدرا جميعا ملك كل واحد منها تجهيل الآخر ، وفي ذلك زوال الربوبية ، وإن لم يقدرا عجز كل واحد منها ، والعجز يُسقط الألوهية ، أو قدر أحدهما دون الآخر فهو الرب والآخر مربوب ، مع ما كان علم الغيب علم الربوبية ، فمن ليس له فهو مربوب . ثم لا يخلو أيضا من قدرة كل واحد منها على غيره في منع ما يروم الفعل بغيره ويريده أو لا ، القدرة وتحقيق عجز وذلك يسقط الربوبية ، كل [؟] فيكون فيها إمكان خروج أو يقدر الواحد خاصة فيكون هو الرب سبحانه من واحد لتقلب.
And also that if there were with God a god, it would not be free from [the scenario] that each has power over an act by which he pleases the other or not, and likewise God the Exalted from him. So if both have power, each one of them would possess the ignorance of the other, and in that is the removal of lordship. And if they do not have power, each one of them is incapable, and incapability negates divinity. Or if one has power without the other, then he is the lord and the other is the subjugated. Moreover, the knowledge of the unseen is the knowledge of lordship; so whoever does not possess it is subjugated. Then also it is not free from [the scenario] that each of them has power over the other to prevent what he intends to do with the other or not, and [if both have] power and the realization of incapability, that negates lordship. Every [؟] then in it there is possibility of exception, or one alone has power, then he is the Lord the Exalted, from one for fluctuation.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of possible relationships between two gods. Maturidi systematically examines four scenarios: (1) both have power over each other—they would each frustrate and 'ignorify' the other, negating lordship; (2) neither has power—incapability, which annuls divinity; (3) one has power over the other—the powerful is lord, the weak is subject (marbūb); (4) by extension, knowledge of the unseen (ʿilm al-ghayb) is essential to lordship—whoever lacks it is subject. He then adds another consideration: if each has the power to prevent the other's intended action, that also negates lordship. The passage concludes that the only coherent possibility is that one alone has power, and He is the Lord. The final words 'min wāḥid li-taqallub' might refer to the impossibility of change or fluctuation in the divine.

Key Terms
قدرة
عجز
زوال الربوبية
إسقاط الألوهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods with equal power could coexist in harmony.
Correction: Māturīdī demonstrates that equal power leads to either mutual frustration (tajhīl) or double incapability, both absurd for divine beings.

Checkpoint
According to Māturīdī, what happens if two hypothetical gods both have power over each other?
A) They cooperate.
B) Each makes the other ignorant.
C) They are both incapable.
D) One submits to the other.

Key Takeaway
The assumption of two gods leads to contradictory outcomes (mutual ignorance or incapability), proving that only one God can exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This logical argument proves that any deficiency—whether ignorance or incapability—invalidates divinity. Do I ever attribute ignorance to Allah by doubting that He knows what is best for me in my trials? What would it look like to trust His knowledge completely today?
?
If I truly internalized that no power exists independently of Allah, how would my fears change? Which fear in my life right now reveals that I am acting as if something else holds ultimate power over me?
?
The argument shows that even entertaining the possibility of another god is absurd. Yet I sometimes subtly rely on my own strength or the approval of others as if they are sources of power. What concrete action can I perform today to redirect that reliance solely to Allah?

One God’s Power and the Necessity of Divine Knowledge
Maturidi presents the third scenario and links divinity with knowledge of the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scenario where one god has power over the other, and why knowledge of the unseen is essential for divinity.

Core Idea
A god who is subject to another is not divine; true lordship requires all knowledge.

Continuing the proof against multiple gods, Imām al-Māturīdī considers a third scenario: one god has power over the other. In this case, the powerful one is the true lord (rabb), and the other is a subject (marbūb). He also highlights that knowledge of the unseen (ʿilm al-ghayb) is a necessary attribute of lordship; its absence indicates subjection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the third scenario
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe that Maturidi presents a clear binary: one powerful, the other subject.
2
Understand the implication: a subjugated god cannot be divine
If one god is subject to another, he is a marbūb, not a god. Divinity requires absolute independence.
3
Recognise the role of knowledge of the unseen
Knowledge of the unseen is defined as the knowledge of lordship. Whoever lacks it is necessarily subjugated.
This is a crucial attribute that distinguishes the true Lord from creatures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه لو كان مع الله إله لا يخلو من أن يقدر على فعل يسره من الآخر أو لا، وكذلك الله سبحانه منه ، فإن قدرا جميعا ملك كل واحد منها تجهيل الآخر ، وفي ذلك زوال الربوبية ، وإن لم يقدرا عجز كل واحد منها ، والعجز يُسقط الألوهية ، أو قدر أحدهما دون الآخر فهو الرب والآخر مربوب ، مع ما كان علم الغيب علم الربوبية ، فمن ليس له فهو مربوب . ثم لا يخلو أيضا من قدرة كل واحد منها على غيره في منع ما يروم الفعل بغيره ويريده أو لا ، القدرة وتحقيق عجز وذلك يسقط الربوبية ، كل [؟] فيكون فيها إمكان خروج أو يقدر الواحد خاصة فيكون هو الرب سبحانه من واحد لتقلب.
And also that if there were with God a god, it would not be free from [the scenario] that each has power over an act by which he pleases the other or not, and likewise God the Exalted from him. So if both have power, each one of them would possess the ignorance of the other, and in that is the removal of lordship. And if they do not have power, each one of them is incapable, and incapability negates divinity. Or if one has power without the other, then he is the lord and the other is the subjugated. Every [؟] then in it there is possibility of exception, or one alone has power, then he is the Lord the Exalted, from one for fluctuation.

Maturidi systematically examines four scenarios: (1) both have power over each other—they would each frustrate and 'ignorify' the other, negating lordship; (2) neither has power—incapability, which annuls divinity; (3) one has power over the other—the powerful is lord, the weak is subject (marbūb); (4) by extension, knowledge of the unseen (ʿilm al-ghayb) is essential to lordship—whoever lacks it is subject. Key Terms

علم الغيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A god who is weaker than another god could still be considered divine.
Correction: Māturīdī argues that the weaker one is a mere subject (marbūb), lacking the essential attribute of lordship: knowledge of the unseen.

A) Knowledge of creation

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face uncertainty about your future, where do you instinctively turn for assurance? Does that reveal a hidden belief that something other than Allah has knowledge of the unseen? What would it take to truly entrust that unknown to Him alone?
?
The passage declares that any lord lacking full knowledge of the unseen is a mere subject. Reflect on the 'gods' you serve—your career, reputation, or relationships. Do you honestly believe they have complete knowledge to guide you? If not, why do you still let them dictate your decisions?
?
To say 'Allah is the Lord' means He has absolute power and knowledge. Today, identify one area of your life where you resist submitting to His authority, perhaps by worrying excessively or relying on your own planning. Will you consciously surrender that area through prayer and trust this week?

The Final Refutation: Power to Prevent and the Only Possibility
Maturidi presents the fourth scenario and the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Māturīdī's final argument about mutual prevention and his conclusion that only One God can exist.

Core Idea
Mutual power to prevent leads to contradiction; only one absolute power remains.

Imām al-Māturīdī concludes his proof by considering whether each of two gods can prevent the other's intended actions. If both can prevent, that results in frustration and incapability, negating lordship. The only coherent possibility is that one alone has power, and He is the Lord, free from any fluctuation.

Guided Path
1
Read the final passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the difficult phrase and the conclusion.
The passage has a garbled part; focus on the overall logic.
2
Understand the scenario of mutual prevention
If each god can prevent the other's intended actions, this leads to a situation where neither can act effectively, i.e., incapability, which negates lordship.
3
Recognise the conclusion: only One God is possible
The only remaining coherent possibility is that one alone has power, and He is the Lord subḥānahu, free from fluctuation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه لو كان مع الله إله لا يخلو من أن يقدر على فعل يسره من الآخر أو لا، وكذلك الله سبحانه منه ، فإن قدرا جميعا ملك كل واحد منها تجهيل الآخر ، وفي ذلك زوال الربوبية ، وإن لم يقدرا عجز كل واحد منها ، والعجز يُسقط الألوهية ، أو قدر أحدهما دون الآخر فهو الرب والآخر مربوب ، مع ما كان علم الغيب علم الربوبية ، فمن ليس له فهو مربوب . ثم لا يخلو أيضا من قدرة كل واحد منها على غيره في منع ما يروم الفعل بغيره ويريده أو لا ، القدرة وتحقيق عجز وذلك يسقط الربوبية ، كل [؟] فيكون فيها إمكان خروج أو يقدر الواحد خاصة فيكون هو الرب سبحانه من واحد لتقلب.
And also that if there were with God a god, it would not be free from [the scenario] that each has power over an act by which he pleases the other or not, and likewise God the Exalted from him. So if both have power, each one of them would possess the ignorance of the other, and in that is the removal of lordship. And if they do not have power, each one of them is incapable, and incapability negates divinity. Or if one has power without the other, then he is the lord and the other is the subjugated. Moreover, the knowledge of the unseen is the knowledge of lordship; so whoever does not possess it is subjugated. Then also it is not free from [the scenario] that each of them has power over the other to prevent what he intends to do with the other or not, and [if both have] power and the realization of incapability, that negates lordship. Every [؟] then in it there is possibility of exception, or one alone has power, then he is the Lord the Exalted, from one for fluctuation.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of possible relationships between two gods. Maturidi systematically examines four scenarios: (1) both have power over each other—they would each frustrate and 'ignorify' the other, negating lordship; (2) neither has power—incapability, which annuls divinity; (3) one has power over the other—the powerful is lord, the weak is subject (marbūb); (4) by extension, knowledge of the unseen (ʿilm al-ghayb) is essential to lordship—whoever lacks it is subject. He then adds another consideration: if each has the power to prevent the other's intended action, that also negates lordship. The passage concludes that the only coherent possibility is that one alone has power, and He is the Lord. The final words 'min wāḥid li-taqallub' might refer to the impossibility of change or fluctuation in the divine.

Key Terms
منع
إمكان خروج
تقلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple gods could have different domains without conflict.
Correction: Māturīdī argues that any mutual prevention or inequality leads to contradiction; only one absolute power is coherent.

Checkpoint
What does Māturīdī conclude is the only coherent possibility?
A) Two equal gods
B) One god has power alone
C) Multiple gods with hierarchies
D) No god exists

Key Takeaway
The only rational conclusion is that one God alone has absolute power, and He is the Lord.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which parts of your daily life do you act as if someone or something other than Allah has the final say—a boss, a family member, your own desires? How does this rational proof challenge that assumption?
?
If you truly internalized that only one absolute power exists and nothing can ultimately prevent His will, what specific fear would you most need to hand over to Him today?
?
What would change in your prayer life if you believed—not just logically but with your heart—that Allah alone holds the power to prevent or allow every outcome? Commit to one concrete action that reflects this belief before the day ends.

A Proof for Sensory Knowledge
Maturidi provides a reductio argument for the validity of sensory knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that total rejection of sensory evidence is self-defeating and that skeptics' real motive is desire for pleasure.

Core Idea
Rejecting all senses is impossible to live by.

This passage presents a clever reductio argument. Maturidi shows that anyone who claims sensory evidence is unreliable must still rely on it to survive. The only consistent explanation for their verbal denial is love of pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Maturidi's argument. Note the description of the skeptic and the hypothetical scenario.
The skeptic is described as someone who dismisses senses due to occasional defects.
2
Identify the reductio ad absurdum
See what would happen if the skeptic truly rejected senses out of ignorance: he would plunge into perils and refuse food, leading to self-destruction.
The argument assumes pure ignorance, not selective doubt.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the skeptic does not self-destruct, his rejection cannot be genuine. He must rely on senses for survival.
The real motive is pleasure, as stated at the end.
4
Reflect on the lesson
How does this show the necessity of sensory knowledge? Consider the implications for everyday life and theological epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتأمل فيه ليناده عن عبادة الرحمن - المؤوف بصره ، أو الذي تنازعه نفسه في المنام ، أو الذي يبعد عنه ، أو يدق عن الإحاطة ، ثم لم يعمل عليه كيد الشيطان فى الصرف عن الملاذ، وكف النفس عن الشهوات ، ويوقيه من الجواهر المؤذية ، وصون النفس عن اقتحام النيران والبحار ، ولو كان عن حقيقة جهل ينطق لكان لا بقاء له ، لما يقتحم المهالك ، ويمتنع عن تناول الأغذية ، فثبت أن الذي دعا إلى ما يقوله حب اللذات والميل إلى الشهوات .
The one who reflects in it being called away from the worship of the Merciful—the one whose sight is afflicted, or the one whose soul contends with him in sleep, or that which is far from him, or that which is too subtle to encompass, then he does not act upon it as the deceit of the devil in turning away from pleasures, restraining the soul from desires, and protecting him from harmful substances, and preserving the soul from plunging into fires and seas. And if he were speaking out of true ignorance, he would have no survival, because he would plunge into perils and refrain from taking nourishment. So it is established that what calls him to what he says is love of pleasures and inclination towards desires.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum proof for the validity of sensory knowledge (al-'iyan). The author describes a person who, due to occasional defects (blindness, dreams, distance, subtlety), dismisses sensory evidence, thinking it is a devilish trick to deprive him of pleasures. The argument then shows that if this person were truly ignorant (i.e., genuinely disbelieving his senses), he would inevitably destroy himself—he would walk into fires, drown in seas, and refuse food. Since he does not do so, his rejection of sense evidence cannot be genuine; rather, it is driven by a desire for pleasures and an aversion to restraint. Thus, the skeptic's position is internally inconsistent: he relies on senses to survive even while verbally denying them.

Key Terms
العيان
المؤوف
الشهوات
الملاذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies the possibility of any sensory error.
Correction: It only argues that total denial of sensory evidence is impossible for practical survival, not that senses are infallible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen to someone who truly rejects sensory evidence out of ignorance?
A) He would survive by extreme caution.
B) He would perish by plunging into dangers and refraining from food.
C) He would become a saintly ascetic.
D) He would be protected by the devil from harm.

Key Takeaway
Total rejection of sensory evidence leads to self-destruction, so skeptics must rely on senses for survival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you dismissed a truth because it demanded you give up a comfort or pleasure? How did that choice affect your relationship with Allah, and what will you do differently now?
?
The skeptic in this passage uses intellectual excuses to avoid worship. What intellectual excuses do you use to delay or neglect an obligation? Will you commit today to obeying without hesitation?
?
If you were truly consistent with your claimed beliefs—that Allah sees and knows all—would your actions change? Name one specific action you will take this week to align your behavior with your faith.

The Reliability of Sensory Evidence
Maturidi defends the validity of sensory evidence and shows its reliance on inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sensory evidence remains valid despite variations in perception, and that its acceptance ultimately depends on rational inference.

Core Idea
Sensory evidence is reliable, even when perception varies, but it requires inference.

Imam Maturidi begins by addressing a common objection: if sensory evidence can sometimes be mistaken (due to sleep, distance, etc.), is it trustworthy? He argues that such variations do not negate its fundamental truth. In fact, even those who deny rational inference rely on inference when testing pleasures or avoiding harms through reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the examples
Identify the examples of differing perception: the afflicted, sleep, distance, subtlety. Notice that Maturidi says these do not reach realities, but when impediments are removed, they do.
This shows that difference does not invalidate the sense.
2
Understand the defense of sensory evidence
Maturidi asserts that the reality of sensory evidence necessitates affirming it. Even deniers of reports rely on it for testing pleasures. Find the specific line about testing.
3
Connect to inference
The passage shows that even the deniers of rational inference use reports and inference when they test pleasures. This is a subtle point.
Maturidi uses their own actions to argue against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما في الذي ذكر من اختلاف الأحوال. وتبين الخلاف دليل كاف على أنه قد علم العيان الخلاف ما ذكر من الحسبان. وعندنا أن ذلك بمعنى العيان: فثبت أن المؤوف، وفي حال المنام، والبعد، والدقة لا يصل إلى حقائق الأشياء وعند الارتفاع يصل. فذلك الذي أوجب من الإختلاف هو حق العيان، لم تجز أن ينكره، وعلى مثله قول مثبتى العيان، ومنكرى الخبر بما قد يظهر فيه الكذب بعد أن ينتشر القول. ثم قد قيل: الإختبار فى العيان اللذيذة والجواهر الشهية في الانتفاع، مما لولا الاخبار عما فيه من اللذة ما احتمل عاقل المخاطرة بنفسه من الإمتحان، وكذلك اتقاء المضار غير من سبق منهم الإمتحان، فما نالوا إلا بالإخبار وعلى ذلك المكاسب والحيل والحذر ونحو ذلك مما يرجع منافع ذلك إلى أبدانهم ودنياهم، وكذلك المضار. فثبت أن الذي بعث هذا إلى التكذيب ما في القول به من اثبات الحرمات، وكف النفس عن الشهوات، فيصير السبب الذي به خدع الشيطان هذا الصنف هو السبب الذي جامع الصنف الأول مع ما يوجد ذلك في العيان من الوجه الذي بينا، ولم يمنع هؤلاء القول به، فمثله في الأول؛ لأنه يظهر الكذب فى الإخبار بما اعترض المخبر من الآفات التي تحملهم عليه.

وبعد، فقد ظهر صدق كثير من الأخبار، فلم يكن أحد الوجهين به أولى من الآخر إلا بدليل يوضح، والله الموفق.

وقد يعامل بالمعاملة الوحشة، وضرب مما يوذيه ويؤلمه حتى يضطر إلى القول بما لا يحتمل معرفته إلا بالخبر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

وعلى مثله قول المقرين بعلم العيان والخبر المنكرين لعلم الاستدلال، على عقله لوجوه المنافع في الدنيا ولعواقب مأمولة، ليس عنده علم من جهة العيان والخبر، وإنما ذلك بالاستدلال، وما فى ذلك من ظنون الإصابة، وكذا معرفة صدق الإخبار وكذبه، مع ما يقال له فى كل شىء يعلم مما ليس فيه علم الحس: علمت ذلك؟ فإن قال: بالخبر، يُسأل عن معرفة صدقه وكذبه، وتدخل مع ما كانت الضرورة تلزم ذلك في جميع الملاذ والمضار مما يتقى ويوتى النظر بما عاين وسمع، ليعلم منشأ العالم أو حدثه وقدمه.

وبعد، فإنه ليس في شيء يمنع الاستدلال له خبر في المنع، أو عيان فكأنه بالاستدلال يمنع القول به، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن معرفة إنسان أو نار أو شيء بالذي شوهد مرة لا يخرج إلا على الاستدلال بالذى عرف، ولو لم يدله للزميه أن لا يعامل أحداً قط، ثم لا يقبل تعليم أحد؛ لأنه لا دليل عنده يعلم أنه من ويجوز أن يوجد بخبره أو لا، ثبت أن كل ذلك استدلال، وهو لازم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

مسألة في دلالة الشاهد على الغائب

قال أبو منصور رحمه فمنهم من يقول: على مثله.
Along with what is mentioned of differing states. And clarifying the difference is sufficient proof that he has known through sensory evidence the difference from what is mentioned of estimation. And with us, that is in the sense of sensory evidence: so it is established that the afflicted one, and in the state of sleep, distance, and subtlety, does not attain to the realities of things, and when the impediment is removed, he attains. So what necessitated the difference is the reality of sensory evidence; it is not permissible to deny it. And upon its like is the statement of affirmers of sensory evidence and deniers of report, due to what may appear in it of falsehood after the statement spreads. Then it was said: testing in sensory delightful things and desirable substances for benefit, such that were it not for the information about what is in them of pleasure, no rational person would risk himself by testing, and likewise avoiding harms without prior testing, they only attain through information, and upon that are gains, strategies, caution, and the like of that which their benefits return to their bodies and their worldly life, and likewise harms. So it is established that what impelled this person to denial is what is in the statement of establishing sanctities and restraining the soul from desires. So the cause by which Satan deceived this group is the same cause which collects the first group, along with what is found in sensory evidence from the aspect we have shown, and these did not prevent affirming it, so its like is in the first; because falsehood appears in the report due to the afflictions that affect the reporter which compel them to it.

And after, the truthfulness of many reports has become apparent, so one of the two aspects is not more entitled to it than the other except by a clarifying proof, and Allah is the Grantor of success.

And he may be treated with harsh treatment and a kind of hitting that pains him until he is compelled to say what cannot be known except through report, and there is no power except with Allah.

And upon its like is the statement of those who affirm knowledge of sensory evidence and report, who deny knowledge of rational inference, upon his intellect for aspects of benefits in this world and hoped-for consequences, he has no knowledge from the direction of sensory evidence or report, and that is only through rational inference, and in that there are conjectures of correctness, and likewise knowing the truthfulness of reports and their falsehood, along with what is said to him regarding everything known that is not sensory knowledge: 'Do you know that?' If he says: 'By report,' he is asked about knowing its truthfulness and falsehood, and it enters with that which necessity necessitates regarding all pleasures and harms, what is avoided and what is approached, by considering what he has witnessed and heard, to know the origin of the world or its temporal origination or its eternity.

And after, there is nothing that prevents rational inference from having a report that prohibits it, or sensory evidence, so it is as if he prevents the statement of rational inference by it, and there is no power except with Allah. And after, the knowledge of a human being or fire or something by what was witnessed once does not come out except through rational inference by what he has known. And if it did not indicate to him, it would necessitate that he never deal with anyone, nor accept anyone's teaching, because he has no proof by which he knows who [the person is] and whether it is permissible that he exists through his report or not. So it is established that all of that is rational inference, and it is necessary, and there is no power except with Allah.

A question concerning the indication of the witnessed to the absent.

Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: among them is one who says: upon its like.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a continuous argument defending the necessity of rational inference (al-istidlal) as the foundation for both sensory evidence and reports. It begins by reiterating the validity of sensory evidence despite occasional errors: the differences in perception (due to affliction, sleep, etc.) do not negate its reality. The same love of pleasures that motivated denial of senses also motivates denial of reports. The author then tackles those who accept sense and report but reject rational inference, showing that even their acceptance involves inference: they rely on reports for testing pleasures and avoiding harms, and to know the truthfulness of reports themselves requires inference. Furthermore, every instance of recognizing a person or object previously seen is based on inference from past knowledge. Without inference, one could not engage with others or accept teaching. Thus, rational inference is unavoidable and necessary. The passage ends with the heading of a new section on the analogy of the witnessed to the absent (دلالة الشاهد على الغائب), which is a key principle in kalam.

Key Terms
العيان
الخبر
الاستدلال
الشهوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because sensory perception can be mistaken (e.g., in dreams), all sensory knowledge is unreliable.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such errors do not negate the fundamental reliability of senses; rather, the ability to recognize errors confirms the standard.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the variation in sensory perception (e.g., in sleep or distance) not negate the validity of sensory evidence?
A) Because variations are rare.
B) Because when impediments are removed, perception reaches realities.
C) Because sensory evidence is always correct.
D) Because reports correct it.

Key Takeaway
Sensory evidence is fundamentally reliable; its occasional errors do not undercut its validity, and even its critics rely on inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have I rejected a truth because it would require me to restrain my desires? What specific 'impediments' (like pride, comfort, or laziness) prevent me from seeing the reality of my own spiritual state, just as illness or distance affects sensory perception?
?
The passage shows that we rely on reports for everyday benefits like avoiding harm. In what areas of my life do I trust human reports (e.g., news, advice) without hesitation, yet doubt God's revelation? What does this inconsistency reveal about my heart?
?
Maturidi argues that the root of denial is the desire to avoid religious obligations. Am I willing to examine my own life and identify where I avoid clear commands because they clash with my desires? Will I commit today to act on one truth I have been resisting?

Rational Inference as the Foundation of Knowledge
Maturidi argues that rational inference is necessary for all knowledge, even for recognizing objects and verifying reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that rational inference is necessary for all knowledge, including the verification of reports and recognition of familiar objects.

Core Idea
All knowledge, even from senses and reports, ultimately requires rational inference.

Building on the previous argument, Maturidi now targets those who accept sensory evidence and reports but reject rational inference. He shows that their own knowledge of benefits and harms comes through inference, and that knowing the truthfulness of reports requires inference. Most strikingly, even recognizing a person or object once seen is an act of inference. Therefore, rational inference is unavoidable and necessary for any knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target group
Find the phrase 'المقرين بعلم العيان والخبر المنكرين لعلم الاستدلال'. This is the group Maturidi argues against.
They accept sense and report but deny inference.
2
Follow the argument about benefits and harms
Maturidi argues that their knowledge of beneficial and harmful things comes from inference, not direct sense or report. Note the examples of 'gains, strategies, caution'.
This shows that practical life depends on inference.
3
Understand the verification of reports
When asked how they know a report is true, they would have to rely on inference. Maturidi says that asking 'Do you know that?' leads to a regression unless inference is used.
This is a key point: even the truthfulness of reports is known by inference.
4
Recognize the final argument about recognition
Even knowledge of a person or fire seen once requires inference from past experience. Without inference, one could not interact with anyone or accept teaching.
This is the strongest point showing inference is indispensable.

وقد يعامل بالمعاملة الوحشة، وضرب مما يوذيه ويؤلمه حتى يضطر إلى القول بما لا يحتمل معرفته إلا بالخبر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.

وعلى مثله قول المقرين بعلم العيان والخبر المنكرين لعلم الاستدلال، على عقله لوجوه المنافع في الدنيا ولعواقب مأمولة، ليس عنده علم من جهة العيان والخبر، وإنما ذلك بالاستدلال، وما فى ذلك من ظنون الإصابة، وكذا معرفة صدق الإخبار وكذبه، مع ما يقال له فى كل شىء يعلم مما ليس فيه علم الحس: علمت ذلك؟ فإن قال: بالخبر، يُسأل عن معرفة صدقه وكذبه، وتدخل مع ما كانت الضرورة تلزم ذلك في جميع الملاذ والمضار مما يتقى ويوتى النظر بما عاين وسمع، ليعلم منشأ العالم أو حدثه وقدمه.

مسألة في دلالة الشاهد على الغائب

So what necessitated the difference is the reality of sensory evidence; it is not permissible to deny it. And upon its like is the statement of affirmers of sensory evidence and deniers of report, due to what may appear in it of falsehood after the statement spreads. Then it was said: testing in sensory delightful things and desirable substances for benefit, such that were it not for the information about what is in them of pleasure, no rational person would risk himself by testing, and likewise avoiding harms without prior testing, they only attain through information, and upon that are gains, strategies, caution, and the like of that which their benefits return to their bodies and their worldly life, and likewise harms. So it is established that what impelled this person to denial is what is in the statement of establishing sanctities and restraining the soul from desires. So the cause by which Satan deceived this group is the same cause which collects the first group, along with what is found in sensory evidence from the aspect we have shown, and these did not prevent affirming it, so its like is in the first; because falsehood appears in the report due to the afflictions that affect the reporter which compel them to it.

And after, the truthfulness of many reports has become apparent, so one of the two aspects is not more entitled to it than the other except by a clarifying proof, and Allah is the Grantor of success.

And he may be treated with harsh treatment and a kind of hitting that pains him until he is compelled to say what cannot be known except through report, and there is no power except with Allah.

And upon its like is the statement of those who affirm knowledge of sensory evidence and report, who deny knowledge of rational inference, upon his intellect for aspects of benefits in this world and hoped-for consequences, he has no knowledge from the direction of sensory evidence or report, and that is only through rational inference, and in that there are conjectures of correctness, and likewise knowing the truthfulness of reports and their falsehood, along with what is said to him regarding everything known that is not sensory knowledge: 'Do you know that?' If he says: 'By report,' he is asked about knowing its truthfulness and falsehood, and it enters with that which necessity necessitates regarding all pleasures and harms, what is avoided and what is approached, by considering what he has witnessed and heard, to know the origin of the world or its temporal origination or its eternity.

And after, there is nothing that prevents rational inference from having a report that prohibits it, or sensory evidence, so it is as if he prevents the statement of rational inference by it, and there is no power except with Allah. And after, the knowledge of a human being or fire or something by what was witnessed once does not come out except through rational inference by what he has known. And if it did not indicate to him, it would necessitate that he never deal with anyone, nor accept anyone's teaching, because he has no proof by which he knows who [the person is] and whether it is permissible that he exists through his report or not. So it is established that all of that is rational inference, and it is necessary, and there is no power except with Allah.

A question concerning the indication of the witnessed to the absent.

Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: among them is one who says: upon its like.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a continuous argument defending the necessity of rational inference (al-istidlal) as the foundation for both sensory evidence and reports. It begins by reiterating the validity of sensory evidence despite occasional errors: the differences in perception (due to affliction, sleep, etc.) do not negate its reality. The same love of pleasures that motivated denial of senses also motivates denial of reports. The author then tackles those who accept sense and report but reject rational inference, showing that even their acceptance involves inference: they rely on reports for testing pleasures and avoiding harms, and to know the truthfulness of reports themselves requires inference. Furthermore, every instance of recognizing a person or object previously seen is based on inference from past knowledge. Without inference, one could not engage with others or accept teaching. Thus, rational inference is unavoidable and necessary. The passage ends with the heading of a new section on the analogy of the witnessed to the absent (دلالة الشاهد على الغائب), which is a key principle in kalam.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
الخبر
العيان
الصدق
الكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that inference is optional or only for complex matters, not for simple recognition.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even recognizing a person after seeing them once requires inference from memory.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about knowledge of a human being or fire after being seen once?
A) It is purely sensory and requires no inference.
B) It only comes through rational inference from what was previously known.
C) It is impossible because memory is unreliable.
D) It must always be verified by multiple witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Rational inference is not optional; it is the foundation of all knowledge, including the simplest recognition and the verification of reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last doubted a news report, did you consciously realize you were using rational inference to evaluate it? How does this awareness change your responsibility to verify information before believing or sharing it?
?
Maturidi says even recognizing a familiar face requires inference. Think of a time you misidentified someone. Did that mistake stem from faulty inference? What does that teach you about the fallibility of your own reasoning, and how might you guard against it?
?
If all knowledge depends on inference, what is the most important inference you have ever made about your own life—about your purpose, your beliefs, or your relationships? Are you willing to critically re-examine that inference, knowing that it might be based on flawed reasoning?
