The Proper Use of Analogy from the Seen to the Unseen
Maturidi critiques the misuse of analogy from the seen to the unseen by proponents of eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi critiques the misuse of analogy to argue for the eternity of the world and clarifies that analogy indicates difference more clearly than similarity.

Core Idea
Analogy shows difference more than similarity, so it cannot prove eternity.

Maturidi engages with a common argument used by those who claim the world is eternal: that the seen world indicates its like, and thus the unseen must be similar. In this passage, he dismantles this reasoning, showing that the seen world points to both similarity and difference, and that the indication of difference is actually stronger. He then explains how proper analogy works, guarding against the idea that the unseen is exactly like the seen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of Maturidi's critical discussion of analogy.
Notice the dense style and the different positions Maturidi presents.
2
Identify the misuse of analogy
Find where Maturidi states that proponents of eternity use the seen indicating its like to argue for the world's eternity.
The phrase 'أثبتوا قدم العالم' is key.
3
Highlight the critique of difference
Maturidi argues that the seen indicates both the like and the difference, and the indication of difference is clearer. Locate the sentence starting with 'يدل على المثل والخلاف'.
He uses examples: temporality/eternity, creator/self-existence.
4
Understand the proper analogy
Maturidi explains that proper analogy requires knowing the modality (كيفية) of the seen, and then informing about the unseen. This does not guarantee exact likeness.
He gives the example of body and fire as general categories.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this guards against comparing God to creation, since the seen world points to its Creator but not as a like.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما كان طريق معرفة الغائب الشاهد، وقياس الشيء نظيره، فيه أثبتوا قدم العالم؛ إذ الشاهد يدل على مثله، فصار الغائب به عالما أيضا، ثم يدل في كل وقت على مثله قبله، وفي ذلك إيجاب القدم للكل. ومنهم من يقول: ما من وقت يتوهم فيه ابتداء العالم إلا وقد يتوهم قبله فيبطل له الغاية. ومنهم من يقول: يدل على المثل والخلاف ودلالته على الخلاف أوضح؛ لأن من شاهد من العالم يدله على حدثه أو قدمه، وقدمه وحدثه ليس هو مثلها ولا نظيرهما، ثم بدله على محدثه أو كون بنفسه. وهما خلافه، ثم يدله على حكمة فاعله وسفهه، واختياره وطبعه، وكل ذلك خلاف ما شاهده، ولا بدله أ على أن له مثلا؛ إذ لو كان يدله لكان أن يتوهم كل من عاين يكون كل العالم مثله، وذلك بعيد، فثبت أن الجوهر لا يحقق رؤية نفسه أن يجب مثله شائبا، ويُحقق أحد الوجوه التي ذكرناها، لكن إذا عرفت كيفية المشاهد. إذا أخبرت بتلك الكيفية الغائب، علمت أنه مثله، لا أن ذلك يحقق المثل. وقد يجوز أن يدل على مثله بهذا الوجه وبما عرف يعني الجسم والنار، فيعرف كل جسم ونار وإن لم يشهده، ولا قوة إلا بالله
What was the way of knowing the unseen through the seen, and measuring a thing by its like, therein they affirmed the eternity of the world; for the seen indicates its like, so the unseen becomes also known by it, then it indicates at every time its like before it, and in that is necessitating eternity for all. And among them is one who says: there is no time in which the beginning of the world is imagined except that before it is imagined, so the limit is invalid for it. And among them is one who says: it indicates the like and the difference, and its indication of the difference is clearer; because whoever observes from the world is led by it to its temporality or its eternity, and its eternity and temporality are not its like nor its parallel, then it leads him to its originator or its self-existence. And they are both different from it, then it leads him to the wisdom or folly of its agent, and His choice or His nature, and all that is different from what he observed, and it does not lead to that it has a like; for if it led to that, then it would be that every onlooker would imagine that every world is its like, and that is far-fetched. So it is established that the substance does not verify the vision of itself that its like is necessitated purely, and it verifies one of the aspects we mentioned. But if you know the modality of the seen, then if you inform about that modality of the unseen, you know that it is its like, not that that verifies the like. And it may indicate its like in this respect and by what is known, meaning body and fire, so every body and fire is known even if one does not witness it. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the method of analogy (qiyas) from the seen world (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib), particularly as used by those who argue for the eternity of the world. Maturidi begins by stating that some have used the principle that the seen indicates its like to prove that the world is eternal, because if the seen world points to a similar cause in every time, then the world must have no beginning. He then presents two opposing views: one that the imagination of a beginning leads to an infinite regress, and another that the seen world indicates both similarity and difference, and that the indication of difference is stronger. The latter argument shows that the observed world can point to either its temporality or its eternity, but these attributes are not similar to the world itself. Moreover, the world can indicate its creator or self-existence, which are different from it. If the world indicated its like, then every observer would imagine that every world is like the one observed, which is absurd. Therefore, the substance (jawhar) does not guarantee that its like is necessitated; rather, it verifies specific aspects. However, if one knows the modality of the seen, one can infer the unseen, but this does not mean the unseen is exactly like the seen. The passage ends by noting that analogy can be used for general categories like body and fire, but not for specific essences. This discussion guards against the misuse of analogy in proving eternity and emphasizes the need to recognize differences between the seen and the unseen.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
مثل
خلاف
قياس
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that analogy from the seen to the unseen proves that God is like His creation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that analogy indicates difference more clearly than similarity, so it does not prove likeness; it can point to a Creator who is different from creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the problem with using analogy from the seen to prove that the world is eternal?
A) The seen world only indicates temporality, not eternity.
B) The seen world indicates both similarity and difference, and difference is more evident, so the analogy does not force eternity.
C) The analogy is invalid because the seen and unseen are completely incomparable.
D) The analogy proves that the world must have a beginning, not eternity.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that the proper use of analogy from the seen to the unseen shows difference more clearly than similarity, preventing erroneous conclusions about God and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume God will treat you a certain way based on how people treat you, are you committing the error Maturidi warns against? What would it mean to let God be different from anything you have seen?
?
Maturidi says observation of the world leads to either its originator or its self-existence. Which path does your life witness to? If you truly believe the world has an Originator, how does that change your response to your failures and successes today?
?
Think of a time you judged someone's unseen intentions based on their outward actions. Maturidi teaches that analogy shows difference more clearly than similarity. How can you apply this caution to your relationships this week, especially when you are tempted to assume the worst?

Modes of Ru'ya and the Need for Knowledge of the Mode
Maturidi states that ru'ya occurs in modes and each mode must be known to properly interpret the term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term ru'ya (vision) is used in multiple modes and that proper knowledge of each mode is required to understand its meaning in a given context.

Core Idea
Ru'ya has multiple modes; each mode dictates its meaning.

This passage is the opening of Maturidi's discussion on the vision of Allah (ru'ya). He immediately clarifies that the word 'ru'ya' is not univocal; it has different modes, and the true reality of each mode is only known through knowledge of that particular mode. Without knowing which mode is intended, one must suspend judgment until the matter is investigated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi introduces the concept of modes (wujuh).
The concept of wujuh is important in Usul al-Fiqh as well.
2
Understand the condition
Maturidi says that the reality of each mode is not known except by knowledge of that mode. This implies that when 'ru'ya' is used, it should be directed to that mode, meaning we need context.
3
Reflect on the implication
If a mode is unknown without explicitly mentioning ru'ya, then one must stop (waqf) regarding its quiddity until investigation. This shows methodological caution.
This connects to the broader principle of tawaqquf in kalam.
4
Prepare for next lesson
Maturidi will go on to clarify that idrak (comprehension) is not the same as ru'ya. This lesson sets the stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و بعد، فإن القول بالرؤية يقع على وجوه، لا يعلم حقيقة كل وجه من ذلك إلا بالعلم بذلك الوجه حتى إذا عبر عنه بالرؤية صرف إلى ذلك، وما لا يعرف له الوجه بدون ذكر الرؤية لزم الوقف في مائيتها على تحقيقها، وأما الإدراك إنما هو معنى الوقوف على حدود الشيء: ألا ترى أن الظل في التحقيق يرى لكنه لا يُدرك إلا بالشمس وإلا كان مرئيا على ما يرى لوقت تسبح الشمس، ولكن لا يدرك بالرؤية إلا بما يتبين له الحد، وكذلك ضوء النهار يرى لكن حده لا يُعرف بذاته، وكذلك الظلمة لأن طرفها لا يرى فيدرك ويحاط به، وبالحدود يدرك الشيء وإن كان. ولذلك ضرب المثل بالقمر: أنه لا يُعرف حده ولا سعته ليوقف ويحاط به ويرى بيقين، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And after, indeed the statement regarding vision (ru'ya) occurs in modes; the reality of each mode is not known except by knowledge of that mode, so that when it is expressed by vision, it is directed to that [mode]. And what does not have its mode known without mentioning vision necessitates stopping regarding its quiddity until its investigation. As for comprehension (idrak), it is only the meaning of standing upon the limits of a thing. Do you not see that the shadow in reality is seen, but it is not comprehended except by the sun; otherwise it would be visible as it is seen at the time the sun moves, but it is not comprehended by vision except when its limit becomes apparent. Similarly, the light of day is seen, but its limit is not known by itself. Likewise, darkness, because its extremity is not seen, so it is perceived and encompassed? And by limits, a thing is perceived even if it is [seen?]. Therefore, the example is given of the moon: its limit and extent are not known so that one may stop and encompass it, yet it is seen with certainty. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 680

Commentary
This passage explores the modes (wujuh) of ru'ya (vision). Maturidi states that ru'ya can occur in different ways, and the true nature of each mode is only known through knowledge of that mode. When the term 'ru'ya' is used, it should be understood according to the appropriate mode. If a mode is unknown without explicit mention of ru'ya, one must suspend judgment about its quiddity until it is investigated. He then clarifies that idrak (comprehension) means standing upon the limits of a thing. To illustrate, he gives examples from the observable world: a shadow is seen, but its limit is not known except by reference to the sun; daylight is seen but its limit is not known by itself; darkness is seen but its extremity is not seen, so it is not fully encompassed. In all these cases, things are seen but their limits are not comprehended. Finally, he gives the example of the moon: we see the moon clearly, but we do not know its exact limit or size, so we cannot fully encompass it. Yet we see it with certainty. This demonstrates that vision (ru'ya) does not require comprehension (idrak) of limits. Thus, affirming ru'ya of Allah does not imply idrak.

Key Terms
الوجوه (wujuh)
الرؤية (ru'ya)
الماهية (mahiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'ru'ya' has a single, univocal meaning whenever it is used.
Correction: Maturidi shows that ru'ya occurs in multiple modes, and its meaning depends on which mode is intended. Without knowing the mode, one cannot understand its true significance.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is required to know the reality of each mode of ru'ya?
A) Knowledge of the mode itself
B) Consensus of scholars
C) Direct revelation
D) Logical deduction

Key Takeaway
Ru'ya has multiple modes; each mode must be known to properly interpret the term, and unknown modes require suspension of judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter ambiguous statements about Allah in religious texts, do you feel the pressure to immediately have an answer, or are you willing to stop and admit your ignorance until you investigate further?
?
Have you ever made a hasty interpretation of a Qur'anic verse or hadith because you assumed you understood the meaning, only to later realize you missed the deeper context? How did that affect your faith?
?
If someone asks you about a matter you do not know, such as the exact nature of seeing Allah, is your first impulse to give a confident answer or to say 'I do not know'? How might this lesson on waqf change your response?

Idrak as Standing on Limits: Examples from Creation
Maturidi defines idrak as standing on limits and gives examples from the natural world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that idrak (comprehension) means knowing the limits of a thing, and that vision (ru'ya) can occur without idrak, as illustrated by examples of shadow, light, darkness, and the moon.

Core Idea
Seeing something does not require knowing its limits; ru'ya and idrak are distinct.

In the second part of this passage, Maturidi clarifies the meaning of idrak (comprehension). He defines it as standing upon the limits of a thing. He then provides several examples from the physical world to show that something can be seen without its limits being known. This leads to the conclusion that ru'ya (vision) does not entail idrak, a key distinction when discussing the vision of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the definition of idrak and follow the examples.
The phrase 'ألا ترى' means 'do you not see' – a rhetorical question.
2
Understand the definition
Maturidi says idrak is 'الوقوف على حدود الشيء' – standing upon the limits of a thing. That means fully encompassing it.
3
Analyze the examples
He gives four examples: shadow (seen but limit known only by the sun), daylight (seen but limit unknown by itself), darkness (extremity not seen), and the moon (known neither in limit nor extent, yet seen). Each shows that vision does not require knowledge of limits.
Note that the shadow example is a bit complex.
4
Draw the conclusion
The moon example especially shows that we see the moon with certainty (باليقين) but do not know its exact size or boundary. Thus, ru'ya and idrak are different.
This is a key proof that affirming ru'ya of Allah does not imply idrak.

Source Text

Misconception: If a thing cannot be fully comprehended (its limits known), it cannot be seen.

Checkpoint

A) The sun
B) The moon

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen countless things today—faces, screens, light, shadows. For how many of them do you truly know their limits? And if you don't, does that shake your certainty that you saw them? Or are you comfortable seeing without comprehending? Why then do you sometimes demand full comprehension from Allah before accepting His signs?
?
This lesson says the moon is seen with certainty even though its limit is unknown. You claim to be certain about many things in your faith—Allah, the afterlife, the Unseen. But can you point to the 'limits' of any of these? Does that uncertainty invade your certainty, or does it teach you that certainty is not dependent on boundary-knowledge? Look at your heart: where do you demand limits before you trust?
?
If idrak (comprehension of limits) is not required for ru'ya (seeing), then what does that mean for your relationship with Allah? You will not comprehend His essence in the hereafter, yet you will see Him. Are you willing to let go of the need to 'limit' or 'contain' Allah in your mind now, and simply see Him with the eye of faith? What is one belief about Allah you have been trying to 'grasp' fully—and can you today surrender that attempt for the sake of seeing Him without limits?

The Analogy of Unseen Knowledge
Maturidi concludes with an analogy from knowledge of the unseen to ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses an analogy from human knowledge to argue that ru'ya (seeing Allah) does not require physical conditions.

Core Idea
Divine matters, like knowledge of the unseen, transcend physical limitations.

In this final argument, Maturidi draws a parallel between two areas of belief: how we gain knowledge about the unseen and how we may see Allah. He points out that in our everyday experience (the witnessed world), our knowledge is limited to physical things. Yet we accept knowledge about Allah and the unseen that goes beyond that. Similarly, he argues, we should accept the possibility of seeing Allah without imposing physical constraints. This short but powerful analogy wraps up his discussion on ru'ya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the structure: a statement about the witnessed world, then a conclusion about the unseen and ru'ya.
The passage is a single sentence in Arabic, so pay attention to the flow.
2
Identify the analogy
What two things are being compared? Maturidi compares knowledge of the unseen to ru'ya. How are they similar? Both go beyond the limits of the witnessed world.
The phrase 'f-mithlu al-ru'ya' (so likewise is vision) is the key.
3
Reflect on the implication
Why does this analogy support ru'ya? If we accept knowledge of Allah without sensory conditions, why not accept seeing Him without physical conditions?
This argument rebuts those who say ru'ya requires physical parameters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه في الشاهد – بكل أسباب العلم – لا يعلم غير العرض والجسم: ثم جاء من العلم بالغائب خارجا منه، فمثله الرؤية، والله اعلم.
And also, in the witnessed world, with all the means of knowledge, nothing other than accident (arad) and body (jism) is known. Then knowledge of the unseen came outside of that, so likewise is vision (ru'ya). And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 681

Commentary
This short passage is Maturidi's concluding argument. He observes that in the witnessed world (al-shahid), by all means of knowledge (asbab al-ilm), we only know accidents (a'rad) and bodies (ajsam). However, knowledge of the unseen (al-gha'ib) — such as knowledge of Allah, angels, etc. — comes from outside that limited framework. By analogy, ru'ya (vision of Allah) should also be understood as transcending the conditions of physical sight that apply in the witnessed world. Just as we accept knowledge of the unseen that goes beyond our normal sensory experience, we should accept ru'ya of Allah without imposing the physical parameters of vision. Maturidi ends with 'And Allah knows best,' acknowledging that ultimate knowledge belongs to Allah.

Key Terms
الشاهد (al-shahid)
الغائب (al-gha'ib)
العرض (arad)
الجسم (jism)
الرؤية (ru'ya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is impossible because physical sight requires conditions like distance, direction, and light, which cannot apply to Allah.
Correction: Maturidi argues that just as knowledge of the unseen is not limited to the conditions of sensory experience, ru'ya should also be understood as transcending the physical limitations of sight in this world. The analogy shows that divine matters operate on a different plane.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is known in the witnessed world?
A) Everything
B) Only accidents and bodies
C) Only Allah
D) Only the unseen

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the analogy of knowledge of the unseen to argue that ru'ya is possible without physical conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider a time when your skepticism about an unseen reality (e.g., angels, the soul) was actually rooted in a false assumption that only the physical is real. How can you apply Maturidi's principle to cultivate a deeper submission to the unseen?

The Third and Fourth Proofs for the Possibility of Seeing God
Maturidi enumerates the third and fourth proofs for the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third and fourth rational proofs presented by Imam Maturidi for the possibility of seeing God, and the analogical reasoning used to support them.

Core Idea
Vision can exist without its usual physical conditions, just as a substance can exist without being a body.

Imam Maturidi continues his argument for the possibility of seeing God by presenting two additional proofs. The third proof draws on everyday experience of seeing shadow, darkness, and light without their physical causes, while the fourth proof uses an analogy from the debate on whether a body is a substance. These rational arguments aim to show that vision does not necessarily require a physical object or specific conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it contains two distinct proofs separated by 'والرابع'.
2
Identify the third proof
The third proof says that we can see shadow, darkness, and light without any of 'those things' (meaning physical bodies or attributes). Reflect on this: shadow is the absence of light, yet we perceive it. This shows vision can occur without a physical object.
The phrase 'من غير شيء من تلك' likely refers to physical conditions.
3
Identify the fourth proof
The fourth proof argues that it is possible for all those meanings (physical conditions) to exist without vision (due to obstruction), and conversely, vision can be realized without those meanings. The author gives an analogy: just as a body can be considered a substance but also exist without being a substance (in the debate), so vision can exist without its usual conditions.
The analogy refers to the answer given to someone who says a body is a substance, when opposed by the argument that the agent (God) and the world are bodies but not like that.
4
Analyze the analogy
Break down the analogy: In the objection, it is argued that since the agent and the world are bodies, they must be substances, but the response is that they are bodies 'not like that' (i.e., not in the usual sense). Similarly, vision can be realized without the typical conditions (like light, distance, etc.).
The key is that attributes can exist independently of their usual substrates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث ما بينا من رؤية الظل والظلمة والنور من غير شيء من تلك. والرابع أنه قد يجوز وجود تلك المعانى كلها مع عدم الرؤية إما بحجب أو فجاز تحقيق الرؤية على نفى تلك المعانى، نحو ما أجيب القائل بالجسم جوهر عند معارضته بالفاعل والعالم أنه جسم لا كذلك، فيجوز وجود ذلك ولا جسم، فمثله في الرؤية.
The third (aspect) is what we have explained regarding the seeing of shadow, darkness, and light without any of those (things). And the fourth is that it is possible for all those meanings to exist without vision, either due to an obstruction, so it is possible for vision to be realized upon the negation of those meanings, similar to the answer given to the one who says that a body is a substance when he is opposed by the agent and the world that it is a body not like that, so it is possible for it to exist without being a body, and similarly for vision.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of two proofs (the third and fourth) for the possibility of seeing God. The third proof argues that shadow, darkness, and light can be seen without any of their usual physical causes, implying that vision can exist independently. The fourth proof argues that all those meanings can exist without vision due to obstruction, and conversely, vision can be realized without those meanings. The author gives an analogy: just as a body can be considered a substance but also exist without being a substance (as in the answer to the objector), so can vision occur without the usual conditions.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision always requires the presence of physical objects and conditions like light, shadow, etc.
Correction: The third proof shows that we can see shadow, darkness, and light without their physical causes, and the fourth proof argues that vision can be realized without those conditions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Vision is not necessarily dependent on physical objects or specific conditions; it can occur independently, as shown by empirical observation and analogical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The fourth proof uses an analogy about God not being 'like that'—meaning He is not limited by our categories. In what ways do you still try to fit God into your own understanding, thinking He must act or be a certain way? Will you surrender those limitations and trust that He is beyond what you can conceive?

Obstacles to Vision Are Relative
Maturidi argues that obstacles to vision are relative and can be removed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that obstacles like distance and fineness are not intrinsic barriers to vision; they can be removed for other observers, implying vision of non-bodies is possible.

Core Idea
Distance and fineness are not absolute; they can be removed for other observers.

Imam Maturidi addresses a common doubt about the possibility of vision when objects are far away or too fine. He argues that these are not inherent obstacles but external conditions that can be removed. By considering the example of the Angel of Death, he shows that what is impossible for one observer may be possible for another. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text slowly, then the translation. Notice the key terms 'بعد' (distance) and 'دقة' (fineness).
OCR has some unclear words, but the overall meaning is clear.
2
Identify the main claim
Maturidi states that distance and fineness are not absolute barriers; they can be overcome when the obstruction is removed.
Look for the phrase 'فارتفع جاز' (so when it is removed, it is possible).
3
Understand the example of the Angel of Death
The Angel of Death sees across the entire earth, showing that what is impossible for humans is possible for others.
This illustrates that vision is not inherently limited by distance.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that obstacles are not intrinsic to the object seen but are external. Thus, if one denies vision of non-bodies, one must affirm it for accidents.
The final part about accidents relates to broader kalam discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن البعد الذى يحجبنا والدقة يجوز أن يبانه بصر غيرنا، فصار ارتفاع الرؤية بالحجاب، فإذا ارتفع جاز، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وبعد، فإن الذي يقوله تقدير برؤية الأجسام، ولم يمتحن بصره بغير الأجسام والأعراض أن كيف سبيل الرؤية له. وبعد، فإن كل جسم يرى وإن كان الدقة والبعد يخجبان، فيجوز ارتفاعها عن بصر غيره فيرى على ما يرى ملك الموت من بأطراف الأرض ووسطها مما لو اعتبر ذلك يبصر البشر لما احتمل الإدراك، فثبت أن الذي قدر به ليس هو سبب تعريف ما يبصر، ولكن سبب تعريف ما يحجب به البصر فإذا ارتفع رأى، صبع ما كان المنفى رؤيته لذاته عرض، وإلا فكل جسم يرى، فإن لزم إنكار الرؤية لما ليس بجسم أو لما لا يرى إلا بما ذكر ليلزم الإقرار به؛ لأن الذي لا يي لذاته هو العرض، وإلا فكل عين يرى، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Moreover, the distance that impedes us and the fineness (of an object) may be perceived by the sight of another, so the removal of vision occurs due to an obstruction. When it is removed, it is possible. And there is no power except with Allah. Moreover, what one says is an estimation based on seeing bodies, and he has not tested his sight with anything other than bodies and accidents to know how the way of vision is for him. Moreover, every body is seen even if fineness and distance impede, so it is possible for the impediment to be removed from the sight of another so that he sees, just as the Angel of Death sees those at the ends of the earth and its middle, which if a human considered that, he would not be able to perceive. So it is established that what he estimated is not the cause of defining what is seen, but rather the cause of defining what obstructs sight. So when the obstruction is removed, he sees. Then what was negated (i.e., the object of vision is an accident?), for it is not seen by its essence except in the case of a body, otherwise every body is seen. If he denies vision of what is not a body or what is not seen except by what was mentioned, then he must affirm it. Because what does not exist by its essence is the accident, otherwise every eye sees. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 682

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the possibility of vision despite physical obstacles. The author states that distance and fineness may not be obstacles for another observer. Vision is hindered only by a veil; when the veil is removed, vision is possible. He then criticizes the objector who limits vision to bodies and accidents without testing. He uses the example of the Angel of Death who can see across the entire earth, demonstrating that what is impossible for humans is possible for others. The author concludes that the obstacles are not intrinsic to the seen object but are external, so removing them allows vision. He ends by affirming that if one denies vision of what is not a body, he must affirm it for accidents, since accidents are not seen by essence.

Key Terms
بعد
دقة
حجاب
ملك الموت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that distance and fineness make vision absolutely impossible.
Correction: The author shows that these are relative; they can be removed for other observers, as in the case of the Angel of Death.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that obstacles to vision can be removed?
A) The sight of the Angel of Death
B) The seeing of shadows
C) The request of Moses
D) The repentance of Moses

Key Takeaway
Distance and fineness are not intrinsic barriers to vision; they can be removed, so vision of non-bodies is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Three Invalid Meanings of 'Why Create?'
Maturidi examines and refutes three possible interpretations of the question about creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's method of refuting three possible interpretations of the question 'why did God create the world?' and why the question itself is considered invalid.

Core Idea
The question 'why create?' is self-defeating—every possible meaning leads to a contradiction.

Maturidi here takes on a fundamental challenge: if God is wise, can we ask why He created? He shows that any way you phrase that question collapses into absurdity. The passage enumerates three possible meanings and refutes each, demonstrating that the question itself is based on a mistaken assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three meanings introduced with 'إما أن نقول... أو يُسأل...'.
2
Identify the three meanings
The passage presents three possible interpretations of the question. Find each one and notice how Maturidi refutes it.
The first meaning asks about this world versus another; the second about timing; the third about the essence of the world.
3
Understand the refutations
For each meaning, Maturidi gives a logical refutation. The first leads to infinite regress, the second confuses creation with time, the third is self-referential.
The third refutation shows the questioner is undermining their own ability to ask.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes with 'وبالله التوفيق' after stating the question is invalid. Reflect on why the question cannot be asked of God.
This illustrates the limits of human reason in questioning divine acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : السؤال لا يعدو معان : إما أن نقول : لم خلق هذا العالم دون أن يخلق غيره ؟ فيكون هذا السؤال فيه كهو في هذا ، وكذلك في قوله : لم لا خلق الخلق ليكون قبل الوقت الذى كان ؟ على أن الخلق ليس هو غير الوقت يصير كونه وقتا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله بل هو إخبار عن كونه في ، أو يُسأل عن حقيقة هذا العالم ، فيكون سؤاله منه ، فكأنه قال : ليم أسأل ؟ وليم عقلت أن أسأل ؟ وليم لا كنت غير عاقل ؟ وذلك فاسد ؛ لأنه نفسه منع عن السؤال ، وبالله التوفيق .
And a group said: The question cannot exceed [the following] meanings: Either we say: why did He create this world and not another? Then this question about it is the same as the question about this [world]. Likewise in His saying: why did He not create creation to be before the time when it was? But creation is not other than time so that it becomes a time. There is no power except with Allah. Rather, it is an informing about its being in [time]. Or one asks about the reality of this world, so his question is from it [the world], and it is as if he said: why am I asking? And why did I understand to ask? And why was I not other than rational? And that is corrupt, because he himself is prevented from asking. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage enumerates three possible meanings of the question 'why create the world?' and refutes each. 1) If asking why this world rather than another, the same question applies to any world, leading to infinite regress. 2) If asking why creation didn't occur earlier, that assumes time is independent, but creation and time are co-extensive. 3) If asking about the essence of the world, the question is self-referential—it asks about the condition of the questioner's own existence. All these are invalid because the question itself is based on a mistaken assumption that God can be interrogated. Maturidi concludes with 'and with Allah is success.'

Key Terms
السؤال
المعاني
الوقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The question 'why did God create the world?' is a valid theological question that can be answered with wisdom or purpose.
Correction: Maturidi shows that any possible interpretation of the question leads to logical absurdity, so the question itself is invalid.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the question 'why did God create this world and not another?'
A) By arguing that God's will is arbitrary.
B) By showing that the same question applies to any possible world, leading to an infinite regress.
C) By claiming this world is the best of all possible worlds.
D) By stating that God's wisdom requires this specific world.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the question 'why did God create?' is logically invalid when all possible meanings are examined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself questioning why Allah allowed a certain trial in your life, could your question be akin to the invalid 'why create?'—assuming you have a right to know a reason beyond His will? How will you respond the next time such a question arises?
?
The third meaning shows that the questioner's own rationality is part of the creation. Have you ever considered that your ability to question is itself a gift from the Creator? Does that change how you approach doubts?
?
Maturidi says the questioner is 'prevented from asking.' Does that thought bring you humility? Will you accept that some questions are not meant to be answered but to remind you of your dependence on Allah?

Rational Judgments

The Rational Foundation for Command and Prohibition
Maturidi presents the rational grounds for command and prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi grounds divine commands in the rational distinction between good and evil.

Core Idea
Good and evil are known by reason; commands follow from that objective moral order.

Maturidi begins his proof by establishing that humans have an innate ability to recognize good and evil through intellect. This rational capacity distinguishes humans from animals and forms the basis for divine commands. In this passage, you will see how he argues that because reason judges actions as good or ugly, commands and prohibitions become necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow from knowledge of God to rational judgments.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وبما في العقل حسن كل حسن'.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage builds on two premises: (1) God distinguished humans with the ability to know commands, and (2) reason recognizes good and evil intrinsically. See how these lead to necessity.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Ask yourself: why does the rational goodness of an action make command necessary? Consider the alternative: without command, would the good be left undone?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ودليل الأمر عندنا والنهي معرفة الأمر والناهي الله البشر من بين البهائم فى تعرف ذلك، لم يحتمل اهمالهم عن ذلك، كما لا يحتمل شيء مما فيه النفع إهماله عنه، وبما في العقل حسن كل حسن ويحسنُ فعل الحسن، وقبح كل قبيح، ثم فى الفعل يقبح فعل القبيح فلزم الأمر والنهى لما كان ما به الأمر والنهى، ولأن الله خلق خلقا يدل على وحدانيته وحكمته، فلم يجز إخلاء الخلق عن معرفة ذلك، فيصير خلقه عبثا، ولما فى رفع الكلفة زوال الخلقة، إذ حصلت للفناء، وكل بان شيئا للنقض لا غير فهو عابث غير حكيم. ثم الوعد والوعيد للترغيب والترهيب، إذ لولا ذلك يذهب نفع الائتمار وضرر العصيان، ولم يكن لمن خلق في فعلهم نفع، فإذا لم يكن للمؤتمر نفع ولا للعاصى ضرر يبطل معنى الأمر والنهي، إذ ليس لنفع الآمر والناهي، فلذلك لزم الوعد والوعيد في الحكمة. مع ما في الأمر والنهي
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The proof for command and prohibition with us is the knowledge of the commander and prohibitor. Allah distinguished humans from beasts in recognizing that. He could not tolerate neglecting them concerning that, just as nothing beneficial tolerates being neglected. And because in the intellect there is goodness of every goodness and the act of goodness is good, and ugliness of every ugly, then in action, performing the ugly is ugly. So command and prohibition became necessary because of what is subject to command and prohibition. And because Allah created a creation that indicates His unity and wisdom, it was not permissible to leave creation without knowledge of that, for then His creation would become futile. And because in lifting the obligation [would be] the dissolution of creation, since it came for an end, and whoever posits something merely for dissolution is frivolous, not wise. Then promise and threat [are] for encouragement and deterrence, for without that the benefit of obedience and the harm of disobedience would be lost, and there would be no benefit for those who are created in their actions. For if there is no benefit for the obedient and no harm for the disobedient, the meaning of command and prohibition is invalidated, since it is not for the benefit of the commander or prohibitor. Therefore, promise and threat are necessary in wisdom. Along with what is in command and prohibition.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's own proof for the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions. He begins by stating that the evidence lies in the knowledge of the commander and prohibitor (i.e., God). God distinguished humans from animals by giving them the ability to know commands and prohibitions. It is not permissible for God to neglect them in this matter, just as nothing beneficial can be neglected. Intellectually, there is objective goodness and ugliness: every good thing is good, and acting according to goodness is good; every ugly thing is ugly, and performing the ugly is ugly. This objective moral distinction makes command and prohibition necessary. Furthermore, God created creation to indicate His unity and wisdom; therefore, creation cannot be left without knowledge of that, otherwise creation would be futile (عبث). If the obligation (التكليف) were removed, creation would dissolve, since it was made for an end. Positing something merely for dissolution would be frivolous and unwise. Then, promise and threat (reward and punishment) are necessary for encouragement and deterrence. Without them, the benefit of obedience and the harm of disobedience would be lost, and created beings would gain no benefit from their actions. If there is no benefit for the obedient and no harm for the disobedient, then the meaning of command and prohibition is nullified, because command and prohibition are not for the benefit of God (the commander). Therefore, promise and threat are required by wisdom.

Key Terms
أبو منصور
الأمر
النهي
الحسن
القبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commands and prohibitions are arbitrary and only known through revelation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that reason itself recognizes good and evil, making commands necessary and knowable through intellect.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the intellect necessitate command and prohibition?
A) Because humans need rules to live together.
B) Because reason recognizes good as good and ugly as ugly, so the good must be commanded and the ugly prohibited.
C) Because God's will is absolute and commands are required to test humans.
D) Because prophets conveyed them.

Key Takeaway
Divine commands are not arbitrary; they follow from the rational distinction between good and evil that God embedded in human intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on your intellect every day to navigate life. But do you actually use it to discern good and evil as Maturidi insists reason can? When was the last time you acknowledged an action as inherently good or evil, independent of what people around you think? If you haven't, are you truly using the gift God gave you to distinguish you from the beasts? Will you commit this week to consciously applying reason to a moral dilemma before checking cultural or societal norms?
?
If divine commands are not arbitrary but grounded in objective reality, what does your hesitation to obey certain commands reveal about your trust in God's wisdom? Are you treating some commands as if they were optional suggestions rather than necessities flowing from the very nature of good and evil? What is one command you have been struggling with, and how will you change your view of it after this lesson?
?
God left humans with no excuse by giving them intellect to know moral truths. Now imagine standing before Him: will you be able to say you used that intellect to seek out what He commands and prohibits, or did you ignore it? What specific step will you take today to align your life with the objective moral order that reason itself acknowledges?

The Wisdom of Promise and Threat
Maturidi argues that promise and threat are necessary for the wisdom of command and prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that promise and threat are necessary for the meaning of command and prohibition.

Core Idea
Without reward and punishment, commands become meaningless and creation futile.

Maturidi continues his proof by connecting divine commands to the purpose of creation. If commands were without consequences, creation would be futile and God would be frivolous. This lesson explores how promise and threat are required by wisdom to give weight to obedience and disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the argument about futility and the necessity of promise (وعد) and threat (وعيد).
Notice the repeated mention of 'عبث' and 'حكمة'.
2
Analyze the futility argument
Maturidi argues that creation indicates God's unity and wisdom, so leaving creation without knowledge would be futile. Also, removing obligation would dissolve creation, making God a frivolous actor.
3
Connect to promise and threat
See how promise and threat provide the incentive that makes obedience beneficial and disobedience harmful, thus validating the purpose of commands.
This ties to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ودليل الأمر عندنا والنهي معرفة الأمر والناهي الله البشر من بين البهائم فى تعرف ذلك، لم يحتمل اهمالهم عن ذلك، كما لا يحتمل شيء مما فيه النفع إهماله عنه، وبما في العقل حسن كل حسن ويحسنُ فعل الحسن، وقبح كل قبيح، ثم فى الفعل يقبح فعل القبيح فلزم الأمر والنهى لما كان ما به الأمر والنهى، ولأن الله خلق خلقا يدل على وحدانيته وحكمته، فلم يجز إخلاء الخلق عن معرفة ذلك، فيصير خلقه عبثا، ولما فى رفع الكلفة زوال الخلقة، إذ حصلت للفناء، وكل بان شيئا للنقض لا غير فهو عابث غير حكيم. ثم الوعد والوعيد للترغيب والترهيب، إذ لولا ذلك يذهب نفع الائتمار وضرر العصيان، ولم يكن لمن خلق في فعلهم نفع، فإذا لم يكن للمؤتمر نفع ولا للعاصى ضرر يبطل معنى الأمر والنهي، إذ ليس لنفع الآمر والناهي، فلذلك لزم الوعد والوعيد في الحكمة. مع ما في الأمر والنهي
Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The proof for command and prohibition with us is the knowledge of the commander and prohibitor. Allah distinguished humans from beasts in recognizing that. He could not tolerate neglecting them concerning that, just as nothing beneficial tolerates being neglected. And because in the intellect there is goodness of every goodness and the act of goodness is good, and ugliness of every ugly, then in action, performing the ugly is ugly. So command and prohibition became necessary because of what is subject to command and prohibition. And because Allah created a creation that indicates His unity and wisdom, it was not permissible to leave creation without knowledge of that, for then His creation would become futile. And because in lifting the obligation [would be] the dissolution of creation, since it came for an end, and whoever posits something merely for dissolution is frivolous, not wise. Then promise and threat [are] for encouragement and deterrence, for without that the benefit of obedience and the harm of disobedience would be lost, and there would be no benefit for those who are created in their actions. For if there is no benefit for the obedient and no harm for the disobedient, the meaning of command and prohibition is invalidated, since it is not for the benefit of the commander or prohibitor. Therefore, promise and threat are necessary in wisdom. Along with what is in command and prohibition.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's own proof for the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions. He begins by stating that the evidence lies in the knowledge of the commander and prohibitor (i.e., God). God distinguished humans from animals by giving them the ability to know commands and prohibitions. It is not permissible for God to neglect them in this matter, just as nothing beneficial can be neglected. Intellectually, there is objective goodness and ugliness: every good thing is good, and acting according to goodness is good; every ugly thing is ugly, and performing the ugly is ugly. This objective moral distinction makes command and prohibition necessary. Furthermore, God created creation to indicate His unity and wisdom; therefore, creation cannot be left without knowledge of that, otherwise creation would be futile (عبث). If the obligation (التكليف) were removed, creation would dissolve, since it was made for an end. Positing something merely for dissolution would be frivolous and unwise. Then, promise and threat (reward and punishment) are necessary for encouragement and deterrence. Without them, the benefit of obedience and the harm of disobedience would be lost, and created beings would gain no benefit from their actions. If there is no benefit for the obedient and no harm for the disobedient, then the meaning of command and prohibition is nullified, because command and prohibition are not for the benefit of God (the commander). Therefore, promise and threat are required by wisdom.

Key Terms
العبث
التكليف
الوعد
الوعيد
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if there were no promise and threat?
A) Humans would still obey out of pure love for God.
B) The meaning of command and prohibition would be invalidated.
C) Creation would still have purpose.
D) God would become unjust.

Key Takeaway
Promise and threat are not extras; they are necessary consequences of the wisdom of creation and the rationality of commands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever obeyed a command of Allah only because you fear punishment or desire reward? If so, what does this lesson reveal about the dignity Allah grants you by making your actions matter? How will you change your intention from mere avoidance of fire to treasuring the very act of obedience as meaningful?
?
Consider a time when you disobeyed and felt no immediate consequence. Did that tempt you to think disobedience is harmless? How does this lesson's reasoning that 'without harm for the disobedient, the meaning of prohibition is invalidated' confront your casual attitude toward sin?
?
If you truly believe that your obedience benefits you and your disobedience harms you, how would that transform your daily choices in worship and character? Will you today begin treating every act of obedience as a gain and every sin as a loss?

The Rational Necessity of Divine Commands and Prohibitions
Maturidi provides a rational proof for the necessity of commands, prohibitions, and divine retribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that divine commands and prohibitions, along with promise and threat, are rationally necessary for human guidance.

Core Idea
Commands and prohibitions are not arbitrary; they are required by reason and divine wisdom.

Imam Maturidi presents a foundational proof from reason and creation that establishes the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions. This passage demonstrates that such guidance is inherent in the nature of human intellect and the purposefulness of creation. By following this argument, you will see how kalam grounds moral obligation in objective rationality.

Guided Path
1
Read the full proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Maturidi builds the argument step by step.
2
Identify the key premises
Look for the major points: human distinction via knowledge, rational good/evil, creation's purpose, and the role of promise/threat.
Each premise builds on the previous.
3
Understand why promise and threat are necessary
Reflect on the final part: without reward and punishment, commands become meaningless. Consider why this is a rational necessity.
This connects to the purpose of incentive.
4
Contrast with arbitrary command
Think about how this argument shows that commands are based on objective goodness and wisdom, not mere will.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ودليل الأمر عندنا والنهي معرفة الأمر والناهي الله البشر من بين البهائم فى تعرف ذلك ، لم يحتمل اهمالهم عن ذلك ، كما لا يحتمل شيء مما فيه النفع إهماله عنه ، وبما في العقل حسن كل حسن وقبح كل قبيح ، ثم فى الفعل يقبح فعل القبيح فلزم الأمر والنهى لما كان ما به الأمر والنهى ، ولأن الله خلق خلقا يدل على وحدانيته وحكمته ، فلم يجز إخلاء الخلق عن معرفة ذلك ، فيصير خلقه عبثا ، ولما فى رفع الكلفة زوال الخلقة ، إذ حصلت للفناء ، وكل بان شيئا للنقض لا غير فهو عابث غير حكيم. ثم الوعد والوعيد للترغيب والترهيب ، إذ لولا ذلك يذهب نفع الائتمار وضرر العصيان ، ولم يكن لمن خلق في فعلهم نفع ، فإذا لم يكن للمؤتمر نفع ولا للعاصى ضرر يبطل معنى الأمر والنهي ، إذ ليس لنفع الآمر والناهي ، فلذلك لزم الوعد والوعيد في الحكمة .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The proof for command and prohibition according to us is that knowledge of the Commander and Prohibitor is a distinguishing characteristic of humans from beasts in recognizing that; it is not possible to neglect them regarding that, just as it is not possible to neglect anything that contains benefit. And in the intellect, every good is good and every evil is evil; then in action, performing the evil is evil, so command and prohibition are necessary because of what they command and prohibit. And because God created a creation that indicates His oneness and wisdom, it is not permissible to leave creation without knowledge of that, for then His creation would be vain. And raising the obligation would result in the removal of creation, since it came into existence for annihilation; and everyone who establishes something for demolition only is frivolous, not wise. Then promise and threat are for encouragement and deterrence, for without that, the benefit of obedience and the harm of disobedience would be lost, and there would be no benefit for the one who acts. If there is no benefit for the obedient and no harm for the disobedient, the meaning of command and prohibition is nullified, since it is not for the benefit of the Commander and Prohibitor. Therefore, promise and threat are necessitated by wisdom.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof (daliil) for the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions. Maturidi argues: First, humans are distinguished from animals by their ability to recognize the Commander and Prohibitor (God). Therefore, they cannot be neglected in that duty, just as any beneficial thing cannot be neglected. Second, reason itself recognizes that good is good and evil is evil, and performing evil is evil; thus commands and prohibitions are necessary. Third, God created the universe to indicate His oneness and wisdom, so it is impermissible to leave creation ignorant of that, for then creation would be vain (ʿabath). If obligations were lifted, creation would be only for annihilation, which would be frivolous, not wise. Finally, promise and threat are necessary to provide incentive and deterrence; without them, obedience would have no benefit and disobedience no harm, and then commands and prohibitions would be meaningless, since God does not benefit from them. Therefore, wisdom necessitates promise and threat.

Key Terms
دليل الأمر والنهي
حسن وقبح
عبث
الحكمة
الوعد والوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine commands and prohibitions are arbitrary expressions of God's will.
Correction: Maturidi argues they are rationally grounded: reason recognizes good and evil, and creation's purpose demands guidance. Without this, creation would be vain and unwise.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why would creation be vain (عبث) without commands and prohibitions?
A) Because humans would not know God
B) Because creation would have no purpose
C) Because God would be unjust
D) Because the intellect would be useless

Key Takeaway
Divine commands, prohibitions, and the promise of reward and punishment are rationally necessary to give purpose to creation and align human actions with objective good and evil.

God's Huwiyya as Existence and His Eternity without Change
Maturidi defines huwiyya as existence and argues for God's eternity and immutability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's definition of huwiyya as existence and his rational argument for God's eternity and immutability.

Core Idea
God's identity is His existence, and He is beyond change.

Maturidi begins by clarifying a key term: huwiyya, often rendered as 'identity', is actually a metaphor for existence in the created world. He then uses this to assert God's eternal nature, arguing that any change would imply createdness. This short but dense passage establishes foundational principles of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Notice the key terms huwiyya, wujud, ahdath, huduth.
The passage is compact; read slowly.
2
Define huwiyya
Note that Maturidi says huwiyya in the witnessed world is a metaphor for existence (wujud) and its interpretation is negation of non-existence. This is the first conceptual move.
This is a linguistic and metaphysical point.
3
Identify the divine attributes
He then affirms that God never ceases nor will cease without change, passing away, transition, motion, or rest. These negations establish His immutability.
4
Follow the argument against change
Maturidi argues that if God were subject to differing states, He would be inseparable from those states, which are events (ahdath). That would necessitate huduth (being brought into existence), contradicting pre-eternity.
This is a rational proof by contradiction.
5
Conclude with pre-eternity
The argument ends by affirming pre-eternity (al-qidam) for God, which is the necessary attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الهوية في الشاهد كناية عن الوجود، وتأويله نفي العدم عنه، والله تعالى لم يزل ولا يزال بلا تغير ولا زوال ولا انتقال من حال إلى حال، ولا تحرك ولا قرار؛ إذ هو وصف اختلاف الأحوال، تختلف الأحوال عليه فهو غير مفارق لها، ومن لا يفارق الأحوال فهن أحداث، فيجب بها الوصف بالإحداث، وفي ذلك سقوط الوجدانية، ثم القدم.
Then identity (al-huwiyya) in the witnessed world is a metaphor for existence (al-wujud), and its interpretation is the negation of non-existence from Him. And God the Exalted never ceases nor will cease without change, without passing away, without transition from state to state, without motion and without rest; because He is the description of the difference of states—states differ upon Him, so He is not separate from them, and whoever does not separate from states, they are events (ahdath), so the description of being brought into existence (al-huduth) is necessitated by them, and in that lies the collapse of being found (al-wujdaniyya), then pre-eternity (al-qidam).
Source pages p. 704

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'huwiyya' in reference to God in the witnessed world as a metaphor for existence, and its interpretation is the negation of non-existence. Maturidi then affirms that God is eternal (lam yazal wa la yazal) without change, passing away, transition, motion, or rest. He argues that if God were subject to differing states, He would be inseparable from those states, which are events (ahdath), thus God would be described as having been brought into existence (huduth), which contradicts His pre-eternity (qidam). Therefore, God's immutability is essential.

Key Terms
الهوية
الوجود
الأحداث
الإحداث
القدم
الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think huwiyya refers to a hypostasis or underlying substance distinct from existence.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that huwiyya is a metaphor for existence itself, and its interpretation is the negation of non-existence. It does not denote a separate entity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does 'huwiyya' in the witnessed world signify?
A) Essence
B) Existence
C) Attribute
D) Non-existence

Key Takeaway
Maturidi defines God's identity as His existence and argues that God's eternity requires absolute immutability, as change would imply createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's identity is simply His existence without any change, what does that reveal about your own constant need for personal growth? How does this truth challenge you to accept your own limitations and rely on His unchanging nature?
?
If God is beyond states and events, what does that mean for your moments of spiritual highs and lows? How can you anchor your faith in His unchanging existence rather than your fluctuating feelings?
?
The passage warns that associating God with change would collapse the ability to 'find' Him. Are there ways you secretly imagine God as changing—perhaps becoming more merciful after your repentance? How can you repent knowing that He always is what He is, without variation?

Maturidi's Refutation of Natural and Voluntary Causes
Maturidi enumerates and refutes two proposed causes for separation and combination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that separation and combination cannot be explained solely by nature or choice, leading to a refutation of materialist views of the world's eternity.

Core Idea
Separation and combination must be by nature, choice, or something else—each leads to absurdity.

Imam Maturidi presents a logical analysis of the possible causes behind the phenomena of separation and combination. By examining both natural necessity and voluntary choice, he shows that each hypothesis leads to contradictions, thereby undermining the materialist claim that the world is eternal and self-sufficient. This passage is a key example of kalam reasoning applied to cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possible causes
Read the first lines to see that Maturidi lists three possibilities: nature (al-ṭab‘), choice (al-ikhtiyār), or another factor. This framework structures the entire argument.
2
Refutation of the 'by nature' hypothesis
Notice how Maturidi argues that if separation or combination were by nature, the original state would necessarily increase. He gives examples from motion and substances to illustrate that nature produces consistent increase, which is not observed.
This shows that the natural hypothesis leads to an absurd consequence.
3
Refutation of the 'by choice' hypothesis
Maturidi then argues that if it were by choice, we would expect consistency: one who chooses separation should always separate, but we see the opposite occur. This contradiction proves choice cannot be the cause.
The argument uses observed evidence from the physical world.
4
Conclusion and further implications
After refuting both, Maturidi points to deeper inconsistencies about the permanence and retention of substances. The text becomes obscure, but the main refutation is clear.
The end is corrupt, but the core argument stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل فى ذلك أنهما عند التباين لا يعدو إما أن كانا كذلك بالطبع أو بالإختيار أو بأخر يجعلها كذلك ، وكذلك المجتمع منه ، ثم التباين والامتزاج لا يعدوان ما ذكرنا ، فإن كانا كذلك بالطبع لوجب ذلك فما كان أصله التباين أو الاجتماع أن يزداد منه . منه.. ألا كل متحرك بالطبع يزداد بحركة وكذا يستحق وكذلك كل جوهر بطبعه يعلو وفاته فوق ، ومن بطبعه يسفل في حال لما الاجتماع أبدا ، وكذا هذه العبرة . بين من يتحرك من جهة اليمين مع الذى يتحرك إلى اليسار ، وفى ذلك بطلان ما قالوا . وإن كان ذلك بالاختيار فالقول بأن كانا على غير ما عليهما فاسد ؛ لأنه لا دليل على تثبت خلاف لما عليه الشاهد : / أن يكون الذي اختياره التباين يقع اجتماع ، أو الذى اختياره الاجتماع يقع معه تباین ، فبطل الاختيار فساد فيلم من بقاء كل جوهر الآخر واحتباسه، وتحقيق ذلك أن احتباس الخير في الشر شر للآخر عن. فعله واختيار ذلك والعالم بكيفية ذلك ، فإن لم يكن بطل معنى عالی منع
Abū Manṣūr, may God have mercy on him, said: The principle in that is that when they [the two states] are in separation, they cannot escape either being that way by nature, or by choice, or by something else that makes them so; similarly for the combined from it. Then separation and mixing do not go beyond what we have mentioned. If they are that way by nature, then it would be necessary that whatever has its origin in separation or combination would increase from it. Indeed, every naturally moving thing increases in movement and similarly deserves, and likewise every substance by its nature rises and its death is above, and what by nature sinks is in a state of constant combination? And likewise this is the lesson between the one who moves from the right side and the one who moves to the left; and in that is the invalidity of what they said. And if that is by choice, then the statement that they are other than what they are is corrupt, because there is no evidence to establish a contrary to what is witnessed: that the one whose choice is separation would have combination occur, or the one whose choice is combination would have separation occur with him; so choice is invalid. [Then there is] corruption concerning the permanence of each substance over another and its retention. And the verification of that is that retention of good in evil is evil for the other from [؟] its act and choice of that, and the world is how? If not, the meaning of high prevention becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 721

Commentary
This passage is Imam Maturidi's analytical argument regarding the nature of separation (tabāyun) and combination (ijtimā‘) as they pertain to the materialists' claims about the eternal state of the world. He states that when these two states occur together (in separation and combination), they must be either by nature (bi-al-ṭab‘), or by choice (bi-al-ikhtiyār), or by some other factor that makes them so. The same applies to the combined state from separation. He then argues that if they are by nature, then a thing's original state (whether separation or combination) would necessarily increase in that state. He gives examples: a naturally moving thing increases in movement; a naturally rising substance rises until its death (or end); a naturally sinking substance is always in a state of [combination?]. This analogy between types of natural motion and the states shows that if separation or combination were by nature, the world would perpetually become more separate or more combined, which contradicts the observed mixture of states. Therefore, their view is invalid. If, on the other hand, the states are by choice, then it is corrupt to claim they are other than what they appear to be, because there is no evidence that a being whose choice is separation would actually result in combination, or vice versa. Thus, the concept of choice is negated, leading to further absurdities about the permanence of substances. Maturidi then explicates that if good retains itself in evil, that would be evil for the other, and the world's state would be unintelligible; if not, the meaning of God's exalted prevention becomes invalid. The passage ends abruptly and is likely incomplete, but it forcefully argues that both natural and volitional explanations for the eternal existence of opposite states lead to contradictions.

Key Terms
الطبع
الاختيار
التباين
الاجتماع
الامة
الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is about physics and natural science rather than theology.
Correction: Maturidi is using logical arguments about nature and choice to refute materialist claims about the world's eternity; it is a theological polemic disguised as natural philosophy.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the possible causes for the states of separation and combination?
A) Nature only
B) Choice only
C) Nature, choice, or another factor
D) Chance and necessity

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that both natural and volitional explanations for the coexistence of separation and combination are incoherent, thus undermining the materialist view of an eternal world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the order of the universe, do you instinctively attribute it to 'nature' or 'chance', forgetting the intentional act of the Creator? How does Maturidi's logical refutation challenge you to recognize the absurdity of those explanations? What one step can you take today to shift your default thinking from secondary causes to the First Cause?
?
In your daily life, are there areas (e.g., your health, relationships, success) where you rely on 'natural processes' or 'your own choices' as if they were independent, without acknowledging Allah's direct control? How can Maturidi's argument that both 'nature' and 'choice' lead to contradictions reshape your reliance on God? Write a specific intention to thank Allah for every small change you experience.
?
Maturidi shows that if things were by nature, they would increase in that state, yet we see mixture and change. Could it be that you have allowed certain 'natural' habits or 'choices' to define your spiritual state, assuming they will persist? Are you ready to break that assumption and turn to the One who causes all changes, asking Him to transform your heart? What specific du'ā will you make for that?

The Invalidity of Questions About God's Timing
Maturidi refutes the question about God's timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why questions about God's timing of creation are invalid according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Questions about 'why not earlier?' are invalid because time is not indicated and pre-eternal creation is impossible.

Maturidi addresses an objection to divine governance: why didn't God create at a different time? He demonstrates that such a question is logically flawed because it assumes a specific time when none is indicated, and it leads to contradictions about pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument flow through.
Notice the phrase 'لا يشار إلى وقت' – no time is indicated.
2
Identify the objection and response
The objection asks why God did not create differently in wisdom. The response dismisses the question about time as invalid.
The objection is found in the first sentence: 'لم لا خلق دونه في الحكمة وحسن التدبير؟'
3
Understand the logical reasoning
Maturidi argues that if creation could have been earlier, the question would only be about pre-eternity, which is impossible because something cannot come into existence in pre-eternity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في التدبير وأولى بالحكمة، وما هو كذلك، فيخرج السؤال على أنه: لم لا خلق دونه في الحكمة وحسن التدبير؟ قال الشيخ رحمه الله: فما ذكرت من الوقت فهو ما يذكر، على أن السؤال في مثله ساقط؛ لأنه لا يشار إلى وقت، وإلا لو كان الخلق قبل ذلك إلى ما لا يحتمل اللسان من عدد الأوقات ممكن، وفي ذلك بطلان السؤال إلا عن قدمه، وذلك تناقض؛ لإحالة وقوع التكوين على الكائن في القدم، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وما ذكر من الحكمة فذلك حق، وما ذكر من الأصلح لا أدري ما أراد به، وما قال من دونه أو مثله فالقول به لا معنى له، ولله تعالى أن يفعل الفعل؛ إذ الخروج عنه يحقق السفه، وذلك يسقط الربوبية. ثم في الحكمة طريقان: أحدهما العدل والثاني الفضل [؟] الأفضال نهاية، فيتكلم في الشيء بأفضل ما يبلغه قوته من الفعل عليه الأفضال يختص به من شاء. وغير جائز خروج فعله عن الحكمة [؟] وضع كل شيء موضعه، لكن له درجات: يوصف فعل بعضها إحسانا وأفضالا، وفعل به ضنها [؟] عدلا وحكمة؛ إذ هما اسمان عامان لكل ما للفاعل فعله، والأول الحكمة لما ذكرت، وكذلك معنى العدل: إنه خاص من حيث كان له تركه فيفعله منعها [؟] محسنا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Regarding governance and what is more worthy of wisdom, and what is like that, the question arises: why did He not create other than this in wisdom and good governance? The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: As for what you mentioned of time, it is what is mentioned, but the question about such a thing is invalid because no time is indicated. Otherwise, if creation before that to a number of times that the tongue cannot bear were possible, then the question would be invalid except about its eternity, and that is a contradiction because it is impossible for bringing into existence to occur on the pre-eternal. And there is no power except with God. What was mentioned of wisdom is true, and what was mentioned of the better (al-aslah) I do not know what is meant by it. And what was said less than it or like it, saying it has no meaning. God Almighty may do the act, because departing from it confirms foolishness, and that would nullify Lordship. Then in wisdom there are two ways: one is justice and the second is bounty [?] the bounties have no end, so one speaks of the thing with the best that his ability reaches in action. Bounty is exclusively for whom He wills. And it is not permissible for His action to depart from wisdom [?] placing everything in its place, but it has degrees: some of His actions are described as beneficence and bounty, and an action with its opposite [?] as justice and wisdom; for they are two general names for everything that the agent does. The first is wisdom as mentioned, and likewise the meaning of justice: it is specific in that He could leave it but does it, so He is beneficent. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 724

Commentary
This passage addresses objections regarding God's governance and wisdom. It begins with a question: why did God not create differently in wisdom and good governance? The response explains that the question about time is invalid because no specific time is indicated; if creation before the present were possible, the question would only be about pre-eternity, which is contradictory since bringing something into existence in pre-eternity is impossible. Then Maturidi affirms that whatever wisdom is attributed to God is true, but he does not understand what is meant by 'the better' (al-aslah). He states that saying God could have done less or equal has no meaning, because God may do as He wills; departing from action would imply foolishness and nullify Lordship. Next, he presents two ways in wisdom: justice and bounty. Bounty has no limit, and God chooses whom to bestow it upon. God's action cannot depart from wisdom, but actions have degrees: some are described as beneficence and bounty, others as justice and wisdom. These terms are general for all actions of the agent. Justice specifically means doing something that could have been left undone, making it an act of beneficence.

Key Terms
التكوين
الكائن في القدم
الوقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could have created the world earlier and that questioning His timing is valid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such a question is invalid because it leads to the impossible idea of creation in pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the question about God creating at a different time invalid?
A) Because time is irrelevant to God's actions.
B) Because no specific time is indicated, and if earlier creation were possible, the question would only be about pre-eternity, which is impossible.
C) Because God has no control over time.
D) Because the question is blasphemous.

Key Takeaway
Questions about God's timing are invalid because they imply an infinite regress and contradict the impossibility of pre-eternal creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself impatient with Allah's timing in your life—whether for a prayer answered late or a trial prolonged—does this lesson reveal that your questioning is logically flawed? How will you silence that inner complaint using the truth that no time is indicated?
?
Have you ever assumed that Allah could have done something better or earlier, thereby doubting His perfect wisdom? Write down one specific instance. Now, in light of Maturidi's argument, what should you have believed instead, and what concrete step will you take to repent from that assumption?
?
The lesson shows that questioning God's timing leads to a contradiction (pre-eternal creation is impossible). Does your soul sometimes secretly wish that Allah had created you at a different time or in different circumstances? Acknowledge that wish before Him now, and commit to trusting that His timing is the best precisely because He is al-Hakim (the All-Wise).

Wisdom: Justice and Bounty, Rejecting al-Aslah
Maturidi discusses wisdom, rejects al-aslah, and divides wisdom into justice and bounty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's wisdom encompasses justice and bounty, and that the concept of al-aslah (the better) is rejected.

Core Idea
God's actions are always wise, but He is not obligated to create the best; wisdom is divided into justice and bounty.

After refuting the question about time, Maturidi turns to wisdom. He affirms that all God's actions are wise, but rejects the notion that God must do what is 'better' (al-aslah). Instead, he divides wisdom into two categories: justice and bounty, explaining that God's actions have degrees but all are placed in their proper place.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the topic shifts from wisdom to al-aslah to the two ways.
The passage contains a rejection of the concept of al-aslah.
2
Understand the rejection of al-aslah
Maturidi says he does not know what is meant by 'the better' and that saying God could have done less or equal has no meaning.
This is a critique of the Mu'tazilite notion that God must create the best.
3
Learn the two ways of wisdom
Maturidi presents two categories: justice (al-`adl) and bounty (al-fadl). Bounty is without limit and is given to whom God wills.
Justice here means doing what could be left undone, making it beneficence.
4
Reflect on degrees of action
Actions have degrees: some are described as beneficence and bounty, others as justice and wisdom. Both are general names for God's actions.
This shows that all divine actions are wise, but not all are bountiful in the same sense.

?

?

?
