The Necessity of Rational Inquiry
Maturidi defends the necessity of rational inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that abandoning rational inquiry leads to certain destruction and why the intellect demands seeking truth.

Core Idea
Abandoning rational inquiry is not safety but inevitable destruction.

Imam Maturidi addresses a critical objection against rational inquiry (نظر). Some claim that avoiding reflection is safer to avoid error and argument. But Maturidi counters that the very nature of the intellect compels us to seek truth and avoid harm. This lesson examines his defense and the logical necessity of using reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the argument flow naturally.
The passage begins with the objection and then presents the rebuttal.
2
Identify the initial objection
Find the statement of those who say abandoning reflection is safer. Note their reasons: uncertainty of attaining truth and opening oneself to arguments.
The phrase 'ترك النظر أسلم' encapsulates their position.
3
Understand the rebuttal's key points
Notice that Maturidi asserts that abandoning reflection leads to destruction inevitably. He grounds this in the intellect's role and human desires for survival.
The intellect obligates reflection (لزوم النظر) not from a prior reflection but from its own nature.
4
Reflect on the role of intellect
Consider how Maturidi links the intellect to discerning good and evil, superiority over animals, and the need to seek what is beneficial.
The intellect refuses to risk the soul without investigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة [دفاع عن العلم والنظر] قال قوم : ترك النظر أسلم ؛ لما لا يأمن الناظر بالظفر بالحق ، ثم فيه فتح باب الحجة على نفسه مما لو امتنع ليأمن العطب من حيث لولا هو لم يتخلله عنه السبيل الذى يظنه أو الباطل ليلزمه حجة الله ؛ إذ بالفكر والبحث إرادة ما يضطر 10 إلى العلم بأن الحق فى ما انكشف له ، مع اشتباه خاطر الرحمن في الأمر والتحذير من خاطر الشيطان. وفى ترك النظر / والبحث أمن ذلك ؛ إذ لم ينكشف له ما يلزمه التمييز ، ولا يخطر بذهنه ما يبعثه على الطلب ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ومن ألزم النظر والبحث فيقول : فى تركه عطبه لا محالة ؛ لأن لزوم النظر ليس عقيب نظر تقدمه بل عقيب الذى به يقع النظر والبحث وهو العقل الذي به يعرف المحاسن والمساوئ ، وبه يعلم فضله على سائر الحيوان ، وبه يعرف ملك تدبير أمر الأنام ، مع ما يأتى عليه ذهنه وفطنته تبدده وفناه بما نال لادة الحياة ، وركبت فيه شهوة البقاء وعظيم ألم أسباب الفناء ، لو اعترضه فلا بد من البحث في درك ما يلائمه ويبقى ألذ الأشياء وأشهاها عنده مما يأبى عقله المخاطرة بروحه في الامتحان بالأشياء دون تكلف ما يُطلعه على الغاز منها فيتقيه ، والنافع من ذلك فيجتلبه إما بالبحث عن تعرف من يثق بخبره وبأمر خيانته فما يدله عليه ، فيصدر في كل ذلك عن رأيه ، أو أن يجهد في الامتحان بنفسه بالقليل الذي
Section: Defense of knowledge and reflection. Some people said: Abandoning reflection is safer; because the one who reflects is not safe from attaining the truth, and also it opens the door of argument against himself such that if he refrains, he would be safe from destruction in the sense that were it not for reflection, the path that he supposes or falsehood would not penetrate him so that the proof of God would be binding upon him. For through thought and research there is an inclination that compels one to knowledge that the truth is in what is revealed to him, along with the confusion of the merciful thought in the matter and the warning from the satanic thought. And in abandoning reflection and research there is safety from that, because what necessitates discrimination is not revealed to him, nor does it occur to his mind what would incite him to seek. And there is no power except with Allah. And one who makes reflection and research obligatory says: In its abandonment there is destruction inevitably; because the obligation of reflection is not subsequent to a prior reflection but subsequent to that by which reflection and research occur, which is the intellect by which one knows good and evil, and by it he knows his superiority over other animals, and by it he knows the king who manages the affairs of people, along with what his mind and intelligence bring upon him of dispersion and finitude due to what he has attained of the pleasure of life, and there is instilled in him the desire for survival and the great pain of the causes of annihilation. If such thoughts confront him, then he must search to attain what suits him and what remains the most delightful and pleasant things for him, while his intellect refuses to risk his soul in testing things without undertaking what would reveal to him their hidden aspects so that he may guard against them, and the beneficial part he attracts either by searching to know someone whose report he trusts and concerning whose trustworthiness he is informed, so that what he indicates to him, then he acts in all that according to his opinion, or he strives to test himself by means of a little which...
Source pages p. 734

Commentary
This passage is a defense of rational inquiry (نظر) and research. It begins by presenting the argument of those who say abandoning reflection is safer, because the seeker may not attain truth and may open himself to arguments. Then it counters by stating that reflection is obligatory because the intellect (عقل) inherently seeks what is beneficial and avoids harm. The passage details the natural human inclinations: desire for survival, pain of death, and the need to find what is beneficial. It argues that one must use intellect to seek guidance from trustworthy sources or to test things oneself. The passage ends mid-sentence but establishes that abandoning reflection leads to destruction.

Key Terms
نظر
عقل
عطب
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that avoiding rational inquiry is safer to avoid error.
Correction: Maturidi argues that abandoning reflection leads to certain destruction because the intellect demands seeking truth and avoiding harm. Without inquiry, one cannot attain what is beneficial or avoid harm.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against abandoning reflection (ترك النظر)?
A) It is safer
B) It leads to destruction because the intellect demands seeking truth
C) It is forbidden by scripture
D) It causes confusion

Key Takeaway
Rational inquiry is not optional; it is an intellectual necessity for survival and truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you actively using your intellect to seek the truth about your Creator, or are you hiding behind the claim that it's safer to avoid questioning? What specific question about your faith are you afraid to ask because it might open the door to arguments? Will you confront it today?
?
You have an intellect that distinguishes you from animals and enables you to know the King who manages all affairs. If you neglect rational inquiry, what excuse will you offer when you stand before Him? How can you begin to use your mind properly starting now?
?
The passage says abandoning reflection leads to inevitable destruction. In which area of your life have you stopped reflecting because it felt safer? What is the cost of that avoidance, and what step will you take today to resume sincere inquiry?

The Necessity of Reflection
Maturidi argues for the necessity of reflection and identifies the satanic source of discouragement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that reflection is a necessary and inescapable duty for every human being, and that avoiding it is a satanic trick.

Core Idea
Reflection is compelled by human nature and intellect, not optional.

Maturidi begins by showing that every person is driven by pleasure and pain, ignorance and desire, to reflect on their own existence. He then warns that the thought urging one to abandon reflection is from Satan, because using the intellect distracts from base desires. This lesson establishes the foundation for why reflection is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi builds his argument from human experience.
The passage contains several conditional statements and parallel structures.
2
Identify the two driving forces
Maturidi mentions natural inclinations (pleasure/pain) and Satanic whispers as forces that influence reflection. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the proof from intellect
Maturidi gives a proof: employing the intellect to know hidden origins and the Maker occupies one from desires, confirming that the thought to abandon reflection is satanic.
This connects to the analogy with limbs in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Ask yourself: Why does Maturidi consider reflection unavoidable? How does he prove that avoiding it is from Satan?
This prepares you for the three outcomes in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الملاذ، يريه عاقبته مما يؤمن عن مثله الملاك لقلته، فيكون فى الأمرين جميعا لزوم البحث. ما يدفعه جهله بما جبل عليه من الشهوات، مما يبعثه عليه نفسه عما يصيبه من المكروه وما يحل به من الألم، إلى النظر في حال نفسه إنه بما صار كذلك، أو هل كان كذلك فى الأبد؟ أو من أي وجه صار كذلك؟ لا يسلم من بعض الخواطر التي تمنعه عن ترك النظر في أحوال نفسه ليعرف به مباديه ما لا بد من أن وليعلم أنه لذلك بنفسه أو بمن له في نفسه تدبير. مع يعرف ما به صلاحه وفساده، وما عليه من النعم وعنه من الدفاع، وفى كل ذلك اضطرار إلى النظر ولزوم الحيجة وبالله التوفيق. مع ما يعلم بيقين أن هذا الذي سيل له ترك النظر هو خاطر الشيطان؛ إذ ذلك عمله ليصده عن ثمرة عقله ويفزعه لأمانته التي لديها ينال الفرضة ويظفر بالبغية، دليل ذلك أن استعمال العقل بالفكر بالأشياء ليعرف ما استتر منها من المبادئ والنهايات، ثم فيما يدله على حدثها ومحدثها يشغله عن شهوات النفس ليعلم أن ذلك هو صنيع الشيطان. على أنه إذ لم يجز إعمال شيء من الجوارح عن المنافع التي جعلت فيها، ولا كفتها عن أعمالها بته، بل يجب كفها عن الوجوه الضارة واستعمالها بالوجود النافية، فالعقل والنظر الذى بهما تعرف المنافع والمضار أحق أن لا يتهاد مع ما كان الناظر عند ورود المخاطر لا يعدو خصالا ثلاثة: إما أن ينضى به نظره إلى العلم بخدثه وأن له محدثا يجزيه بالإحسان ويعاقبه بالإساءة، فيجتنب ما يسخطه ويقبل على ما يرضيه فيسعد وينال شرف الدارين، أو يفضى به إلى نفى ما ذكرنا فيتمتع بصنوف اللذات، أما العقاب فينتظره في الآخره، أو يفضى به إلى العلم باستغلاق باب العلم بحقيقه ما دعى إليه، فيستريح قلبه ويزول عنه الوجل الذي يعتريه إذا فزعته الخواطر، فيعلم إذا أنصف أنه على ربح في نظره من كل وجه، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
From pleasures [or: delights], He shows him its consequence, which secures him from the like of the angel [??] due to its scarcity. So in both matters there is necessity of investigation. His ignorance pushes him, due to the desires he was created upon, from what his soul urges him about the disliked that befalls him and the pain that descends upon him, to reflect on his own state: how did he become such, or was he always such from eternity, or from which aspect did he become such? He is not safe from some thoughts that prevent him from abandoning reflection on his own states to know thereby his origins, what is necessary from [??] and to know that he is therefore [dependent] on himself or on one who has governance over him. And he knows what constitutes his well-being and corruption, and the favors upon him and the defense from him. And in all that there is compulsion to reflection and necessity of the proof. And with God is success. Along with what he knows with certainty that this which calls him to abandon reflection is a thought from Satan; for that is his work to turn him away from the fruit of his intellect and to frighten him from his trust by which he attains the opportunity and gains the goal. The proof of that is that employing the intellect in thinking about things to know what is hidden from them of origins and ends, and then what guides him to their temporality and their Maker, occupies him from the desires of the soul, so that he knows that is the doing of Satan. Moreover, since it is not permissible to employ any limb away from the benefits for which they were created, nor to restrain them from their functions [??], but rather they must be restrained from harmful ways and employed in beneficial ways, then the intellect and reflection, by which benefits and harms are known, are more deserving not to be neglected. And the one reflecting, when dangers occur, does not exceed three characteristics: either his reflection leads him to knowledge of his temporality and that he has a Maker who rewards for good and punishes for evil, so he avoids what displeases Him and turns to what pleases Him, thus he is happy and attains the honor of both abodes; or it leads him to denial of what we mentioned, so he enjoys various pleasures, but punishment awaits him in the Hereafter; or it leads him to knowledge of the closure of the door of knowledge of the reality of what he was called to, so his heart rests and the fear that afflicts him when thoughts frighten him leaves him, so he knows, if he is fair, that he is at profit in his reflection from every aspect. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 735

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of reflection (al-nazar) and an enumeration of its three possible outcomes. Maturidi begins by stating that a person is naturally inclined to avoid harm and seek pleasure, but ignorance and desires push him to reflect on his own state. He cannot avoid thoughts that tempt him to abandon reflection, but he must know his origins and whether he is self-sufficient or dependent on another. Then Maturidi asserts that the thought urging him to stop reflecting is from Satan, because using the intellect to know hidden things distracts from sensual desires. He argues by analogy: just as limbs must be used for benefit and prevented from harm, the intellect and reflection are even more deserving of proper use. Then he presents the three outcomes for one who reflects when facing dangers: first, knowledge of his createdness and a Creator who rewards and punishes, leading to happiness; second, denial, leading to temporary pleasure but eventual punishment; third, the conclusion that the door of knowledge is closed, which brings relief from fear. In all cases, reflection is profitable. The passage emphasizes that reflection is always beneficial and that avoiding it is a Satanic trick.

Key Terms
نظر (reflection)
خاطر (thought)
شيطان (Satan)
عقل (intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reflection on theological matters is optional and can be abandoned without consequence.
Correction: Maturidi shows that reflection is compelled by human nature and that abandoning it is a satanic trick, so it is actually obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the source of the thought that tells a person to abandon reflection?
A) The person's own intellect
B) Satan
C) Natural desires
D) The angel

Key Takeaway
Reflection is an inescapable duty because human nature compels it, and Satan tries to prevent it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you felt a thought telling you to stop thinking about your purpose or origins? How can you be sure it was not from your own laziness but from Satan, as Maturidi describes?
?
Think of a recent moment of pain or pleasure. Did it drive you to investigate your own state and your relationship with your Creator? If not, what stopped you?
?
Is there an area of your life where you are actively avoiding reflection—perhaps about your soul, your death, or your accountability—because it feels uncomfortable? What would it cost you to face that now?

The Three Outcomes of Reflection
Maturidi enumerates three outcomes of reflection and declares all of them profitable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible outcomes of reflection according to Maturidi and why all of them are profitable.

Core Idea
Every honest reflection leads to one of three outcomes, all beneficial.

Having established the necessity of reflection, Maturidi now presents what happens when a person actually reflects when facing dangers. He lists three possible results: knowledge of the Creator, denial, or the realization that the truth is inaccessible. Remarkably, he argues that all three are profitable for the seeker. This lesson explores these outcomes and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the three characteristics (خصال ثلاثة) and the consequences of each.
2
Identify the three outcomes
List the three outcomes: leading to knowledge of the Creator and reward; leading to denial and punishment; or leading to the conclusion that the door of knowledge is closed, bringing relief.
3
Understand the final judgment
Maturidi concludes that in all three cases, the person is at profit. Why? Because even the third outcome brings relief from fear, and the first two have clear consequences.
This shows that reflection always has positive benefits.

Why Ignorance Is No Excuse Before God
Maturidi answers an objection about ignorance as an excuse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ignorance due to abandoning reflection is not an acceptable excuse before God according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Ignorance is no excuse when evidence is available.

The objector raises a dilemma: in worldly affairs, a servant's excuse of ignorance is accepted by his master. Why then does God not accept the same excuse? Maturidi's answer reveals a crucial difference: in worldly matters, evidence for the command may be absent, but God has provided clear evidence, prompting reflection. The servant's failure is his own abandonment of reflection, making his excuse invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection from the one who questions why ignorance is not accepted by God, based on the analogy of a worldly master accepting the excuse 'I did not know.'
Notice the force of the analogy: if a master accepts it, why wouldn't God?
2
Identify Maturidi's answer
Read Maturidi's response: the excuse is accepted in worldly matters only because evidence is missing. But God has provided evidence, thoughts, and signs. So the failure is due to abandoning reflection, which is the servant's own action.
The key distinction is the availability of evidence.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The servant is refuted because his excuse arises from his own action of turning away from the evidence. Understand why this makes the excuse unacceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال: إذ لا عذر فى الشاهد للعبد أقبل من قوله لسيده: لم أعلم أن فعلى يسخطك فأتركه، ولو علمت ذلك لا نزجرت مما فعلت، لم لا كان ذلك في حكمة الله مقبولا؟ قيل: ذلك إنما حسن بيننا لارتفاع ما به يُعرف الأمر من الدليل عليه، وأما الله سبحانه فقد جعل لعبده على الأمر بما أمره به دليلا وحرك ذهنه بالخواطر ونبهه بصنوف العبر، فإنما أتى من قبل تركه النظر، وذلك فعله، فيصير بما هو معتذرا محجوجا؛ إذ بفعله أعرض عن ذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
If he said: Since in the witnessed realm there is no excuse for the servant that is accepted from his saying to his master: 'I did not know that my action angers you, so I abandon it, and if I had known that, I would have refrained from what I did,' why is that not accepted in the wisdom of God? It is said: That is only considered good among us due to the absence of what makes the command known by evidence. But as for God the Exalted, He has made for His servant evidence for the command with which He commanded him, and He moved his mind with thoughts and alerted him with kinds of signs. So the failure came only from his abandonment of reflection, and that is his own action. Thus he becomes, while making an excuse, refuted; because by his action he turned away from that. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage is a second objection and answer. The objector argues that in worldly matters a servant's excuse 'I did not know' is accepted by his master, so why is it not accepted by God? The answer explains that in worldly matters the excuse is accepted because the evidence for the command may be absent. But God has provided clear evidence (dalil), moved the mind through thoughts (khawatir), and alerted through various signs ('ibar). The servant's failure is due to his own abandonment of reflection (tark al-nazar), so his excuse is invalid and he is refuted (mahjuj).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ignorance is always a valid excuse before God.
Correction: Ignorance is only an excuse when evidence is absent. Since God provides clear evidence and prompts reflection, ignorance due to abandoning reflection is not excusable.

Checkpoint

B) Because evidence is absent in worldly matters but present from God

D) Because the servant is lying

Key Takeaway
Ignorance is not an excuse when God has provided evidence and the servant abandons reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you avoid a clear obligation, do you tell yourself 'I didn't know it was that important'? How does Maturidi's teaching expose that excuse as a cover for your own refusal to reflect? What will you do the next time you catch yourself making that excuse?
?
You have received signs (عبر) and inner thoughts (خواطر) from God—a nudge to pray, a reminder of a sin, an insight into truth. Have you ever ignored them and later claimed ignorance? In light of this lesson, will you commit to acting on the next sign you receive, no matter how small?
?
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' (no power except with God). If your failure to reflect is your own action, what does it mean to rely on God's power to change? What specific step can you take today to replace negligence with reflection?

Ibn Shabib's Argument for Temporality of Bodies
Ibn Shabib's argument for the temporality of bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Shabib's argument that bodies are temporal (originated) because they are never free from motion or rest, both of which are temporally originated.

Core Idea
Bodies are temporal because they are never free from motion or rest.

Ibn Shabib, a theologian, presented a concise argument for the temporality (huduth) of bodies. The argument relies on the observation that bodies are always accompanied by either motion or rest, both of which are undeniably temporal events. This reasoning is a classic example of the kalam cosmological argument. Although the passage is incomplete, the core logic is clear and worth studying.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ibn Shabib introduces the two states: rest (sukun) and motion (haraka).
The original term 'الظعن' may mean 'travel' or 'departure'.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument has three parts: (1) bodies are never free from motion or rest, (2) both are originated, (3) therefore the body is originated. Look for these in the text.
The conclusion 'therefore the body is originated' is implied.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: Why must motion and rest be originated? How does that necessitate the temporality of the body? The commentary will clarify.
4
Note the incomplete nature
The passage is cut off at the end of the page. Consider what might have followed—perhaps a rebuttal or further elaboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة ابن شبيب في حدث الأجسام. واحتج محمد بن شبيب فى حدث الأجسام بما لا يخلو من سكون هو مقام، وحركة هي الظعن، وهما محدثان بما يختلف على الإثنين المكان بما تقدم.
Discussion of Ibn Shabib on the temporality of bodies. And Muhammad ibn Shabib argued for the temporality of bodies by what is never free from rest, which is staying, and motion, which is departure, and both are originated (temporal) by what differs on the two from place as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage is a brief presentation of Ibn Shabib's argument for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies. The argument is that bodies are never free from either rest (sukun), which is staying in place, or motion (haraka), which is departure. Both rest and motion are originated (muhdathan). Since the body is never without them, it must also be originated. The argument is incomplete and may have continued on the next page.

Key Terms
حدث (temporality/createdness)
جسم (body)
سكون (rest)
حركة (motion)
محدث (originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion and rest are eternal and not temporally originated.
Correction: Ibn Shabib argues that motion and rest are clearly temporal because they begin and end; they are not eternal. Therefore, whatever is always accompanied by them must also be temporal.

Checkpoint

B) Bodies are never free from motion or rest, both of which are temporal
C) Bodies change color
D) Bodies are perceived by senses

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib argued that bodies are temporal because they are never without motion or rest, both of which are temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your body is constantly in motion or rest. If you truly believe this proves your body is temporary and will perish, why do you still cling to worldly comforts and pleasures as if you will live forever? What one attachment can you begin to loosen today because of this truth?
?
The argument shows that everything that never freezes change must be created. Your heart experiences constant change—feelings, desires, intentions. If your heart is always changing, how can it be eternal? Are you investing in what will last, or only in what will pass away? Commit to one action today that prepares your heart for what is permanent.

Ibn Shabib's Objection on Sensory Disagreement
Maturidi presents and answers Ibn Shabib's objection about seeing one thing as two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi addresses the epistemological challenge of sensory disagreement using the concept of defect as a veil.

Core Idea
Sensory disagreement does not prove relativism; it reveals the role of defects.

This lesson presents an objection raised by Muhammad ibn Shabib concerning conflicting sensory perceptions. How can we know the truth when two people see the same object differently? Maturidi's response hinges on the idea that a defect in the sense organ acts as a veil, preserving the objectivity of reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the objection and the reasoning presented.
Pay attention to the example of seeing one thing as two.
2
Analyze Ibn Shabib's reasoning
Understand his claim that each eye sees from a different direction, and his proof that losing one eye leads to missing that direction.
This reasoning challenges the idea of a single truth.
3
Understand Maturidi's answer
Identify the implied answer: the defect is a veil that distorts perception, so disagreement does not negate reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما عارض بأشياء ظهر له خلافه، ولو لم يكن علم البتة بطل ما به يافع من ظهور الخلاف، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وسأل محمد بن شبيب نفسه بما يرى الشيء الواحد شيئين، وآخر يرى شيئا واحدا، فأيها الحق ؟ فزعم أن الأول حسبه كذلك لنظره، يبصره من جهته، يرى بكل عين غير الجهة التي يرى بالآخر، دليله أنه لو أعور لا يرى . الآفة، فيه فيعلم أن الآفة حجاب،
What contradicted him with things that appeared contrary to him, and if there were no knowledge at all, what he had would be nullified by the appearance of contradiction, and there is no power but in Allah. And Muhammad ibn Shabib asked himself about what one sees as a single thing as two, and another sees as one thing, so which is the truth? He claimed that the first sees it as such by his sight, he sees it from his direction, he sees with each eye a different direction from that which he sees with the other, his proof being that if he were one-eyed he would not see. The defect, in it, so he knows that the defect is a veil.
Source pages p. 753

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from Muhammad ibn Shabib regarding sensory disagreement. He raises a common epistemological challenge: if two people perceive the same object differently, how can we determine which perception is true? He gives the example of one person seeing a single thing as two, another seeing it as one. He claims that the first person sees correctly from his own perspective, with each eye viewing from a different angle. He argues that if one eye were blind, he would not see that specific angle, implying that multiple perspectives yield multiple truths. The answer, as implied by the text, is that the defect (آفة) acts as a veil (حجاب) that distorts perception, and thus disagreement does not negate the underlying reality. Maturidi follows the author's order: first the objection, then the reasoning, then the implied resolution.

Key Terms
آفة
حجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory disagreement proves that truth is relative or that there is no objective reality.
Correction: Disagreement in sensory perception is due to defects or conditions in the perceiver (like a veil), not because reality itself is contradictory. Objective truth remains.

Checkpoint
What did Muhammad ibn Shabib use as proof for his claim that the first person sees correctly?
A) The object is really two things
B) Each eye sees from a different direction
C) The second person is blind
D) The defect is a veil

Key Takeaway
Sensory disagreement arises from defects in perception, not from reality being subjective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a disagreement with someone about something you both observed, do you first assume truth is subjective, or do you honestly examine the 'آفة' in your own perception that might be distorting reality? What specific defects might you be overlooking?
?
How often do you blame external conditions or others when your perception is wrong, rather than recognizing that the defect lies within your own instruments—your eyes, your mind, your biases? Can you recall a time when you insisted you were right only to discover later that your own 'حجاب' was the problem?
?
If sensory disagreement does not prove relativism, then how do you live that belief when your own senses tell you one thing and a trustworthy source tells you another? What does that demand of you in terms of humility and seeking the truth?

The Principle of Sensory Variation
The faqih explains and defends sensory knowledge variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sensory knowledge varies according to the state of the senses, and how this principle refutes the claim that sensory disagreement negates objective reality.

Core Idea
Sensory variation reflects changes in the perceiver, not in the reality of the perceived object.

The faqih opens with a foundational principle: what we perceive through our senses depends on the condition of those senses. This is not a flaw but a feature of sensory knowledge. He then explains several factors—like distance, atmospheric interference, and defects—that cause variation, and shows that this actually confirms, not denies, the existence of a stable reality. The passage is a subtle defense of objective truth against skeptical arguments based on sensory disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: a general principle, then detailed causes, then a logical conclusion against the skeptic.
The Arabic has some unclear phrases due to OCR; use the translation to aid comprehension.
2
Identify the causes of sensory variation
List the factors the faqih mentions: subtlety of the object, distance, atmospheric covering, and defects in the sense organ itself. Ask yourself how each affects perception.
These are all conditions of the sense, not changes in the object.
3
Understand the argument against skepticism
The faqih uses sensory variation to argue that disagreement does not prove reality is relative. Instead, the variation itself is known through the senses, so the skeptic's claim is self-defeating.
This is a rebuttal to those who say 'since senses differ, there is no true reality.'
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this principle applies to theological arguments. It shows that our limited and variable perception does not undermine our knowledge of a fixed, objective truth—including truths about Allah.
This connects to the larger theme of rational judgments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : والأصل في هذا ونحوه أن علم الحس يختلف باختلاف أحوال الحس، يعلم ذو الحواس ما به من فبالحاسة يتعلم خلاف الحقيقة عند الآفة، وحقيقته ممتدا ارتفاعها، وذلك يكون في الذي وقعت عليه الحاسة لطافة أو بعد أو ستر الجو بما يغشاه، ومرة يكون من في البصر، وعلى ذلك شأن كل حاسة، وذلك كله معلوم بالحواس، فلا نقيض عليه ما أنه على هذا القول يبطل القول بالخلاف، وبه يحتج، أو يثبت فيبطل قوله بنفى الحقيقة؛ إذ ثبت الاختلاف، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ويعلم الذي يذكر بالقرب منه أو بالزيادة من الضوء ليعلم حقيقته إن ضعف بصره عن إدراكه بالآفة، فنى مثل هذه الأحوال يظهر، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The jurist, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle in this and similar matters is that sensory knowledge varies according to the states of the sense. The possessor of senses knows what is in him, for through the sense he learns contrary to reality when there is a defect, and its reality is extended in height. That occurs in what the sense falls upon, whether subtlety, distance, or covering of the atmosphere by what enshrouds it, and sometimes it is from the vision, and likewise the case for every sense. All that is known through the senses, so there is no contradiction to it that upon this statement the statement of disagreement is invalidated, and by it he argues, or it is established, then his statement denying reality is invalidated, since disagreement is established. And there is no power but in Allah. And he knows what is mentioned by its nearness or by increase of light so that he knows its reality if his sight is too weak to perceive it due to a defect, and in such states it appears, and there is no power but in Allah.
Source pages p. 753

Commentary
This passage is a explanatory statement by the jurist (likely Abu Mansur al-Maturidi) establishing the principle that sensory knowledge varies according to the conditions of the sense organ. He explains that a person with senses learns something contrary to reality when a defect (آفة) is present. The reality of the object remains extended (in height), but perception changes due to factors like subtlety (smallness), distance, or an atmospheric covering. He specifies that this applies to vision and analogously to all senses. Knowledge of these variations comes through the senses themselves, so there is no contradiction: either the objector's claim that sensory disagreement proves no truth is invalidated, or if his claim is based on sensory evidence, it actually confirms disagreement but does not negate reality. The passage then adds that one can perceive an object better by approaching or increasing light, unless the sight is too weak due to a defect. The author follows the order: first a general principle, then a detailed explanation of causes, then the logical consequence against the opponent's denial of reality.

Key Terms
علم الحس
آفة
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensory variation means that reality itself is relative and there is no objective truth.
Correction: The faqih argues that variation is due to conditions of the sense, not changes in the object, and that the variability itself is known through the senses, so it actually supports the existence of a fixed reality.

Checkpoint
According to the faqih, what causes sensory knowledge to vary?
A) The object changes its nature
B) The states of the sense
C) The person's intention
D) The time of day

Key Takeaway
Sensory variation is a result of conditions affecting the senses, and this fact does not undermine objective reality; rather, it confirms it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you realise your senses have deceived you (e.g., misjudging a person's tone, seeing something in dim light), do you default to doubting the reality of what you perceived, or do you investigate the condition of your sense? This lesson demands you examine yourself: how often do you let sensory error lead you to relativism rather than seeking clarity?
?
Think of a recent disagreement where both parties were 'sure' of what they saw or heard. Did you conclude that 'truth is subjective'? If so, how can you apply the faqih's principle — that variation is due to conditions, not unreality — to restore confidence in objective truth while acknowledging your own perceptual limits?
?
You have just read that even the knowledge of variation comes through the senses. If you genuinely believe this, does it change how you handle moments of doubt? Take one specific area of your faith where sensory evidence (or lack thereof) causes you to waver. What concrete action will you take this week to seek clarity about your own sense condition, rather than rejecting the reality itself?

The Honey Argument: Subjectivity of Taste
Maturidi answers Ibn Shabib's honey argument about bitterness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the case of a person with jaundice tasting honey as bitter to argue that perceived qualities can be subjective and dependent on the observer's condition.

Core Idea
Bitterness perceived in honey may come from the taster, not the honey itself.

Imam Maturidi addresses a clever objection from Ibn Shabib: if someone with jaundice (صاحب السفراء) finds honey bitter, does that mean honey is actually bitter? The objection challenges the consistency of sensory evidence. Maturidi's brief answer points to a defect in the taster, and then he reports two explanatory opinions. This passage invites us to consider whether qualities we perceive are inherent in objects or arise from our own conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the key terms: سفراء (bile/jaundice), مرارة (bitterness), ذائق (taster). Try to identify Maturidi's own answer and the two opinions he reports.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Analyze Maturidi's answer
Maturidi first gives his own answer: 'this is from his own defect in what he tastes with.' Reflect on what this means: the cause of the bitterness is in the person, not the honey.
3
Understand the two opinions
Ibn Shabib reports two views: (1) bitterness is in the honey and becomes stronger upon contact with bile; (2) bitterness is in the taster's bile and mixes with sweetness, causing a bitter sensation. Note how these differ in locating the source of bitterness.
The passage does not explicitly choose between them, but Maturidi's initial answer favors the second view.
4
Connect to broader kalam
Consider why this argument matters. It shows that sensory perceptions can be misleading due to the observer's condition. This undermines naive realism and supports the idea that rational judgment is needed to determine objective reality.
This relates to the topic of rational judgments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابنا في صاحب السفراء الذي يجد العسل مرا، هذا من نفسه الآفة فيما يجد به الطعم، ولا قوة إلا بالله مع ما يعلم هو وقال ابن شبيب : أختلف فيه، قال قوم : في العسل مرارة، فاذا اتصل بما فى ذائقه فيقوى (المرارة) فيجده (مُرا) . وقال قوم : إن في ذائق صاحب الصفراء مرارة المرة الصفراء، فلما اتصلت حلاوة العسل بالمرة التي في الذائق وتحركت فى ذائقه وجد حسها كذلك
And our answer regarding the person with bile who finds honey bitter: this is from his own defect in what he tastes with, and there is no power but in Allah with what he knows. And Ibn Shabib said: There is disagreement about it. Some people said: In honey there is bitterness, so when it contacts what is in his taster it strengthens (the bitterness), so he finds it (bitter). And some people said: In the taster of the person with bile is the bitterness of yellow bile, so when the sweetness of honey contacted the bitterness that is in the taster and moved in his taster, he found its sensation likewise.
Source pages p. 753

Commentary
This passage presents a further objection from Ibn Shabib regarding a case where someone with jaundice (صاحب السفراء) finds honey bitter. Maturidi gives a brief answer: it is due to a defect in the taster himself. Then he reports Ibn Shabib's account of two opinions: one group thinks bitterness is actually in the honey and becomes stronger upon contact with the taster's bile; another group thinks the bitterness comes from the yellow bile in the taster, and the sweet honey mixes with that bile, causing the sensation of bitterness. The passage does not explicitly choose between these two, but the earlier answer indicates the defect is in the taster. This is a classic kalam argument about whether qualities perceived are objective or subjective.

Key Terms
سفراء
مرارة
ذائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That honey is objectively bitter because a person with jaundice tastes it as bitter.
Correction: The bitterness may arise from the taster's condition (bile) rather than being an inherent property of honey.

Checkpoint

A) Because honey contains bitterness
B) Because his taster has a defect
C) Because bile changes the honey
D) Because his sense of taste is mistaken

Key Takeaway
Perceived qualities like bitterness may depend on the observer's condition, not the object alone, showing the need for rational judgment beyond sensory experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a person or situation you currently judge harshly. Could your own internal 'bile'—anger, resentment, or unchecked assumption—be distorting your perception? What would it take to set that aside for 24 hours and re-evaluate?
?
When have you been absolutely certain that something was 'bitter' (bad, wrong, unfair) only to later realize the problem was within you? What do you need to admit to yourself today to avoid that blindness?
?
If your own physical or emotional state can change how you taste honey, how much more might it distort your view of a spouse, a child, or a fellow believer? Name one concrete step you can take this week to check your internal state before rendering a verdict on someone else.

The Three States of Sensory Perception
Maturidi explains the principle of human directions and the three states of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains the three states of sensory perception and why variation in perception does not undermine objective reality.

Core Idea
Perception varies due to direction, defect, or veil, but reality remains knowable.

Maturidi, in this passage, addresses a key epistemological question: if different people perceive things differently, does that mean there is no true reality? He explains that the human body has multiple directions, each facing a corresponding aspect of the perceived object. Perception can vary due to defects, veils, or mixing, but the underlying reality is still known through the senses when conditions are proper. Let's explore his threefold classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: حدود, جهات, آفة, ساتر, اختلاط, درك.
2
Understand the principle of directions
Maturidi states that each direction of the human body faces a corresponding direction of the perceived object. Normally, you perceive only what faces you directly. This principle is the foundation for the three states.
Think of how your eyes face forward; you don't see behind you.
3
Identify the three states
The passage explicitly lists three states: 1) by turning the direction, you perceive nothing; 2) with all veils removed, you perceive reality; 3) mixing of conditions leads to variable perception. Notice how each state relates to the presence or absence of obstacles.
These are the 'three states' (أحوال ثلاثة) mentioned.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's conclusion
Maturidi ends by asserting that all these variations are true sensory experiences, but sensory knowledge itself has no disagreement about reality. This is a strong defense of objective truth despite subjective variation.
Consider how this answer potential skepticism about sense perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأصل فى هذا أن الإنسان إذا اشتمل على حدود وجهات فكل جهة منه تقابل جهة من المدرك، لا يُدرك بتلك الجهة غير الجهة التي قابلته، فإذا اعترضت الافة في جهته التى بها يدرك مقابلها أو غشى مقابلها شيء ستره فيذهب مقدار ذلك من الجهة ومقابلها، فيكون كالإدراك بغير الجهة التي هي لذلك النوع من الإدراك، فيكون الأحوال ثلاثة : بقلب الجهة لا يدرك منه شيئا البتة، وتقريرها مع ارتفاع السواتر كلها فيدرك به حقيقة المدرك، أو الاختلاط، فعلى تفاوت ذلك يتفاوت الدرك، وكل ذلك حق، الحس معلوم بالحس، فلم يرد في علم الحس اختلاف البتة في الحقيقة، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The principle in this is that when a human is composed of boundaries and directions, each direction of him faces a direction of what is perceived. He does not perceive by that direction other than the direction that faced it. So if a defect occurs in his direction by which he perceives its opposite, or something covers its opposite and veils it, then a measure of that direction and its opposite goes away, so it becomes like perception by a direction other than the direction that is for that type of perception. So the states are three: by turning the direction he perceives nothing at all; its confirmation with the removal of all veils, so he perceives the reality of what is perceived; or mixing. So according to the variation of that, perception varies. And all that is true; the sense is known by the sense. So there is no disagreement at all in sensory knowledge regarding reality. And there is no power but in Allah.
Source pages p. 754

Commentary
This passage is a fundamental epistemological principle by Maturidi. He explains that the human body has multiple 'directions' or 'aspects' (جهات), each facing a corresponding direction of the perceived object. Normally, each direction perceives only what faces it. However, if a defect (آفة) affects that direction, or if something veils the opposite, then perception is altered, as if by a different direction. He then enumerates three states (أحوال ثلاثة): first, by turning away the direction (بقلب الجهة), one perceives nothing at all; second, when all veils are removed, one perceives the reality of the object; third, a mixing (الاختلاط) of conditions leads to variable perception. He insists that all these variations are true as sensory experiences, and sensory knowledge itself has no disagreement about reality. This is a sophisticated defense of objective reality despite subjective variation.

Key Terms
حدود
جهات
آفة
ساتر
اختلاط
الدرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That perception varies means reality is uncertain.
Correction: Maturidi explains that variation is due to directional obstruction or mixing, and when veils are removed, the senses perceive the true reality, so objective knowledge is possible.

Checkpoint
How many states of perception does Maturidi enumerate?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that although perception can vary due to direction, defect, or mixing, the senses reveal reality when veils are removed, affirming objective knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you dismissed a truth because your perception was veiled—by your own direction, a defect in your understanding, or a cover of desire. How will you remove that veil now, and what will you do before the day ends to see that truth clearly?
?
If the senses can be trusted when all veils are removed, then what veils have you allowed to remain between you and the reality of your own shortcomings? Name one specific veil you have been hiding behind, and commit to removing it by tomorrow.
?
You have read that 'there is no disagreement at all in sensory knowledge regarding reality' when the sense is sound. In what area of your faith have you used the excuse of 'different perceptions' to avoid accepting a clear truth? What step will you take today to align your perception with that truth?

The Skeptical Objection from Dreams and Impossible Perceptions
Maturidi presents the skeptical objection from dreams and the impossibility of dream perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptical challenge that equates wakeful and dream perceptions and Maturidi's initial response based on the impossibility of certain dream contents.

Core Idea
Dreams contain impossible scenarios that wakeful people cannot accept; this reveals a basic distinction.

Maturidi begins by criticizing those who explain natural phenomena as mere arbitrary occurrences. He then presents a skeptical objection: if dream perceptions can be false, how can we trust wakeful ones? The objector claims that the difference is simply that the mind imagines impossible things during sleep, like seeing oneself dead. Maturidi's analysis of such impossible perceptions provides the first step in defending the reliability of wakeful knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the skeptical objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how the objector compares wakeful and dream experiences and proposes a distinguishing feature.
The objector is a hypothetical skeptic, not Maturidi himself.
2
Look for the critique of natural explanations
The passage opens with a critique of those who attribute natural phenomena to nature or chance. Understand why Maturidi calls this an arbitrary ruling upon God.
3
Examine the objector's distinction
The objector says the difference is that in sleep, the mind imagines impossible things (e.g., seeing oneself dead). Note the examples and why they are considered impossible.
A dead person cannot perceive, so seeing oneself dead is a contradiction.
4
Reflect on the insufficiency of this distinction
Consider why simply pointing to impossible dream contents may not fully establish the truth of wakeful perception. This sets up the need for a deeper argument.
The passage ends before the full response; the next lesson covers it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسبح في خلق، وبهذا الطبع جبل: بعض الجواهر يطير في السماء، وآخر الماء، والثالث يمشى على وجه الأرض، فتكلف الاعتلال لمثل هذا تحكم على رب العالمين، واعتلال بما لم يؤذن له، ولا له به درك، وليس ذلك من نوع ما ضمن الشرع فيه من تحقيق الأعيان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم عارض نفسه بما يرى النائم، فيخرج على ما يرى، فلعل أمر اليقظان على هذا، أو ما يعلم يعلم ذا من ذا فزعم أن الذي يفرق بين الأمرين أنه في العقل في حال النوم، نحو أن يرى نفسه ميتا، والميت لا يعلم أو يرى رأسه ملقى في حجره، ومثله لا يحتمل رؤية اليقظان. فإن قيل: كيف يتوهم النائم المحال، لا يثبت في الوهم؟ قيل: عند ما يرى نفسه في المنام لا يعتقدها حية ميتة، وذلك هو وكذلك إذا رأى رأسه ملقى لا يتوهمه في مكانين. وزعم أن العلم بصحة ما في اليقظة وفساد ما في النوم اكتساب، دليله ما ذكرت، قال: وقد يرى في المنام ما يصح، ذلك إنما بملك بريه أو بما ذلك في الأصحاء أو بعض ذلك. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: والأصل في هذا ما في الأول: إن النائم ذو آفة تعرفها بما يعلم به يقظته، وذلك حق اليقظة، إنه يرى في النوم مضطرا، وفي اليقظة لا، وكذلك يبقى ألم ما يضرب في حال اليقظة، ويعرف لذة ما به يغتذى، يوجب وليس بيننا وبين هؤلاء في هذه الأحوال مسألة، إنما بيننا إلزام حق اليقظة وتحقيقه بضرورة بما ذكرنا، ثم تغير ذلك؛ إنما ذلك للآفات التي تعترض. وجملته أن الطبيعة أو النجوم أو الأغذية لا يحتمل أن تولد ذلك، ولا فيها ما ذلك، وأن لكل شيء من ذلك مضرة ومنفعة، وما به الغلبة والاعتدال، فلا يحتمل وجود مثله بالطبع ولا بالنجم من حيث خروج ذلك على ما فيه من الحكمة والاتقان، وما يوجبه الطبع لا يحتمل ذلك، وقد مر بيان ذلك، والله الموفق.
He floats in creation, and by this nature is disposed: some gems fly in the sky, another in water, and the third walks on the face of the earth. To burden oneself with a justification for such a thing is an arbitrary ruling upon the Lord of the worlds, and a justification by what He has not permitted, and for which he has no perception, and that is not of the kind that the Law has guaranteed for the verification of entities, and there is no power except with God. Then he objected to himself with what the sleeper sees, so he goes out upon what he sees. Perhaps the matter of the wakeful one is like this, or he does not know how to know this from that. So he claimed that what distinguishes between the two matters is that it is in the mind during sleep, such as seeing oneself dead, and the dead does not know, or seeing one's head placed in his lap, and the like is not possible for the wakeful to see. If it is said: How does the sleeper imagine the impossible, which does not establish in the imagination? It is said: When he sees himself in the dream, he does not believe it to be alive or dead, and that is it; likewise when he sees his head placed, he does not imagine it in two places. And he claimed that knowledge of the correctness of what is in wakefulness and the corruption of what is in sleep is acquired, its proof is what I mentioned. He said: And one may see in the dream what is correct; that is only by the kingdom of his Lord or by what is in the healthy or some of that. The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: The basis in this is what is in the first: that the sleeper has a defect that he knows by what he knows of his wakefulness, and that is the truth of wakefulness; he sees in sleep involuntarily, and in wakefulness not; likewise the pain of what strikes him remains in the state of wakefulness, and he knows the pleasure of what he is nourished by. So it necessitates, and there is no dispute between us and these people in these states; what is between us is only the imposition of the truth of wakefulness and its verification by necessity through what we mentioned. Then its change is only due to the defects that intervene. Its summary is that nature or the stars or foods cannot generate that, nor is there in them that which does so, and that everything has harm and benefit, and what determines domineering and moderation, so the existence of the like of it is not possible by nature nor by the star, because it issues in accordance with the wisdom and perfection in it, and what nature necessitates cannot have that. The clarification of that has passed, and God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 755

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who argue that the perceptions of wakefulness and dreams are equivalent, thus undermining certain knowledge. It begins by noting that some people try to explain natural phenomena (like birds flying, water flowing) as mere natural contingencies, which is an arbitrary imposition on God's creation. Then Maturidi presents a self-objection: what about dreams? If dream perceptions can be false, how can we trust wakeful ones? The objector claims that the difference is that during sleep the mind imagines impossible things (like seeing oneself dead), which a wakeful person would not accept. Maturidi responds by analyzing the dream state: the dreamer does not actually believe the impossible; for example, seeing oneself dead does not entail believing oneself simultaneously alive and dead. Furthermore, he notes that knowledge of the truth of wakefulness is acquired (iktisāb), but its basis is the necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) that distinguishes it from dreams. The jurist (al-faqīh) adds that the sleeper has a defect (āfa) that prevents true perception, while the wakeful person perceives with necessity. Thus the distinction is not arbitrary but grounded in the nature of cognition. Finally, Maturidi concludes that natural causes like nature, stars, or food cannot produce such knowledge, because they lack the requisite wisdom and perfection; only God can. The passage reinforces the Sunni doctrine of the reliability of wakeful sense perception and rational necessity against skeptics.

Key Terms
اليقظة
النوم
المحال
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi dismisses the objection by simply asserting that dreams are false and wakefulness is true without argument.
Correction: Maturidi actually engages with the objection dialectically; he presents the objector's argument and then responds step by step.

Checkpoint
According to the objector in this passage, what distinguishes wakeful from dream perception?
A) The level of sensory detail
B) The presence of impossible scenarios in dreams
C) The duration of the experience
D) The influence of the stars

Key Takeaway
The skeptical attempt to equate wakefulness and dreams fails because dream contents include impossibilities that wakeful experience does not, but this alone is not a sufficient epistemological defense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever had a dream so vivid that upon waking you doubted whether you were still dreaming? What did you rely on to decide you were awake? Did that reliance feel certain? This passage challenges you to examine the foundation of that certainty. Take a moment to recall a time when you were unsure if an experience was real. How did you ultimately know? Can you be sure that your current state is not a dream? Write down the single reason you would give. Now ask yourself: is that reason itself not subject to doubt?
?
The objector in this passage suggests that wakeful perception might be as unreliable as dream perception. If that were true, what would change about how you live your life right now? Would you still pray, fast, or trust the promise of the afterlife? This is not an academic exercise. Your answer reveals what you actually base your faith on. Without reading further, what is the deepest reason you know that you are not dreaming right now? Is that reason provided in this lesson? If not, you are left with the very problem Maturidi is wrestling with. Do not rest until you have a clear answer that satisfies you.
?
Maturidi mentions impossibilities like seeing your own head in your lap. In your daily life, you do not see such things. But have you ever felt a strong conviction about something that later turned out to be completely wrong — as impossible as those dreams? How do you distinguish between a mere mistake and a true impossibility? This lesson forces you to confront the reliability of your own mind. Can you prove that your mind is not systematically deceiving you? If you cannot, then what holds your faith together? Do not brush this aside. Sit with the discomfort.

The Epistemological Distinction: Wakeful Necessity vs. Dream Defect
Maturidi provides the full refutation: dreamers do not believe impossibilities, wakeful knowledge is necessary, and natural causes cannot produce it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's deeper argument that wakeful knowledge is necessary and distinct from dream perception due to the sleeper's defect (āfa) and the impossibility of natural causes producing true knowledge.

Core Idea
Wakeful knowledge is established by necessity, while dreams are impaired by a defect that prevents necessary perception.

Maturidi responds to the objector's claim by analyzing the psychological state of the dreamer. He argues that the dreamer does not actually believe the impossible; rather, the dream content is not properly judged. Then he introduces the concept of acquired knowledge versus necessary knowledge, and the jurist adds that the sleeper has a defect that prevents him from having the same certainty as the wakeful person. Finally, Maturidi rejects naturalistic explanations for the difference, affirming that only God can be the source of such necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's response to the imagination objection
Follow how he refutes the idea that the dreamer genuinely believes impossibilities. He argues that the dreamer does not actually hold contradictory beliefs.
This is a key point: the dreamer does not affirm the impossible as true.
2
Distinguish acquired from necessary knowledge
Maturidi acknowledges that the knowledge distinguishing wakefulness from dreams is acquired, but its basis is necessary (ḍarūra). Understand this distinction.
He also allows for true dreams by divine permission or in healthy states.
3
Understand the sleeper's defect (āfa)
The jurist adds that the sleeper has a defect that forces involuntary perception without full certainty, unlike wakeful perception which is accompanied by necessity.
This defect is the root cause of the epistemological difference.
4
Appreciate the rejection of natural causes
Maturidi concludes by arguing that nature, stars, or food cannot generate such necessary knowledge; only God can, due to His wisdom and perfection.
This reinforces the role of divine causation in epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسبح في خلق، وبهذا الطبع جبل: بعض الجواهر يطير في السماء، وآخر الماء، والثالث يمشى على وجه الأرض، فتكلف الاعتلال لمثل هذا تحكم على رب العالمين، واعتلال بما لم يؤذن له، ولا له به درك، وليس ذلك من نوع ما ضمن الشرع فيه من تحقيق الأعيان، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم عارض نفسه بما يرى النائم، فيخرج على ما يرى، فلعل أمر اليقظان على هذا، أو ما يعلم يعلم ذا من ذا فزعم أن الذي يفرق بين الأمرين أنه في العقل في حال النوم، نحو أن يرى نفسه ميتا، والميت لا يعلم أو يرى رأسه ملقى في حجره، ومثله لا يحتمل رؤية اليقظان. فإن قيل: كيف يتوهم النائم المحال، لا يثبت في الوهم؟ قيل: عند ما يرى نفسه في المنام لا يعتقدها حية ميتة، وذلك هو وكذلك إذا رأى رأسه ملقى لا يتوهمه في مكانين. وزعم أن العلم بصحة ما في اليقظة وفساد ما في النوم اكتساب، دليله ما ذكرت، قال: وقد يرى في المنام ما يصح، ذلك إنما بملك بريه أو بما ذلك في الأصحاء أو بعض ذلك. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: والأصل في هذا ما في الأول: إن النائم ذو آفة تعرفها بما يعلم به يقظته، وذلك حق اليقظة، إنه يرى في النوم مضطرا، وفي اليقظة لا، وكذلك يبقى ألم ما يضرب في حال اليقظة، ويعرف لذة ما به يغتذى، يوجب وليس بيننا وبين هؤلاء في هذه الأحوال مسألة، إنما بيننا إلزام حق اليقظة وتحقيقه بضرورة بما ذكرنا، ثم تغير ذلك؛ إنما ذلك للآفات التي تعترض. وجملته أن الطبيعة أو النجوم أو الأغذية لا يحتمل أن تولد ذلك، ولا فيها ما ذلك، وأن لكل شيء من ذلك مضرة ومنفعة، وما به الغلبة والاعتدال، فلا يحتمل وجود مثله بالطبع ولا بالنجم من حيث خروج ذلك على ما فيه من الحكمة والاتقان، وما يوجبه الطبع لا يحتمل ذلك، وقد مر بيان ذلك، والله الموفق.
He floats in creation, and by this nature is disposed: some gems fly in the sky, another in water, and the third walks on the face of the earth. To burden oneself with a justification for such a thing is an arbitrary ruling upon the Lord of the worlds, and a justification by what He has not permitted, and for which he has no perception, and that is not of the kind that the Law has guaranteed for the verification of entities, and there is no power except with God. Then he objected to himself with what the sleeper sees, so he goes out upon what he sees. Perhaps the matter of the wakeful one is like this, or he does not know how to know this from that. So he claimed that what distinguishes between the two matters is that it is in the mind during sleep, such as seeing oneself dead, and the dead does not know, or seeing one's head placed in his lap, and the like is not possible for the wakeful to see. If it is said: How does the sleeper imagine the impossible, which does not establish in the imagination? It is said: When he sees himself in the dream, he does not believe it to be alive or dead, and that is it; likewise when he sees his head placed, he does not imagine it in two places. And he claimed that knowledge of the correctness of what is in wakefulness and the corruption of what is in sleep is acquired, its proof is what I mentioned. He said: And one may see in the dream what is correct; that is only by the kingdom of his Lord or by what is in the healthy or some of that. The jurist, may God have mercy on him, said: The basis in this is what is in the first: that the sleeper has a defect that he knows by what he knows of his wakefulness, and that is the truth of wakefulness; he sees in sleep involuntarily, and in wakefulness not; likewise the pain of what strikes him remains in the state of wakefulness, and he knows the pleasure of what he is nourished by. So it necessitates, and there is no dispute between us and these people in these states; what is between us is only the imposition of the truth of wakefulness and its verification by necessity through what we mentioned. Then its change is only due to the defects that intervene. Its summary is that nature or the stars or foods cannot generate that, nor is there in them that which does so, and that everything has harm and benefit, and what determines domineering and moderation, so the existence of the like of it is not possible by nature nor by the star, because it issues in accordance with the wisdom and perfection in it, and what nature necessitates cannot have that. The clarification of that has passed, and God is the giver of success.
Source pages p. 755

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who argue that the perceptions of wakefulness and dreams are equivalent, thus undermining certain knowledge. It begins by noting that some people try to explain natural phenomena (like birds flying, water flowing) as mere natural contingencies, which is an arbitrary imposition on God's creation. Then Maturidi presents a self-objection: what about dreams? If dream perceptions can be false, how can we trust wakeful ones? The objector claims that the difference is that during sleep the mind imagines impossible things (like seeing oneself dead), which a wakeful person would not accept. Maturidi responds by analyzing the dream state: the dreamer does not actually believe the impossible; for example, seeing oneself dead does not entail believing oneself simultaneously alive and dead. Furthermore, he notes that knowledge of the truth of wakefulness is acquired (iktisāb), but its basis is the necessary knowledge (ḍarūra) that distinguishes it from dreams. The jurist (al-faqīh) adds that the sleeper has a defect (āfa) that prevents true perception, while the wakeful person perceives with necessity. Thus the distinction is not arbitrary but grounded in the nature of cognition. Finally, Maturidi concludes that natural causes like nature, stars, or food cannot produce such knowledge, because they lack the requisite wisdom and perfection; only God can. The passage reinforces the Sunni doctrine of the reliability of wakeful sense perception and rational necessity against skeptics.

Key Terms
الاكتساب
الضرورة
الآفة
الطبع
النجم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Maturidi denies that dreams can ever be true.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that true dreams are possible, but they come from God's kingdom or from a healthy state, not from the standard dream defect.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, what distinguishes the truth of wakefulness from the falsity of dreams?
A) Wakefulness is acquired knowledge and dreams are innate.
B) The sleeper has a defect that prevents necessary knowledge, while the wakeful person perceives by necessity.
C) Dreams are always false and wakefulness is always true.
D) The difference is only a matter of social convention.

Key Takeaway
Wakeful knowledge is grounded in necessity and divine causation, while dreams are marred by a defect that prevents necessary certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in a state of confusion or doubt, how do you distinguish between a fleeting whim and a necessary truth? Do you rely on the same criteria Maturidi describes?
?
Maturidi says that natural causes cannot produce the necessary knowledge you have of wakefulness. If you believe your knowledge is from God, does that change how you act on it today?
?
Do you ever treat your own desires or assumptions as if they were necessary truths? When have you mistaken a 'dream' for reality in your spiritual life?

The Mixture of Benefit and Harm Points to a Single Creator
Maturidi introduces the purpose of harm and interlinking of substances to prove a single manager

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the observed mixture of benefit and harm in substances to argue for a single, wise Creator.

Core Idea
The interlinking of benefit and harm in creation disproves multiple gods.

Maturidi begins his proof by establishing that harm must have a purpose, otherwise creation would be meaningless. He then observes that every substance is created with the potential for both benefit and harm, and these are interconnected across different substances. This interconnection, he argues, points to a single manager.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the initial premise
What reason does Maturidi give for the existence of harm? Underline the phrase that explains its purpose.
Harm is not random; it has a purpose in creation.
3
Notice the claim about substances
How does Maturidi describe each substance? What capacity does it have?
Every substance is created capable of both benefit and harm.
4
See how coherence refutes multiple creators
Maturidi argues that if there were multiple creators, their creations would conflict. Find the sentence that explains why the substance of good and evil would not mix. How does the actual consistency of creation disprove this?
The coherence and connection of benefits across different substances shows they share a single director.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الضرر الذي لولا ذلك لم يكن لخلقهم معنى ، فخلقهم الله على ما خلق من الاختلاف لهذين [السببين] . ثم بلطفه خلق كل جوهر محتملا للنفع والضرر ، يحل به لغيره ، وأوصل منفعة كل جوهر بغيره من الجواهر التي فيها المضار ؛ ليعلم الناظرون أن مدير ذلك كله واحد ، وأنه لو كان من مختلف لتدافع الخلق ؛ لأن جوهر الخير إذ لا يجى منه غير الخير ، ومن جوهر الشر غير الشر ، لكان صنع وإفساده ما يقوم مع مثله عالى ، فدل كل واحد منهما في بعض صنع الآخر الاتساق وتعلق منافع بعض ببعض على فساد هذا . على أنا إذا لم نقل بأن الكل لواحد لم يحتمل القول منا لعدد ؛ إذ لم يقدر واحد منهم على افراد الذي منه يدل عليه ، ولا أعلم عليه علما يدل عليه ، لم يجب بمثله حق / المعرفة به والعلم ما مع ا بحاله ، فيفسد العلم جميعا ؛ لجهل الأصل الذى كل أنواع العلم وفروعه به . ينفع أحد الجوهرين يضر الآخر ، وفى ذلك يلاقى الضار النافع ، فيبطل به نفع البتة ؛ لما معه المانع عنه . وفى وجود العالم وما فيه لكل منهم نفع هو الدليل الحق على أن مدير ذلك كله واحد ، لحبس كل ضار عن عمله من وجه ضرره باللطف لتقبل ما أراد من النفع ليصل إلى من أراد نفعه . وهكذا هذه القصة فيمن أراد ضرره ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
From the harm, without which there would be no meaning to their creation. So Allah created them according to the difference for these two reasons. Then by His gentleness He created every substance capable of benefit and harm, occurring to others, and He connected the benefit of each substance to other substances that contain harms, so that the observers know that the manager of all that is one, and that if it were from different [gods], the creation would conflict; because the substance of good does not produce anything but good, and the substance of evil produces nothing but evil, and its making and corruption would be what stands with its like high, so each of them in some making of the other indicates consistency and the connection of benefits of some to others upon the corruption of this. Moreover, if we do not say that all is for one, then the statement from us of number would not be possible; since none of them is able to single out that from which he indicates, nor do I know upon it a knowledge that indicates, it would not be necessary by the like of it the right of knowledge of it and the knowledge with its state, so all knowledge would be corrupted due to ignorance of the origin by which all types of knowledge and their branches exist. One of the two substances benefits and the other harms, and in that the harmful meets the beneficial, so benefit is utterly nullified by what is with it of the preventing factor. And in the existence of the world and what is in it for each of them is benefit — that is the true evidence that the manager of all that is one, because He restrains every harmful [thing] from its action from the aspect of its harm by gentleness to accept what is intended of benefit to reach whom He intends to benefit. And such is this story regarding whom He intends to harm. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 766

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a single, wise Creator based on the observed mixture of benefit and harm in the natural world. The author, Imam al-Maturidi, begins by stating that harm (الضرر) must have a purpose in creation, otherwise creation would be meaningless. Allah created different substances with opposing natures: some are beneficial, some harmful. Each substance is created capable of both benefit and harm, and Allah connects the benefit of one substance to the harm of another. This interdependence demonstrates that there is a single manager (مدير) of all things, because if there were multiple gods, their creations would conflict. For example, a substance of pure good cannot produce evil, and a substance of pure evil cannot produce good; but in reality we see both benefit and harm interwoven. This coherence (الاتساق) and interlinking of benefits refutes the notion of multiple creators. Maturidi then argues that if we do not attribute all to one Creator, we cannot validly speak of a number of gods, because none of them could be identified or known, leading to the corruption of all knowledge. He further explains that if one substance benefits and another harms, they would cancel each other out without a single director. The existence of the world and its order—where each thing has its benefit and harm is restrained—proves that the manager is one. This argument is part of Maturidi's rational theology (kalam) to establish divine unity (tawhid).

Key Terms
ضرر
نفع
جوهر
مدير
اتساق
اختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That good and evil come from two separate creators (dualism).
Correction: Maturidi argues that the interlinking of benefit and harm in creation disproves dualism because a pure good substance cannot produce harm and a pure evil substance cannot produce good, yet we see them connected. This proves a single wise creator who manages both.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the fact that benefit and harm are interconnected in different substances indicate?
A) That there are two separate creators, one for good and one for evil
B) That there is a single manager of all things
C) That substances are eternal and need no creator
D) That benefit and harm are random

Key Takeaway
The interconnection of benefit and harm across substances proves that a single, wise Creator manages all things, because multiple creators would lead to conflict and inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience harm or difficulty, do you truly believe it is woven into a coherent plan by a single, wise Manager? How would your response change if you were certain of this?
?
The passage says Allah created substances capable of both benefit and harm and interconnected them. Do you see your own life as a place where benefit and harm are deliberately interlinked by God? What harm have you experienced that later revealed hidden benefit?
?
If you were to stand before Allah and He asked you, 'Did the mixture of good and evil in your life lead you to recognize My oneness or to doubt it?' what would you say?

The Necessity of One Creator for Valid Knowledge and Order
Maturidi argues from epistemological necessity and observed order to a single Creator

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that without acknowledging a single Creator, all knowledge would be corrupted, and that the orderly restraint of harm confirms His oneness.

Core Idea
Without one Creator, we cannot even know who to worship, and all knowledge collapses.

Maturidi continues his proof by highlighting the epistemological consequences of denying a single Creator. If there were multiple gods, we could not identify any of them, leading to complete ignorance. Moreover, the observed restraint of harm in the world demonstrates a single wise manager who controls benefit and harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Focus on the logical steps Maturidi takes.
2
Understand the epistemological argument
Maturidi argues that if we do not attribute all to one Creator, we cannot validly speak of a number of gods. Why? Because we would have no way to identify any individual god. Find the sentence that explains this.
Without one origin, knowledge of the divine becomes impossible, leading to the corruption of all knowledge.
3
See the problem of conflicting substances
What happens when one substance benefits and another harms? How does this show the need for a single director?
Without a single director, benefit would be nullified by harm.
4
Recognize the final evidence from order
Maturidi points to the existence of the world and its order as true evidence. How does the restraint of harm serve as proof?
The fact that harmful things are restrained from their full effect by gentleness (اللطف) shows a wise manager who allows benefit to reach whom He wills.
