Intellect and the Necessity of Commands
Maturidi argues that rational moral knowledge necessitates commands and prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi grounds the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions in the intellect's natural recognition of good and evil.

Core Idea
The intellect's own moral judgment makes commands and prohibitions rationally necessary.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid presents the rational foundation for divine commands. Maturidi argues that the intellect itself recognizes objective moral values—truth and justice as good, falsehood and injustice as evil. From this natural recognition, he deduces that commands to do good and prohibitions against evil are not arbitrary but rationally necessary, and that reward and punishment logically follow. Let's read his argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read Maturidi's argument carefully. Notice how he starts by stating what the intellect deems good and evil.
Pay attention to the sequence of ideas.
2
Identify the premises
Identify the two key premises: (1) intellect deems truth/justice good, falsehood/injustice evil; (2) hearts revere the good and despise the evil.
3
Trace the conclusion
See how Maturidi moves from these premises to the conclusion that commands and prohibitions are necessary by rational necessity.
The phrase 'bi-ḍarūrat al-ʿaql' is crucial.
4
Consider the implications
Reflect on how reward and punishment are then presented as necessary to honor those who follow reason and punish those who follow desire.

و بعد، فإنه قد حسن في العقول الصدق والعدل وقبح فيها الجور والكذب ، فجعل الفريق الأول عظيما في القلوب كريما، والثانى حقيرا مهينا، فيصير العقول آمرة بكسب ما يعلى شرف من رزق منها ، وناهية عما فيه هو أن صاحبها ، فيجب الأمر والنهي بضرورة العقل ، ثم الثواب ليتم الكرامة لمن اختار سبلها والقيام بوفائها ، والعقاب لمن آثر هواه على إشارة العقل .
And after that, truth and justice are deemed good in the intellects, and injustice and falsehood are deemed ugly. So the first group (truth and justice) is made great and noble in hearts, and the second (injustice and falsehood) is made low and contemptible. Thus the intellects become commanding to acquire what elevates the honor of the one endowed with them, and forbidding what [corrupts?] its possessor. So commands and prohibitions become obligatory by necessity of the intellect. Then reward completes the honor for those who choose its paths and fulfill its duties, and punishment for those who prefer their desires over the intellect's indication.
Source pages p. 777

Commentary
This final passage presents an argument from rational moral knowledge. Maturidi states that intellects naturally deem truth and justice to be good, and injustice and falsehood to be evil. Accordingly, people's hearts revere the former and despise the latter. Intellects then essentially command the pursuit of what elevates one's honor and forbid what degrades one. From this, Maturidi concludes that commands and prohibitions are rationally necessary. Furthermore, reward and punishment are also necessary: reward to honor those who follow the intellect's guidance, and punishment for those who follow their desires instead of reason.

The Necessity of Divine Commands from Creation's Nature
Maturidi argues that creation of harm and benefit implies commands and prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Maturidi, God's creation of beings capable of harm and benefit necessarily implies the existence of divine commands and prohibitions.

Core Idea
God's wisdom in creation demands commands to guide beings toward benefit and away from harm.

Maturidi, the great Hanafi theologian, offers a rational proof that divine commands are not arbitrary but flow from the very nature of creation. By examining how humans respond to pain and pleasure, he shows that a wise Creator must have intended outcomes that require guidance. This passage lays the foundation for understanding why revelation is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style and the logical connections Maturidi draws.
2
Identify the key premise
Maturidi begins by noting that creation is divided into harmful and beneficial, and each being is susceptible to pain and pleasure. This is the foundational observation.
This premise is not arbitrary; it is based on observable reality.
3
Trace the logical implication
From this premise, Maturidi argues that such a creation could only be for specific outcomes (reward and punishment), which in turn complete fear and hope. Commands and prohibitions then become necessary to guide beings toward those outcomes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that wisdom itself entails command and prohibition, especially given that God created humans in the best stature, implying a need for guidance.
This connects to the broader theme of divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بينا من اختلاف الممتحنين في الغنى والحكمة لم يجز تقدير أحدهما بالآخر، ولا يحتمل حكيم [يفعل الشر في الحكمة الربوبية؟]، فتكلفه الذي ذكر خطأ وبعد، فإنه إذ جعل الخلق قسمين: ضارا ونافعا، وجعل كل واحد [؟] محتملا للألم واللذة، لم يحتمل أن يجعلهم كذلك إلا لعواقب يحذرهم بها ويرغبهم فيها من الوعيد بالشدائد والوعد بالملاذ، وبذلك تتم الرغبة والرهبة، والله الموفق. وبعد، فإذ خلق الخلق، وجعل البعض منافعا لبعض، وإن لم يكن له في ذلك نفع لغناه. وكذلك المضار، فمثله يأمر وينهى بمنافع بعض ببعض واتقاء المضار، مع ما يأمر بما ينفعهم. كما خلقهم وجعل لهم عما ذلك، وينهى يضرهم، وأيضا إن في الحكمة الأمر والنهى: إن الله خلق البشر في أحسن تقويم، والله الموفق.
We have shown from the disagreement of the tested ones concerning richness and wisdom that it is not permissible to measure one by the other, and a wise person does not commit evil in divine wisdom? So his exertion which he mentioned is error and far. For when He made creation two divisions: harmful and beneficial, and made each one [؟] susceptible to pain and pleasure, it is not plausible that He made them so except for outcomes by which He warns them and desires them—from warning with hardships and promising with delights—and thereby fear and hope are completed, and God is the Grantor of success. And after, when He created creation, and made some benefits for others, even though He has no benefit in that due to His self-sufficiency. Likewise the harms, so such [a being] commands and prohibits with benefits of some for others and avoidance of harms, along with what He commands that benefits them. Just as He created them and made for them that? and prohibits what harms them. Also, indeed in wisdom there is command and prohibition: indeed God created humans in the best stature, and God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 777

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for the necessity of divine commands and prohibitions based on God's creation of beings susceptible to pain and pleasure. Maturidi begins by noting that from the diversity of people's experiences with wealth and wisdom, one cannot measure one by the other. He then argues that since God created creatures as harmful and beneficial and capable of feeling pain and pleasure, He must have intended specific outcomes—warning with hardships and promising with delights—to complete the motives of fear and hope. This implies that God issues commands and prohibitions to guide humans toward benefit and away from harm. Maturidi further notes that God created some beings as benefits for others without needing them Himself, demonstrating His self-sufficiency. He concludes that wisdom itself entails command and prohibition, as God created humans in the best stature, necessitating guidance through commands.

Why Reason Needs Prophets
Maturidi explains that reason's moral judgments necessitate commands, rewards, and messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational moral judgments lead to the necessity of divine commands and prophetic guidance.

Core Idea
The intellect's moral judgments are incomplete without prophets.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing that the human intellect naturally deems truth and justice good, and injustice and falsehood bad. But he argues that this innate moral sense alone is not sufficient for complete guidance—it requires divine commands and messengers to specify what is truly beneficial and harmful. In this passage, he traces the logical chain from rational moral judgment to the necessity of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from moral judgment to the need for messengers.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the rational moral judgments
What does the intellect deem good and bad? How does that affect the heart's estimation of actions? Notice that Maturidi says the intellect becomes commanding and prohibiting by its own nature.
This is the foundation of his argument.
3
Trace the necessity of reward, punishment, and prophets
Why does Maturidi say that reward and punishment follow from the intellect's judgments? And how does that lead to the necessity of messengers? Look for the phrase 'فيجب الأمر والنهي بضرورة العقل' and the conclusion about messengers.
This step connects the moral framework to prophethood.

And in what we have mentioned is the necessity of affirming messengers to guide them to the landmarks of justice and truth and the harms of their opposites, indicating everything whose quiddity is obscure, so that the state of affairs be consistent with praise, and God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 777

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from rational moral judgment for the necessity of divine commands and prophetic guidance. Maturidi states that the intellect inherently deems truth and justice good, and injustice and falsehood bad. As a result, the intellect naturally honors the former and demeans the latter, leading it to command the acquisition of what elevates honor and prohibit what diminishes it. This makes command and prohibition necessary by the very nature of reason. He then argues that reward and punishment are required to complete the system of honor—reward for those who follow the intellect's guidance, punishment for those who follow desire. From this rational framework, Maturidi concludes that messengers are necessary to provide detailed direction on the landmarks of justice and truth, and to clarify matters whose true nature is obscure, so that human affairs align with praise and divine approval.

The Three Rational Categories: Necessary, Impossible, and Possible
Maturidi enumerates the three rational categories and clarifies the role of messengers regarding the possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational categories that govern all judgments about the world, and why the role of messengers is crucial in the realm of the possible.

Core Idea
The intellect knows necessity and impossibility, but only revelation guides what is best in the possible.

Maturidi begins by stating that everything in the world falls under one of three rational categories: necessary, impossible, and possible. He explains how the intellect relates to each, and then points to the vital function of messengers in the domain of the possible, where the intellect alone cannot determine the best course.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three principles: impossible, necessary, and possible. Find each one and notice how Maturidi defines them.
The possible is described as an intermediate (wāsiṭ).
3
Understand the intellect's role
Note that the intellect determines necessity and impossibility definitively, but does not dictate a specific direction in the possible.
This is why revelation is needed.
4
Reflect on the role of messengers
Ask yourself why messengers are needed for the possible. The commentary will help you answer.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصول ثلاثة: ممتنع وواجب وواسط وهو الممكن، وعلى ذلك جميع أمر العالم. فالواجب في العقل على جهة لا يجوز مجيء الخبر بغيره، وكذلك الممتنع، ويجيء في الممكن؛ إذ هو المنقلب من حال إلى حال، ويد إلى يد، وملك إلى ملك، وفي ذلك ليس في العقل إيجاب جهة ولا امتناع من جهة فتجىء الرسل ببيان الأولى من ذلك في كل حال، والله الموفق.
Then the principles are three: impossible, necessary, and an intermediate which is the possible. Upon that is the entire matter of the world. So the necessary is in the intellect in a way that report cannot come with other than it, and likewise the impossible. And it (report) comes in the possible, for it is that which changes from state to state, hand to hand, and possession to possession. And in that there is not in the intellect an affirmation of a specific direction nor an impossibility of a direction. So the messengers come to clarify the preferable (al-awla) of that in every state. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 782

Commentary
This passage classifies the three fundamental categories that govern all rational judgments about the world. Maturidi states that the principles (al-usul) are three: (1) the impossible (mumtaniʿ), (2) the necessary (wājib), and (3) the intermediate (wāsiṭ) which is the possible (mumkin). Everything in the world falls under these categories. The necessary is that which the intellect affirms as unavoidable, such that a report (khabar) cannot come with its opposite; the impossible is that which the intellect rejects as untenable, also not subject to alternative report. The possible (al-mumkin) is that which is subject to change from one state to another, from hand to hand, from possession to possession. In the realm of the possible, the intellect alone does not necessitate a particular direction nor does it deem any direction impossible. Therefore, messengers come to clarify what is preferable (al-awla) in each situation. This establishes the role of revelation: while the intellect can grasp necessity and impossibility, it cannot decide among possibilities without divine guidance, and messengers provide that guidance by showing the best course. The passage ends with 'God is the granter of success' (wa-Allah al-muwaffiq).

Key Terms
ممتنع
واجب
ممكن
الأصول الثلاثة
العقل
الخبر
الرسل
الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the intellect can determine all moral or practical choices, but Maturidi shows it only knows necessity and impossibility; the possible requires revelation to choose the best.
Correction: The intellect cannot decide among multiple possibilities; messengers provide guidance on what is preferable.

Checkpoint
What do messengers provide in the realm of the possible (al-mumkin) according to Maturidi?
A) A proof that the possible is impossible
B) A clarification of what is preferable (al-awla)
C) A determination of necessity
D) A rejection of all possibilities

Key Takeaway
The three rational categories—necessary, impossible, and possible—are the foundation of all rational thought, and messengers are necessary to guide us in the realm of the possible where intellect alone is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If you truly believe that only revelation can guide you in the possible, what step will you take today to actively seek that guidance?

The Three Rational Categories and the Role of Messengers
Maturidi classifies existence into three rational categories and explains how messengers clarify the best course in possible matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of existence into three rational categories—necessary, impossible, and possible—and the essential role of messengers in providing guidance in contingent matters where reason is indeterminate.

Core Idea
Reason determines necessity and impossibility, but only revelation can show the best path in the possible realm.

Imam Maturidi begins by laying down the intellectual foundation upon which his entire theological system rests. He identifies three exhaustive categories that cover everything in existence. From these, he deduces the indispensability of prophetic guidance. This passage is the key to understanding Maturidi's rationalist yet revelation-dependent approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the three terms: ممتنع, واجب, ممكن.
2
Identify the three categories
Note the three rational categories: impossible (mumtani'), necessary (wajib), and possible (mumkin). Understand that these cover the entire order of the world.
3
Understand the rule for reports
For necessary and impossible, reason dictates that no true report can contradict them. For possible matters, reports can come since the possible is changeable.
The word 'khabar' (report) refers to prophetic messages.
4
Recognize the limitation of reason
In possible matters, reason does not affirm or negate any specific course. This shows that reason alone is insufficient for determining the best action.
5
See the role of messengers
Since reason is silent on the best in possible matters, messengers are sent to clarify what is best (al-awla) in every situation.
This establishes the necessity of prophethood.

In that, there is in the intellect neither affirmation of a specific course nor negation of a specific course, so the messengers come to clarify the best course in every state. And Allah is the Grantor of success.

Commentary
This passage defines the three fundamental rational categories—impossible (mumtani'), necessary (wajib), and possible (mumkin)—and explains their relationship to prophetic reports. Maturidi states that these three categories underpin the entire order of the world. Reason determines that reports cannot contradict the necessary or impossible categories. However, regarding possible matters, reason alone cannot determine which course is superior, because the possible is changeable. Therefore, messengers (rusul) are sent to clarify what is best (al-awla) in each situation. This establishes a key role for prophethood: providing guidance in contingent affairs where reason is indeterminate.

The Two Types of Rational Judgment and the Example of Sacrifices
Maturidi classifies rational good and evil into two types and explains the example of sacrifices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between unchanging rational judgments and those that change based on context, and how this applies to the example of animal sacrifice.

Core Idea
Some moral judgments are fixed by reason; others depend on circumstances and divine command.

Imam Maturidi responds to the objection that a wise God cannot command what reason deems evil. He first classifies rational judgments into two types: those that are absolute and those that are relative to context. This lesson explores that classification and its application to the ritual of animal sacrifice, showing how revelation can address changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the opening lines where the deniers' claim is stated and the initial response is given.
Notice the objection: God cannot command what is rationally evil.
2
Identify the two types of rational judgments
Find the classification: one type is unchanging (e.g., thanking a benefactor), the other changes based on consequences or context.
The second type includes examples like retribution against a wrongdoer.
3
Examine the example of sacrifices
Understand how the second type applies to animal sacrifices: slaughter is permissible because it is kinder to the animal.
The argument shows that revelation can permit something that seems harmful.
4
Connect to the broader principle of justice
Note that justice is always good, but some acts become good or evil by divine command, like the slaughter of animals. Messengers only bring justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم من أنكر الرسل بما لا يأمر الحكيم بما يقبح في العقل ؛ إذ لو جاز مجيء الخبر بمثله الجاز ذلك فى إباحة الجور والكذب . فأجيب بأن ما حسنه العقل وقبحه ، النوعان أحدهما لا يتغير ، نحو شكر المنعم وقبح [السفيه] والثانى هو الذي يُحسنه العقل للعاقبة أو للمقدمة أو الحال نحو ما يحسن في العقل .... المنهمك في الفساد الباغي على وجه الانتقام ، فجائز ورود الشرع بمثله . وعلى ذلك أمر الذبائح ولو قدر الإباحة فيه كان كل حي يموت ، والذبح أروح إليه . وأيسر عليه فيكون بمعنى الأشياء المباحة . والثاني أن العدل في الجملة حسن والجور فى الجملة قبح ، لكن من الأشياء مما يظهر قبحه بالنهى وحسنه بالأمر ، وذلك نحو تقلب أحوال المرء وانتقاله ، وعلى ذلك ذبح الحيوان ، إذ جاءت به الرسل ، لا يأتون إلا بالعدل - و على الثنوية ما أجاز النور ضرر الظلمة لما رأى من المصلحة ، وفى مثله ذكر الوراق أن الرسل لو جاءوا إلى التمسك بحجج العقول فهم منا ، وإن جاءوا إلى خلافها فقد / جعلها الله حججا ، لم يجز الغير إلا بالتغيير ، وفى ذلك زوال الخطاب . عارضه [ابن] الروندي بما يرى أسود الرأس ثم يراه أبيض ، أتغير بصره أو تغير الشيء على البصر ؛ إذ ليس هو بأسود لما يراه البصر ، فمثله أمر ما يراه العقل عدلا للامر ، وكذلك هذا فى القيام والقعود وكل الأحوال ، ومثله الحجامة والأكل والشرب ، قد تحسن هذه الأحوال على اختلافها ، ولم يجب به تغير العقل حتى يحسن فيه الذي كان يحسن بخلافه ، فمثله أمر الرسل . ثم قد يجوز تحمل المؤن العظام لعواقب محمودة واختيار المضار لسلامة محمودة نحو التجارات والاجارات والزراعات والأدوية وأنواع الجراحات ، وكذلك اختيار ترك النفع لنفع أرجح منه ، وعلى ذلك أمر الشرائع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And he claimed who denied the messengers that the Wise does not command what is rationally evil; for if a report like that were possible, then it would be possible to permit injustice and lying. So it was answered: Concerning what reason deems good and evil—two types: one does not change, like thanking the benefactor and the ugliness of folly; the second is that which reason deems good because of the consequence, or the premise, or the circumstance, like what reason deems good for the one engrossed in corruption and aggressive for the purpose of retaliation—so it is possible for revelation to come with something similar. And upon that is the matter of sacrifices; if permissibility were decreed in it, every animal that dies would be permissible, and slaughtering is more comforting to it and easier upon it, so it becomes in the meaning of permissible things. And the second: justice is in general good, and injustice is in general evil, but among things there are those whose evil appears through prohibition and whose goodness appears through command, like the changing states of a person and his transition, and upon that is the slaughter of animals—since the messengers came with it, they only come with justice. And against the dualists: that which the light permitted as harm to darkness because it saw an interest. And in similar context, al-Warraq mentioned that if the messengers came adhering to the proofs of intellect, they would be from us; and if they came contrary to them, then God has made them proofs—it is not permissible to change except by alteration, and that would nullify discourse. Ibn al-Rawandi confronted him with the example of one who sees a black head then sees it white: has his sight changed, or has the thing changed for the sight? For it is not black due to what the sight sees. So similar is the matter of what reason deems just for a matter. And similarly regarding standing, sitting, and all states. And similar is cupping, eating, and drinking; these states may be good despite their differences, and it does not necessitate a change in reason such that it would deem good what it used to deem bad. So similar is the matter of the messengers. Then, it is possible to endure great hardships for praiseworthy outcomes and to choose harms for praiseworthy safety, like trades, leases, agriculture, medicines, and types of surgeries—and likewise, choosing to forgo a benefit for a more preponderate benefit. And upon that is the matter of religious laws. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 799

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of the objection that a wise being (God) cannot command what is rationally evil, which would imply that if prophets were to report such, it would permit injustice and lying. The answer first classifies rational judgments into two types: (1) unchanging judgments, such as the goodness of thanking a benefactor and the ugliness of folly (السفيه), and (2) changeable judgments based on consequences, premises, or context—for example, the permissibility of retribution against a persistent wrongdoer. Revelation can bring commands that fall under the second type. The example of animal sacrifice is given: if all dead animals were permissible, slaughter would be unnecessary; however, slaughter is meant to be easier for the animal and thus falls under permissible things. A second point distinguishes between universal principles (justice always good, injustice always evil) and specific acts whose moral status depends on divine command, like the varying states of a person or the slaughter of animals. This aligns with the messengers' actions, which are always just. Against the dualists (Manichaeans), the argument notes that light permits harm to darkness for an interest. Al-Warraq argues that if prophets follow reason, they are like us; if they contradict it, then reason itself is a proof, and any change would nullify discourse. Ibn al-Rawandi counters with an analogy: if someone sees a head as black then as white, either his sight changed or the object changed. Similarly, what reason deems just for a matter may change due to changing circumstances (like standing, sitting, cupping, eating), without reason itself changing. Finally, the passage notes that people endure great hardships for beneficial outcomes (trade, farming, medicine), and similarly, religious laws may involve choosing a lesser harm for a greater benefit. The conclusion is a tawfiq phrase.

Key Terms
الحكيم
القبيح في العقل
شكر المنعم
السفيه
العدل
الجور
الذبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think reason alone determines all moral values without considering context or divine command.
Correction: Maturidi shows that some judgments are fixed, while others vary based on consequences and divine revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of an unchanging rational good?
A) Thanking the benefactor
B) Slaughtering an animal
C) Retribution against a wrongdoer
D) Permitting lying

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's distinction between fixed and contextual rational judgments explains how revelation can command acts that appear contrary to reason in certain circumstances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma that seems unclear, do you tend to rely solely on your own reasoning, or do you submit to divine commands even if they seem counterintuitive? How does this lesson challenge your instinct to trust your own moral judgment without considering context or revelation?
?
Think of a time when you justified an action because it seemed 'good' to you, but later found out it was prohibited. How does the distinction between fixed and contextual rational judgments in this lesson help you understand your mistake and the role of revelation?
?
The example of animal sacrifice shows that what seems harsh (slaughter) is actually merciful because it is commanded. Is there an act you struggle to accept because it seems harsh or unjust? How can this lesson help you trust that the messenger only comes with justice, even when your reason falters?

The Objection of Ibn al-Rawandi and the Analogy of Hardships
Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's objection by showing that changes in what is rational depend on circumstances, not on reason itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Rawandi's objection to the idea that rational judgments are fixed and how Maturidi uses the analogy of enduring hardships to justify prophetic laws.

Core Idea
Rational judgments can change due to circumstances, just as people endure hardships for greater benefits.

This lesson continues Maturidi's response by addressing Ibn al-Rawandi's clever objection: if what appears rationally good can change, does that mean reason itself changes? Maturidi answers with examples from daily life and the principle of choosing lesser harms for greater benefits, showing how religious laws align with this rational pattern.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist argument and al-Warraq's point
Read the part about dualists and al-Warraq to see the background of the discussion.
Dualists argued that light permits harm to darkness for an interest.
2
Read Ibn al-Rawandi's objection about changing perception
Ibn al-Rawandi uses the analogy of a head appearing black then white to argue that rational judgments can change without reason changing.
This challenges the idea that reason's judgment is fixed.
3
Analyze the analogy of standing, sitting, cupping, eating
These examples show that the same action can be good or bad in different contexts without reason changing.
Reason itself remains consistent; only the application varies.
4
See the hardship analogy applied to religious laws
People endure hardships for beneficial outcomes (trade, farming, medicine). Similarly, religious laws may involve hardship for greater benefit.
This justifies why prophets command seemingly burdensome acts.

Two Types of Rational Good and Bad
Maturidi defines two types of rational good and bad and defends revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the two types of rational good and bad and how it allows revelation to bring rulings that are conditionally good.

Core Idea
Not all rational judgments are absolute — some depend on consequences and context.

This passage from Maturidi responds to those who deny prophethood based on the claim that God cannot command what reason deems bad. Maturidi introduces a crucial distinction: rational good and bad are of two types, one unchanging and one dependent on circumstances. This distinction opens the door for revelation to legislate in areas where reason sees conditional good.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Start by reading the objection: the deniers claim that a wise God would not command what intellect considers bad, otherwise injustice and lying would be permissible.
The objection sets up the problem that the passage answers.
2
Identify the Two Types
The answer divides rational judgments into two categories. Find and understand each: one is absolute and unchanging, the other depends on consequences, preliminaries, or situation.
This classification is key to kalam methodology.
3
Apply to Revelation
See how the second type allows revelation to permit acts like animal slaughter, which intellect deems conditionally good. The prophets only bring justice.
The example of slaughter demonstrates the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم من أنكر الرسل بما لا يأمر الحكيم بما يقبح في العقل؛ إذ لو جاز مجيء الخبر بمثله الجاز ذلك فى إباحة الجور والكذب . فأجيب بأن ما حسنه العقل وقبحه ، النوعان أحدهما لا يتغير ، نحو شكر المنعم وقبح [السفيه] والثانى هو الذي يُحسنه العقل للعاقبة أو للمقدمة أو الحال نحو ما يحسن في العقل .... المنهمك في الفساد الباغي على وجه الانتقام ، فجائز ورود الشرع بمثله . وعلى ذلك أمر الذبائح ولو قدر الإباحة فيه كان كل حي يموت ، والذبح أروح إليه . وأيسر عليه فيكون بمعنى الأشياء المباحة . والثاني أن العدل في الجملة حسن والجور فى الجملة قبح ، لكن من الأشياء مما يظهر قبحه بالنهى وحسنه بالأمر ، وذلك نحو تقلب أحوال المرء وانتقاله ، وعلى ذلك ذبح الحيوان ، إذ جاءت به الرسل ، لا يأتون إلا بالعدل - و على الثنوية ما أجاز النور ضرر الظلمة لما رأى من المصلحة ، وفى مثله ذكر الوراق أن الرسل لو جاءوا إلى التمسك بحجج العقول فهم منا ، وإن جاءوا إلى خلافها فقد جعلها الله حججا ، لم يجز الغير إلا بالتغيير ، وفى ذلك زوال الخطاب
And the one who denied the messengers claimed that the Wise does not command what is deemed bad by the intellect; for if it were permissible for a report to come with the like of it, then that would be permissible in permitting injustice and lying. So it was answered that what the intellect deems good and bad are of two types: one is unchangeable, like gratitude to the Benefactor and the badness of [foolishness]; the second is that which the intellect deems good for the consequence, or for the preliminary, or for the situation, like what the intellect deems good regarding one engrossed in corruption who rebels vengefully. So it is permissible for the Law to come with its like. And upon that is the matter of slaughterings; if permissibility were possible in it, every living thing would die, and slaughtering is more merciful to it and easier upon it, so it is in the sense of permissible things. And the second: justice is in general good, and injustice is in general bad, but there are things whose badness appears by prohibition and goodness by command, like the changing of a person's states and his transition, and upon that is the slaughtering of animals, since the messengers came with it—they only come with justice. And against the dualists what the light allowed harm to the darkness due to benefit it saw. And regarding its like, al-Warraq mentioned that if the messengers came to adhere to the proofs of intellects, they are from us, and if they came contrary to them, then God made them proofs; no other is permissible except by change, and in that is the removal of discourse.
Source pages p. 799

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer combined with a definition and a refutation of dualists. It begins with an objection raised by those who deny prophethood: the Wise Being (God) would not command what reason deems bad, because if a report (revelation) could come with such a thing, it would also permit injustice and lying. The answer establishes a fundamental kalam classification: rational judgments of good and bad are of two types. The first type is absolute and unchanging, such as the goodness of thanking the benefactor and the badness of foolishness. The second type is conditional: the intellect deems something good or bad based on its consequences, its purpose, or the situation. For example, punishing a rebellious wrongdoer is deemed good for a vengeful purpose, even if it seems bad in itself. Thus, revelation can bring rulings that fit this second type. The example of animal slaughter is given: if it were simply permissible for all, every animal would die anyway, but slaughter is actually more merciful and easier for the animal, so it falls under permissible things. The passage then clarifies that justice is generally good and injustice generally bad, but some actions become good or bad only through command or prohibition, like the changing of states. Slaughtering animals is such an act, and the prophets only bring justice. The dualists object that light cannot harm darkness because of benefit, but al-Warraq responds by saying that if the messengers adhered to rational proofs, they would be no different from us; if they came contrary to reason, God made those proofs themselves as proofs, and change is only possible through alteration, which would remove the entire discourse of revelation.

Key Terms
ما حسنه العقل
النوعان
شكر المنعم
المنهمك في الفساد
الذبائح
الثنوية
الوراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All rational judgments of good and bad are absolute and unchanging.
Correction: Only the first type is absolute; the second type depends on consequences and context, allowing revelation to legislate conditionally.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the second type of rational good and bad?
A) Unchanging, like gratitude to the Benefactor
B) Based on consequences, preliminaries, or situation
C) Dependent on divine command alone
D) Always bad, never good

Key Takeaway
The intellect's judgments of good and bad are not monolithic; understanding their two types is essential for reconciling reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever judge an action as 'bad' without considering the full context that God may have commanded or permitted it for a wise purpose? How can you train your heart to submit to revelation even when your immediate rational instinct says otherwise? Commit this week to pause and ask: 'What does Allah command in this situation?' before your own judgment.
?
The passage teaches that gratitude to the Benefactor is an absolute good that never changes. When was the last time you truly felt deep, active gratitude to Allah for a specific blessing in your life—not just said 'Alhamdulillah' but let it transform your behavior? What blessing will you thank Him for right now with a concrete act?
?
The dualists questioned God's wisdom in allowing harm. Have you ever secretly questioned God's justice when you faced hardship or saw injustice? How does this lesson's explanation of conditional good and bad help you trust that every decree of Allah—whether it seems good or bad to you—is ultimately just and wise? Will you now accept a difficulty you are facing as a mercy from Allah, even if you do not understand it?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
Refuting Ibn al-Rawandi: Change in Perception Does Not Change Reason
Maturidi refutes Ibn al-Rawandi's objection on perception change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defends the constancy of rational judgment against Ibn al-Rawandi's objection that changes in perception imply changes in reason.

Core Idea
Objects and contexts change, not reason.

Ibn al-Rawandi raises a powerful objection: if the same thing appears black then white, either the sight changed or the thing. He applies this to rational judgments, suggesting that our perception of justice can change. Maturidi responds by showing that just as everyday actions like standing or cupping are good in different contexts without reason changing, so too the messengers' rulings vary according to circumstances. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the objection and response come through.
Notice the analogy of the head changing color.
2
Identify the objection and its analogy
Ibn al-Rawandi argues that since the object (head) appears different, either sight changed or the object changed. He then equates sight with intellect. Find where he says this.
The objection is at the beginning.
3
Understand Maturidi's response and its extension
Maturidi counters with examples from daily life: standing, sitting, cupping, etc. He then extends to human practices like enduring hardships and finally to the laws. See how each example shows reason remaining constant while context changes.
The response ends with 'la quwwata illa billah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عارضه [ابن] الروندي بما يرى أسود الرأس ثم يراه أبيض ، أتغير بصره أو تغير الشيء على البصر ؛ إذ ليس هو بأسود لما يراه البصر ، فمثله أمر ما يراه العقل عدلا للامر ، وكذلك هذا فى القيام والقعود وكل الأحوال ، ومثله الحجامة والأكل والشرب ، قد تحسن هذه الأحوال على اختلافها ، ولم يجب به تغير العقل حتى يحسن فيه الذي كان يحسن بخلافه ، فمثله أمر الرسل . ثم قد يجوز تحمل المؤن العظام لعواقب محمودة واختيار المضار لسلامة محمودة نحو التجارات والاجارات والزراعات والأدوية وأنواع الجراحات ، وكذلك اختيار ترك النفع لنفع أرجح منه ، وعلى ذلك أمر الشرائع ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Ibn al-Rawandi objected to him with what one sees: the head is black, then he sees it white—has his sight changed, or has the thing changed upon the sight? Since it is not black according to what the sight sees, so likewise is the matter of what the intellect sees as justice for the matter. And similarly this in standing and sitting and all states. And its like is cupping, eating, and drinking; these states may be good despite their differences, and it is not necessary that the intellect change so that what was good becomes good in its opposite. So likewise is the affair of the messengers. Then one may endure great hardships for praiseworthy outcomes and choose harms for praiseworthy safety, like trades, rentals, agriculture, medicines, and types of surgeries, and likewise choose to forgo a benefit for a more preferable benefit, and upon that is the affair of the laws. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 799

Commentary
This passage presents an objection by Ibn al-Rawandi and its response. The objection uses an analogy: when one sees a head as black and later as white, either the sight has changed or the thing itself has changed. Since the head is not black according to the sight, similarly what the intellect sees as just or unjust may change due to the object, not the intellect. He then applies this to the messengers: their rulings might seem contradictory but are actually based on changing situations. The response (which is the author's own argument) shows that just as standing, sitting, cupping, eating, and drinking can be good in different contexts without reason itself changing, so too can the messengers bring different rulings. The passage then expands: people endure great hardships for good outcomes, choose harms for safety, and forgo a benefit for a greater benefit. This is the principle underlying the laws (shara'i'). The phrase 'there is no power except through God' ends the section, affirming divine assistance.

Key Terms
ابن الروندي
تغير البصر
القيام والقعود
الحجامة
تحمل المؤن
الشرائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Changes in moral judgments indicate that reason is unreliable or changing.
Correction: The passage shows that the object or context changes, not reason itself. Reason remains constant; it is the circumstances that vary, leading to different judgments.

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of the black and white head illustrate?
A) The intellect is unreliable
B) The object changes, not the faculty of sight or reason
C) The messengers contradict themselves
D) All rational judgments are absolute

Key Takeaway
Rational judgments can vary with context because the object changes, not because reason itself is unstable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you change your moral stance on an issue, do you secretly worry that your reason has become unstable? This lesson teaches that reason itself is constant; the object or context changes. Look into your own life: is there a moral judgment you have recently revised? Was it truly because the context changed, or did you compromise the principles of reason? Commit to examining your object before questioning your faculty.
?
The passage mentions enduring great hardships for praiseworthy outcomes. What is one praiseworthy outcome in your life that you are avoiding because of the hardship it requires? How does knowing that your reason remains constant—that it is the object, not your intellect, that presents difficulty—empower you to pursue it with confidence? What is one step you can take today toward that ends?
?
Maturidi compares cupping, eating, and drinking to the messengers' laws. These actions are good or bad depending on need. Think of a divine command you find difficult to accept—is it because you assume your reason must change to accept it, or because you have not yet understood how the context and greater benefit make it rational? Ask God to show you the wisdom behind His command, and resolve to trust that your reason, when properly applied, will see its justice.

The Two Types of Good and Bad
Maturidi classifies things into intrinsic and consequential good/bad, using slaughter as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's twofold classification of actions into those good/bad in themselves and those dependent on consequences, and why this necessitates prophethood.

Core Idea
Some actions are always good or bad; others depend on their consequences and need divine guidance.

Maturidi begins by clarifying that moral judgments are not all uniform. While some actions are intrinsically good or bad, many others—like slaughter—can be either praiseworthy or blameworthy depending on context and outcome. This classification leads directly to the necessity of prophets who can communicate God's commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening support
Notice how Maturidi first aligns with al-Warraq: the intellect condemns harming without cause and requires loving for others what one loves for oneself. Slaughter is an exception because of its consequences.
This sets up the problem: some acts like slaughter are not intrinsically bad.
2
Identify the two types
The passage states: 'Things are of two types: one type is good in itself and its opposite bad; the second is that where a thing and its opposite are good depending on need and consequences.' Mark these clearly.
3
Understand the implication for prophethood
Since the second type requires knowledge of praise/blame of consequences, one must rely on a knower of such (God or messenger). This establishes the need for revelation.
This is a key argument for prophethood in kalam.
4
Apply the classification to slaughter
Slaughter is not intrinsically bad; it can be good for vengeance or repelling harm. Thus it falls into the second type, and its ruling comes from permission or prohibition.
This exemplifies how the classification works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيد الوراق الذي بينا أن فى العقل ذم الإساءة إلى من لم يؤذ ، وإن لم يحب لغيره ما يحب المرء لنفسه ، والذبائح خارجة من ذلك ؛ فهذا لأنه توهمه مفردا من العامل ، فاذا تأمل حسن العواقب والسلامة مع عقيب المنافع وكذلك فضل راحة وفى الذبائح ذلك ونحن نقول وبالله التوفيق : إن الأشياء نوعان : أحدهما مما يحسن لنفسه ويقبح ضده وكل خلافاته ، والثانى ما يحسن الشيء وخلافاته على حسب الحاجة وقيام الدلالة من حمد العواقب وذمها ، فلزم القول فى هذا بمن يعرف أحوال الحمد والذم ، فيخرج الأمر عليه على أنه لا بد لمن يكون يعتمد على عقله والاختلاف المتناقض ذلك سببه ، أو إلى مخصوص من العقل ، وفى ذلك القول بالرسول . ثم أمر الذبائح لا يحتمل أن يكون قبحها لنفسها لما يحل فى موضع الانتقام ويحسن في العقول إذا تفكر فى ذلك دفع الأذى والمكروه ، أو تقع العواقب فبطل قبح ذلك لنفسه ، فلزم جواز المحنة فيه بالترك والإذن ، وفى ذلك إباحة . وأيضا أن كل شيء حسنه العقل فهو لا يقبح بحال ، وكذلك القبيح من الحسن ، وكل شيء قبح لنفار الطبع بما يتوهم حلوله في جوهر المتوهم فينفر طبعه لألمه ، ثم هذا قد يجوز أن يذهب ذلك بالاعتياد نحو القصابين والذين اعتادوا القتال ، فثبت أن النهى عنه طبيعي لا عقلى ، فتغير ذلك من العادة يزول ، وذلك نحو جواهر من الحيوان طبعه التوحش ، وعلى ذلك طبع الجميع عن الأحمال الثقيلة ، ثم تصير الرياضة وتعويد غيره كأنها على ذلك طبعت ، فعلى ذلك أمر الحيوان . وأيضا أن كل حي إذ هو يموت ، ثم لم يلحق أحد به لاممه ، فمثله إذا جاء الإذن ممن هو له وأحق من يقول الثنويه لأوجه : أحدها استجازتهم تباين النور والظلمة ثم الامتزاج ثم التباين ، وفى ذلك تفرق بين كل مقترنين وتميز بين كل ممتزجين ، وذلك معنى الذبح . والثانى أن الألم إما أن يحل بجوهر النور فيصير محتملا للاذى
And al-Warraq supported that which we explained: that in the intellect there is condemnation of harming one who has not harmed, and that one does not love for another what one loves for oneself; slaughterings are outside that. This is because he imagined it in isolation from the agent, but when one reflects on the good of the consequences and safety along with the outcomes of benefits, and likewise the virtue of ease, in slaughterings that is the case. And we say—and success is from God—: Things are of two types: one type is that which is good in itself and its opposite and all its contradictories are bad; the second is that where a thing and its opposites are good depending on need and the presence of indication from praise of consequences or blame of them. So it is necessary to affirm in this one who knows the states of praise and blame, and the matter proceeds on that: there must be for one who relies on his intellect—and contradictory difference is its cause—or to a specific [part] of the intellect, and in that is the affirmation of the messenger. Then the matter of slaughterings cannot be that their badness is for themselves, because it occurs in a place of vengeance and is deemed good by intellects when one reflects on repelling harm and nuisance, or the consequences occur, so its badness for itself becomes invalid. Therefore, it is permissible to test it by prohibition and permission, and in that is permissibility. Also, everything that the intellect deems good is not bad at all, and likewise the bad is [not] from the good. And everything that is deemed bad due to natural aversion, because of what is imagined to be resolved in the substance of the one imagining, so his nature recoils from its pain—then it is possible that this disappears by habit, like butchers and those accustomed to fighting. So it is established that the prohibition concerning it is natural, not rational; that change by habit disappears. That is like substances of animals whose nature is wildness, and upon that is the nature of all regarding heavy loads; then through exercise and habituating otherwise, it becomes as if they were created upon that. So upon that is the matter of animals. Also, every living thing dies, and no one has reproached it for that. So likewise, when permission comes from the one to whom it belongs and who is more entitled. The dualists say for [several] aspects: one of them is their deeming permissible the separation of light and darkness, then mixing, then separation—in that is the separation of every pair and the distinction of every mixture, and that is the meaning of slaughter. The second is that pain either resolves in the substance of light, so it becomes capable of enduring harm...
Source pages p. 800

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a classification, an argument, and a refutation. It begins by supporting al-Warraq's point: the intellect condemns harming the innocent and requires loving for others what one loves for oneself; slaughterings are outside that because when one considers consequences and benefits, slaughter becomes acceptable. Then the author (Maturidi) states the central classification: things are of two types. The first type is good in itself, and its opposite and all contradictories are bad. The second type is such that both a thing and its opposite can be good depending on need and the praise or blame of consequences. This implies the necessity of a knower of praise and blame (God or the messenger). Since contradictory differences arise when relying on reason alone, one must refer to a specific part of intellect or to the messenger—hence the necessity of prophethood. The example of slaughter is revisited: its badness cannot be intrinsic, because it can be good for vengeance or for repelling harm; thus it is permissible for prohibition and permission. Then the passage argues against natural aversion as a basis for moral judgment: things deemed bad due to natural repulsion can be overcome by habit, as with butchers and soldiers. This proves that the prohibition is natural, not rational. Habit can change nature, as seen in taming wild animals or training to bear heavy loads. Finally, the passage addresses the dualists: they permit the separation and mixing of light and darkness, which is analogous to slaughter (separation of soul and body). The dualists also argue that pain affects the substance of light.

Key Terms
النوعان
يحسن لنفسه
حمد العواقب
الرسول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all moral rules are absolute and known by reason alone, so there is no need for revelation.
Correction: Maturidi shows that while some actions are intrinsically good/bad, many depend on consequences, which require knowledge of outcomes only God or His messenger can provide.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the second type of thing is defined by:
A) Always being good in itself
B) Its goodness depending on need and the praise/blame of consequences
C) Being naturally repulsive
D) Being exclusively bad

Key Takeaway
Actions are not all alike: some are intrinsically good or bad, while others depend on consequences and require prophetic guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you were absolutely certain an action was either right or wrong, only to later question your certainty. Did you seek guidance from revelation before acting, or did you rely solely on your own intellect? What might you do differently next time you face such a moral dilemma?
?
The passage says that relying on reason alone can lead to contradictory differences (الاختلاف المتناقض). Consider a moral disagreement you have with someone else. Could your position be influenced by consequences you haven't fully considered? Are you open to correcting your stance through prophetic guidance, even if it contradicts your initial intuition?
?
Slaughter is used as an example where external factors like vengeance or repelling harm change its moral value. Is there an action in your life—perhaps a habit or a relationship—that you have assumed is simply 'good' or 'bad' without examining its consequences and divine guidance? What would it cost you to honestly reassess that assumption today?

Refuting Natural Repulsion as a Moral Basis
Maturidi argues that natural repulsion is not a rational basis for prohibition and refutes dualists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that natural aversion is not a rational basis for prohibition, as it can be overcome by habit, and how this refutes the dualists' argument about slaughter.

Core Idea
Natural repulsion is not morality; habit can overcome it, and true moral judgment is rational.

Having established that some actions depend on consequences, Maturidi now addresses the objection that natural repulsion makes certain acts inherently bad. He argues that natural aversion is changeable through habit, citing butchers and warriors. The passage concludes with a refutation of the dualists who misuse the concept of separation.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial principle
Start with 'everything that the intellect deems good is not bad at all, and likewise the bad is not from the good.' This establishes that rational judgment is consistent.
This principle grounds the argument that morality is rational, not natural.
2
Understand the argument on natural aversion
The passage says that things deemed bad due to natural repulsion can be overcome by habit, as seen with butchers and those accustomed to fighting. Hence the prohibition is natural, not rational.
This proves that natural aversion does not indicate intrinsic badness.
3
See the analogy with animal training
Just as wild animals can be tamed and heavy loads become bearable through exercise, habit can change nature. This supports the claim that natural repulsion is changeable.
4
Analyze the refutation of dualists
The dualists permit the separation of light and darkness, which is analogous to slaughter (separation of soul and body). They cannot consistently oppose slaughter while permitting that.
This final argument uses consistency against the dualists.

Source Text

?

The Quran as Rational Proof of Prophethood
Maturidi enumerates three rational proofs from the Quran: inimitability, unique theological arguments, and accurate prophecies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi presents the Quran itself as a rational proof of prophethood through its inimitability, theological arguments, and prophecies.

Core Idea
The Quran is its own rational proof: unmatched eloquence, profound theology, and true prophecies.

Maturidi argues that the Quran's miraculous nature is not just a claim but can be rationally recognized by those skilled in language. Moreover, its unique theological content and accurate predictions provide further rational evidence. This passage ties together three aspects of the Quran's miraculousness as a coherent proof of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the prerequisite
Understand that recognizing the Quran's inimitability requires expertise in eloquence. Maturidi emphasizes that only those well-versed in literary arts can perceive its miraculous nature.
This shows that rational proof is accessible to informed reason.
2
Examine the theological content
The Quran contains profound arguments for tawhid (divine unity) and resurrection, which were unparalleled at the time of revelation. No human could have produced such arguments.
This highlights the Quran's unique intellectual depth.
3
Consider the prophecies
The Quran includes reports of future events and explanations of ongoing calamities that the intellect can verify. This predictive accuracy points to divine origin.
Prophecies serve as empirical confirmation.
4
Synthesize the three proofs
See how inimitability, theological arguments, and prophecies together form a multi-faceted rational proof that the Quran is from God, thus confirming the prophethood of Muhammad.
Each aspect complements the others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقلية فما بين الله من شأن القرآن الذي إنما يعرف خروجه عن احتمال الخلق من بالغ في فنون الآداب وعرف جواهر الكلام وأصنافه، ثم ما فيه وسع المحاجة في توحيد الرب وأدلة البعث مما لم يكن يومئذ على وجه الأرض من من يدعى ذلك، ثم ما فيه من الأنباء وما يكون أبدا ومن بيان النوازل التي تكون، مما في استعمال العقول تطلع عليه.
As for the rational [proofs], it is what God has shown concerning the affair of the Quran, which is only known to be beyond the capacity of creation by one who has excelled in the arts of literature and known the jewels of speech and its types; then what it contains of ample argumentation concerning the tawhid of the Lord and the proofs of resurrection, which at that time there was no one on the face of the earth who claimed that; then what it contains of reports of things that will be forever and of explanation of calamities that occur, which the use of intellects can attain to.
Source pages p. 803

Commentary
This passage presents rational (ʿaqliyya) proofs of prophethood, specifically focusing on the Quran. Maturidi argues that the Quran's miraculous nature is recognized by those versed in eloquence—it is beyond human capacity. Furthermore, the Quran contains profound arguments for divine unity (tawḥīd) and resurrection, which no one at the time could produce. It also includes prophecies of future events and explanations of ongoing events that the intellect can verify. Thus, the Quran itself is a rational proof of the Prophet's truthfulness.

Correction: Maturidi shows that the Quran itself is the rational proof through its characteristics.

If you knew a catastrophic event would happen tomorrow, you would change your behavior. The Quran has already informed you of the greatest calamity—the Day of Reckoning. How will you prepare starting today?

Five Rational Proofs of Prophethood by Abu Zayd
Abu Zayd enumerates five rational proofs of the Prophet's authenticity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five rational proofs Abu Zayd presents for the authenticity of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him).

Core Idea
Rational proofs show prophethood is reasonable, not just blind acceptance.

Abu Zayd, an early Muslim scholar, provided a rational framework to demonstrate that the coming of the Prophet Muhammad was not a random event but consistent with God's ways. In this passage, he enumerates five rational proofs, each supported by Quranic verses. These proofs address historical continuity, timing, need, prominence, and prior expectation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Abu Zayd uses Quranic verses to support each proof.
2
Identify the five proofs
The passage lists five rational proofs. Find each one and note the Quranic verse cited. See how each proof answers a potential objection.
The proofs are sequential: first, second, third, fourth, fifth.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Each proof is grounded in observable patterns: historical precedent, timing of need, societal condition, geographical prominence, and prior human desire. Ask yourself how each makes the Prophet's coming more credible.
This shows that reason and revelation are complementary.
4
Consider the additional point
After the five proofs, Abu Zayd discusses the Prophet's unlettered upbringing as further evidence. Notice how it strengthens the argument that his knowledge came from God.
The passage is incomplete, but the intent is clear: the Prophet's lack of formal learning points to divine origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر أبو زيد أن الحسية من الآلات فما جاءت الآثار كافية. وأما العقلية فهى على وجوه: أحدها أن أمره لم يكن مستغربا، بل كان مستمرا على العادة بوجود مثله في الأمم، فلذلك يبطل وجه الرد عليه في أول وهله. قال الله تعالى: وَإِنْ مِنْ أُمَّةٍ إِلَّا خَلَا فِيهَا نَذِيرٌ، وقال: ثُمَّ أَرْسَلْنَا رُسُلَنَا بَتَرَا. والثاني موافقة مجيئه وقت الحاجة إليه؛ إذ كان زمان فترة ودروس العلم، مع جرى عادة الله بمعاقبة أسباب الهداية عند زوال أهله عن الهدى. قال الله تعالى: قَدْ جَاءَكُمْ رَسُولُنَا يُبَيِّنُ لَكُمْ. والثالث كون المبعوث فيهم بموضع الحاجة إليه و الخلاء جنسه عن أسباب العلم بقوله: هُوَ الَّذِي بَعَثَ فِي الأُمِّيِّينَ رَسُولًا مِنْهُم وغيره. والرابع كونه في أظهر الأماكن للخلق؛ إذ هو معالم أهل الآفاق في الدنيا، وقال الله تعالى: وَكَذَلِكَ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَيْكَ. والخامس منى القوم ذلك وإظهار الرغبة في ذلك، وإذا اقترح مقترح على ربه إزالة حماته لم يكن تعجب قطع معذرته، قال الله تعالى: وَلَوْ أَنَّا أَهْلَكْنَاكُم بِعَذَابٍ مِنْ قَبْلِهِ، قال: وَأَقْسَمُوا بِاللَّهِ جَهْدَ أَيْمَانِهِمْ لَئِنْ جَاءَهُم نَذِيرٌ. فهذه الخمسة ثم حاجتهم به في أحوالهم ثم ما حاجتهم بما في أحوال النبي، منها أنه نشأ فى قوم لم يكن لهم كتب ولا دراسة، مع ما لم يفارق قومه، وما كان لهم يفارق قومه، وما كان لهم كتب قد سبق له الارتياض في دراستها كان في ضمن تلك: لو طرأ عليهم طارئ لا يجها مكانه، وذلك قوله: أَمْ لَمْ يَعْرِفُوا رَسُولَهُمْ، وقوله: تِلْكَ مِنْ أَنْبَاءِ الْغَيْبِ نُوحِيهَا. وقد نشأ أميا، والأمى لا يأخذ عن الكتب ولا يستطيع التحفظ من الأفواه غاية الحفظ، إنما يكون ضبطه بصور معقولة روحانية يرتفع بها عن المهم، دليله ما لا يوجد عن
And Abu Zayd mentioned that the sensory [proofs] are from the instruments, so the transmitted reports are sufficient. As for the rational [proofs], they are of several aspects: The first is that his matter was not unprecedented, but rather it was continuous with the custom of the existence of his like among the nations; therefore the possibility of rejection at first glance is invalidated. God Almighty said: 'And there is no nation but a warner has passed among them', and He said: 'Then We sent Our messengers successively.' The second is the agreement of his coming at the time of need for him; for it was a time of interval and the effacement of knowledge, with the custom of God punishing the causes of guidance when its people depart from guidance. God Almighty said: 'Our messenger has come to you to make clear to you.' The third is that the one sent among them was in a place of need for him and the emptiness of his kind from the means of knowledge, by His saying: 'He it is who sent among the unlettered a messenger from among them' and others. The fourth is that he was in the most prominent places for creation; for he is the landmark of the people of the horizons in the world, and God Almighty said: 'And thus We have revealed to you.' The fifth is that the people wished for that and showed desire for it, and if a proposer proposes to his Lord to remove his protectors, there is no wonder in cutting off his excuse. God Almighty said: 'And if We had destroyed them with a punishment before it', and He said: 'And they swore by Allah their strongest oaths that if a warner came to them...' So these five [aspects] complete their need for him in their circumstances, then what their need includes in the circumstances of the Prophet. Among them is that he grew up among a people who had no books nor study, while he did not leave his people, and they did not have books that he had previously trained in studying them; it was implicit in that: if a newcomer had come to them, he would not have known his place. And that is His saying: 'Do they not recognize their messenger?' and His saying: 'These are from the news of the unseen which We reveal to you.' And he grew up unlettered, and the unlettered does not take from books nor can he memorize from mouths to the utmost degree of memorization; rather his mastery is by rational spiritual forms by which he rises above the base. Its proof is what is not found from...
Source pages p. 804

Commentary
This passage, attributed to Abu Zayd, classifies proofs into sensory (ḥissiyya) and rational (ʿaqliyya). Sensory proofs from transmitted reports are sufficient. Then it enumerates five rational proofs: (1) The Prophet's coming was not unprecedented; similar messengers came to previous nations, so it cannot be rejected outright. (2) His coming coincided with a time of need—a period of interval between prophets and decline of knowledge, consistent with God's custom. (3) He was sent among a people in need, specifically the unlettered Arabs, as the Quran says. (4) He appeared in the most prominent place (Mecca), a known landmark. (5) The people themselves had previously wished for a warner and sworn they would follow one, so his coming cuts off their excuse. After these five, the passage discusses the Prophet's upbringing among an unlettered people, without prior study, and his being unlettered himself; yet he brought knowledge far beyond human capacity, proving it is from God. The text ends mid-sentence, but the argument is that the Prophet's unlettered state proves his knowledge is divine revelation.

وجوه

أمية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational proofs for prophethood are abstract and not grounded in history.
Correction: Abu Zayd's proofs are based on historical patterns, timing, societal needs, and prior human desires, making them concrete and observable.

Checkpoint

B) Five

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is rationally supported by observable patterns in history, timing, need, geography, and human expectation.

Proof of Temporality and Necessity of Reason
Maturidi argues for reason's necessity in proving creation's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's dialectical argument that proving the temporality of bodies requires reason, otherwise a circularity arises when relying solely on revelation.

Core Idea
Reason is indispensable in establishing the proof for the world's createdness.

Maturidi presents a tight dialectical argument to show that the proof of the temporality of bodies cannot rely exclusively on revelation without falling into circular reasoning. He demonstrates that unless reason is admitted as a valid source of knowledge, the whole enterprise of proving createdness collapses. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is short but dense; note the dialectical structure.
2
Identify the circularity
Notice how Maturidi sets up the objection: if one says the proof comes from revelation, he asks for proof of revelation's truth. If the answer is 'the temporality of things', then a circle emerges: temporality is known only through revelation, but revelation's truth is known only through temporality.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the path of knowledge is cut off unless reason is admitted. This establishes reason as necessary for foundational theological proofs.
The reference to Christ shows the polemical context against Christian claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نتكلم فى دليل حدث الأجسام ، فإن صيره العقل لزمه في ذلك . الأشياء فإن قال : السمع ، قيل : ودليل صدق المسموع ما هو ؟ فإن قال : حدوث اء ، يصير حدوث الأشياء لا يعلم إلا بالسمع ، وصدق لا يعلم إلا بحدوث الأشياء ، فانقطع سبيل معرفة فيها إلا أن يقر بالعقل فيلزمه ذلك في المسيح .
Then we speak about the proof of the temporality of bodies, for if reason makes it temporal, it follows for him in that. Things. For if he says: Hearing (revelation), it is said: And what is the proof of the truth of what is heard? For if he says: The temporality of things, then the temporality of things is not known except through hearing, and the truth [of hearing] is not known except through the temporality of things, so the path of knowledge in them is cut off unless he admits reason, and then that follows for him concerning the Messiah.
Source pages p. 812

Commentary
This passage introduces a dialectical argument about the proof of the temporality of bodies (huduth al-ajsam), a foundational theological topic in kalam. Maturidi states that if one tries to prove the temporality of bodies using reason (al-'aql) alone, it would be binding. However, if someone claims that the proof comes from revelation (al-sam'), then a problem arises: What is the proof that the revelation is true? If they answer 'the temporality of things', then a circularity occurs — the temporality of things is only known through revelation, but the truth of revelation is only known through the temporality of things. Thus the epistemic path is cut off ('fankata'a sabil al-ma'rifa') unless one admits reason as a valid source of knowledge. This then has implications for the Christian claim about Christ: if reason is admitted, it must be applied to Christ as well, implying his temporality and thus refuting Christian doctrine. The passage demonstrates the necessity of reason in establishing foundational truths, even in a scriptural context.

Key Terms
دليل حدث الأجسام
السمع
العقل
حدوث
صدق المسموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is rejecting revelation as a source of knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi is not rejecting revelation; he is showing that attempting to prove the temporality of bodies solely through revelation leads to circular reasoning unless reason is admitted as a primary source.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if one tries to prove the temporality of bodies only through revelation?
A) It becomes an unassailable proof
B) It leads to circularity and the path of knowledge is cut off
C) It is confirmed by the consensus of scholars
D) It is strengthened by the miracles of the prophets

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that rational proof is foundational, and reliance solely on revelation for the temporality of bodies leads to an epistemic circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a religious claim, do you ever accept it simply because it is in the Quran or hadith, without asking yourself why you believe that source is true? Maturidi’s argument exposes a hidden circularity in that approach. Will you take time this week to examine the rational basis for your trust in revelation?
?
Imagine a friend asks you why you believe the world was created. If you reply 'because the Quran says so,' what would you say when they ask you why you believe the Quran? This passage forces you to have a deeper answer. Do you have one that does not simply repeat the same evidence? If not, what are you willing to do to develop that rational foundation?
?
Maturidi ends by linking this argument to the Messiah. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah or the unseen that you have never subjected to rational scrutiny because they feel 'sacred'? Could it be that your reluctance to use reason actually weakens your faith rather than protects it? What is one belief you will intentionally examine with reason this week?

The Two Kinds of Knowledge and Two Divisions of the Path
Maturidi enumerates three pairs related to trial, knowledge, and path.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi grounds knowledge in direct perception (ʿiyān) as the foundation for revelation, and how the trial is structured through difficulty and ease.

Core Idea
Knowledge is rooted in direct perception, not mere tradition.

Maturidi sets the stage by explaining that the test of human beings involves both difficulty and ease. To navigate this test, God provided two types of knowledge: manifest and hidden, and established a path with two divisions, beginning with direct perception. This passage establishes the epistemological hierarchy in Maturidi's thought.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trial matters
Read the first part of the passage where Maturidi mentions that God tested people with two matters: difficult and easy, without trial they undertake both. Notice that the outcome returns to God.
This shows that trial is essential for human development.
2
Recognize the two types of knowledge
Identify the two aspects of knowledge: manifest (ẓāhir) and hidden (khafī). See how this distinction allows rational people to excel based on effort and endurance.
Knowledge is the means to attain virtue.
3
Grasp the two divisions of the path
Find the statement that God made His path two divisions: one is direct perception (ʿiyān), which is the most specific means and free from ignorance. The second division is implied as revelation (samʿ).
Direct perception serves as the foundation for what is hidden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم امتحنهم ، إذ أبت عقولهم احتمال أمثالهم ، ورغبت في محاسن ما حُسن من الأعمال واجتناب ما قبح من ذلك . ثم جعل ما فيه محنتهم أمرين : العسير واليسير ، والسهل / والصعب؛ بلا محنة يتعاطون الأمرين جميعا؛ لما إليه مرجع ما أقدموا عليه وامتنعوا ، وعلى ذلك جعل الأسباب التى بها التوصل لهم إلى الأصل الذي به يرتقى إلى كل درجة وينال كل فضيلة ، وهو العلم على وجهين : على الظاهر البين والخفى المستور ليتفاضل بذلك أوايا العقلي على قدر تفاضلهم في الاجتهاد واحتمال ما كرهته الطباع ونفرت عنه النفس ، وعلى ذلك جعل سبيله قسمين: أحدهما العيان الذى هو أخص الأسباب ، وهو الذى ليس معه جهل ، ليكون أصلا لما خفى منه
Then He tested them, because their minds refused to endure likes of themselves, and desired the virtues of what was good of deeds and avoidance of what was ugly of that. Then He made the trial in that two matters: the difficult and the easy, and the simple/hard; without trial they undertake both matters; because to Him is the return of what they advanced upon and refrained from. And upon that He made the means by which they attain the origin by which one rises to every rank and attains every virtue, and it is knowledge in two aspects: the manifest/bright and the hidden/concealed, so that the rational ones may excel thereby according to their excellence in effort and enduring what natures disliked and souls recoiled from. And upon that He made His path two divisions: one of them is direct perception (al-ʿiyān), which is the most specific of means, and which has no ignorance with it, to be a foundation for what is hidden from it.
Source pages p. 821

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of trial. Maturidi says that since their minds refused to endure their equals and desired virtuous deeds, God tested them with two kinds of things: difficult and easy, simple and hard. They involve themselves in both without trial in a sense, because the outcome returns to God. Then he states that the means to attain the origin of all virtue is knowledge, which is of two types: manifest and hidden. This distinction allows rational people to excel in proportion to their effort and endurance. Accordingly, God made His path two divisions: the first is direct perception (al-ʿiyān), which is the most specific means and has no ignorance, serving as a foundation for what is hidden. This underscores the primacy of direct evidence in knowledge.

Key Terms
العيان
العلم
الظاهر
الخفى
العسير
اليسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that revelation (samʿ) is the only valid source of knowledge in Islam.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that direct perception (ʿiyān) is the most specific means and the foundation for what is hidden, implying that revelation is built upon it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the foundation for hidden knowledge?
A) Revelation (samʿ)
B) Direct perception (ʿiyān)
C) Reason alone
D) Tradition

Key Takeaway
Direct perception (ʿiyān) is the foundational source of knowledge, upon which revelation and other means are built.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, Maturidi says direct perception (ʿiyān) is the foundation for hidden knowledge. When you face a moral dilemma, do you truly start from what you can see and know directly, or do you leap to conclusions without that foundation? Will you commit today to testing your assumptions against clear observation before seeking spiritual guidance?
?
The passage states that rational beings excel according to their 'effort and enduring what natures disliked.' What is the one thing your soul recoils from that you know you must face to gain deeper knowledge? Are you willing to endure that discomfort starting now?
?
Knowledge is divided into manifest and hidden. Which aspect do you tend to neglect? If you ignore the hidden because it is difficult, you may be avoiding the very test God placed before you. What hidden truth have you been avoiding, and what step will you take today to seek it?
