Six Reasons the Unambiguous Becomes Hidden
Maturidi enumerates six reasons why the unambiguous (muḥkam) becomes hidden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six specific reasons Maturidi identifies for why the unambiguous (muḥkam) becomes hidden from some people.

Core Idea
The muḥkam does not hide by itself; factors within us obscure it.

Maturidi begins by addressing a key puzzle: if the truth is clear (muḥkam), why do people miss it? He enumerates six distinct reasons, ranging from natural inclination to deficient research. This lesson unpacks each cause so you can see how human tendencies can veil the obvious.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of reasons
Read the Arabic from 'وإنما خفي المحكم' to 'لا قوة إلا بالله'. Identify each of the six reasons mentioned, noting the connecting 'أو' (or).
The passage uses the word 'لِمعان' (meanings/reasons) to introduce the list.
2
Reflect on each reason
Think about how each reason—pleasure, habit, taqlid, lack of effort, overconfidence in intellect, and deficient research—can cause even clear truths to appear ambiguous.
3
Connect to the universal witness
Notice that Maturidi says these reasons operate 'despite the universal witness of things to tawḥīd'. How does this emphasize the depth of the obstacle?
The phrase 'على شهادة كلية الأشياء' highlights that creation itself testifies to God's oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما خفي المحكم على من لم يبلغه لمعان: إما ميل طبيعة الجوهر إلى ما يتلذذ به، أو لإلف بعض ما اعتاده، أو لتقليد من وثق به، أو تقصير في الطلب، أو لثقة منه بعقله أحب أن يسوي عليه حكمة الربوبية دون اتبع عقله ما ألقي في سمعه، فصار به المحكم عنده متشابها، أو تقصير في البحث؛ إذ الوجوه التي هي وجوه الشبهة على الذين عدلوا عن التوحيد على شهادة كلية الأشياء له بذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأصل ذلك أن الله تعالى خلق البشر على طبائع تميل إلى الملاذ الحاضرة وتدعو صاحبها إليه، وتزينها في عينه بما ركب فيه من الشهوات إلى ما إليه مثل طبعه، وهي تنفر عما فيه ألمه وتعباه، فيصير طبعه أحد أعداء عقله في التحسين والتقبيح، وإن كان ما حسنه العقل وقبحه ليس له زوال ولا تغير من حال إلى حال، وما حسنته الطبيعة وقبحته هو في حد الانقلاب والتغير من حال إلى حال بالرياضة والقيام على ذلك بالكف عما ألفه، والصرف إلى ما ينفر عنه يحسن القيام عليه، على ما يحتمل الطبع قبوله نحو المعروف من أمر الطيور والبهائم، إنها بطبعها تنفر عما أريد بها من أنواع منافع البشر، ثم يحسن قيام أهل البصر بذلك لصير طبع عليه بالميل إليه كالمستوحش، ومما طبع على النفار عنه كالمطبوع عليه. وعلى ذلك أمر نفار الطبع عن القتل والذبح في البشر، ثم سهولة ذلك عليه في الحيوان. وما يدرك حسنه بالعقل وقبحه، فلا يزال يزداد على ما فيه ادراكه.
And indeed the unambiguous (al-muḥkam) is hidden from one who has not attained it due to meanings: either inclination of the substance of nature towards what it delights in, or familiarity with some of what it is accustomed to, or taqlīd of one he trusts, or deficiency in seeking, or confidence in his intellect such that he loves to equalize upon it the wisdom of lordship, without his intellect following what was cast into his hearing, so the unambiguous becomes ambiguous to him, or deficiency in research; for the aspects that are aspects of doubt upon those who deviated from tawḥīd, despite the universal witness of things to it, and there is no power except with God. The root of that is that God, exalted, created human beings upon natures that incline to present pleasures and call their possessor to them, and adorn them in his eye with what He placed in them of desires towards that to which his nature is similar, and they are averse to what has pain and fatigue; so his nature becomes one of the enemies of his intellect in declaring good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ), even though what the intellect declares good or evil has no removal or change from state to state, while what nature declares good or evil is in the realm of reversal and change from state to state through discipline and perseverance upon that by refraining from what one is accustomed to and turning to what one is averse to, if the nature can accept it, like the known matter of birds and beasts: by nature they are averse to the types of human benefits intended for them, then the people of insight succeed in that until their nature becomes inclined to it like the domesticated, and what it was instinctively averse to becomes like the innate. And upon that is the matter of the aversion of nature to killing and slaughter in humans, then the ease of that upon them in animals. And what is perceived as good or evil by the intellect never ceases to increase in its perception.
Source pages p. 822

Commentary
This passage explains why the unambiguous (muḥkam) becomes hidden for some people. Maturidi lists six reasons: (1) natural inclination toward pleasure, (2) habit, (3) taqlīd of a trusted authority, (4) lack of effort in seeking, (5) overconfidence in one's own intellect, wanting to make it equal to divine wisdom, and (6) deficiency in research. These lead people to see the muḥkam as ambiguous. The root cause is that human nature (ṭab‘) inclines to immediate pleasures and abhors pain, making it an enemy of the intellect in judging good and evil (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ). While the intellect's judgments of good and evil are stable and unchanging, nature's judgments are volatile and can be changed through discipline (riyāḍa). Maturidi gives examples: birds and beasts can be trained to accept things they naturally avoid, and humans can overcome aversion to killing animals despite initial repulsion. The intellect's perception of good and evil only increases with awareness.

Key Terms
المحكم (al-muḥkam)
المتشابه (al-mutashābih)
الطبع (al-ṭab‘)
العقل (al-‘aql)
التحسين والتقبيح (al-taḥsīn wa al-taqbīḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think the Qur'an is inherently ambiguous or contradictory.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the ambiguity arises from human factors, not from the text itself.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which reason refers to blindly following a trusted person?
A) Taqlīd
B) Īlf (habit)
C) Taqṣīr fī al-ṭalab
D) Thiqah bi ‘aqlihi

Key Takeaway
The muḥkam becomes hidden due to six specific human factors, not because of any deficiency in the truth itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these six reasons do you see most clearly in yourself right now when you struggle to accept a clear truth from the Quran or Sunnah? Commit to identifying one and taking a specific step to remove that veil.
?
Have you ever trusted a scholar or teacher so deeply that you never questioned their teachings, even when the clear proofs seemed to point elsewhere? How can you honor scholars without falling into taqlīd that obscures the muḥkam?
?
When you read this passage, did you feel that 'overconfidence in one's intellect' is a danger you personally face? In what area of your life do you need to humble your intellect before divine wisdom?

Nature vs. Intellect: The Root of Error
Maturidi explains the root conflict between nature and intellect, and how nature's judgments can be changed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains the conflict between human nature (ṭab‘) and intellect (al-‘aql) in moral judgment, and why the intellect's judgments are stable while nature's judgments are changeable.

Core Idea
Nature is the intellect's enemy in declaring good and evil.

Having listed the surface reasons for the muḥkam being hidden, Maturidi now digs deeper into the root cause: the inherent conflict between human nature and intellect. He explains that nature inclines to immediate pleasures and abhors pain, making it oppose the intellect's stable moral judgments. This lesson explores that conflict, including how discipline can reshape nature's tendencies, illustrated with examples from animals and human practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root cause
Read the passage after 'وأصل ذلك'. Note the phrase 'فيصير طبعه أحد أعداء عقله' — nature becomes an enemy of the intellect in moral judgment.
2
Contrast nature's vs. intellect's judgments
Maturidi says what the intellect declares good or evil is stable and unchanging, while nature's judgments are changeable. Reflect on the implications.
Look for the contrast: 'ليس له زوال ولا تغير' vs. 'هو في حد الانقلاب والتغير'.
3
Examine the examples
Consider the examples of birds/beasts and human aversion to killing. How do they show that nature can be trained to accept what it initially rejects?
This illustrates the possibility of moral improvement through discipline (riyāḍa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint

A) They are changeable through discipline
B) They are stable and unchanging

Key Takeaway
The root of error is nature's opposition to the intellect; the intellect's moral judgment is stable, while nature's can be reformed through discipline.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson teaches that nature's judgments can change through perseverance. What is one deeply ingrained habit—something you have excused as 'just how I am'—that you now see must be reformed? Name it, and commit to a small daily practice to begin the change.
?
If the intellect's perception of good and evil increases with awareness, how intentional are you about feeding your intellect? When was the last time you truly studied a source like this to sharpen your moral reasoning—not just for knowledge, but to transform your judgments? What will you study next?

Rational Judgments
intermediate

The Conflict Between Natural Disposition and Intellect in Moral Judgment
Maturidi contrasts the mutable judgments of natural disposition with the stable judgments of intellect, establishing intellect as the moral arbiter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that intellect, not natural disposition, is the authoritative judge of good and evil, and how this grounds objective morality.

Core Idea
Intellect perceives unchanging reality; disposition only sees fleeting pleasure and pain.

How do we determine what is truly good or evil? Our natural instincts often pull us toward pleasure and away from pain, but does that make pleasure good and pain evil? Imam Maturidi delves into this question, explaining that while our dispositions (ṭabʿ) incline us to certain judgments, they are unstable and changeable. The intellect (ʿaql), on the other hand, perceives the unchanging realities of good and evil. In this passage, Maturidi establishes that intellect must be the criterion for moral judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read Maturidi's argument carefully, noting the contrast between disposition and intellect.
The passage is dense; read it twice if needed.
2
Identify the main claims
List the key points Maturidi makes about disposition and intellect.
Look for terms like 'stable' and 'changing'.
3
Understand the examples
Examine the examples of birds/beasts and human aversion to killing—they show how disposition can be trained.
Notice how training makes the disposition accept what it naturally flees.
4
Grasp the conclusion
See why intellect is the proof (ḥujja) and not disposition.
Intellect sees both present and absent, revealing true reality.
5
Reflect on the implication
How does this affect understanding of muḥkam and mutashābih?
Confusion arises when disposition overrides intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل ذلك أن الله تعالى خلق البشر على طبائع تميل إلى الملاذ الحاضرة وتدعو صاحبها إليه، وتزينها في عينه بما ركب فيه من الشهوات إلى ما إليه مثل طبعه، وهي تنفر عما فيه ألمه وتعب، فيصير طبعه أحد أعداء عقله في التحسين والتقبيح، وإن كان ما حسنه العقل وقبحه ليس له زوال ولا تغير من حال إلى حال، وما حسنته الطبيعة وقبحته هو في حد الانقلاب والتغير من حال إلى حال بالرياضة والقيام على ذلك بالكف عما ألفه، والصرف إلى ما ينفر عنه يحسن القيام عليه، على ما يحتمل الطبع قبوله نحو المعروف من أمر الطيور والبهائم، إنها بطبعها تنفر عما أريد بها من أنواع منافع البشر، ثم يحسن قيام أهل البصر بذلك لصير طبع عليه بالميل إليه كالمستوحش، ومما طبع على النفار عنه كالمطبوع عليه. وعلى ذلك أمر نفار الطبع عن القتل والذبح في البشر، ثم سهولة ذلك عليه في الحيوان. وما يدرك حسنه بالعقل وقبحه، فلا يزال يزداد على ما فيه إدراكه. يبديهة الأحوال؛ ولذلك جعل الله العقول حجة، لا ميل الطباع؛ إذ أجرى قلمه على أهلها، وإن شاركوا في الطباع غيرهم ممن ليست لهم عقول سليمة، وألزم أهلها اتباع ما أراهم العقل حسنه، وإن كان في الطبع النفار واجتناب ما في العقل قبحه، وإن كان في طبيعة الجوهر قبوله، إذ العقل يرى صاحبه حقيقة ما عليه الشيء، والطبع – أعني طبع الجوهر – لا يوضح؛ ذلك طبع الجوهر لا تبصر به ولا يمثل غير الحاضر، والعقل يدرك به ما حضر وغاب، وبه يحضر على الطبع ما غاب، حتى يصير له كالشاهد مما يكرهه ويتلذذ به، وعنده تسهل المحنة وتخف مؤن الذي يكرهه الطبع. وعلى ذلك تقدير الكلام والعبارات إنها وإن كانت تختلف في الحسن والقبح على الأسماع فإنها لا تغير في الحقوق؛ إذ هي تتغير، ويجوز أن تؤدى عبارة واحدة بلسانين يكون أحدهما أحلى من الآخر، والحسن لنفسه والحق لا يختلف لاختلاف المعبرين، فلهذا لم يقدر حسن الأشياء بطبع الخلقة ولا بحسن العبارة، وإنما قدر بالعقل الذي لا يرى الحسن قبيحا. وهو الأصل الذي يلزم تسوية كل أمر من الأمور عليه، وذلك كعلم العيان الذي لا يحتمل التغير، ولا يناقضه جهل، فيكون هو أصلا لكل خفي مستور، وكذلك أمر العقل وما أراه أصل لكل أمر مطبوع. ولما بينا من مخالفة الطبائع في التزيين المعقول وفي التقبيح تعذر على كثير من الخلق إدراك ما أراهم العقل والطبع، فصار بذلك المحكم عندهم في صورة المتشابه، والمتشابه في صورة المحكم، وهكذا أريد درك كل شيء بغير سبيله، فنسأل الله أن يعصمنا عن رؤية الباطل بصورة الحق، والحق بصورة الباطل، فإنه قوي مدبر قدير.
The foundation of that is that God the Exalted created human beings with dispositions that incline toward immediate pleasures and call their possessor to them, and adorn them in his eye with what He placed in him of desires toward that which is like his nature, and they flee from what involves pain and fatigue. So his disposition becomes one of the enemies of his intellect in deeming good and bad, even though what the intellect deems good or bad has no disappearance or change from one state to another, while what nature deems good or bad is subject to transformation and change from state to state through discipline and undertaking that by refraining from what one is accustomed to and turning to what one is averse to, such that one can undertake it, according to what the disposition can accept, like the known matter of birds and beasts: they naturally flee from the types of human benefits intended for them, then the experts succeed in making them accustomed to it, until it becomes as natural as affection for what was feared, and aversion for what is natural. Similarly is the matter of the disposition's aversion to killing and slaughter in humans, then its ease regarding animals. And what is perceived as good or bad by the intellect increases in its perception. By the nature of states, therefore God made intellects a proof, not the inclination of dispositions, for He has written His decree upon their possessors, even though they share dispositions with others who do not have sound intellects. And He obligated their possessors to follow what the intellect shows them as good, even if the disposition is averse, and to avoid what the intellect deems bad, even if the nature of the substance accepts it. For the intellect sees for its possessor the reality of what the thing is, while the disposition—I mean the disposition of the substance—does not clarify; that is because the disposition of the substance does not see nor represent anything other than the present. The intellect perceives what is present and absent, and by it, the absent is brought to the disposition until it becomes like a witness for it of what it dislikes and delights in. With the intellect, hardship becomes easy and the burden of what the disposition dislikes is lightened. Similarly, the estimation of speech and expressions: even if they differ in beauty and ugliness to the ears, they do not change the truths, for they vary, and it is possible for one expression to be delivered in two tongues, one sweeter than the other. Beauty is in itself, and truth does not differ due to the difference of those who express. Therefore, the beauty of things was not determined by the nature of creation nor by the beauty of expression, but rather by the intellect that does not see good as ugly. It is the principle that must govern every matter, like direct knowledge that does not admit change and is not contradicted by ignorance, so it becomes a foundation for every hidden and concealed thing. Similarly, the matter of the intellect and what it shows is a foundation for every conditioned matter. Since we have explained the opposition of dispositions in adorning what is intellectually beautiful and in deeming ugly, it became difficult for many creatures to perceive what the intellect and disposition show them. Thus, the clear (muḥkam) came to appear to them in the form of the ambiguous (mutashābih), and the ambiguous in the form of the clear. Thus, every thing is sought to be grasped by other than its proper way. So we ask God to protect us from seeing falsehood in the form of truth and truth in the form of falsehood, for He is Strong, Disposer, Almighty.
Source pages p. 822

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of the conflict between natural disposition (ṭabʿ) and intellect (ʿaql) in judging good and evil. Maturidi begins by stating that God created humans with dispositions inclined toward immediate pleasures and averse to pain, making the disposition an enemy of the intellect in moral judgment. He notes that intellectual judgments of good and evil are stable and unchanging, while natural judgments are mutable through discipline and habit. He gives examples from nature (birds, beasts, human aversion to killing then ease with animals) to show how disposition can be trained. Maturidi then emphasizes that God made intellects the proof (ḥujja), not inclinations. Even though humans share dispositions with those lacking sound intellects, the intellect is obligated to follow what it deems good despite dispositional aversion. The intellect perceives reality of things, while the disposition only sees the present; the intellect brings the absent to the disposition, making judgment easier. He extends this to speech and expressions: their aesthetic variation does not affect the truth of what is expressed; beauty and truth are independent of expression. Therefore, the measure of good and evil is the intellect, which is stable like direct knowledge (ʿilm al-ʿiyān). Because of the opposition between disposition and intellect, many people fail to see what both show them, causing them to mistake muḥkam for mutashābih and vice versa. Maturidi concludes with a prayer for protection from confusion.

Key Terms
طبع (ṭabʿ/ disposition)
عقل (ʿaql/ intellect)
تحسين وتقبيح (taḥsīn wa taqbīḥ/ deeming good and bad)
حجة (ḥujja/ proof)
علم العيان (ʿilm al-ʿiyān/ direct knowledge)
محكم (muḥkam)
متشابه (mutashābih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good and evil are determined by natural disposition or societal convention.
Correction: Maturidi argues that good and evil are objective and known by the intellect, which is unchanging and perceives true reality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why did God make intellects a proof (ḥujja) rather than the inclination of dispositions?
A) Because dispositions are always evil
B) Because intellect sees the reality of things, both present and absent, while disposition only sees the present
C) Because dispositions cannot be trained
D) Because intellect is the same as disposition

Key Takeaway
Intellect, not natural disposition, is the reliable arbiter of good and evil because it perceives unchanging reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when your natural aversion (e.g., fear, discomfort) stopped you from doing something your intellect told you was right. How did you resolve that conflict? If you didn't, what would it take to follow your intellect now?
?
Maturidi says the intellect brings the absent reward to the disposition, making hardship easy. What is one difficulty you are currently facing where you have not yet brought the long-term benefit to mind? Will you take a minute to vividly imagine that reward and see if your disposition softens?
?
The passage warns that many people see falsehood as truth and truth as falsehood. In what area of your life do you suspect you might be vulnerable to this confusion—perhaps an opinion you hold strongly but have not critically examined using your intellect?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
5 min
Refutation of Deniers of Necessary Knowledge
Maturidi dismisses those who object by denying necessary and self-evident knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that those who deny necessary and self-evident knowledge cannot be engaged in rational debate, and why such debate is pointless.

Core Idea
Denying self-evident truth makes rational argument impossible.

In this brief but powerful passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a group who raised objections based on supposed similarities in divine attributes. He quickly dismisses their objections by pointing out that they have rejected the very foundations of knowledge—necessary and self-evident truths. Without that common ground, no meaningful debate can occur. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by a dismissal.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فى حد العيان' (in the realm of the self-evident).
2
Identify the conditional logic
Maturidi says that the objection would be necessary (لازم) if there were an intellect capable of perception. Identify what the condition is and what it implies.
The condition is not met, because the objectors deny necessary knowledge.
3
Understand the two types of knowledge rejected
The objectors deny 'علم الضروريات' (necessary knowledge) and 'ما هو فى حد العيان' (what is self-evident). These are the bases of rational discourse.
Necessary knowledge is that which the mind must accept, like that something exists. Self-evident knowledge is directly perceived.
4
Draw the conclusion about debate
Since they reject these fundamentals, Maturidi says 'لا معنى لمناظرتهم' (there is no point in debating them). Conclude why this is a valid dismissal.
Debate requires shared premises; they have none.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الناس من عارضهم عند ظنهم وقوع التشابه بالعلم والوجود والكون وغير ذلك ، وذلك لازم لو كان ثمة عقل يحتمل الإدراك ، ولكنهم قوم أنكروا علم الضروريات وما هو فى حد العيان ، فلا معنى لمناظرتهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And among the people are those who objected to them based on their assumption of similarity occurring in knowledge, existence, and being, and other such things. And that would be necessary if there were an intellect capable of perception. But they are a people who denied necessary knowledge and what is in the realm of the self-evident. So there is no point in debating with them. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 826

Commentary
This passage identifies a group of objectors who raised objections based on alleged similarities in attributes like knowledge, existence, and being. Maturidi responds that their objection would be cogent only if there existed an intellect capable of such perception. However, these people have denied necessary knowledge (ʻilm al-ḍarūriyyāt) and what is self-evident (mā huwa fī ḥadd al-ʻiyān). Since they reject the very foundations of rational discourse, debating with them is pointless. The phrase 'there is no power except with Allah' is a formula of reliance on God.

Key Terms
علم الضروريات (necessary knowledge)
حد العيان (realm of the self-evident)
مناظرة (debate/argumentation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is dismissing all opponents who raise objections about divine attributes.
Correction: Maturidi is specifically dismissing those who deny necessary and self-evident knowledge. Other opponents who accept rational foundations may be debated validly.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say there is no point in debating the objectors mentioned?
A) They have no intellect.
B) They deny necessary and self-evident knowledge, the basis of rational discourse.
C) They are too many.
D) They only object about trivial matters.

Key Takeaway
Debating with those who deny self-evident truths is futile because they lack the common ground necessary for rational discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever argued with someone who rejected a basic truth you thought was obvious? Did your argument change anything? What does this lesson reveal about the foundation of productive discussion?
?
Are there any self-evident truths about Allah or His attributes that you subtly suppress because they challenge your comfort or convenience? How will you confront that today?
?
When you face opposition to your faith, do you check first whether you and the other person share any common ground? If not, what futile debates have you engaged in?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
Proof from Good and Bad in Actions
Maturidi argues that ignorance of goodness/badness proves divine origin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the fact that humans are ignorant of the true extent of good and bad in their actions to prove that these moral qualities must originate from God.

Core Idea
True goodness and badness of actions are beyond human knowledge, so they must come from God.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a subtle argument based on the moral qualities (husn and qubh) that we observe in actions. He notes that while actions appear good or bad, the doer often does not know the full extent of that quality. This ignorance, he argues, shows that the moral quality cannot originate from the human agent, but must come from a higher source—God. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key observation
Maturidi observes that actions emerge with goodness or badness, but the doer does not know that they reach that state. The doer's own perception of beautifying the action may differ from reality.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since humans are ignorant of the true extent of goodness and badness, the action cannot be from themselves. Ignorance does not make an action bad, nor knowledge make it good.
4
Consider the objection and rebuttal
An objector might say that actions are inherently good or bad. Maturidi rebuts that if an action itself has moral quality, then God has more right to that quality, since the thing itself is ignorant.
The phrase 'ignorant of what it is upon' is key.
5
Reflect on the final argument
If moral qualities could exist without an originator, then anything could exist without an originator, which leads to atheism—a departure from Islam.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا نجد أفعال العباد تخرج على حسن وقبح ، لا يعلم أهلها أنها تبلغ كونها على ذلك ، وهي في الحسن ذلك ولا في القبح ، بل هم عندهم نفسهم في تحسينها وتزيينها تخرج على غير ذلك ، بأن جعل أفعالهم على ما هي عليه ليست لهم ، ولو جاز لهم، وهم لا يعرفون مبلغ الحسن والقبح ، فاذا لا جهل يقبح الفعل ولا علم يحسنه ، فثبت أن فعلهم من هذا الوجه ليس لهم . اللهم إلا أن يقولوا : هي لأنفسها كانت كذلك ، فإذا استقام حسن الفعل وقبحه لأتمرا له الفعل نفسه ، فالله تعالى به أحق من الشيء من نفسه ؛ إذ الشيء بحيث نفسه جاهل بما عليه . ما لو جاز كون حسن وقبح بلا منشى له الجاز كون كل شيء بلا وفي ذلك الخروج من الإسلام ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also we find that the actions of servants emerge upon goodness and badness, while their doers do not know that they reach that state, and they are in that goodness or that badness, but rather they themselves, in their own view, in their beautifying and adorning them, emerge upon other than that, because their actions are made as they are, not theirs. And if it were permissible for them—while they do not know the extent of goodness and badness—then ignorance does not make an action bad, nor knowledge makes it good. So it is established that their action from this aspect is not theirs. Unless they say: they were like that in themselves. So if goodness and badness of an action are established, then the action itself would be commanded, but God has more right to it than the thing itself, since the thing, by its own self, is ignorant of what it is upon. If it were permissible for goodness and badness to exist without an originator, then it would be permissible for everything to exist without [originator], and in that is departure from Islam, and there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 829

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument: actions have intrinsic moral qualities (husn and qubh) that the doer does not fully comprehend. Maturidi observes that people often beautify their actions in their own minds, but the actual goodness or badness may be different. Since they do not know the true extent of these qualities, their own judgment cannot be the source; therefore, the action's moral quality must come from an external source—God. If someone claims that the action itself inherently possesses goodness or badness without an originator, then anything could exist without a creator, which leads to atheism. Thus, the existence of moral qualities in actions points to a divine origin.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
فعل
منشأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can independently determine the goodness or badness of their actions without divine guidance.
Correction: Maturidi argues that humans are ignorant of the true extent of moral qualities, so these qualities cannot originate from human judgment; they must come from God.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that people do not know the true goodness or badness of their actions?
A) That actions have no inherent goodness or badness
B) That the moral quality of actions must come from God
C) That people are always correct in judging their actions
D) That actions are neutral and only God's command makes them good or bad

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses human ignorance of moral qualities to prove that these qualities must originate from God, not from the actions themselves or human judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge an action of yours as good or bad, are you confident that your judgment aligns with God's? How does Maturidi's point about your ignorance of the full moral measure affect your willingness to assume your actions are righteous? Will you now pause before assuming your deeds are good and seek God's guidance through revelation?
?
Think of one action you are proud of today. Maturidi says you do not know its true moral status. Does this humble you? How will this shape your attitude toward seeking forgiveness and relying on God's mercy rather than your own sense of rightness?
?
If you had to admit that you cannot fully know whether your actions are truly good or bad, how would that change the way you approach your daily decisions? What steps will you take to ensure that your moral compass is aligned with God's revealed standards rather than your own assumptions?

The Necessity of Qiyas for Knowing God's Creation
Maturidi argues for the necessity of qiyas in proving God's creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that qiyas (analogy) is necessary for proving that God creates all actions, and how he uses the Qur'anic verse 'Creator of everything' to support this.

Core Idea
Without qiyas, we cannot know the Creator; qiyas bridges the seen and unseen.

Maturidi begins by establishing that reasoning from the seen world (al-shahid) is essential for knowledge of the unseen. He argues that if we reject analogy (qiyas), we cannot prove the existence of the Creator because we cannot perceive Him directly. Therefore, qiyas must be used to infer that the meanings found in creatures' actions are also created by God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two options for qiyas
Read the first line carefully: qiyas is either used in this discussion or not. This sets up a conditional argument.
Notice the structure: 'either ... or ...'.
2
Understand the consequence of rejecting qiyas
If qiyas is not used, the opponents' method fails because God is not perceivable by senses. Therefore, qiyas becomes obligatory.
Maturidi is refuting those who rely solely on sensation.
3
See how the Qur'an and servant's non-creatorship prove the point
The verse 'Creator of everything' and the fact that a servant does not become a creator by his action together establish that God creates all actions.
This argument uses both reason and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن القياس ثما لا يخلو من أن يكون مستعملا فيما نحن فيه أو لا، فإن كان لا يستعمل بطل مذهب الخصوم في معرفة الصانع لارتفاع الحواس عنه، فيجب معرفته بذلك، وهو على الاستدلال بالشاهد بم تجب جميع المعاني التي هي للعالم بأعراضه موجودة في أفعال الخلق، فلئن لم يجب القول بخلقها لم يجز معرفة خلق البتة إلا بالسمع، فيجب به استعمال العموم بقوله: "خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ"؛ إذ لا سبيل إلى وجود خلق شيء باسم الخاصية له، أو يلزم القول بالقياس من الوجه الذي ذكر، ثم لم يصر العبد بفعله خالقا، ثبت أنه بغيره. مع ما إذ كان سبيل معرفة الفاعل إنما هو بأثار الفعل، ثم كان الإيمان من أحسن الأفعال في العقول وأنور الأشياء وأتمها وأجلها قدرا وأبينها لمرضاته، فلو قلنا: إن الله غير خالق له دخل علينا أمران في ذلك: أحدهما تفضيل من يطيع الله بالايمان وغيره على الله بما خلق من الأقذار والأنتان والخبائث والقبائح من الجواهر، مع ما كان ما حسن من الجواهر لا يبلغ قدر الذي ذكر من العبادات في الحسن والخير وإذا كان كذلك، ومعلوم تفاضل الفاضلين بتفاضل أفعالهم أوجب ذلك فضل العبد على الله في الفعل والخلق، وهذا بالمعتزلة أولى؛ لأنهم زعموا أن فعل الكفر قبيح شر من جميع الوجوه، وليس كذلك أمر القردة والخنازير، فمثله فعل الإيمان من جميع جواهر الحسان، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also that analogy (al-qiyas) is not free from being used in what we are discussing or not. If it is not used, then the doctrine of the opponents concerning knowledge of the Maker becomes invalid due to the absence of sensory perception of Him, so knowledge of Him is obligatory by that (analogy). And it is based on inference from the witnessed (al-shahid) that all the meanings that belong to the world with its accidents are present in the actions of creatures. For if the statement of their creation is not necessary, then knowledge of creation at all is not permissible except through revelation (al-samʿ). So it is necessary to use the general statement by His saying: 'Creator of everything' (Qur'an 6:102), since there is no way to the existence of the creation of something by its specific name, or the statement of analogy from the aspect mentioned is necessary. Then, since the servant does not become a creator by his action, it is established that it is by another (i.e., God). Moreover, when the way of knowing an agent is only through the traces of the act, and then faith (al-iman) is among the best of actions in the intellects, and the most luminous of things, the most complete, the greatest in worth, and the clearest for His pleasure, if we say: God is not its creator, then two things enter upon us in that. One of them is preferring one who obeys God through faith and other things over God, because God created filth, stench, wickedness, and ugly things among jewels, while what is beautiful among jewels does not reach the worth of what was mentioned of acts of worship in beauty and goodness. And if it is so, and it is known that the superiority of two excellent ones is by the superiority of their actions, that necessitates the superiority of the servant over God in action and creation. And this applies more to the Mu'tazila, because they claimed that the act of unbelief (kufr) is ugly and evil in all respects, but the matter of apes and pigs is not so. So the same applies to the act of faith from among all beautiful jewels. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 833

Commentary
This is a multi-part argument. First, Maturidi discusses the necessity of using qiyas (analogy) in proving the existence of God and the creation of actions. If qiyas is not used, then the opponents' method of knowing the Maker fails because we cannot perceive Him with senses. Therefore, we must use qiyas. Then he argues that the same attributes (meanings) found in the world are also present in the actions of creatures. If we do not admit that God creates those actions, then we can know creation only through revelation. But the Quran states 'Khaliq kulli shay'' (Creator of everything), so we must use this general text. Moreover, the servant does not become a creator by his action, so it is established that God creates them. Then he presents a second argument: the way to know an agent is through the traces of the act. Faith (iman) is the best, most luminous, most complete, and clearest action. If God is not its creator, then two problems arise. One: we would be preferring the obedient believer over God, because God created filthy things like filth and stench, while faith is superior. Two: since the superiority of beings is by their actions, this would mean the servant is superior to God in action and creation. This is particularly a problem for the Mu'tazila, who claim that unbelief is ugly in all respects, but ugly things like apes and pigs exist. By analogy, the act of faith is among the beautiful things, so it must be created by God. Otherwise, the absurdity remains.

Key Terms
قياس
شاهد
سمع
خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies human responsibility for actions.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying human acquisition (kasb) but affirming that God is the ultimate creator of all actions, including human acts. Human responsibility is maintained through the concept of acquisition.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if qiyas (analogy) were not used in proving the existence of the Maker?
A) Knowledge of the Maker would still be possible through sense perception.
B) The doctrine of the opponents concerning knowledge of the Maker would become invalid.
C) The Qur'an would be unnecessary.
D) The servant would become a creator.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that without qiyas, we cannot know God as Creator of actions; the Qur'an and the fact that creatures are not creators confirm that God creates all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had to prove that God is the Creator of every action in your life, and you could not use either qiyas or revelation, how would you convince yourself? After reading this lesson, do you see the necessity of both reason and scripture in your own conviction, or do you rely on one more than the other?
?
Do you ever attribute your own successes or failures to your own ability, forgetting that the very capacity to act is created by God? The source shows that a servant does not become a creator by his action. Where in your life today do you need to consciously remind yourself that God is the true Creator of your actions, not just in theory but in the moment?
?
When you face a situation where human reasoning seems insufficient to understand God's decree, do you fall back on the general statement 'Creator of everything' to settle your heart? Or do you doubt, thinking you must know the specific reason? This lesson declares that without qiyas we cannot know the Maker. How does that challenge you to trust both your God-given reason and the revealed word more deeply?

Rational Judgments

6 min
Refuting the Mu'tazila: The Absurdity of Denying God's Creation of Faith
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing the absurdity if God is not creator of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if God were not the creator of faith, it would lead to the absurd conclusion that a believer is superior to God in action, which particularly refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
Denying God's creation of faith puts a creature above the Creator.

Maturidi now shifts to a reductio ad absurdum argument against those who deny that God creates all actions, especially faith. Since faith is the best and most luminous action, if God did not create it, then the believer would be superior to God in creating good actions, because God created only filthy things in contrast. This absurdity is especially damaging to the Mu'tazila, who claim that unbelief is inherently ugly, yet they fail to see that by analogy, faith must be created by God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise: knowing an agent by his acts
Maturidi begins by stating that the way to know an agent is through the traces (effects) of his actions. This is a basic epistemological principle.
This principle is used to evaluate actions.
2
Identify the two absurdities if God is not creator of faith
If God is not the creator of faith, two things follow: (1) preferring the believer over God, because God created filth while faith is superior, and (2) the servant would be superior to God in action and creation.
Maturidi explicitly says 'دخل علينا أمران' (two things enter upon us).
3
Connect to the Mu'tazilite position
This conclusion applies especially to the Mu'tazila because they claim unbelief is ugly in all respects, yet ugly things like apes and pigs exist. By analogy, faith is beautiful and must be created by God.
Maturidi uses a comparison to highlight the inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن القياس ثما لا يخلو من أن يكون مستعملا فيما نحن فيه أو لا، فإن كان لا يستعمل بطل مذهب الخصوم في معرفة الصانع لارتفاع الحواس عنه، فيجب معرفته بذلك، وهو على الاستدلال بالشاهد بم تجب جميع المعاني التي هي للعالم بأعراضه موجودة في أفعال الخلق، فلئن لم يجب القول بخلقها لم يجز معرفة خلق البتة إلا بالسمع، فيجب به استعمال العموم بقوله: "خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ"؛ إذ لا سبيل إلى وجود خلق شيء باسم الخاصية له، أو يلزم القول بالقياس من الوجه الذي ذكر، ثم لم يصر العبد بفعله خالقا، ثبت أنه بغيره. مع ما إذ كان سبيل معرفة الفاعل إنما هو بأثار الفعل، ثم كان الإيمان من أحسن الأفعال في العقول وأنور الأشياء وأتمها وأجلها قدرا وأبينها لمرضاته، فلو قلنا: إن الله غير خالق له دخل علينا أمران في ذلك: أحدهما تفضيل من يطيع الله بالايمان وغيره على الله بما خلق من الأقذار والأنتان والخبائث والقبائح من الجواهر، مع ما كان ما حسن من الجواهر لا يبلغ قدر الذي ذكر من العبادات في الحسن والخير وإذا كان كذلك، ومعلوم تفاضل الفاضلين بتفاضل أفعالهم أوجب ذلك فضل العبد على الله في الفعل والخلق، وهذا بالمعتزلة أولى؛ لأنهم زعموا أن فعل الكفر قبيح شر من جميع الوجوه، وليس كذلك أمر القردة والخنازير، فمثله فعل الإيمان من جميع جواهر الحسان، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also that analogy (al-qiyas) is not free from being used in what we are discussing or not. If it is not used, then the doctrine of the opponents concerning knowledge of the Maker becomes invalid due to the absence of sensory perception of Him, so knowledge of Him is obligatory by that (analogy). And it is based on inference from the witnessed (al-shahid) that all the meanings that belong to the world with its accidents are present in the actions of creatures. For if the statement of their creation is not necessary, then knowledge of creation at all is not permissible except through revelation (al-samʿ). So it is necessary to use the general statement by His saying: 'Creator of everything' (Qur'an 6:102), since there is no way to the existence of the creation of something by its specific name, or the statement of analogy from the aspect mentioned is necessary. Then, since the servant does not become a creator by his action, it is established that it is by another (i.e., God). Moreover, when the way of knowing an agent is only through the traces of the act, and then faith (al-iman) is among the best of actions in the intellects, and the most luminous of things, the most complete, the greatest in worth, and the clearest for His pleasure, if we say: God is not its creator, then two things enter upon us in that. One of them is preferring one who obeys God through faith and other things over God, because God created filth, stench, wickedness, and ugly things among jewels, while what is beautiful among jewels does not reach the worth of what was mentioned of acts of worship in beauty and goodness. And if it is so, and it is known that the superiority of two excellent ones is by the superiority of their actions, that necessitates the superiority of the servant over God in action and creation. And this applies more to the Mu'tazila, because they claimed that the act of unbelief (kufr) is ugly and evil in all respects, but the matter of apes and pigs is not so. So the same applies to the act of faith from among all beautiful jewels. And there is no power except with God.

Commentary
This is a multi-part argument. First, Maturidi discusses the necessity of using qiyas (analogy) in proving the existence of God and the creation of actions. If qiyas is not used, then the opponents' method of knowing the Maker fails because we cannot perceive Him with senses. Therefore, we must use qiyas. Then he argues that the same attributes (meanings) found in the world are also present in the actions of creatures. If we do not admit that God creates those actions, then we can know creation only through revelation. But the Quran states 'Khaliq kulli shay'' (Creator of everything), so we must use this general text. Moreover, the servant does not become a creator by his action, so it is established that God creates them. Then he presents a second argument: the way to know an agent is through the traces of the act. Faith (iman) is the best, most luminous, most complete, and clearest action. If God is not its creator, then two problems arise. One: we would be preferring the obedient believer over God, because God created filthy things like filth and stench, while faith is superior. Two: since the superiority of beings is by their actions, this would mean the servant is superior to God in action and creation. By analogy, the act of faith is among the beautiful things, so it must be created by God. Otherwise, the absurdity remains.

Key Terms
إيمان
معتزلة
تفضيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies human responsibility for faith.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying human acquisition of faith; he is arguing that God's creation of faith is necessary to avoid the absurdity that a creature is superior to the Creator.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim would follow if God were not the creator of faith?
A) Faith would be impossible.
B) The believer would be superior to God in action and creation.
C) God would be unjust.
D) The Mu'tazila would be correct.

Key Takeaway
To avoid the absurdity of a creature being superior to the Creator, we must affirm that God creates faith, as He creates all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of obedience, do you ever feel a sense of pride or self-sufficiency? How does the argument that your faith is created by God, not by you, challenge that feeling?
?
If you truly believed that every good action of yours is created by God, how would that change the way you view others who struggle with sin? Would you still consider yourself superior?
?
The Mu'tazila denied God's creation of faith to protect human freedom, but this led to elevating the servant above the Creator. Are there areas in your life where you unconsciously prioritize your own autonomy over acknowledging God's absolute control?

Proof by qiyas that God creates all actions
Maturidi uses analogical reasoning to prove God creates all actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses analogical reasoning (qiyas) to prove that God is the Creator of all actions.

Core Idea
Qiyas from the witnessed world shows that all actions are created by God, not by the servant.

This passage presents Maturidi's argument that if qiyas is not used, the opponent's method fails. Starting from the witnessed world (shahid), we see that all meanings found in creation exist in human actions. Therefore, either we accept that God creates all actions (as per the Qur'an), or we resort to qiyas. The conclusion is that the servant does not become a creator by his action, so the action is created by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a conditional structure: if qiyas is not used, then another problem arises.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The passage begins with a conditional: qiyas is either used in this matter or not. Follow both branches to see where they lead.
The first branch (not using qiyas) leads to the opponent's method being invalid. The second branch (using qiyas) leads to the conclusion.
3
Understand the inference from the witnessed world
Maturidi argues that all the meanings that exist in the world's accidents also exist in human actions. This supports the idea that actions are created, just like the world.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The argument ends with the servant not becoming a creator by his action, so it is established that the action is created by God. This is the key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن القياس ثما لا يخلو من أن يكون مستعملا فيما نحن فيه أو لا، فإن كان لا يستعمل بطل مذهب الخصوم فى معرفة الصانع لارتفاع الحواس عنه، فيجب معرفته بذلك، وهو على الاستدلال بالشاهد بم تجب جميع المعاني التي هي للعالم بأعراضه موجودة في أفعال الخلق، فلئن لم يجب القول بخلقها لم يجز معرفة خلق البتة إلا بالسمع، فيجب به استعمال العموم بقوله: "خالق كل شيء" إذ لا سبيل إلى وجود خلق شيء باسم الخاصية له، أو يلزم القول بالقياس من الوجه الذي ذكر، ثم لم يصر العبد بفعله خالقا، ثبت أنه بغيره.
Also, qiyas (analogical reasoning) is not free from being either used in what we are discussing or not. If it is not used, the doctrine of the opponents in knowing the Maker becomes invalid because senses cannot reach Him, so knowledge of Him is necessary by that [reasoning]. And it is based on inference from the witnessed (shahid) that all the meanings which belong to the world with its accidents exist in the actions of creatures. So if the statement that they are created is not required, then knowing creation at all is not permissible except through revelation (sam'). So it is necessary to use the general [verse] by His saying: 'Creator of everything' (Qur'an 6:102), since there is no way to know the creation of something by the name of its specific property, or the statement by qiyas from the mentioned aspect is required. Then, since the servant does not become a creator by his action, it is established that it is by another.
Source pages p. 833

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by qiyas (analogical reasoning) to demonstrate that God is the Creator of all actions. The author begins by stating that qiyas must either be applicable to this issue or not. If it is not applicable, then the opponent's method for knowing the Creator (i.e., by inference from the witnessed world) collapses because the senses cannot perceive God directly. Therefore, they must rely on some form of reasoning. The author then argues that inference from the witnessed world shows that all the attributes and meanings found in created things exist in the actions of creatures. Consequently, either we must admit that these actions are created by God (since the Qur'an declares Him Creator of everything), or we must resort to qiyas in a way that does not make the servant a creator. The conclusion is that since the servant does not become a creator by his action, it is established that the creation of the action is by God, not by the servant.

Key Terms
القياس (qiyas)
الصانع (the Maker)
الشاهد (the witnessed)
السمع (revelation)
خلق (creation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qiyas is used here to prove the existence of God rather than to prove God's creation of actions.
Correction: The passage specifically uses qiyas to argue that all actions are created by God, not to prove God's existence. The conclusion is about creation, not existence.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion Maturidi draws from the qiyas argument in this passage?
A) The servant is a creator of his actions
B) God is the Creator of all actions
C) Qiyas is invalid in theological matters
D) The opponents have no method to know God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's qiyas proof establishes that God is the Creator of all actions, as the servant does not become a creator by his action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Impossibility of Human Creation: Objection and Response
Al-Maturidi raises a self-objection and responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's argument that if humans cannot create bodies, they cannot truly create accidents either, since creation means bringing something from non-existence to existence.

Core Idea
Creating means bringing from nothing—something humans cannot do.

Al-Maturidi anticipates a potential objection: if humans can create accidents, why can't they create bodies? He responds by clarifying the very meaning of creation—it is bringing something from non-existence into existence. Since both accidents and bodies would require the same definition, the objection actually exposes the inconsistency in claiming humans create anything at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the rhetorical structure: a question followed by an answer.
The answer begins with 'Abu Mansur said:'.
2
Identify the objection and response
The objection is that if humans can create accidents, they should be able to create bodies. The response is that the definition of creation is the same: emergence from non-existence.
The author says the objection is not properly formulated.
3
Understand the logical consistency
Al-Maturidi argues that since humans cannot create bodies, they cannot truly create accidents either. True creation belongs only to Allah.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' underscores dependence on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بأنه إذا قدرتم على إخراج الأعراض من العدم إلى الوجود لم لا جاز أن يقدروا على ذلك في الجسم ؟ قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وليس هذا تقدير السؤال ، ولكن بما ليس معنى خلق الجسم إلا خروجه من العدم ووجوده بعد أن لم يكن ، وبه وصفتم أنفسكم في فعل الأعراض ، كيف لا جاز الوصف بخلق الجسم وليس ثمة غير ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he objected to himself: If you are able to bring accidents from non-existence into existence, why is it not permissible that they are able to do that in the body? Abu Mansur said: This is not a proper formulation of the question, but rather because the meaning of creating the body is nothing other than its emergence from non-existence and its existence after it was not, and by this you have described yourselves in the act of accidents, how is it not permissible to describe creation of the body when there is nothing else? And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 851

Commentary
This passage presents a self-objection: if humans can create accidents (like colors or motions), why can they not create a body? The author responds that the definition of creating a body is exactly the same as bringing it from nonexistence to existence, which is what you claim for yourselves in accidents. So if you can do that for accidents, you should be able to do it for bodies as well. But since you cannot, it reveals the inconsistency and shows that true creation belongs to God alone.

Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that the definition of creation (bringing from non-existence) is identical for both. If the definition is the same, then if humans could truly create accidents, they would also be able to create bodies. Since they cannot, they do not truly create either.

Key Takeaway

Power Is Simultaneous with Action
Al-Maturidi argues from common speech and accident metaphysics that power is simultaneous with action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Maturidi's two-part argument that human power (qudra) must exist at the same time as the action it enables, based on everyday language and the nature of accidents.

Core Idea
Power is an accident; accidents do not endure, so power must be simultaneous with action.

This lesson presents Al-Maturidi's reasoning for why human power (قدرة) cannot precede the action it enables. He begins with everyday language—people say 'I cannot' to excuse themselves, revealing that power can be absent. Then he provides a deeper philosophical proof: power is an accident, and accidents cannot persist over time. This leads to the conclusion that power exists only at the moment of action.

Guided Path
1
Observe the linguistic evidence
Read the opening where Al-Maturidi cites common speech patterns ('I cannot due to preoccupation') and notice what they imply about the presence or absence of power.
2
Examine the philosophical proof
Study the argument that power is an accident (عرض) and that accidents do not endure (لا تبقى) because they cannot subsist by themselves.
This is a key metaphysical principle in kalam.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand why the non-endurance of accidents forces power to be simultaneous with the action, and how the closing formula 'There is no power except with God' reinforces the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم وجود القول ظاهر في الخلق : لا أقدر لشغلى بكذا ، أو لا أستطيع بنقل هذا على ولم يجز أن يكون الله ينطق ألسن الخلق على غير تمانع منهم بما هو كذب في الحقيقة ، وهم يعلمون أن استطاعة الأسباب والأحوال فثبت أن وراء ذلك عندهم قدرة يذكرونها مع الاعتذار فى الأفعال لا فى الجمل التي ترجع الأوهام إلى الأحوال ، ولا قوة إلا بالله والنظر في ذلك أن القوة إذ ليست هى من أجزاء الجسم فهى عرض في الحقيقة ، والأعراض لا تبقى ؛ إذ لا يجوز بقاء ما يحتمل الفناء إلا ببقاء هو غيره ، والعرض لا يقبل الأغيار بما لا قيام له بذاته ، ومحال بقاء الشيء ببقاء في غيره ، فبطل البقاء . ثم فساد حقيقة الأفعال بأسباب متقدمة إذا لم تكن هي وقت الفعل فمثله قوة الفعل ، فيلزم القول بالكون مع الفعل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then the existence of the saying is apparent in creation: 'I cannot due to my preoccupation with such' or 'I cannot carry this upon' and it is not permissible that God makes the tongues of the creation speak without their opposition what is actually false, and they know that the capability of causes and states, so it is established that behind that they have a power they mention with excuse in actions, not in the totalities that revert imaginations to states, and there is no power except with God. And consideration of that: that power, since it is not from the parts of the body, is an accident in reality, and accidents do not endure; for it is not permissible for something that potentially perishes to endure except by an endurance that is other than it, and the accident does not receive otherness because it has no subsistence by itself, and it is impossible for a thing to endure by an endurance in something else, so endurance is invalid. Then the corruption of the reality of actions by preceding causes if they are not at the time of the action, so likewise the power of the action, so it necessitates the statement of simultaneity with the action, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 859

Commentary
This passage is a two-part argument about the nature of power (qudra/قدرة). First, the author observes common speech: people say 'I cannot due to occupation' or 'I cannot carry this,' implying that power is sometimes absent. This shows that people understand power as something that can be present or absent. Then the author provides a philosophical analysis: power, not being a part of the body, is an accident (arad/عرض). Accidents, by their nature, do not endure (la tabqa/لا تبقى) because they cannot subsist by themselves. An accident cannot persist through an endurance that is other than itself. Therefore, power cannot exist prior to the action. If causes preceded the action, the action would be corrupted. Hence, power must be simultaneous with the action (al-kawn ma'a al-fi'l/الكون مع الفعل). The author concludes with the formula 'There is no power except with God.'

Key Terms
قدرة
قوة
عرض
بقاء
كون مع الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think power is a substance that can endure separately from action.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that power is an accident, and accidents cannot endure by themselves; therefore power must exist only at the moment of action.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of Al-Maturidi's argument about the temporal relationship between power and action?
A) Power exists before the action
B) Power exists after the action
C) Power is simultaneous with the action
D) Power is unrelated to action

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi proves that power (qudra) must be simultaneous with action because it is an accident that cannot endure, a conclusion supported by both common speech and metaphysical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I cannot' in your daily life, do you recognize that your power is actually absent at that moment, not just hidden? If power is only at the instant of action, then every time you act, you are receiving a fresh gift. How does that humble you before Allah, the true source of all power?
?
If power does not endure, then you have no stored reserve of ability. Your past successes do not guarantee future ones. Does your heart truly depend on Allah moment by moment, or do you rely on a false sense of accumulated strength? What would it mean to live each action as if your entire capacity is being created right then?
?
Al-Māturīdī uses everyday apologies—'I can't because I'm busy'—to prove that people instinctively know power is not always present. When you make excuses, are you honest about your lack of power, or do you hide behind pride? How can the reality that power is only with Allah free you from both arrogance and despair?

Why Power Cannot Exist After the Action
Al-Maturidi refutes power existing after the action as logically impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Maturidi argues that power cannot logically exist after an action is performed.

Core Idea
Power after an action is contradictory: it would be capability for the impossible.

Al-Maturidi here tackles a specific theological claim: that power (qudra) might exist after an action has taken place. Using a simple logical argument, he shows that this idea is self-contradictory. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if power were... then it would be...'.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the logical problem
Identify what Al-Maturidi means by 'power for the action after it'. He argues that if power comes after the action, it would be a capability for something one is already incapable of doing—since the action is past.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Recognise that calling this scenario 'contradictory and corrupt' (متناقض فاسد) closes the argument. The final phrase 'and there is no power except with God' reaffirms that true power belongs only to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن القوة إذ هي كانت القدرة للفعل بعدها لكانت لما هو عنه عاجز ، وذلك متناقض فاسد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also that power, if it were capability for the action after it, it would be for that which one is incapable of, and that is contradictory and corrupt, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 860

Commentary
This passage is a brief refutation of the idea that power (qudra/قدرة) exists after the action. The author argues: if power were for the action but came after it, then it would be a capability for something that the agent is already incapable of doing (since the action is past). This is contradictory (mutanaqid/متناقض) and corrupt (fasid/فاسد). Thus, power cannot exist after the action; it must be prior or simultaneous.

Key Terms
قوة
قدرة
متناقض
فاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think power can be stored or remain after an action for the same action.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that power after the action would be for something impossible, making the concept contradictory.

Checkpoint

A) Because power is an accident
B) Because it would be capability for something one cannot do
C) Because God decreed it
D) Because actions are eternal

Key Takeaway
Power after an action is logically impossible because it would require capability for what is already impossible to perform.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Power Is Known Only Through Its Effect
Al-Maturidi argues that power is known only through reason at the time of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Maturidi's argument that power (al-quwwa) is not known in itself but only through reason at the moment of action.

Core Idea
Power is only known at the time of its effect, not before.

Imam al-Maturidi presents a concise epistemological argument about power. He asserts that power cannot be known by its own essence or defined independently. Instead, its reality is made known through reason, but reason itself comes into existence only when power acts. This establishes that power is only testified to at the moment of its existence, not prior to it. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: قوة (power), حد (definition), عقل (reason), شهد (testify).
The passage is a single sentence but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the statement that power is not known by itself. Then see what exception is given: except through what God placed from reason.
3
Trace the argument's logic
Follow the relationship between power and reason: reason does not exist before power, but power brings reason into existence. Therefore, reason testifies to power only at the time of power's existence.
Think about why this means power cannot be known prior to action.
4
Reflect on the epistemological implication
Consider why this argument matters for theology: it shows that power is known only through its effect, not through independent reasoning before action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن القوة ليست تعلم لذاتها ولا لها حد يعلم حقيقتها سوى ما جعل الله على حقيقة كونها من العقل ، والعقل ليس بموجود قبل كونه ، وبها وجوده ، ثبت أنه شهد لها وقت كونه لا قبله ، والله الموفق .
And also that power is not known by itself, nor does it have a limit by which its reality is known except what God has placed on the reality of its existence from reason, and reason does not exist before its existence, and by it is its existence, it is established that it testified for it at the time of its existence, not before it, and God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 860

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the epistemology of power. The author states that power (al-quwwa/القوة) is not known in itself (li dhatiha/لذاتها), nor does it have a definition (hadd/حد) that reveals its reality except what God has placed from reason (al-'aql/العقل) regarding the reality of its existence. Reason itself does not exist before the power's existence. The power is the means (biha/بها) for the existence of reason. Therefore, reason testifies to power at the time of its existence, not before. This shows that power cannot be known prior to the action; it is only attested at the moment of efficacy.

Key Terms
قوة
حد
عقل
شهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can be known through unaided reason prior to action.
Correction: Al-Maturidi argues that reason testifies to power only at the time of its existence, not before, because reason itself is brought into existence by power.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Maturidi, when does reason testify to the existence of power?
A) Before the action
B) At the time of the action
C) After the action
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Power is not known in itself but only through reason at the moment of action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day tomorrow, do you confidently assume you will have the power to fulfill each task? How does this lesson's teaching that power is only known at the moment of action challenge that assumption? What would change in your heart if you truly believed you have no power until you are actually acting?
?
Think of a time you felt completely self-reliant and sure of your ability to do something, only to fail. How does this lesson reveal the arrogance in that confidence? Will you now begin your daily tasks with a plea to Allah, acknowledging that your power is not guaranteed until He grants it at each instant?
?
The author says power is not known by itself. Do you often evaluate your own worth or capability without looking to Allah's granting? How can you train yourself to see every act of ability as a fresh gift from Allah, known only when it happens, and not as a permanent possession?

A Capable Person Cannot Exist Without Action
Al-Maturidi argues capable person must act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Maturidi’s rational argument that a person with capability (qudra) must necessarily act, and why it is impossible for a capable person to exist without action.

Core Idea
Capability without action is as impossible as action without capability.

This passage from Imam Al-Maturidi’s Kitab al-Tawhid presents a key rational argument grounding Islamic theology: that a person who is truly capable (qadir) cannot exist without performing an action. By drawing a parallel with the impossibility of an incapable person acting, Al-Maturidi establishes a necessary connection between capability and action. He then addresses potential objections by distinguishing between life and death, showing that while life can exist without action (as in sleep), capability itself cannot.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
First, carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the absolute language: 'la yujad qadir ghayr fa'il al-battah' — a capable person who is not acting does not exist at all.
2
Understand the parallel argument
Al-Maturidi compares the impossibility of a capable person not acting to the impossibility of an incapable person acting. Consider why both are equally impossible by reason.
3
Examine the distinction between life and death
The author explains that death is general incapacity, while life is not general power. This distinction allows for life to exist without action (like sleep), but capability cannot be separated from action even for a moment.
4
Reflect on the rational basis
The argument is built on the idea that capability and action are inherently linked. Why would Al-Maturidi insist on this? Consider the implications for human agency and divine power.
This connects to the broader theme of Allah’s exclusive power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه لا يوجد قادر غير فاعل البتة ، كما لا يوجد عاجز فاعلا ، كما لا يجوز العجز ووجوده ، إذ هما جميعا في الخروج عن الموجود واحد ، مع القول بالبعد عن ذلك من طريق العقل من حيث يضاد المعنى في الحقيقة ، وليس بالموت ونحوه معتبر ، لأن الموت عجز في الجملة ، وليست الحياة بقدرة فى الجملة ، ولما يجوز وجود الحياة أوقاتا لا فعل معها ، ولا يجوز وجود قادر وقتين لا فعل له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also that a capable person who is not acting does not exist at all, just as an incapable person who is acting does not exist, as it is not permissible for incapacity and its existence, for both are the same in being outside the existent, with the statement of distance from that by way of reason, in that it contradicts the meaning in reality, and death and the like are not considered, because death is incapacity in general, and life is not power in general, and it is not permissible for life to exist at times with no action accompanying it, and it is not permissible for a capable person to exist for two times without action, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 860

Commentary
This passage is an argument that a capable person (qadir/قادر) must necessarily act; it is impossible for a capable person to exist without acting, just as it is impossible for an incapable person to act. The author states that both cases (capable not acting, incapable acting) are equally impossible ('adam al-wujud/عدم الوجود). He then distinguishes between death and life: death is general incapacity, while life is not general power. Life can exist without action at times (e.g., sleep), but a capable person cannot exist for two consecutive moments without action. This supports the doctrine that power is always accompanied by action.

عجز
فعل
حياة
موت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person can have power (qudra) and not use it for a period.
Correction: Al-Maturidi argues that a capable person without action is as impossible as an incapable person acting. Capability necessarily entails action; they cannot be separated.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Maturidi, what is the status of a capable person who does not act?
A) It is possible for a short time
B) It is impossible
C) It happens in sleep
D) It is up to God's will

Key Takeaway
Capability (qudra) is necessarily linked to action; a capable person cannot exist without acting, just as an incapable person cannot act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If capability without action is impossible, then what does your failure to act on a good intention reveal about your actual capability? Are you truly capable, or are you deceiving yourself?
?
You often say 'I could do that, but I'm just not doing it.' After reading Al-Maturidi's argument, how can you reconcile your claim of capability with your lack of action? When will you close the gap between your supposed power and your real action?
?
This passage ends with 'la quwwata illa billah'. In light of this, how much of your daily actions are performed with a conscious awareness that your power is lent to you by Allah? How would your sense of urgency change if you truly believed that capability demands immediate action?
