Power is Known Only Through Its Effect
Argues that power is not known in itself but only through the intellect's recognition of its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why power (quwwa) is not an inherently knowable attribute and why its reality is only known through the intellect's recognition of its effect.

Core Idea
Power is known only when it acts, not before.

This lesson explores a key argument from Imam Maturidi: power is not a self-evident attribute. Rather, its reality is only accessible through the intellect's recognition of its effect. The passage establishes that the intellect itself does not pre-exist the power, so it can only attest to power at the moment of its existence. This has important implications for understanding divine attributes and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow become familiar before you interpret.
Notice the concise, logical style of Imam Maturidi.
2
Understand that power has no intrinsic definition
The passage first states that power is not known by its essence nor has a limit revealing its reality. This sets up the problem: power is not self-disclosing.
3
Identify the role of the intellect
The only way power's reality is known is through the intellect ('aql), which God places. But the intellect does not exist before power; therefore, it can only testify to power at the moment of its existence, not before.
This undercuts any notion of power as a pre-existing static attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أن القوة ليست تعلم لذاتها ولا لها حد يعلم حقيقتها سوى ما جعل الله على حقيقة كونها من العقل، والعقل ليس بموجود قبل كونه ، وبها وجوده ، ثبت أنه شهد لها وقت كونه لا قبله ، والله الموفق
And also that power is not known in its essence, nor does it have a limit by which its reality is known, except what God has placed concerning the reality of its existence from the intellect. And the intellect does not exist before its existence, and by it (power) is its existence. So it is established that it (the intellect) testified for it (power) at the time of its existence, not before it. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 860

Commentary
This passage argues that power (quwwa) is not an independently knowable entity. It has no intrinsic definition that reveals its reality; rather, its reality is only known through the intellect ('aql) that God has placed in creation. The intellect itself, however, does not exist before the existence of the power. Therefore, the intellect can only attest to the power at the moment of its existence (i.e., when an action occurs), not before. This undercuts the idea that power is a pre-existing state or a permanent attribute that can be known apart from its effect. The conclusion is that power is known only through its manifestation, and this knowledge is granted by God.

Key Terms
قوة (quwwa)
عقل ('aql)
ذات (dhat/essence)
حد (hadd/limit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power is a self-evident attribute that can be known independently of its effect.
Correction: The passage clarifies that power has no intrinsic knowability; its reality is only accessible through the intellect's perception of its effect at the moment of action.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when does the intellect testify to the existence of power?
A) Before the existence of power.
B) At the time of its existence.
C) After the action ceases.
D) In eternity.

Key Takeaway
Power is only known through the intellect's recognition of its effect at the moment of action, not as a pre-existing attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you waiting to see God's power in your life before you trust Him, or do you recognize that His power is only known when He acts — and that act may be happening right now? What would change if you lived as if every moment is the manifestation of His power?
?
You have abilities and strengths you assume you possess without ever testing them. How does this lesson confront the way you think about your own power? What 'power' do you claim to have that you have never actually exercised?
?
The intellect only testifies to power after the act. Think of a time you doubted God's power because you did not see its effect yet. How does this lesson call you to repent of demanding to see before believing? Will you now accept that power is known only when He wills to show it?

Refuting Ka'bi on Obliging the Impossible
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's claim that obliging the impossible is necessarily bad by distinguishing two meanings of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that obliging what cannot be done is necessarily bad, and the distinction between physical power and actual capacity.

Core Idea
Not all impossibilities are equal: true capacity includes the existence of the act at its time.

Imam Maturidi engages with a theological objection from al-Ka'bi concerning the nature of divine obligation. Al-Ka'bi argued that obliging something impossible is self-evidently bad. Maturidi's response turns on a subtle distinction between different meanings of 'power' (qudra). This passage unpacks that distinction and defends the possibility of divine obligations that seem impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how Maturidi introduces al-Ka'bi's claim.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's claim
Al-Ka'bi claims that obliging the impossible is bad by intuition. Locate the exact phrasing.
The term 'badiha' (intuition) is key.
3
Understand the distinction in power
Maturidi distinguishes between two types of power: apparent physical health and actual capacity at the time of action. See how he refutes the claim based on this.
4
Analyze the objection and conclusion
The passage presents an objection ('It is said to him') and its resolution. Note how the conclusion ties back to the idea of truthfulness.
The final phrase 'la quwwata illa billah' is a pious formula.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما ذكره الكعبى مما يبين وهمه في قضاياه . زعم أن تكليف ما لا يطاق قبيح فى العقل بالبديهة ، وهذا انما هو فى العقل الذي لا يعرف الطاقة غير القوة الظاهرة وهى الصحة ، وأما غيرها فليس كما يقول ، بل كلف الله صاحب يعلم أنه لا يستطيع ، وكذلك تكليف ما يجهل مثله فى البديهة. يقال له : وكذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق لوقت الفعل قبيح في العقل والذى ادعيته من القبح إنما هو فى عقول من يحيل وجود الفعل ولا قوة وذلك وقت الفعل، فصار قوله عند التحصيل هو القبيح فى العقل إن صدق فيها ادعى ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then we mention what al-Ka'bi mentioned that shows his error in his claims. He claimed that obliging what cannot be done (taklif ma la yutaq) is bad in reason by intuition (badiha). This is only in the reason that does not know capacity (taqa) except as apparent power (qudra), which is physical health. As for other than that, it is not as he says. Rather, God obligated someone knowing that he cannot, and likewise obliging what is unknown like it in intuition. It is said to him: Likewise, obliging what cannot be done at the time of the act is bad in the reason, and what you claimed of badness is only in the minds of those who deny the existence of the act, and there is no power at that time of the act. So his statement becomes, upon examination, that it is bad in reason if he is truthful in what he claimed. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 865

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's claim that imposing an obligation that cannot be fulfilled (taklif ma la yutaq) is self-evidently bad in reason (qabih fi al-aql bi al-badiha). Maturidi argues that this judgment applies only if one understands 'power' (qudra) as merely physical ability (like health). However, true capacity involves more than just physical readiness; it includes the existence of the act at its time. Al-Ka'bi's opponent objects that obliging something impossible at the time of action is bad, but this badness only appears to someone who denies that the act can occur at that moment. Thus, al-Ka'bi's claim reduces to the fact that if he is truthful, the obligation is considered bad. The passage concludes with the tasbih formula, common in Maturidi's style.

Key Terms
تکلیف ما لا یطاق (taklif ma la yutaq)
بدیهة (badiha)
قبیح (qabih)
قوة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obliging something impossible is always bad in reason.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this depends on the meaning of power; if power is understood as actual capacity at the time of action, the judgment changes.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ka'bi claim about obliging what cannot be done (taklif ma la yutaq)?
A) It is good in reason.
B) It is bad in reason by intuition.
C) It is permissible if God wills.
D) It is a matter of divine wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi by distinguishing physical power from actual capacity at the time of action, showing that not all impossible obligations are universally bad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from God that seems impossible for you, do you immediately assume it is unfair, or do you consider that He may be testing a deeper capacity He will grant you? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in His wisdom?
?
How often do you judge others' inability to fulfill obligations while ignoring that God may have empowered them differently? Think of a specific person you have been harsh towards—will you now acknowledge that their capacity at the time of action is known only to God?
?
If you truly believe that 'there is no power except through God,' why do you still rely on your own physical health and abilities as if they are the only measure of what you can do? What would change if you lived with the constant awareness that God can enable you to do what seems impossible?

The Nature of Ugliness in Actions
Maturidi argues against inherent ugliness of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that actions are not inherently ugly but become ugly due to harm or consequence, and why this matters for divine attributes.

Core Idea
Ugliness in actions is not intrinsic but arises from harm or consequence.

Maturidi addresses a key objection from opponents who claim that certain actions are inherently ugly based on primary intellect. He dismantles this claim by showing that what we consider ugly in the witnessed world is due to harm or lack of benefit, not the act's essence. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for understanding divine actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the flow of Maturidi's argument.
Notice the repeated phrase 'ثبت أن' which signals conclusions.
2
Identify the objection being refuted
Find the opponent's claim that actions are inherently ugly based on 'بديهة العقل' (primary intellect). Maturidi argues that this claim is false.
The opponent uses analogy (القياس) from the witnessed world.
3
Understand Maturidi's conclusion
Focus on the final lines: actions like immoralities and disbelief are not ugly in themselves; ugliness is due to harm or lack of benefit.
This has implications for God's actions – they are not bound by human notions of intrinsic ugliness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تم دفع ما عورض بخلق شيء لا ينتفع به بما لم يدل ذلك على تكليف الزمين، فهذا يبين أن الذي قال ببديهة العقل كذب، وأنه إنما ادعى القياس على ما وافقه خصمه عليه لا غير، ثم حصل بما عارض به خصمه مما ادعى ببديهة العقل على من يثبت صدقا مما هو كذب في الشاهد. ثم أجاب بما اختزاز النفع بالفعل لا يحق عليه، ثبت أن ما قبح منه لم يقبح لعينه، وكذلك يجد خصمه مما يقدر عليه لا يجوز التكليف به، ثبت أن ذلك لم يقبح لنفسه نحو الفواحش والكفر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
He repelled the objection concerning creating something not beneficial, by [saying] that this does not indicate obligation upon the bounden. So this shows that the one who claimed [based on] the primary intellect is lying, and that he only claimed analogy according to what his opponent agreed upon, nothing else. Then he obtained by what he objected to his opponent, from what he claimed by primary intellect, against one who affirms truth from falsehood in the witnessed world. Then he answered with the avoidance of benefit from the act not being incumbent upon him, so it is established that what was ugly from him is not ugly in itself. And likewise his opponent finds that what is within [God's] power, it is not permissible to obligate it, so it is established that that is not ugly in itself, such as immoralities and disbelief. There is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage is a proof and conclusion. Maturidi begins by referencing a previous objection: 'creating something not beneficial.' He dismisses it by noting that such creation does not imply any obligation on humans. He then argues that an opponent who claims to know by 'primary intellect' (badihah al-aql) that certain actions are inherently ugly is mistaken; in fact, this opponent is merely arguing by analogy with what his adversary already accepts. Maturidi further points out that in the witnessed world (al-shahid), ugliness is attributed to actions because of harm or lack of benefit, not because of an intrinsic quality. Therefore, actions like immoralities (fawahish) and disbelief (kufr) are not ugly in themselves but due to their consequences. The key principle here is that divine acts are not subject to human judgments based on inherent ugliness.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
القياس
الشاهد
القبح
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi denies moral distinctions entirely; he does not believe any action is truly ugly or good.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny moral distinctions; he only denies that ugliness is an intrinsic attribute of the act's essence. Actions can be ugly due to consequences, but this does not apply to divine acts in the same way.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for labeling an action 'ugly' (qabih) in the witnessed world?
A) The action's essence or nature.
B) The action's benefit or harm.
C) The action's frequency of occurrence.
D) The action's conformity to religious law.

Key Takeaway
Actions are not intrinsically ugly; ugliness is attributed based on harm or benefit in the witnessed world, and this principle applies even to actions like disbelief and immoralities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If ugliness is not inherent in any action, where does your own revulsion toward disobedience come from? Is it the act itself you hate, or what it leads to? How does this reshape your understanding of Allah's justice when He punishes?
?
This passage argues that even kufr (disbelief) is not ugly in itself. How does that truth confront your assumptions about the nature of sin? If disbelief is not inherently evil, then what makes it the greatest crime? Does your heart tremble at the thought that it is only Allah's command that renders it so?
?
Maturidi says the opponent's claim is based on analogy, not primary intellect. Are there beliefs you hold about right and wrong that you have never questioned, assuming they are self-evident? What if they were just cultural or inherited analogies? Will you re-examine them in light of revelation?

Power is created for one action only
Maturidi argues power is created for either good or evil, not both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that power (qudra) is created for a specific action and cannot be turned from its intended use.

Core Idea
Power is like a sword – created for one purpose, not neutral.

This passage from Imam Maturidi tackles a key question in Kalam: Is human power (qudra) neutral, able to be used for either good or evil? Maturidi argues it is created for one specific action. Through a penetrating self-objection and answer, he clarifies the nature of power and refutes the idea that it can be turned arbitrarily.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the full Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow without analysis.
Pay attention to the self-objection structure.
2
Identify the self-objection
Notice that Maturidi presents an objection from an imaginary opponent: if power is created for good, how can the servant turn it to evil?
3
Understand the analogy
The analogy compares power to a sword or a dirham (coin), which are each created for a single function, not like water that can be heated or cooled.
Swords have a single use (cutting); dirhams are for buying – they cannot be repurposed.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
Maturidi argues that since power cannot produce both good and evil simultaneously nor remain idle, it must be created for one specific action.
Also, requesting strength for good implies that evil is possible; otherwise the request would be meaningless.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider how this argument shapes Maturidi's view of human responsibility: power is given for a specific action, and the servant's role is not to turn that power but to act within its capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض نفسه بما إذ كانت [ ١٤٧ ب] القدرة مخلوقة للخير كيف قدر العبد على قلبها ؟ / أن ذا ليس كالذي يسخن ويبرد ، لكنه كالسيف والدرهم . قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : فيقال له : القدرة إذ لا تحتمل الفعلين ولا تركها ، ١٥ وما عارضت به محتمل ، ثبت أن القدرة مخلوقة لأحدهما لا لهما ، ثم لا يحتمل المخلوق بجهة واحدة قلبها عنها من نحو الذي ذكرت مما يسخن به ويبرد ، ليم لا دل أنها وهو ما كان بها ، ويبين لك العرف الظاهر في الخلق بسؤال القوة على الخير ، ولو كانت لا تحتمل الشر لكان لا معنى لتخصيص ذلك ، خلقت لأحدهما ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he objected to himself: When power is created for good, how is the servant able to turn it? That is not like that which heats and cools, but like the sword and the dirham. Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: So it is said to him: Since power does not admit both actions nor the absence of them, and what you objected with is possible, it is established that power is created for one of them, not for both. Then the created thing from one aspect does not admit turning it from that aspect like the thing you mentioned by which one heats and cools, because it does not indicate that it is what is with it, and the manifest custom in creation clarifies to you by requesting strength for good; and if it did not admit evil, there would be no meaning to specifying that. It was created for one of them, and there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 879

Commentary
This passage presents a self-objection and its answer. The objector asks: if power is created for good, how can the servant turn it to evil? Maturidi answers by analogy: power is not like an instrument that can be used for opposite effects (like water that can be heated or cooled); rather it is like a sword or a dirham, which are created for a single purpose. Since power cannot produce both good and evil simultaneously nor be idle, it must be created for one specific action. The very fact that strength is requested for good shows that power could also be used for evil; otherwise, requesting strength for good alone would be meaningless. Thus power is created for one of the two possibilities, but the choice of use is not from the power itself.

Key Terms
قدرة
قلب
سيف
درهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That power is neutral and can be turned to either good or evil by the servant.
Correction: Maturidi insists power is created for one specific use and cannot be turned; the analogy of the sword shows it has a fixed purpose.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to explain that power cannot be turned from its intended use?
A) A fire that burns and gives light.
B) A sword or a dirham, not like something that heats and cools.
C) A pen that writes both good and bad.
D) A mirror that reflects both light and darkness.

Key Takeaway
Power (qudra) is created for a single specific action and cannot be turned from its purpose, as shown by the analogy of a sword or dirham.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever stop to think that the very power to commit that sin was created by Allah for that specific act? How does this realization change your view of your own accountability and your need for sincere repentance?
?
You often ask Allah for strength to do good. Do you truly believe that He is the one who creates that power in the moment, and without it you cannot act? How does this affect your daily reliance on Him, especially when you feel weak?
?
If every power is created for a single action, then your ability to do a good deed is a direct gift from Allah. How does this humble you, and what does it mean for your gratitude and your fear of misusing that gift?

The Principle of Effective Will and Divine Dominion
Maturidi presents two rational proofs for the necessity of effective divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason demands that an agent's action must conform to its will, and how this applies to Allah as the sole sovereign.

Core Idea
Reason shows that a sovereign's will must be effective in his own dominion.

Maturidi begins with a foundational rational principle: if an action diverges from the agent's will, it implies another will is at work. He then applies this to Allah, arguing that any inconsistency in His will would compromise His sole sovereignty. This lesson covers the first two proofs, establishing that Allah's will is necessarily effective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the logical flow: first a general statement, then a specific application to Allah's dominion.
Notice how Maturidi uses 'ضرورة العقل' (necessity of reason) as the starting point.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two distinct arguments: (1) if action opposes will, another will intervenes; (2) bringing something into another's dominion without permission is weakness.
The second proof begins with 'وأيضا أن إحداث شىء'.
3
Understand the implication for Allah
Both proofs lead to the conclusion that Allah's will must be effective. If not, it would imply a second will or weakness, both impossible for the Lord.
Reflect on why this is foundational for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العبرة بما يوجبه ضرورة العقل ، وهى توجب ذلك ؛ إذ أن فعله يخرج على غير الذي يريده من الحسن والقبح ، واللذة والألم ، والمحبة والسخط ، ثبت أن لغيرهم في خروج فعلهم على ما خرج إرادة على تلك الإرادة يخرج [الفعل ] . وأيضا أن إحداث شىء فى سلطان آخر وفي مملكته من حيث لا يشاء ولا يريده آية الضعف والقهر ، ومن ذلك وصفه محال أن يكون رباً وإلها ؛ لذلك لزم وصف الله بذلك . وأيضا أن الله تعالى لو أراد أن يكون الكثير غير الذى يعلم أنه يكون ، غير الذي أخبر أنه يكون ، أراد أن يكون كذابا سفيها ، ومن تلك إرادته لم يجز أن يكون إلها وربا . وأيضا في الحكمة أن كل من صنع أمرا يريد غير الذي يكون به ، يكون جاهلا بالعواقب أو عابثا بالفعل ، والله تعالى ينجل عن هذين الوصفين . ألا أن یری بنا لشيء يعلم أنه لا يكون ، كان ذلك منه عبثا ، ولو كان به شيء غير الذي يريده كان جاهلا به. وأيضا أن الخطأ المعروف في الشاهد نوعان : أحدهما خروج الفعل على تقدير يجهله ، والثانى وقوعه في غير الذي يريده ، فلو كان الله تعالى يريد بما أعطى غير الذى يكون به لكان يكون فعله خطأ ، على ما عرفنا الله تعالى من فعل الخطأ .
Then the consideration is what the necessity of reason necessitates, and it necessitates that; since if His (Allah's) action comes out contrary to what He wills of good and evil, pleasure and pain, love and displeasure, it is established that for others, in the coming out of their action upon what it came out, there is an will upon that will, and the action comes out [according to that will]. And also that to bring about something in another's dominion and kingdom, where it does not will nor desire it, is a sign of weakness and coercion, and from that, describing Him (Allah) as such is impossible for Him to be a Lord and God; therefore it is necessary to describe Allah with that (i.e., effective will). And also that if Allah Almighty willed the many to be other than what He knows it will be, other than what He informed that it will be, He would will to be a liar and foolish, and from that His will would not be permissible for Him to be a God and Lord. And also in wisdom, that everyone who does an action intending something other than what comes to be from it, is ignorant of consequences or acting playfully, and Allah Almighty is exalted above these two descriptions. Indeed, if He sees for us something that He knows will not be, that would be from Him playfulness, and if there were something other than what He wills with it, He would be ignorant of it. And also that recognized error in the witnessed world is of two types: one is the action occurring upon a estimation that is unknown; the second is its occurring in something other than what is intended. So if Allah Almighty willed with what He gives something other than what comes to be from it, then His action would be erroneous, according to what He has taught us of error in action.
Source pages p. 891

Commentary
This passage presents several rational proofs (adilla aqliyya) for the necessity that Allah's will be effective and that nothing occurs contrary to it. Maturidi argues from reason (darurat al-aql) that if an agent's action diverges from their will, then there must be another will causing that divergence. Applying this to Allah, if His action were contrary to His will, it would imply another will influencing His domain, which is impossible. Secondly, introducing something into another's dominion without their permission is a sign of weakness; since Allah is the sole sovereign, His will must be supreme. Thirdly, if Allah willed something other than what He knows and informs will happen, that would imply He is a liar or foolish—impossible for God. Fourthly, if one intends something but another outcome occurs, that indicates ignorance or jest; Allah is free from both. Finally, Maturidi distinguishes two types of error in human actions: error due to ignorance of the outcome, and error due to the action resulting in something unintended. If Allah's will could be separate from what occurs, His action would be erroneous, which is impossible. Thus, Allah's will is always effective and identical with what occurs.

Key Terms
ضرورة العقل
الإرادة
السلطان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that events can occur without Allah's will, or that His will is not always effective.
Correction: Maturidi shows that rational necessity requires Allah's will to be effective, as any deviation would imply another will or weakness.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if an agent's action does not conform to its will, what does that imply?
A) The agent is weak
B) Another will is causing the action
C) The action is impossible
D) The agent is ignorant

Key Takeaway
Reason demands that Allah's will be effective in all that occurs, as any inconsistency would undermine His sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful trial, do you truly believe that it occurs by Allah's will, or do you sometimes feel that events have slipped out of His control? If you believe in His effective will, why do you still feel anxiety? What would change in your response to hardship if you internalized that everything happens according to His will?
?
The passage argues that bringing something into another's dominion without permission is a sign of weakness. In your own life, do you ever try to assert control over things that are not truly yours—like outcomes, people, or the future? How does recognizing Allah's absolute sovereignty change the way you view your own desires and plans?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's will is effective in every aspect of your life—including your successes and failures—how would it affect your gratitude and patience? What practical step can you take today to remind yourself of this truth?

The Incoherence of Willing Contrary to Knowledge
Maturidi provides two proofs from knowledge and wisdom against a non-effective divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why willing something other than what one knows will happen implies lying or foolishness, and why this cannot apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Willing contrary to knowledge is a sign of ignorance or jest.

Maturidi presents two additional rational arguments focusing on Allah's knowledge and wisdom. The first shows that if Allah willed something other than what He knows will happen, He would be willing to be a liar and foolish—impossible for God. The second argues that intending one outcome but producing another indicates ignorance or jest, which are also impossible for Allah. This lesson solidifies the connection between divine will, knowledge, and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two 'وأيضا' markers that introduce each proof.
2
Analyze the knowledge proof
Understand that Allah knows and informs what will happen. If He willed differently, He would be a liar and foolish—contradicting His attributes.
This proof relies on Allah's omniscience and truthfulness.
3
Analyze the wisdom proof
If an agent intends one outcome but another occurs, it shows ignorance of consequences or jest. Allah is exalted above both.
The proof includes an elaboration: if Allah sees something that He knows will not be, that would be playfulness.

The Impossibility of Error in Divine Action
Maturidi uses the concept of error to argue for the effectiveness of divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reasoning about error in human actions proves that Allah's will must be effective, and that attributing error to Allah is impossible.

Core Idea
If Allah's will could be ineffective, His action would be erroneous—impossible for God.

Maturidi concludes his rational arguments by examining the nature of error. He identifies two types of error in human actions: error due to ignorance of the outcome, and error due to unintended results. Applying this to Allah, if His will were separate from what occurs, His action would fall into error, which is impossible. This lesson consolidates the necessity of divine will being perfectly effective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the definition of error and its two types.
The passage begins with 'وأيضا أن الخطأ المعروف في الشاهد نوعان'.
2
Understand the two types of error
First type: action based on unknown estimation. Second type: action resulting in unintended outcome.
These are the only ways an action can be erroneous in human experience.
3
Apply to Allah
If Allah intended one thing but another occurred, His action would be erroneous by the second type. This is impossible for Allah, so His will must be effective.
This proof ties together the earlier arguments.

Five Rational Proofs for God's Universal Will
Maturidi presents five rational proofs that God must will all events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational necessity for God's universal will through five distinct proofs from necessity, sovereignty, truthfulness, wisdom, and the nature of error.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses all events; otherwise, He would be weak, ignorant, or foolish.

Imam Maturidi presents a series of rational arguments demonstrating that nothing occurs outside God's will. These proofs build on basic rational principles: if an agent's act does not match his intention, another will must be at play; if something happens in a sovereign's domain without his will, it shows weakness; and if God willed contrary to His knowledge, He would be a liar. Each proof reinforces the conclusion that God's will is universal and effective. Let's examine these proofs in order.

Guided Path
1
First proof: from necessity of reason
Read the first argument: if an act does not result as intended, another will is required. Notice how Maturidi grounds this in rational necessity.
This proof establishes that all actions, even those of others, are under God's will.
2
Second proof: from sovereignty
Consider the argument from dominion: causing something in another's kingdom without their will indicates weakness. Since God is sovereign, He wills all events in His creation.
This highlights God's absolute authority.
3
Third proof: from truthfulness
Reflect on the proof that if God willed something contrary to His knowledge, He would be a liar and foolish. Such attributes are impossible for God.
This connects God's will to His knowledge and truthfulness.
4
Fourth proof: from wisdom
Examine the argument that intending one outcome but getting another implies ignorance or jesting. God is above both.
This shows that God's will is perfectly aligned with outcomes.
5
Fifth proof: from the nature of error
Analyze the two types of error: ignorance of the outcome or act occurring contrary to intention. If God's will were not effective, His act would be like error, which is impossible.
This reinforces that God's will is always realized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعلم كل أحد ثم العبرة بما يوجبه ضرورة العقل ، وهى توجب ذلك ؛ إذ أن فعله يخرج على غير الذي يريده من الحسن والقبح ، واللذة والألم ، والمحبة والسخط ، ثبت أن لغيرهم في خروج فعلهم على ما خرج إرادة على تلك الإرادة يخرج [الفعل ] ، والله الموفق . وأيضا أن إحداث شىء فى سلطان آخر وفي مملكته من حيث لا يشاء ولا يريده آية الضعف والقهر ، ومن ذلك وصفه محال أن يكون رباً وإلها ؛ لذلك لزم وصف الله بذلك ، والله الموفق وأيضا أن الله تعالى لو أراد أن يكون الكثير غير الذى يعلم أنه يكون ، غير الذي أخبر أنه يكون ، أراد أن يكون كذابا سفيها ، ومن تلك إرادته لم يجز أن يكون إلها وربا ، ولا قوة إلا بالله كان وأيضا في الحكمة أن كل من صنع أمرا يريد غير الذي يكون به ، يكون جاهلا بالعواقب أو عابثا بالفعل ، والله تعالى ينجل عن هذين الوصفين . ألا أن یری بنا لشيء يعلم أنه لا يكون ، كان ذلك منه عبثا ، ولو كان به شيء غير الذي يريده كان جاهلا به. وأيضا أن الخطأ المعروف في الشاهد نوعان : أحدهما من خروج الفعل على تقدير يجهله ، والثانى وقوعه في غير الذي يريده ، فلو كان الله تعالى يريد بما أعطى غير الذى يكون به لكان يكون فعله خطأ ، على ما عرفنا الله تعالى من فعل الخطأ ، وبالله التوفيق
Every one knows, then the consideration is based on what the necessity of reason requires, and it requires that; since his act comes out contrary to what he intends, of good and evil, pleasure and pain, love and anger, it is established that for others there is, in the emergence of their act as it emerges, a will upon that will, and the act emerges. And God is the Grantor of success. Also, causing something to occur in another's dominion and in his kingdom without his will and desire is a sign of weakness and coercion, and describing Him with that is impossible for Him to be a Lord and God; therefore it is necessary to describe God with that. And God is the Grantor of success. Also, if God Most High willed that the many be other than what He knows it will be, other than what He informed it will be, He would will to be a liar and foolish, and from that will it is not permissible for Him to be a God and Lord. There is no power except with God. Also, in wisdom, whoever does a deed intending other than what comes to be by it is ignorant of consequences or jesting in action, and God Most High is exalted above these two descriptions. However, if He intended by us something that He knows will not be, that would be jest from Him, and if something occurred to Him other than what He intends, He would be ignorant of it. Also, known error in the witnessed realm is of two types: one is the act's departure from a estimation he is ignorant of, and the second is its occurrence contrary to what he intends. If God Most High intended by what He gives other than what comes to be by it, His act would be error, according to what we know God Most High to be free from the act of error. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 891

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rational proofs (each introduced by 'وأيضا') to show that God must will everything that occurs. The first proof from reason's necessity: if an agent's act does not result as intended, there must be another will causing it; therefore, God's will is behind all acts. The second proof from sovereignty: introducing something in another's domain without their will indicates weakness; since God cannot be weak, He must will all events in His creation. The third proof from knowledge: if God willed something contrary to His knowledge, He would be a liar and foolish, which is impossible for God. The fourth proof from wisdom: intending one outcome but getting another implies ignorance or jesting; God is above both. The passage then elaborates on this: if God intended something He knows will not happen, that would be jest; if something happened contrary to His intention, that would be ignorance. Finally, it distinguishes two types of error in human acts: error due to ignorance of the outcome, and error due to the act occurring contrary to intention. If God's will were not effective, His act would be like error, which is impossible for God.

ضرورة العقل
سلطان
كذاب
سفيه
جاهل
عابث
خطأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These proofs imply that God is the direct agent of evil.
Correction: The proofs affirm that nothing occurs outside God's will, but do not specify moral agency; they establish that all events are within His will, not that He directly does evil.

Checkpoint

A) God cannot lie because it is impossible for Him
B) If God willed something other than what He knows, He would be a liar and foolish
C) God's will is independent of His knowledge
D) God's will is not necessary for events

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's rational proofs establish that God's will is necessary and universal, covering all events without exception.

Proof that Power Must Precede Action
Maturidi argues that power must exist before action, using sensory analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that power (qudra) must logically precede action, not occur concurrently.

Core Idea
Power is the cause of action; therefore, it must precede the action.

This lesson examines a key passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid, where he establishes that divine power must exist prior to the action it produces. Using an analogy from sensory perception, he argues that the removal of incapacity is the cause that makes action conceivable. This reasoning helps clarify the relationship between Allah's power and created actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently, then its translation. Observe the keywords: القدرة (power), العجز (incapacity), الفعل (action).
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to logical connectors.
2
Analyze the Analogy
Notice how Maturidi uses the example of vision. When you are blind (incapable of seeing), seeing is inconceivable. When blindness is removed, seeing becomes conceivable. The removal is the cause, not the act itself.
This analogy supports the logical priority of power.
3
Understand the Role of Cause
Maturidi directly states that power (qudra) is the cause (sabab) of the action in reality. Just as the removal of incapacity is the cause of conceivability, power is the cause of the action's existence.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Examine the Conclusion
The passage ends with 'there is no power except by God,' affirming that all power is from Allah. Reflect on how this ties back to the necessity of power preceding action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يوضح ذلك أن الفعل غير متوهم في حال العجز، ومتوهم وجوده في حال ارتفاع العجز، فكان توهمه مع الارتفاع أرفع وأبلغ من توهمه مع الوجود؛ إذ هو سبب، فكذلك القدرة التي هي سبب الفعل في الحقيقة، ويؤيد ذلك فساد الدرك مع ذهابه بما تقدم من البصر، وكذا السمع وعمل كل الحواس، فلذلك المنع بالبصر كان فساد فعل الاختيار العجز، وفقد القدرة أوضح مع الوجود، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And among what clarifies that is that the action is not conceivable in a state of incapacity, and its existence is conceivable in a state of removal of incapacity. So its conception with the removal is higher and more emphatic than its conception with existence, because it is a cause. Likewise, the power (al-qudra) that is the cause of the action in reality. And that is supported by the corruption of perception when it goes away, as by the earlier example of sight, and likewise hearing and the operation of all the senses. Therefore, the prevention by sight was the corruption of the act of choice is incapacity, and the loss of power is clearer with existence, and there is no power except by God.
Source pages p. 919

Commentary
This passage is a proof that power (qudra) must exist before the action, not at the time of the action. Maturidi uses an analogy: an action is inconceivable during incapacity (like blindness for seeing), but becomes conceivable when the incapacity is removed. The conception of the action when the barrier is removed is stronger than the conception when the action already exists, because the removal of the barrier is a cause. Similarly, power is the cause of the action in reality. To support this, he refers to the senses: when sight is lost, perception is corrupted; likewise for hearing and other senses. The prevention of sight is essentially the corruption of choice due to incapacity, and the loss of power is even more obvious when existence (of the action) is considered. He ends with the phrase 'there is no power except by God,' affirming that all power is from God.

Key Terms
القدرة
العجز
الفعل
السبب
البصر
السمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that power must exist at the exact moment of the action, not logically before it.
Correction: Maturidi argues that power must logically precede the action because it is the cause; the cause must be prior to the effect.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the conception of an action when incapacity is removed is 'more emphatic' than when the action exists?
A) Because the removal of incapacity is the cause
B) Because the action itself is less real
C) Because existence is only metaphorical
D) Because incapacity is permanent

Key Takeaway
Power must logically precede the action it produces, just as the removal of incapacity precedes the conceivability of an action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own actions, do you truly acknowledge that your power to act is a gift from God that must logically precede every action, or do you take it for granted? How would your day change if you consciously remembered that before every action, your capacity is a divine grant?
?
If you believe that power must precede action, what does that imply about your dependence on God? Are you living as if your capacity is your own, or are you constantly aware that without His granting of qudra, you could do nothing?
?
Reflect on a recent sin. Did you consider that the power to sin was also given by God? How should this lead you to humility and repentance, knowing that your power is a trust from Him?

The Will Argument: Action without Choice is Compulsion
Maturidi proves that Mu'tazili will theory entails compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical proof that the Mu'tazila theory of will (irada) inevitably leads to compulsion (jabr), exposing an internal contradiction in their doctrine.

Core Idea
If will only precedes action, then action without will is compulsion.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a sharp logical critique of the Mu'tazila definition of will. He shows that their own premises—that will is choice of action and exists only before the action—lead to the conclusion that the action occurs without choice, i.e., under compulsion. This argument aims to expose an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position on human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that Maturidi is stating an argument (wajh) against the Mu'tazila.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila premise about will
Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila define will as choice of action, and it only precedes action, not coexisting with it. Understand this premise.
This is the key assumption Maturidi works with.
3
Follow Maturidi's logical deduction
Trace the logic: since will is absent at the time of action, the action occurs without choice. The initial choice is gone, and compulsion can enter the second moment. Therefore the action is compelled.
Notice the temporal distinction between the time of willing and the time of acting.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the Mu'tazila are actually affirming compulsion (jabr), albeit falsely claiming otherwise. They are 'false Jabriyya' while true Jabriyya are honest.
This is a polemical point meant to show inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه آخر أن قول المعتزلة : إن الإرادة هي اختيار الفعل ، وإنما تكون متقدمة على الفعل، وليست بموجودة معه، وأنه وجد في وقت الوجود بلا إرادة منه ولا اختيار ، وحق اختيار الأول عنه زائل ، إذ يجوز ورود الاضطرار في الوقت الثاني ، ومحال وروده في الوقت الذي فيه الاختيار، والاختيار قائم ، ثبت أن فعله في التحصيل ليس باختيار. وأنه اضطرار ، وعلامة الجبر هذا. وبهذا الفعل يوجبون العداوة والولاية والخلود في الجنة والنار ، الواقع وقت وقوعه بلا اختيار ولا قدرة ولا أمر أيضا ولا نهى ، فمن تأمل ذلك وجده عند التحقيق قول الجبرية في التصريح ، لكن هو لا جبرية كاذبة ، وأولئك جبرية صادقة
And another proof: that the Mu'tazila say: will (al-irada) is the choice of the action, and it only precedes the action and does not exist with it, and that it occurred at the time of existence without any will or choice from him. And the right of the first choice is gone from him, because it is possible for compulsion to enter in the second time, and it is impossible for it to enter in the time in which there is choice, and choice is established. So it is proven that his action in attainment is not by choice, but is compulsion, and this is the sign of jabr (compulsion). And by this action they impose enmity and friendship and eternal punishment and reward, occurring at the time of its occurrence without choice or power and also without command or prohibition. Whoever reflects on this finds it in reality the explicit statement of the Jabriyya, but they are false Jabriyya, and those (true Jabriyya) are truthful Jabriyya.
Source pages p. 920

Commentary
This passage is an argument (wajh) proving that the Mu'tazila position on will leads to compulsion. Maturidi summarizes their view: will is the choice of the action, it exists before the action but not at the same time as the action. Thus, when the action occurs, there is no will or choice. Since the initial choice is no longer present, and compulsion can enter at the second moment (the time of action), but it is impossible for compulsion to enter when choice is present, it follows that the action is not by choice but by compulsion. This is the characteristic of jabr (compulsion). Maturidi then notes that on this basis, the Mu'tazila assign eternal reward and punishment for actions that occur without choice, power, command, or prohibition. Thus, their view is actually a form of jabr, but they are lying jabriyya, whereas the true Jabriyya are truthful about affirming jabr.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الاختيار
الاضطرار
الجبر
الجبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi accuses Mu'tazila of being identical to the Jabriyya.
Correction: Maturidi says their logic leads to jabr, making them 'false Jabriyya'—they affirm compulsion implicitly while denying it, unlike true Jabriyya who are honest.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the Mu'tazili view of will lead to compulsion (jabr)?
A) Because will is simultaneous with action
B) Because will precedes action and is absent at action time
C) Because will is eternal
D) Because will is created by God

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila definition of will, by separating it from action, logically transforms choice into compulsion, revealing an inconsistency in their doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a sin by thinking 'it was beyond my control' or 'Allah willed it'? What does Maturidi's argument teach you about the relationship between your will and your actions? Are you willing to take full responsibility for every action, knowing that at the moment of action you had the capacity to choose?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending free will, but their logic led to compulsion. In your own life, are there beliefs or habits you hold that, upon honest reflection, lead to conclusions you would reject? Are you ready to examine your own positions for hidden inconsistencies?
?
Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazila assign reward and punishment for actions that, by their theory, are compelled. How do you reconcile the reality that you will be held accountable for deeds that may feel automatic or ingrained? Does your daily practice of Islam reflect a belief that at every moment you are truly choosing, or do you sometimes act on autopilot? What would change if you truly believed every act is a fresh choice?

Second Argument: Intrinsic Evil of Kufr and Impossibility of Pardon
Maturidi argues that kufr is intrinsically evil and its punishment cannot be pardoned, unlike other sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that kufr is intrinsically evil and therefore cannot be pardoned, while other sins can be subject to forgiveness.

Core Idea
Kufr is evil in itself; its punishment cannot be lifted because its prohibition cannot be removed.

Maturidi continues his argumentation by presenting a second rational proof for the distinction between kufr and other sins. He bases this argument on the intrinsic moral nature of acts: some actions are inherently evil and can never become permissible, while others are not. This passage lays out that logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
Pay attention to the contrast between kufr and 'other sins' (سائر المأثم).
2
Identify the two categories
Note how Maturidi describes kufr (قبيح لعينه) and other sins (المأثم). What is the key difference in their moral nature?
The term 'قبيح لعينه' is crucial—it means ugly in its very essence.
3
Understand the consequences
For kufr: because it is intrinsically evil, it cannot be made permissible (إطلاق ورفع الحرمة), so its punishment cannot be pardoned. For other sins: they can have their prohibition removed in intellect, so their punishment can be pardoned.
The phrase 'في الحكمة' (in wisdom) links the ruling to divine wisdom.
4
Contrast the outcomes
Kufr's punishment is fixed and cannot be lifted; other sins' punishment can be pardoned. This distinction shows the unique severity of kufr.
Reflect on why this argument matters for Islamic theology about forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثانى أن الكفر قبيح لعينه ، لا يحتمل الإطلاق ورفع الحرمة ، فعلى ذلك عقوبته في الحكمة لا يحتمل الارتفاع والعفو عنه ، وسائر المأثم جائز رفع الحرمة عنها في العقل وإباحة ما له العقوبة ، فمثله عقوبته ، والله الموفق .
And the second (argument) is that disbelief (kufr) is ugly in its essence (qabih li-aynihi), it does not admit being set free (from prohibition) or removal of prohibition (raf' al-hurma), so accordingly its punishment in wisdom (hikma) does not admit being lifted or pardoning (afw) from it. And other sins (al-ma'athim) — it is permissible in intellect to remove the prohibition from them and to permit that for which there is punishment, so their punishment is like that (i.e., can be pardoned). And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 961

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-thani) for the distinction between kufr and other sins. Maturidi states that disbelief is intrinsically ugly or evil (qabih li-aynihi) — it is evil in its very essence. Therefore, it cannot admit being set free (itlaq) from prohibition or having its prohibition removed (raf' al-hurma). Consequently, according to divine wisdom (al-hikma), its punishment cannot be lifted or pardoned. In contrast, other sins (al-ma'athim) can, in intellectual consideration, have their prohibition removed and even be made permissible, thus their punishment can be similar—i.e., forgiving them is possible. This argument relies on the intrinsic moral nature of the acts: kufr is inherently evil and can never become good, whereas other sins are not inherently evil in the same way; they can be permitted under certain circumstances, so forgiveness remains a possibility. The phrase 'والله الموفق' acknowledges that success is from Allah.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه
الإطلاق
رفع الحرمة
المأثم
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume all sins are equally pardonable.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the intrinsic evil of kufr makes it uniquely unpardonable; other sins can be pardoned because their prohibition can be removed in intellect.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why can kufr never be pardoned while other sins can?
A) Because kufr is committed more often.
B) Because kufr is intrinsically evil and cannot be made permissible.
C) Because Allah is less merciful to disbelievers.
D) Because other sins are smaller in degree.

Key Takeaway
The intrinsic evil of kufr precludes any possibility of its punishment being pardoned, while other sins, being not intrinsically evil, admit of forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If kufr is intrinsically evil and its punishment cannot be pardoned, what does that reveal about the state of your own heart? Are you cultivating any thoughts, doubts, or attachments that contradict pure tawhid? What step will you take today to uproot any hidden kufr from your soul?
?
The lesson shows that other sins, though not intrinsically evil, can be forgiven. Does this truth make you complacent about minor sins, or does it drive you to greater gratitude and diligence in seeking forgiveness? How will you respond to Allah's mercy today?
?
Maturidi argues that God's wisdom demands punishment for kufr. When you see the severity of this, does it make you more sincere in your worship? Are you treating Allah's commands with the weight they deserve, or are you taking them lightly? What change in your daily life will reflect the seriousness of this truth?

First Reason: Intercession as Ignorant Estimation
Maturidi gives the first reason against intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's first argument that intercession in the afterlife is invalid because it requires knowledge that only Allah possesses.

Core Idea
Intercession assumes knowledge of the unknown; only Allah knows all.

Imam Maturidi begins his refutation of intercession in the afterlife by pointing out a fundamental problem: intercession would involve estimating or judging the state of another person. This estimation would have to come from someone who does not have complete knowledge of that person's deeds and faith. Yet Allah alone is the Knower of all realities. To support this, the Imam quotes Qur'anic verses where even the prophets disavow knowledge of their communities' responses, showing that such knowledge belongs exclusively to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the first reason carefully. Note the two key parts: the statement of the reason and the Qur'anic evidence.
The word 'المتشرد' is uncertain in the OCR; it likely means 'المتفرد' (the unique).
2
Identify the core argument
The first reason is that intercession involves 'تقدير الأمر' (estimation of the affair) by one who is ignorant. Identify why this is problematic according to the author.
Think about who would be doing the interceding and what knowledge they would need.
3
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
The author cites two verses: the gathering of the messengers where they say 'we have no knowledge', and Jesus saying he only said what he was commanded. Reflect on how these verses support the argument.
These verses show that even the prophets disclaim knowledge of people's deeds.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that the servants of Allah disavowed knowledge and acknowledged that Allah alone possesses it. This undercuts any basis for intercession that relies on such knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فنقول وبالله التوفيق : الوجه في الآخرة لا معنى له لوجهين : أحدهما أنه فى تقدير الأمر عند من يجهله ، والله جل ثناؤه هو العليم بحقيقة ذلك . بل غيره مما يجوز عليه خفاء الحقايق كقوله يوم يجمع الله الرسل فيقول : مَاذَا أَجَيْتُم قالوا لا علم لنا ، وقال عيسى : مَا قُلْتُ لَهُمْ إِلَّا مَا أَمَرْتَنِي بِهِ فكان في ذلك عبد الله ، وهم قد تبروا عن العلم بذلك وأقروا بأن الله هو المتشرد بعلم ذلك . ولا قوة إلا بالله . والثانى أن ثمة كتب يقرأ فيها أعمال بني آدم وما سبق منهم من صغير أو كبير فهى الكافية في التقدير إن كان في حق الاحتجاج ، وإن كان في حق الإعلام فعلم الله يهم مغن عن ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: So we say, and with Allah is success: The matter of intercession in the afterlife has no meaning for two reasons: First, it is an estimation of the affair by one who is ignorant of it, while Allah, exalted is His praise, is the Knower of the reality of that. Rather, others are those upon who concealment of truths is possible, as in His saying (the day Allah gathers the messengers and says): 'What answer were you given?' They say: 'We have no knowledge.' And Jesus said: 'I said to them only what You commanded me.' So in that, the servants of Allah disavowed knowledge of that and acknowledged that Allah is the unique possessor of knowledge of that. And there is no power except with Allah. Second, there are books in which the deeds of the children of Adam and what preceded from them, small or great, are read; they are sufficient for estimation if it is a matter of argumentation, and if it is a matter of notification, then the knowledge of Allah is sufficient for that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 965

Commentary
This passage contains Imam Maturidi's refutation of intercession in the afterlife. He begins by stating that intercession has no meaning in the afterlife for two reasons. The first reason is that intercession involves estimating or judging the state of someone by a person who is ignorant of that person's true state, whereas Allah alone knows the full reality. He supports this by citing Qur'anic verses where the messengers on the Day of Judgment disclaim knowledge of their communities' responses, and Jesus declares he only said what he was commanded. This shows that even the prophets disavow knowledge of matters that belong only to Allah. Therefore, any intercession that presupposes knowledge of another's status is invalid. The second reason is that there are books (presumably the records of deeds) that contain all human actions, small and great. These books are sufficient for estimating a person's status if the matter is one of argumentation; and if it is a matter of notification, then Allah's knowledge is enough. Thus, there is no need for intercession as a means of informing or arguing about a person's state.

Key Terms
الوجه في الآخرة
تقدير الأمر
الرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets and angels know the deeds and states of all people, so they can intercede based on that knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows that even prophets disclaim knowledge of people's responses and deeds, so intercession cannot rely on such knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Imam Maturidi gives against intercession in the afterlife?
A) It is a form of shirk
B) It involves estimation by an ignorant person
C) It is forbidden by the Qur'an
D) It only applies to sinners

Key Takeaway
Intercession in the afterlife is invalid because it requires knowledge of others' deeds that only Allah possesses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson shows that even the prophets disavowed knowledge of their followers' deeds. Have you ever put your trust in a teacher, shaykh, or righteous person to guarantee your standing with Allah, rather than relying on your own obedience and sincerity? What would change if you truly believed that no one can intercede based on knowing your inner state?

Second Reason: Records of Deeds are Sufficient
Maturidi gives the second reason against intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Maturidi's second argument that intercession in the afterlife is unnecessary because the records of deeds are sufficient for both argumentation and notification.

Core Idea
Books of deeds suffice; Allah's knowledge makes intercession redundant.

Imam Maturidi presents his second reason that intercession in the afterlife has no meaning: there exist books that record all human deeds, both small and great. These records are sufficient for any possible purpose that intercession might serve. If the intercession is meant to argue or plead on someone's behalf, the records themselves provide the evidence. If it is meant to inform Allah of someone's state, Allah already knows everything, so such notification is redundant.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the second reason. Notice the two scenarios mentioned: 'الاحتجاج' (argumentation) and 'الإعلام' (notification).
The word 'يهم' is uncertain; it likely means 'يعم' (encompasses) or 'يكفي' (suffices).
2
Understand the two scenarios
The author considers two possible purposes for intercession: argumentation (pleading a case) or notification (informing Allah). He argues that in both cases, intercession is unnecessary.
Think about what intercession is supposed to accomplish.
3
Analyze the sufficiency of records
The 'كتب' (books) contain all deeds. For argumentation, they are sufficient evidence. For notification, Allah's knowledge is enough because He already knows all.
This directly refutes any need for an intercessor to provide information or argue.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. This reinforces that all power and knowledge belong to Allah alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : فنقول وبالله التوفيق : الوجه في الآخرة لا معنى له لوجهين : أحدهما أنه فى تقدير الأمر عند من يجهله ، والله جل ثناؤه هو العليم بحقيقة ذلك . بل غيره مما يجوز عليه خفاء الحقايق كقوله يوم يجمع الله الرسل فيقول : مَاذَا أَجَيْتُم قالوا لا علم لنا ، وقال عيسى : مَا قُلْتُ لَهُمْ إِلَّا مَا أَمَرْتَنِي بِهِ فكان في ذلك عبد الله ، وهم قد تبروا عن العلم بذلك وأقروا بأن الله هو المتشرد بعلم ذلك . ولا قوة إلا بالله . والثانى أن ثمة كتب يقرأ فيها أعمال بني آدم وما سبق منهم من صغير أو كبير فهى الكافية في التقدير إن كان في حق الاحتجاج ، وإن كان في حق الإعلام فعلم الله يهم مغن عن ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: So we say, and with Allah is success: The matter of intercession in the afterlife has no meaning for two reasons: First, it is an estimation of the affair by one who is ignorant of it, while Allah, exalted is His praise, is the Knower of the reality of that. Rather, others are those upon who concealment of truths is possible, as in His saying (the day Allah gathers the messengers and says): 'What answer were you given?' They say: 'We have no knowledge.' And Jesus said: 'I said to them only what You commanded me.' So in that, the servants of Allah disavowed knowledge of that and acknowledged that Allah is the unique possessor of knowledge of that. And there is no power except with Allah. Second, there are books in which the deeds of the children of Adam and what preceded from them, small or great, are read; they are sufficient for estimation if it is a matter of argumentation, and if it is a matter of notification, then the knowledge of Allah is sufficient for that. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 965

Commentary
This passage contains Imam Maturidi's refutation of intercession in the afterlife. He begins by stating that intercession has no meaning in the afterlife for two reasons. The first reason is that intercession involves estimating or judging the state of someone by a person who is ignorant of that person's true state, whereas Allah alone knows the full reality. He supports this by citing Qur'anic verses where the messengers on the Day of Judgment disclaim knowledge of their communities' responses, and Jesus declares he only said what he was commanded. This shows that even the prophets disavow knowledge of matters that belong only to Allah. Therefore, any intercession that presupposes knowledge of another's status is invalid. The second reason is that there are books (presumably the records of deeds) that contain all human actions, small and great. These books are sufficient for estimating a person's status if the matter is one of argumentation; and if it is a matter of notification, then Allah's knowledge is enough. Thus, there is no need for intercession as a means of informing or arguing about a person's state.

Key Terms
كتب الأعمال
الاحتجاج
الإعلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intercession is needed to inform Allah of a person's good deeds or to plead their case.
Correction: The passage argues that records of deeds are sufficient for any argument, and Allah's knowledge makes notification redundant.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what makes intercession unnecessary in the afterlife?
A) The prophets will intercede regardless
B) The records of deeds are sufficient for argumentation and Allah's knowledge for notification
C) Intercession is only for believers
D) The angels will argue on behalf of people

Key Takeaway
The records of deeds and Allah's omniscience make intercession in the afterlife completely unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your record of deeds alone stands before Allah on Judgment Day, with no intercessor to change it, do you live today as if every small and great action is being written with ultimate significance? How will you change the way you view your daily choices?
?
The passage says Allah's knowledge makes any 'notification' via intercession unnecessary. When you sin, do you ever think, 'I'll ask someone to intercede for me later,' neglecting that Allah already knows your state and that your deeds are already recorded? What is your heart truly relying on?
?
Imam Maturidi says the books are sufficient for argumentation. If your deeds were the only evidence presented, would you feel confident in your case before Allah? What would you plead, and how does that motivate you to act now?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Rational Proof: Faith Is in the Heart
Maturidi presents a rational argument that faith is tasdiq in the heart, not verbal declaration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that true faith is internal affirmation in the heart, not mere verbal utterance.

Core Idea
Faith is a heart's bond, not a tongue's phrase.

Imam Maturidi uses reason to show that faith cannot be simply what we say with our lips. He points to situations where speech is impossible or forbidden—like during prayer—yet a person remains a believer. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage (even if corrupted) and the English translation to grasp the flow of Maturidi's argument.
The Arabic text is difficult; rely on the translation for clarity.
2
Identify the core claim
Notice that Maturidi begins by stating that faith (iman) is tasdiq in the language, and its reality is in the heart, not the tongue.
3
Follow the prayer objection
Focus on the example of prayer: during salah, a person is forbidden to say 'I believe...' yet is still a believer. This shows faith cannot be mere speech.
This is the key rational evidence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that faith is permanent, not subject to abrogation, and resides in the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل فلأنه دين، والأديان تعقد، وما به اعتقادات الأديان القلوب، ما كان الإيمان في اللغة التصديق. وحقيقته الذي لا ذلك المذاهب. مع امل القهر والجبر، هو الدين في القلب؛ إذ لا يجري سلطان أحد من الخلق، ثلة ذلك أنه يجوز أن لا يكون لسان ولا يحتمل رفع الدين الحق ولا الإيمان صبع ما كان ذلك من المحال أحد، ثبت أن والرسل من حق ذلك القلب ناع فعل الإيمان عن الممتحن في حال الخطاب بحال وباللسان عامة الأوقات الخلق يمر بدونه بل من الأحوال أحوال ينهى المرء فيه أن يقول: آمنت بالكتب والنبيين والبعث ونحو ذلك، نحو الكون في الصلاة، فيصير الإيمان على القول بحيث ينهى، ودين الإسلام بحيث يفسد عبادته، والله جعله شرطا نواز وجعله دائما لا يتغير ولا يتبدل ولا يجوز فيه النسخ، ثبت أنه على غير.
As for reason, because it is a religion, and religions are contracted, and what with it are beliefs of religions are hearts. Faith in language is tasdiq (affirmation). And its reality which is not that of the sects. With the hope of coercion and compulsion, it is the religion in the heart; since no authority of any creature runs over it. A group of that is that it is possible that there be no tongue, nor does it bear the lifting of the true religion nor faith at all, for that is impossible. It is established that the messengers from the right of that heart [unclear] the act of faith from the tested one in the state of address, and by the tongue generally the creatures pass without it. Rather, from the states are states in which a person is forbidden to say: I believe in the books, prophets, resurrection and the like, such as being in prayer. Then faith becomes by speech in a way that is forbidden, and the religion of Islam in a way that corrupts his worship. And God made it a condition [unclear] and made it permanent, not changing, not altering, and not subject to abrogation. It is established that it is upon other.
Source pages p. 976

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) that true faith (iman) is a matter of the heart, not the tongue. Imam Maturidi begins by asserting that religion is a bond (din) that is contracted, and what underlies religious beliefs is the heart. He notes that in the Arabic language, faith is tasdiq (affirmation or assent), and its reality is not what various sects claim—especially those who equate faith with mere verbal declaration under coercion. He argues that the heart is the seat of faith because no human authority can control it, whereas the tongue can be compelled. He then provides a key argument: there are circumstances in which a person has no tongue (e.g., mute) or is forbidden to speak—such as during prayer. In prayer, one is prohibited from making a verbal declaration of faith (e.g., 'I believe in the books, prophets, resurrection'), yet one is still considered a believer. If faith were merely speech, then forbidding it during prayer would mean that the person is not a believer at that moment, which is absurd. Furthermore, the religion of Islam would be corrupted if one's worship were invalidated by the absence of speech. Maturidi concludes that faith is an internal, permanent condition that does not change with the tongue's actions, and it cannot be subject to abrogation.

Key Terms
عقل (reason)
دين (religion)
تصديق (affirmation)
قلب (heart)
صلاة (prayer)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that faith is simply the verbal utterance of the shahada or other declarations.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this view leads to contradictions, such as a person in prayer being unable to speak yet still being a believer.

Checkpoint
What example does Maturidi give to prove that faith cannot be merely verbal declaration?
A) The obligation of zakat
B) The prohibition of speaking during prayer
C) The story of Prophet Ibrahim
D) The event of Isra and Mi'raj

Key Takeaway
Faith is an internal affirmation in the heart, not a verbal formula, as shown by the impossibility of maintaining faith if it were tied to speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I believe' with your tongue, is it always a true reflection of what is settled in your heart? Or have you ever spoken words of faith while your heart was distracted or doubtful? What would it take for your heart to truly affirm what your tongue says?
?
In moments when you cannot express your faith—perhaps out of fear, or in a situation where it is forbidden to speak—does your heart remain steady? Or do you feel a gap between your inner certainty and outer silence? How can you strengthen the heart's bond so that no external circumstance can shake it?
?
Consider a time when you performed an act of worship, like prayer, while your tongue was silent but your mind wandered. Was that still an act of faith? Maturidi argues that faith is the heart's permanent condition, not momentary speech. Does your heart's condition change with your moods, or is it anchored in a conviction that does not waver? If it wavers, what are you willing to change today to make it firm?
